Chapter

Wave and Oscillation

Any motions that repeat themselves are called oscillation. But any motion or event that repeats itself at regular interval of time is said to be periodic motion. The motions of earth round the sun, the swinging motion of pendulum, the vibration of guitar string are some examples of periodic motion, and oscillatory motion.

In above examples of mechanical oscillations, the body undergoes linear or angular displacement. There are some non mechanical oscillations also that involve the variation of quantities such as voltage, charge or even the electric and magnetic field. These are the examples of electromagnetic oscillations.

1.1 Simple Harmonic Oscillator

When an object moves in an oscillatory motion such that its acceleration is always directed toward a certain fixed point and varies directly as its distance from that point, the object is said to execute simple harmonic motion (SHM). The fixed point of reference is referred as the mean position or centre.

The force acting on the body for such motion to take place should be directed towards that fixed point and should be proportional to the displacement of the object from that fixed point. The force acted restores the object back to its equilibrium position. Therefore such force is often termed as restoring force.

Therefore for SHM to take place,
* the motion should be periodic
* the object if displaced from mean position, a restoring force tending to bring it to the mean position and directed towards the mean position must act on the object
* the restoring force should be directly proportional to displacement of the body from its mean position.

1.2 Differential Equation of Simple Harmonic Oscillator

Consider a particle of mass m executes Simple Harmonic Motion. Let the displacement of the particle at any instant t be y. The restoring force F acting on the particle by definition is,


[Page 3]
2

F ∝ y ... 1.1
or, F = -ky
Where k is the constant of proportionality and is called force constant or stiffness factor. The negative sign in equation 1.1 indicates that restoring force and the displacement are in opposite direction which is necessary for repeated periodic motion.

Since F = mass × acceleration = m (d²y)/(dt²), so equation 1.1 becomes,

m (d²y)/(dt²) + ky = 0

Or, (d²y)/(dt²) + k/m y = 0

Here, ω = √(k/m) and is often called the angular velocity of object executing SHM. So,

(d²y)/(dt²) + ω²y = 0 ... 1.2

Equation 1.2 is the general differential equation of SHM.

Example 1.1
An oscillatory motion of a body is represented by y = a e^(iωt) where y is displacement in time t, a is its amplitude and ω is angular frequency. Show that the motion is simple harmonic.
Solution:
Here y = a e^(iωt)
Differentiating,
dy/dt = iω a e^(iωt)
Making second differentiation again,
d²y/dt² = -ω² a e^(iωt) = -ω² y
or, d²y/dt² + ω²y = 0.
This is the differential equation of SHM. So y = a e^(iωt) represents SHM.

Example 1.2
Show that the velocity of simple harmonic oscillator at any instant leads the displacement by a phase angle of π/2. Hence find the general expression for the velocity of a simple harmonic oscillator.
Solution:
We know that the displacement of a simple harmonic oscillator at any instant of time t can be written as
y = a sin (ωt + φ)
The velocity is the rate of change of displacement so, velocity

v = dy/dt = aω cos (ωt + φ)
= aω sin (ωt + φ + π/2)
Comparing the equations for displacement and velocity, the velocity of simple harmonic oscillator at any instant t leads the displacement by a phase difference of π/2. However, the velocity varies simple harmonically with the same frequency.
But sin (ωt + φ) = y/a
∴ cos (ωt + φ) = √(1 - sin²(ωt+φ)) = √(1 - y²/a²) = √(a² - y²)/a
Also v = aω cos (ωt + φ) = aω √(a² - y²)/a = ω √(a² - y²)
This velocity will be maximum if displacement is zero. Therefore, Vmax = ω a at mean position.

1.3 The Block-Spring System
Consider a block of mass m is oscillating at the end of a mass less spring as shown in figure 1.1. Lets assume that the net force acting on the block is that exerted by the spring, which is obtained from Hook's law.
F = - kx
Where x is the displacement from the equilibrium position. When x is positive, F is negative. In this way, the force always tends to restore the block to its equilibrium position x = 0. From Newton's law the force in the block is
F = ma
or, a = F/m = - k/m x

This shows the acceleration is directly proportional to the displacement and is always in the opposite direction. And this is the requirement for SHM. The acceleration a is d²x/dt² therefore we can write,

d²x/dt² + k/m x = 0 ... 1.3

Figure 1.1 The restoring force in a block spring system

Comparing this equation with differential equation of SHM, the block-spring system executes simple harmonic motion with an angular frequency ω given by


[Page 4]
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

ω = √(k/m) ... 1.4

In this way the time period of a block-spring system is given by,

T = 2π/ω = 2π √(m/k) ... 1.5

The period is independent of the amplitude which is requirement for SHM. From the equation 1.5, for a given spring constant (k), the period increases with the mass of the block. Therefore a massive block oscillates more slowly. For a given block, the period decreases as k increases indicating a stiffer spring produces a quiker oscillations.

Example 1.3
A 2 kg mass hangs from a spring. A 300 g body hung below the mass stretches the spring 2 cm further. If the 300 g body is removed and the mass is set in oscillation, find the period of motion.

Solution:
When 300 g body is hung, the spring stretches 2 cm further. So,
F = mg = kx
or, k = mg/x = (0.300 x 9.8 x 100)/2 = 147 N/m.

Again time period of oscillation,
T = 2π √(m/k) = 2π √(2/147) = 0.73 sec.

3.4 Energy in Simple Harmonic Motion
The force exerted by an ideal spring is conservative. If there is no friction, the energy of simple harmonic motion like block-spring system is constant. The solution of differential equation of SHM is,

x = A sin (ωt + φ)

The potential energy of the system is,

U = 1/2 kx² = 1/2 kA² sin² (ωt + φ) ... 1.6

Similarly kinetic energy of the system is,

K = 1/2 mv² = 1/2 m(dx/dt)² = 1/2 mω² A² cos² (ωt + φ) ... 1.7

Therefore the total mechanical energy,

E = K + U = 1/2 mω² A² cos² (ωt + φ) + 1/2 kA² sin² (ωt + φ)

or, E = 1/2 m (k/m) A² cos² (ωt + φ) + 1/2 kA² sin² (ωt + φ) [ ... ω² = k/m ]

or, E = 1/2 kA² ... 1.8

This is the total energy of a block-spring system. It shows the total energy of any simple harmonic oscillator is constant. The total energy is also found to be proportional to the square of the amplitude. The variation of K and U as function of displacement x is shown in figure 1.2. When x = ± A, k is zero and the total energy is equal to the maximum U, E = Umax = 1/2 kA².

Similarly when x = 0, U is zero and the total energy is equal to the maximum K.E. = Kmax = 1/2 kA². So the total energy in simple harmonic motion is a constant quantity.

1.5 Simple Pendulum
A simple pendulum is an idealized system in which a point mass is suspended at one end of a mass-less string. Consider a simple pendulum of length l and a bob of mass m is suspended at the free end of the pendulum as shown in figure 1.3. The other end of the string is suspended at the rigid support. The distance along the arc from the lowest point is x = lθ, where θ is the radian angle made to the vertical. The weight of the mass is mg and acts vertically downward. This force due to gravity can be resolved into two rectangular components; mg cos θ is the force along the string and mg sin θ perpendicular to the string. The tension on the string is T and is balanced by the component mg cos θ. The Newtonian force is balanced by the component of force due to gravity along the perpendicular direction:

-mg sin θ = m (d²x/dt²) ... 1.9

The negative sign is for the way x is defined. Therefore this component of force due to gravity acts as restoring force. From Taylor's series of expansion one can write,

sin θ = θ - θ³/3! + θ⁵/5! - ...

Thus for small angles sin θ ≈ θ where θ is in radians. From circular measurement

x = lθ


[Page 5]
So we can write,
d²x/dt² = l d²θ/dt²

Now equation 1.9 becomes,
d²θ/dt² + (g/l)θ = 0 ... 1.10

This equation is similar to the equation of simple harmonic motion
d²y/dt² + ω²y = 0

Therefore within the small angle approximation, simple pendulum execute simple harmonic motion with an angular frequency
ω = √(g/l)

Providing the time period
T = 2π/ω = 2π √(l/g) ... 1.11

The time period does not depend on either the mass or amplitude. The solution of equation 1.10 is
θ = θ₀ sin (ωt + φ)
Where θ₀ is angular amplitude.

1.6 The Physical Pendulum
A rigid mass capable of oscillating about a horizontal axis passing through any point of the mass constitutes a physical pendulum. Such type of arrangement executes simple harmonic motion for small angular displacements. A practical example of a physical pendulum is an arm or a leg of animals. Consider a rigid object of mass m be suspended at point O. The centre of gravity of the object is G and is at a distance of l from O. Since l is the distance from the pivot to the centre of mass, the restoring torque is mgl sin θ, toward decreasing θ. From the rotational dynamics the torque produced τ is given by τ = I α, with α as the angular acceleration.

So we may write for balanced condition,
- mgl sin θ = I d²θ/dt²

Here I is the moment of inertia about the given axis. For small angular displacement we may write
d²θ/dt² + (mgl/I)θ = 0 ... 1.12

This is the equation for simple harmonic motion indicating the motion of physical pendulum is simple harmonic. The moment of inertia of the pendulum about an axis passing through O and perpendicular to its plane is mK² + ml², K being the radius of gyration about an axis passing through the centre of gravity of the pendulum. Therefore the above equation will become,
d²θ/dt² + (mgl / (mK² + ml²))θ = 0.

Comparing this equation with differential equation of SHM, the time period of oscillation will become,
T = 2π √((K² + l²) / lg) ... 1.13
or,
T = 2π √((K²/l + l) / g)

The quantity (K²/l + l) is called the equivalent length of the simple pendulum. If the location of the centre of mass and l are known then a measurement of the time period allows us to determine the moment of inertia of the body.

If the angular amplitude of a pendulum is large, the small angle approximation will not be valid. This results the oscillations are no longer simple harmonic. The period increases as the angular amplitude increases. However frictional losses cause the amplitude and thereafter the period of physical pendulum to diminish in time.

The point O' at a distance equal to the length of an equivalent simple pendulum from the point of suspension on straight line passing through centre of gravity is centre of oscillation.

1.7 Bar Pendulum
A bar pendulum consists of a metal bar. It has a large number of holes as shown in figure 1.5. Two knife edges are kept which fit into the holes. It is an example of physical pendulum. The time period can be measured by fixing the knife edges in each corresponding holes. The distance of each hole from the centre of gravity is measured. If a graph between


[Page 6]
8 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Wave and Oscillation 9

the distance from centre of gravity and corresponding time period is plotted, it looks like as shown in figure 1.6.

Figure 1.5 A Bar Pendulum
Figure 1.6 Graph between time period and distance from centre of gravity in a bar pendulum.

It can be seen from the graph that there are two points on each side of the bar where the time periods are equal. In total there are four points in the pendulum where the time periods are equal, (the points A, B, C and D in figure 1.6).

The time period of the bar pendulum is given by equation 1.13 as,

T = 2 π sqrt((K^2 + l^2) / lg)

or, T^2 = 4 π^2 ((K^2 + l^2) / lg)

or, 4 π^2 K^2 + 4 π^2 l^2 - T^2 lg = 0

or, 4 π^2 l^2 - T^2 lg + 4 π^2 K^2 = 0

Solving this quadratic equation,

l = (gT^2 +/- sqrt(g^2 T^4 - 64 π^4 K^2)) / 8 π^2 ... 1.14

It is clear from this equation 1.14 that there are two different points having equal value of time period T, on each side of the bar pendulum.

1.8 Determination of Radius of Gyration (K)

Two points not equal from centre of gravity G in the bar pendulum are Q and O' on both sides of it. One corresponds to centre of suspension and other as centre of oscillation. Let their distances be l1 and l2 from the centre of gravity as shown. Thus the equivalent length of the pendulum is L = l1 + l2. Using the relation for time period we can write

T = 2 π sqrt((K^2 + l1^2) / l1 g) = 2 π sqrt((K^2/l1 + l1) / g)

In other way,

T = 2 π sqrt(L/g)

So we can write,

K^2 / l1 + l1 = L = l1 + l2

or, K^2 = l1 l2 ... 1.15

In this way the value of radius of gyration K about the axis passing through the centre of gravity of a bar pendulum can be determined.

1.9 Points of Suspension and Oscillation are Interchangeable

Let the time period be T1 for a point A at distance l1 from centre of gravity. Similarly the time period be T2 for a point C at distance l2 from centre of gravity in figure 1.6. Then periods are,

T1 = 2 π sqrt((K^2 + l1^2) / l1 g) = 2 π sqrt((K^2/l1 + l1) / g)

and T2 = 2 π sqrt((K^2 + l2^2) / l2 g) = 2 π sqrt((K^2/l2 + l2) / g)

But from equation 1.15 we have,

K^2 = l1 l2

So, K^2 / l1 = l2 and K^2 / l2 = l1

Figure 1.7 Point of oscillation and point of suspension


[Page 7]
10
Hence K²/l₁ + l₁ = K²/l₂ + l₂ = l₁ + l₂
∴ T₁ = T₂
This proves the points of suspension and oscillation are interchangeable. If A is the point of suspension C is the point of oscillation. If C is the point of suspension A is the point of oscillation.

1.10 Minimum Time Period
We know the time period of a physical pendulum is given by
T = 2 π √((K² + l₁²)/(l₁g))

Where l₁ is the distance of the point of suspension from centre of gravity and K is the radius of gyration about the centre of gravity. If point of oscillation from centre of gravity is l₂ then
K²/l₁ = l₂

In the equation of time period, the time period will be minimum if K²/l₁ + l₁ is
minimum. Differentiating K²/l₁ + l₁ with respect to l₁ we get

d/dl₁ [K²/l₁ + l₁] = - K²/l₁² + 1

For the time period to be minimum the differentiated quantity should be equal to zero.

- K²/l₁² + 1 = 0
or, K² = l₁²
or, l₁ l₂ = l₁²
∴ l₁ = l₂.

Therefore the time period will be minimum when the points of suspension and point of oscillation are equidistance from the centre of gravity. If the point of suspension is at centre of gravity, l = 0. In such case the time period will be infinity.

Example 1.4
The angular displacement of a simple pendulum is given by θ = 0.1 π sin (2πt + π/6) rad. The mass of the body is 0.4 kg. When θ = 0.05 π find (a) the length of the simple pendulum, (b) the velocity of the bob at t = 0.25 sec.

Solution:
(a) Comparing the given equation with θ = θ₀ sin (ωt + Φ)
θ₀ = 0.1 π rad
Φ = π/6
ω = 2 π rad / s.
ω² = g/l
Now,
or, l = g/ω² = 9.8 m/s² / (2π rad / s)² = 0.25 m

(b) Again
x = lθ
dx/dt = l dθ/dt
or, v = (0.25 m) d/dt [0.1 π sin (2πt + π/6)]
= (0.25 m) (0.1 π) (2 π) cos (2πt + π/6)
= 0.064 m/s

Example 1.5
A heavy circular ring of radius R oscillates in a vertical plane about a horizontal axis at a distance x from the centre. Show that the time period is minimum when x = R

Solution:
The radius of gyration for a circular ring about an axis passing through the centre of gravity and perpendicular to its planes is, K² = R²
or, K = R.
Here l₁ = x
For the time period to be minimum
l₁ = l₂
or, K² = l₁l₂ = l₁² = x²
Since K² = R²
or, x² = R²
or, x = R.

Example 1.6
A thin, straight, uniform rod of length l = 1 m and mass m 100 g hangs from a pivot at one end. (a) What is its period for small oscillations? (b) What is the length of a simple pendulum that will have the same period?

Solution:
The moment of inertia of a thin rod about an axis through one end is, I = 1/3 ml². Since centre of mass
is at the centre, h = l/2, then


[Page 8]
12 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

T = 2π √ (I / mgh) = 2π √ (ml^2 * 2 / 3 * m * g * l)
= 2π √ (2l / 3g) = 1.64 sec.
To have the same period, a simple pendulum must have a length L.
T = 2π √ (L / g)

or, L = gT^2 / 4π^2 = g / 4π^2 * 4π^2 * (2l / 3g) = 2l / 3.

1.11 Torsional Pendulum

Consider a body such as a disc, suspended at the end of a wire as shown in figure 1.8. The other end of the wire is fixed at a rigid support. When the disc is rotated, the wire is twisted by an angle θ. In this case the restoring torque is created on it obeying Hook's law. The disc is set into torsional vibration. The restoring torque is directly proportional to the angular displacement of the wire.

τ ∝ θ
or, τ = -Cθ ... 1.16

Where C is called the couple per unit twist or torsional constant. Mathematically it is πηr^4 / 2l where r = radius and l is length of the wire. Such an oscillating system is called torsional pendulum. The rotational form of Newton's second law, τ = Iα, is,

τ = I * d^2θ / dt^2 ... 1.17

From equation 1.16 and 1.17 we may write,

d^2θ / dt^2 + C / I * θ = 0 ... 1.18

This is the equation of a simple angular harmonic oscillator with an angular frequency

ω = √(C / I) ... 1.19

and a period,

Wave and Oscillation 13

T = 2π √(I / C) ... 1.20

Here we have not used the small angle approximation. The motion is SHM as long as the elastic limit of the system is not exceeded. The balance wheel in a hairspring in a watch is an example of torsional pendulum.

Let one regular body of given moment of inertia I1 is placed on the disc. The new time period is,

T1 = 2π √(I + I1 / C) ... 1.21

Similarly if one irregular object of moment of inertia I2 is placed, then the time period is,

T2 = 2π √(I + I2 / C) ... 1.22

From equation 1.20 and 1.21,

T1^2 / T^2 = I + I1 / I

or, T1^2 - T^2 / T^2 = I1 / I ... 1.23

From equation 1.20 and 1.22, one can write

T2^2 / T^2 = I + I2 / I

or, T2^2 - T^2 / T^2 = I2 / I ... 1.24

Now from equation 1.23 and 1.24,

T2^2 - T^2 / T1^2 - T^2 = I2 / I1

or, I2 = (T2^2 - T^2 / T1^2 - T^2) I1 ... 1.25

In this way by knowing the time period for regular and irregular objects, the moment of inertia of an unknown object can be calculated.


[Page 9]
14 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Wave and Oscillation 15

Example 1.7
A wire has a torsional constant of 2 N.m/rad. A disk of radius R = 5 cm and mass M = 100 g is suspended at its centre. What is the frequency of torsional oscillations?

Solution:
The moment of inertia of a disc is 1/2 M R². The time period of a torsional pendulum is given by
T = 2π √I/C
Thus the frequency is
f = 1/T = 1/2π √C/I = 1/2π √(2 * 2) / (M R²)
= 1/2π * 4 / √(0.1 * (0.05)²)
= 20.13 Hz.

Example 1.8
A solid sphere of mass 2 kg and diameter 0.30 m is suspended on a wire. Find the period of angular oscillation for small displacements if the torque constant of the wire is 6x10⁻³ Nm/rad.

Solution:
We know time period of a torsional pendulum is,
T = 2π √I/C
= 1/2π √(2 * 2 * (0.15)² / 5 * 6 * 10⁻³) [I = 2/5 m R²]
= 10.9 sec.

1.12 Free Oscillation
Consider a simple pendulum in vacuum. If its bob is displaced from its mean position and released, it executes SHM. The time period of oscillation of such pendulum depends on the length of the pendulum and the acceleration due to gravity. The pendulum continues to oscillate for infinite time with the same time period. Therefore there is no loss of energy by friction. The amplitude of such vibration remains unchanged. Such vibration is called free or undamped vibrations.

For a simple harmonically vibrating particle, let the displacement made by the mass m be x. The kinetic energy in such case is given by,
K = 1/2 m (dx/dt)²

The potential energy of the system is 1/2 k x² where k is the restoring force per unit displacement. Hence the total energy of the system at any instant will become,
E = 1/2 m (dx/dt)² + 1/2 k x²

The total energy remains constant in free vibration
∴ 1/2 m (dx/dt)² + 1/2 k x² = constant ... 126

Differentiating this equation with respect to time,
1/2 m * 2 * dx/dt * d²x/dt² + 1/2 k * 2x * dx/dt = 0
Or, d²x/dt² + k/m x = 0 ... 127
This equation is similar to differential equation of SHM providing ω² = k/m. The solution of such an equation is x = a sin ωt. So the general solution of undamped free oscillation as in equation 1.27 will be,
x = a sin (√(k/m) t) ... 128
Which gives the frequency of oscillation as,
f = 1/2π √(k/m) ... 129

1.13 Damped Oscillation
So far we have ignored the inevitable energy losses that occur in real situations. Such losses may arise from external resistance or from internal friction within a system. This type of oscillation is damped oscillation. Practically all oscillations are damped. The energy of such a damped oscillator decreases in time. This causes the amplitude also to decreases in time.

For a pendulum there is frictional force of air. Hence energy is dissipated in each vibration. After a long time the vibration die out. Such a vibration is called damped vibration. The dissipative force is proportional to velocity of particle at that instant, and velocity decreases exponentially in the time. So
F = -b dx/dt ... 130
Where b is the damping constant and is measured in kg/s. The differential equation of motion in this case will be,
F = -kx - b dx/dt = m d²x/dt²


[Page 10]
16 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

or, m d²x/dt² + b dx/dt + kx = 0.
or, d²x/dt² + (b/m) dx/dt + (k/m)x = 0 ... 1.31

This form of differential equation arises in other mechanical or non mechanical damped oscillation as well. The solution to equation 1.31 is
x = a e^{-(b/2m)t} sin (w't) ... 1.32

The damped angular frequency w' is given by,
w' = sqrt(k/m - b²/4m²) ... 1.33

and thus the frequency is,
f' = w'/2π = 1/2π sqrt(k/m - b²/4m²)

The damped angular frequency w' is less than the natural angular frequency (w₀),
w₀ = sqrt(k/m). If there is no friction then b would be zero and w' is again equal to sqrt(k/m).

From the exponential relation in the solution in equation 1.32, the amplitude decreases to zero in long time. The time duration in which the amplitude drops to 1/e of its initial value is called mean life time of oscillation. In equation 1.32, the amplitude drops to 1/e of its initial value if t = τ = 2m/b and is the mean life time.

(i) For w' to be real, b/2m < w₀ or b < 2 m w₀ must be satisfied. When w' is real, the oscillations are under damped as indicated in figure 1.9. The amplitude thus decays in under damped case as,
A = A₀ e^{-(b/2m)t}

(ii) When b > 2 m w₀ (b² > 4 m k), this results w' is an imaginary number. In such case there is no oscillation and the system moves slowly back to its equilibrium situation. Such an oscillation is called overdamped.

(iii) In equation 1.33 if b = 2 m w₀, then w' = 0. In such case also there will be no oscillation. This situation is the representation of critical damping. In critical damping the system returns to equilibrium condition in shortest time. Such oscillations are used in the movements of electrical meters to damp the oscillations of the needle.

We should note here the relation for damped energy is
E_avg = 1/2 m w₀² x² e^{-(b/m)t}

Example 1.9
A mass of 1 kg is suspended from a spring of spring constant 25 N/m. If the undamped frequency is 2/√3 times the damped frequency, what will be the damping factor?

Solution:
We know the damped frequency f' is given by
f' = 1/2π sqrt(k/m - b²/4m²)
If there is no damping b = 0. In such case undamped frequency,
f = 1/2π sqrt(k/m)
Given, f/f' = 2/√3
sqrt(k/m) / sqrt(k/m - b²/4m²) = 2/√3
k/m / (k/m - b²/4m²) = 4/3
or, (k/m) / (k/m - b²/4m²) = 4/3
or, 3(k/m) = 4(k/m) - 4(b²/4m²)
or, 3(k/m) = 4(k/m) - b²/m²
or, k/m = b²/m²
or, b² = k m
b² = 25 * 1
b = 5 kg/s

Example 1.10
A 0.5 kg block is attached to a spring (k = 12.5 N/m). The damped frequency is 0.2 π lower than the natural frequency. (a) What is the damping constant? (b) How does the amplitude vary in time? (c) what is critical damping constant?


[Page 11]
18 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Solution:
(a) The natural frequency is,
ω₀ = √(k/m) = √(12.5/0.5) = 5 rad/s
The damped frequency ω' = 0.998 ω₀ = 4.99 rad/s

Also,
ω' = √(ω₀² - (b/2m)²)
or, (b/2m)² = ω₀² - (ω')²
or, (b/2m)² = 4 = (5² - 4.99²)
or, b = 0.316 kg/sec

(b) The amplitude variation with time is given by,
A(t) = A₀e^(-bt/2m)
= A₀e^(-t)

The critical damping constant is obtained when
b² = 4 mω₀
or, b = √(4mω₀)
= √(4 * 0.5 * 5)
= 3.16 kg/s
This is larger than in (a).

1.14 Forced Oscillation
Practical oscillation left to itself eventually die out after certain times. But we can maintain a constant amplitude oscillation by applying a periodic external force with a definite period and frequency. The phenomenon of setting a body into continuous vibrations with the help of a strong periodic force having a frequency different from the natural frequency of the body is called forced vibrations. A body can be forced to vibrate with any frequency depending upon that of the applied force, but the vibrations die out as soon as the applied force is removed.

Let the applied force is sinusoidal and represented as Fext = F₀ sin ωt where ω is angular frequency applied externally. Newton's second law in such a forced oscillator yields,
m (d²x/dt²) + b (dx/dt) + kx = F₀ sin ωt ... 1.34

If the force is applied, first the motion will be complex. After some time the system comes to steady state oscillation. The energy dissipated by damping is balanced with the external energy. The solution of such differential equation is,
x = A₀ sin (ωt + Φ₀) ... 1.35

Where Φ₀ is the phase angle between the displacement x and the external forces Fext. Here the amplitude A₀ remains constant. Substituting the value of x from 1.35 in 1.34 we get,
A₀ = F₀ / √(m²(ω₀² - ω²)² + b²ω²) ... 1.36

and Φ₀ = tan⁻¹ [bω / m(ω₀² - ω²)] ... 1.37

The amplitude of forced harmonic motion A₀ depends on the difference between the applied and the natural frequency. If we plot A₀ as a function of the applied frequency ω, the graph will look like as shown in figure 1.10. Furthermore the same figure shows amplitude and applied frequency for three different values of damping constant b. The curve A is the case of light damping, curve B is the case of heavy damping and curve C is the case of over damped motion. The amplitude becomes large when the applied frequency ω is near the natural frequency ω₀. When the damping is small, the amplitude near ω = ω₀, will become very large. This condition is called as the situation of resonance.

Figure 1.10 Amplitude of forced harmonic oscillator as a function of applied frequency ω.

Therefore when a periodic force of frequency equal to the natural frequency of a body is applied, the body gains amplitude slowly and finally begins to vibrate with a large amplitude. Hence resonance is defined as the phenomenon of setting a body into vibrations with its natural frequency by the application of an external periodic force of the same frequency. Such frequency is called resonant frequency. In other words when the amplitude is maximum, the state of vibration is called resonance. This resonance is also termed as amplitude resonance.

If the damping constant b = 0, resonance occurs at ω = ω₀ and the resonant peak of A₀ becomes infinite. In real system b cannot be equal to zero and this resonant peak is finite.

1.15 Quality Factor
Sharpness of resonance refers to fall in amplitude with change in frequency on each side of the maximum amplitude. As the frequency of the applied force ω is increased or decreased from its resonant value ω₀, the value of the amplitude always decreases, when the amplitude at resonance falls rapidly as the frequency ω of the applied force is changed slightly from its resonant value the resonance is said to be sharp.

The sharpness of resonance is inversely proportional to the damping constant b and can be represented in terms of quality factor Q. Quality factor of a damped oscillator defines the quality of oscillator so far as damping is concerned. Less is the damping higher is the quality factor Q. It is defined mathematically as 2π times the ratio of energy store in the


[Page 12]
20 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Wave and Oscillation 21
system to the energy lost per cycle. It is some times called figure of merit of harmonic
oscillator. The figure of merit is defined as the ratio of the frequency at velocity resonance
(maximum velocity response) to the full bandwidth at half maximum power. Quality factor or
figure of merit is a measure of sharpness of resonance. Hence,

Q = 2π Energy stored / Energy loss per cycle = mω0 / b * ω0 / ω1 - ω2 ... 1.38

The smaller the damping constant b the larger the Q value becomes and the higher the
resonance peak. The quality value is also a measure of the width of the peak. If ω1 and ω2 be
the frequencies at which the amplitude becomes A0 / √2, then the quantity (ω1 - ω2) is called the
width of the resonance peak. Thus quality factor Q is related with (ω1 - ω2) by,

Δω / ω0 = 1 / Q ... 1.39

When the quality factor has a larger value, the resonance peak will become narrower
related to its height. Hence a larger Q value represents a system of high quality with narrow
resonance peak.

Further, quality factor can also be determined from the damped harmonic oscillator. As we
know the relation for energy per cycle of damped harmonic motion is

Eavg = 1/2 mω^2 x0^2 e^-bt/m ... 1.40

As b/m = 2 δ = 1/τ, where b is the damping constant, 2δ is the logarithmic decrement
and τ is the relaxation time. Then above equation becomes

Eavg = [1/2 mω^2 x0^2] e^-2δt

or, Eavg = E0 e^-2δt ... 1.41

Now average power dissipation is given as

P = - dEavg / dt = - d/dt (E0 e^-2δt)

= 2δ (E0 e^-2δt) = 2δ Eavg

or, Eavg / P = 1 / 2δ ... 1.42

Moreover, quality factor can also be defined as 2π times the ratio of energy stored to the
average energy loss per period, that is,

Q = 2π Energy Stored / Energy loss per period

= 2π Eavg / P x T = 2π (Eavg / P) = ω/2δ (Eavg / P)

Q = ω / 2δ from Eq. 1.42

∴ Q = ωτ

Also, Q = mω / b

This relation shows that quality factor depends on the damping constant of the
oscillating system. Therefore, for free oscillation, b = 0, and quality factor is infinity.

Example 1.11
Consider the solution of differential equation in free vibration is represented by x = a e^-0.25t
sin ωt. The equation for displacement of a point on a damped oscillator is given by x = 5 e^-0.25t sin
π/2 meter. Find the velocity of the oscillating point at t = T/4, where T is the time period of oscillator.

Solution:
Comparing the given equation with the solution we get.
x = a e^-0.25t sin ωt
x = 5 e^-0.25t sin πt/2
ω = π/2
Hence T = 2π / ω = 2π / π/2 = 4 sec.
Differentiating the given solution with respect to time,
dx/dt = 5 (- 0.25) e^-0.25t sin πt/2 + 5 x π/2 e^-0.25t cos πt/2
Now, v = dx/dt at t = T/4 will be.
v = - 1.25 e^-0.25 (T/4) sin (π/2 (T/4)) + 5 x π/2 e^-0.25 (T/4) cos (π/2 (T/4))

Since T = 4 sec. T/4 = 1 sec
and v = - 1.25 e^-0.25 = - 1.25 x (0.368) = - 0.46
= - 0.974 m sec.
The negative sign indicates the velocity is in opposite direction.


[Page 13]
22 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Wave and Oscillation 23

1.16 Additional Examples:
1. For the damped oscillator of the given figure, m = 250 g, k = 85 N/m, and b = 70 g/s. (a) What is the period of the motion? (b) How long does it take for the amplitude of the damped oscillations to drop to half its initial value? (c) How long does it take for the mechanical energy to drop to one-half its initial value?

Solution:
(a) We have b < √km = 4.6 kg/s, the period is approximately that of the undamped oscillator.
Since T = 2π√m/k = 2π√(0.25kg / 85N/m) = 0.34s

(b) The amplitude at time t is displayed as xme^(-bt/2m)
The amplitude has the value xm at t = 0. Thus, we must find the value of t for which
xme^(-bt/2m) = ½xm
Cancelling xm and taking the natural logarithm of the equation that remains, we have ln(½) on the right side and
ln(e^(-bt/2m)) = -bt/2m
on the left side. Thus,
t = -2m ln(½) / b = -(2)(0.25kg) ln(½) / 0.070kg/s = 5.0 s.
Because T = 0.34 s, this is about 15 periods of oscillation.

(c) The mechanical energy at time t is ½ kxm^2 e^(-bt/m)
The mechanical energy has the value ½ kxm^2 at t = 0. Thus we must find the value of t for which
½ kxm^2 e^(-bt/m) = ½ (½ kxm^2).
If we divide both sides of this equation by ½ kxm^2 and solve for t as we did above, we find
t = -m ln(½) / b = -(0.25kg) ln(½) / 0.070kg/s = 2.5s.
This is exactly half the time we calculated in (b), or about 7.5 periods of oscillation.

2. A 0.12 kg body undergoes simple harmonic motion of amplitude 8.5 cm and period 0.20 s. (a) What is the magnitude of the maximum force acting on it? (b) If the oscillations are produced by a spring, what is the spring constant?

Solution:
(a) The acceleration amplitude is related to the maximum force by Newton's second law:
Fmax = m am.
We know that the acceleration amplitude is am = ω^2 xm,
where ω is the angular frequency (ω = 2 πf).
Again, the frequency is the reciprocal of the period: f = 1/T = 1/0.20 = 5.0 Hz,
so the angular frequency is ω = 10π (understood to be valid to two significant figures). Therefore,
Fmax = mω^2xm = (0.12 kg) (10π rad/s)^2 (0.085 m) = 10 N.

(b) But
ω = √k/m, or k = mω^2 = (0.12 kg)(10π rad/s)^2 = 1.2x10^2 N/m.

3. An oscillator consists of a block attached to a spring (k = 400 N/m). At some time t, the position (measured from the system's equilibrium location), velocity and acceleration of the block are x = 0.100 m, v = -13.6 m/s, and a = -123 m/s^2. Calculate (a) the frequency of oscillation, (b) the mass of the block and (c) the amplitude of the motion.

Solution:
(a) We have,
a = -ω^2x and ω = √-a/x = √123m/s^2 / 0.100m = 35.07 rad/s.
Therefore, f = ω/2π = 5.58 Hz.

(b) Again
ω = √k/m provides, m = 400N/m / (35.07rad/s)^2 = 0.325 kg.

(c) By energy conservation, ½ kxm^2 (the energy of the system at a turning point) is equal to the sum of kinetic and potential energies at the time t described in the problem.
½ kxm^2 = ½ mv^2 + ½ kx^2 => xm = √(m/k)v^2 + x^2.
Consequently, xm = √(0.325kg / 400N/m)(13.6m/s)^2 + (0.100m)^2 = 0.400 m.

4. A block is on a horizontal surface (a shake table) that is moving back and forth horizontally with simple harmonic motion of frequency 2.0 Hz. The coefficient of static friction between block and surface is 0.50. How great can the amplitude of the SHM be if the block is not to slip along the surface?

Solution:
The maximum force that can be exerted by the surface must be less than μsFN or else the block will not follow the surface in its motion. Here, μs is the coefficient of static friction and FN is the normal force exerted by the surface on the block. Since the block does not accelerate vertically, we know that FN = mg, where m is the mass of the block. If the block follows the table and moves in simple harmonic motion, the magnitude of the maximum force exerted on it is given by
F = mam = mω^2xm = m(2πf)^2xm
Where am is the magnitude of the maximum acceleration, ω is the angular frequency, and f is the frequency.
Now substituting, F = m(2πf)^2xm and FN = mg into F < μsFN
We obtain, m(2πf)^2xm < μsmg.
The largest amplitude for which the block does not slip is
xm = μsg / (2πf)^2 = (0.50)(9.8m/s^2) / (2πx2.0Hz)^2 = 0.031m.
A larger amplitude requires a larger force at the end points of the motion. The surface cannot supply the larger force and the block slips.

5. Two particles execute simple harmonic motion of the same amplitude and frequency along close parallel lines. They pass each other moving in opposite directions each time their displacement is half their amplitude. What is their phase difference?

Solution:
They pass each other at time t, at x1 = x2 = ½ xm where
x1 = xm cos (ωt + φ1) and x2 = xm cos (ωt + φ2).


[Page 14]
24 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

From this, we conclude that cos (ωt + φ1) = cos (ωt + φ2) = ½,
and therefore that the phases are either both equal to π/3 or one is π/3 while the other is – π/3. Also at this
instant, we have v1 = v2 = 0 where
v1 = -xω sin (ωt + φ1) and v2 = -xω sin (ωt + φ2).
This leads to sin (ωt + φ1) = sin (ωt + φ2).
This leads us to conclude that the phases have opposite sign. Thus, one phase is π/3 and the other phase
is – π/3.
The ωt term cancels if we take the phase difference, which is seen to be π/3 – (– π/3) = 2π/3.

6. Two particles oscillate in simple harmonic motion along a common straight-line segment of length
A. Each particle has a period of 1.5 s, but they differ in phase by π/6 rad. (a) How far apart are they
(in terms of A) 0.50 s after the lagging particle leaves one end of the path? (b) Are they then moving in
the same direction, toward each other, or away from each other?

Solution:
(a) Let
x1 = r cos ωt = A/2 cos (2πt / T)

be the coordinate as a function of time for particle 1 and
x2 = A/2 cos (2πt / T + π/6)

be the coordinate as a function of time for particle 2.
Here T is the period. Note since the range of the motion is A, the amplitudes are both A/2. The
arguments of the cosine functions are in radians. Particle 1 is at one end of its path (x1 = A/2) when t = 0.
Particle 2 is at A/2 when 2πt/T + π/6 = 0 at t = -T/12.
That is, particle 1 lags particle 2 by one-twelfth a period.
We want the coordinates of the particles 0.50 s later, that is, at t = 0.50 s,
x1 = A/2 cos (2π * 0.50s / 1.5s) = -0.25 A

and
x2 = A/2 cos (2π * 0.50s / 1.5s + π/6) = -0.43 A.
Their separation at that time is x1 - x2 = -0.25 A + 0.43 A = 0.18 A.

(b) The velocities of the particles are given by
v1 = dx1/dt = -πA/T sin (2πt/T) = -πA/T sin (2π * 0.50s / 1.5s)

and
v2 = dx2/dt = -πA/T sin (2πt/T + π/6) = -πA/T sin (2π * 0.50s / 1.5s + π/6)

Hence, they are both negative-valued, indicating that the particles are moving in the same direction.

7. A 10 g particle undergoes SHM with amplitude of 2.0 mm, a maximum acceleration of magnitude
8.0 * 10^3 m/s^2, and an unknown phase constant φ. What are (a) the period of the motion, (b) the
maximum speed of the particle, and (c) the total mechanical energy of the oscillator? What is the
magnitude of the force on the particle when the particle is at (d) its maximum displacement and (e)
half its maximum displacement?

Solution:
We have the acceleration amplitude
a_m = ω^2 x_m
where ω is the angular frequency and x_m = 0.0020 m is the amplitude.
Thus, a_m = 8000 m/s^2 leads to ω = 2000 rad/s.
Using Newton's second law with m = 0.010 kg, we have
F = ma = m [-a_m cos (ωt + φ)] = - (80 N) cos (2000t - π/3)
where t is in seconds.

Wave and Oscillation 25

(a) We have, T = 2π/ω = 3.1 * 10^-3 s.
(b) The relation v_m = ωx_m can be used to solve for v_m or we can pursue the alternate (through related)
approach of energy conservation. Here we choose the latter.
Further, the spring constant is k = ω^2 m = 40000 N/m.
Then, energy conservation leads to
1/2 k x_m^2 = 1/2 m v_m^2
or,
v_m = x_m sqrt(k/m) = 4.0 m/s.
(c) The total energy is 1/2 k x_m^2 = 1/2 m v_m^2 = 0.080 J.
(d) At the maximum displacement, the force acting on the particle is
F = kx = (4.0 * 10^4 N/m)(2.0 * 10^-3 m) = 80 N.
(e) At half of the maximum displacement, x = 1.0 mm, and the force is
F = kx = (4.0 * 10^4 N/m)(1.0 * 10^-3 m) = 40 N.

8. The balance wheel of an old-fashioned watch oscillates with angular amplitude π rad and period
0.500 s. Find (a) the maximum angular speed of the wheel, (b) the angular speed at displacement π/2
rad, and (c) the magnitude of the angular acceleration at displacement π/4 rad.

Solution:
(a) We take the angular displacement of the wheel to be θ = θ_m cos (2πt/T), where θ_m is the amplitude
and T is the period.
Differentiating with respect to time, the angular velocity:
Ω = dθ/dt = -(2π/T) θ_m sin (2πt/T).
The symbol Ω is used for the angular velocity of the wheel so it is not confused with the angular
frequency. The maximum angular velocity is
Ω_m = 2πθ_m / T = (2π * π rad) / 0.500 s = 39.5 rad/s.
(b) When θ = π/2, then θ/θ_m = 1/2; cos (2πt/T) = 1/2, and
sin (2πt/T) = sqrt(1 - cos^2(2πt/T)) = sqrt(1 - (1/2)^2) = sqrt(3/2)
Thus,
Ω = -(2π/T) θ_m sin (2πt/T) = -(2π / 0.500 s) (π rad) (sqrt(3)/2) = -34.2 rad/s.
During another portion of the cycle its angular speed is + 34.2 rad/s when its angular displacement is
π/2 rad.
(c) The angular acceleration is
α = d^2θ/dt^2 = -(2π/T)^2 θ_m cos (2πt/T) = -(2π/T)^2 θ.
When θ = π/4,
α = -(2π / 0.500 s)^2 (π/4) = -124 rad/s^2,
or,
|α| = 124 rad/s^2.

9. A thin uniform rod (mass = 0.50 kg) swings about an axis that passes through one end of the rod
and is perpendicular to the plane of the swing. The rod swings with a period of 1.5 s and an angular


[Page 15]
26 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

amplitude of 10°. (a) What is the length of the rod? (b) What is the maximum kinetic energy of the rod as it swings?

Solution:
(a) The rotational inertia of a uniform rod with pivot point at its end is
I = mL²/12 + mL² = mL²/3
Hence, T = 2π√(I/Mgl) = 2π√(mL²/3 / MgL)
or, L = 3gT²/8π²
so that L = 0.84 m

(b) By energy conservation
E(bottom of swing) = E(end of swing)
Km = Um
Where U = Mgl (1 - cos θ) with l being the distance from the axis of rotation to the centre of mass. If we use the small angle approximation (cos θ ≈ 1 - ½ θ² with θ in radians) we obtain
Um = (0.5 kg)(9.8 m/s²)(L/2)(½ θ²)
Where θm = 0.17 rad. Thus, Km = Um = 0.031 J. If we calculate (1 - cos θ) straight forwardly (without using the small angle approximation) then we obtain within 0.3% of the same answer.

10. In given figure, a stick of length L = 1.85 m oscillates as a physical pendulum. (a) What value of distance x between the stick's centre of mass and its pivot point O gives the least period?

Solution:
Referring to the centre of mass as C (assumed to be the geometric centre of the stick), we see that the distance between O and C is h = x.
The parallel axis theorem leads to
I = mL²/12 + mh² = m(L²/12 + x²)
Thus the time period,
T = 2π√(I/mgh) = 2π√(m(L²/12 + x²) / mgx) = 2π√((L²/12 + x²) / gx)
(a) Minimizing T by differentiating with respect to x we get,
d/dx (12gT²/2π) = d/dx ((L²/12 + x²)/x) = -L²/12x² + 1 = 0
which yields x = L/√12 = 1.85m/√12 = 0.53 m as the value of x which should produce the smallest possible value of T.

(b) With L = 1.85 m and x = 0.53 m, we obtain T = 2.1 s from the expression derived in part (a).

11. The amplitude of a lightly damped oscillator decreases by 3.0 % during each cycle. What percentage of the mechanical energy of the oscillator is lost in each cycle?

Wave and Oscillation 27

Solution:
Since the energy is proportional to the amplitude squared, we find the fractional change (assumed small) is
E-E/E = dE/E = d(x_m²)/x_m² = 2x_m dx_m / x_m² = 2 dx_m / x_m
Thus, if we approximate the fractional change in x_m as dx_m/x_m, then the above calculation shows that multiplying this by 2 should give the fractional energy change.
Therefore, if x_m decreases by 3 %, then E must decrease by 6 %.

12. A 1000 kg car carrying four 82 kg people travels over a "washboard" dirt road with corrugations 4.0 m apart. The car bounces with maximum amplitude when its speed is 10 km/h. When the car stops, the people get out, by how much does the car body rise on its suspension?

Solution:
With M = 1000 kg and m = 4 * 82 kg, we may write
ω = 2π/T = √(k/(M+4m))
If d = 4.0 m is the distance travelled (at constant car speed v) between impulses, then we may write
T = v/d, in which case the above equation may be solved for the spring constant:
2π/(d/v) = √(k/(M+4m))
k = (M+4m)(2πv/d)²
or,
Before the people got out, the equilibrium compression is x_i = (M+4m)g / k
Therefore, with v = 16000/3600 = 4.44 m/s, we find the rise of the car body on its suspension is
x_i - x_f = 4mg/k = 4mg / (M+4m)(2πv/d)² = 0.050 m.

13. A block is in SHM on the end of a spring with position given by x = x_m cos (ωt + φ). If φ = π/5 rad, then at t = 0 what percentage of the total mechanical energy is potential energy?

Solution:
Its total mechanical energy is equal to its maximum potential energy ½ k x_m², and its potential energy at t = 0 is ½ k x²
where x₀ = x_m cos(π/5)
The ratio is therefore cos²(π/5) = 0.655 = 65.5%.

14. A simple pendulum of length 20 cm and mass 5.0 g is suspended in a race car travelling with constant speed 70 m/s around a circle of radius 50 m. If the pendulum undergoes small oscillations in a radial direction about its equilibrium position, what is the frequency of oscillation?

Solution:
Since the centripetal acceleration is horizontal and Earth's gravitational g is downward, we can define the magnitude of an "effective" gravitational acceleration using the Pythagorean Theorem:


[Page 16]
28 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

geff = sqrt(g^2 + (v^2/R)^2)
Then, f = 1/2pi * sqrt(geff/L) = 1/2pi * sqrt((sqrt(g^2 + (v^4/R^2)))/L)
With v = 70 m/s, R = 50 m, and L = 0.20 m, we have f = 3.5 s^-1 = 3.5 Hz.

34 The displacement of simple harmonic oscillator is given by y = r sin (ωt + φ). If the oscillations started at t = 0 from a position y₀ with velocity dy/dt = v₀, show that tan φ = ωy₀/v₀ and y = [y₀² + v₀²/ω²]¹/².
35 Prove that if at any instant of motion kinetic energy is suddenly increased by a blow in the ratio (1+β):1, the amplitude of the swing is increased in the ratio √(a + βv² / a²ω²) : 1 where a is amplitude, and v is velocity of SHM.
36 A uniform rod of length l oscillates through small angles about a point a distance x from its centre.
a) Prove that its angular frequency is ω = √(gx / (l²/12 + x²)).
b) Show that its maximum angular frequency occurs when x = l/√12.
c) What is the length of the rod if maximum angular frequency is 2π rad/sec?
37 (a) Prove that the fractional change in time period of a simple pendulum when the acceleration due to gravity g changes by Δg, is ΔT/T = -1/2 (Δg/g).
b) The pendulum keeps correct time at a point where g = 9.8 ms⁻², but is found to loose 4 sec each day at higher elevation. Find the approximate value of g at this new location.


[Page 18]
Wave Motion

2
Chapter

A wave is a disturbance that travels or propagates without the transport of matter.
Wave motion travels through a medium because of the periodic motion of the medium about
their mean position, at the same time, the disturbances passes over from one particle to the
other. The ripple on a pond, visible light, the sound we hear, earth quake, tsunami, radio
signals are few examples of waves.

Mechanical waves, such as water waves, earth quake, tsunami, sound waves travel
within or on surface of a material that has some elastic properties. There should be some
mechanism that tends to restore the medium to its equilibrium state. But non mechanical
waves, such as light or radio signals can propagate even through a vacuum.

In a wave motion, the physical condition due to a disturbance generated at some point
in the medium is propagated to other points in the medium. In case of wave motion, it is not
matter that is propagated. The dynamic condition relates to momentum and energy. Therefore
in a wave motion it is not the matter but momentum and energy are transferred from one
point to other.

2.1. Wave Characteristics

Wave motion is a disturbance produced in a medium by repeated periodic motion of
the particles of the medium. The wave travels forward but the particles in the medium vibrate
about their mean positions. There is a regular phase change between various particles of the
medium. This is because the neighbouring particles start vibration at different times.

The velocity of the wave is different than the velocity with which the particles of the
medium are vibrating about their mean positions. The wave travels with a uniform velocity.
However the velocity of the particles is different at different positions. The particle velocity
is maximum at equilibrium or mean position and becomes zero at the extreme position of the
particles. In general there are two types of wave motions: transverse and longitudinal.

In a transverse wave, the displacement of the particles is perpendicular to the direction
of travel of the wave. In a longitudinal wave the displacement of the particles is along the
direction of the wave velocity or wave motion.


[Page 19]
34 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
2.2. Progressive Wave Equation
Consider a particle O in a medium. Let the displacement at any instant be
y = a sin ωt ... 2.1
Also consider another particle P at a distance x from O to its right. Let the wave is
travelling from left to right with a velocity v. The displacement of particle at P will be,
y = a sin (ωt - φ) ... 2.2
Where, φ is the phase difference between the particles at O and P. We knew the path difference λ creates a phase difference of 2π. Thus the phase difference φ is created by a path difference of x. Therefore we may write
φ = (2π/λ)x ... 2.3
We also know the angular frequency ω is
ω = 2π/T
But since v = λ/T, T = λ/v yields the above equation as
ω = 2πv/λ ... 2.4
Substituting equations 2.3 and 2.4 in 2.2 provides,
y = a sin (2πv/λ t - 2π/λ x)
or, y = a sin 2π/λ (vt - x) ... 2.5
This is the equation for a simple harmonic wave. For a wave travelling from right to left, the equation is,
y = a sin 2π/λ (vt + x) ... 2.6
These equations represent a harmonic waves moving in positive and negative x directions.

Wave Motion 35
Equation 2.5 and 2.6 can be expressed in terms of wave number k = 2π/λ and angular frequency ω as
y = a sin (ωt - kx), left to right
and y = a sin (ωt + kx), right to left.
2.3. Differential Equation of Wave Motion
We have deduced the general equation of travelling simple harmonic wave as
y = a sin 2π/λ (vt - x)
Differentiate this equation with respect to time, t, we have,
dy/dt = 2πva/λ cos 2π/λ (vt - x) ... 2.7
Again upon differentiation,
d²y/dt² = - 4π²v²a/λ² sin 2π/λ (vt - x) ... 2.8
We also find the following expressions if we differentiate the general equation with respect to x,
dy/dx = - 2πa/λ cos 2π/λ (vt - x) ... 2.9
and d²y/dx² = - 4π²a/λ² sin 2π/λ (vt - x) ... 2.10
Using equations 2.7 and 2.9 we may write,
dy/dt = -v dy/dx ... 2.11
Similarly from equations 2.8 and 2.10 we find the following relation
d²y/dt² = v² d²y/dx² ... 2.12
This final expression, equation 2.12, is second order differential equation of wave motion.


[Page 20]
36
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
2.4. Wave Velocity and Particle Velocity
We are assuming the velocity of a transverse wave as v. We have deduced the general equation of a simple harmonic wave as,
y = a sin 2π/λ (vt - x)
The wave is propagating in a direction from left to right as in figure 2.2. The particle velocity of the medium of the string is the rate of change of displacement y or dy/dt. The direction of the particle velocity is as shown in figure 2.2. Equation 2.7 provides the value of particle velocity as
Figure 2.2 The velocity of a particle on a string is perpendicular to the wave velocity v.
v_particle = dy/dt = 2πva/λ cos 2π/λ (vt - x)
The maximum speed of the particle is given by
(v_particle)max = 2πv/λ a = ω a ... 2.13
Similarly the particle acceleration (ap) can be obtained with equation 2.8 as,
ap = d^2y/dt^2 = - 4π^2 v^2 a/λ^2 sin 2π/λ (vt - x)
Providing the maximum particle acceleration as
(ap)max = - 4π^2 v^2 a/λ^2 = - ω^2 a ... 2.14
The negative sign shows that the acceleration of the particle is directed towards its mean position. Also we have obtained
dy/dx = - 2πa/λ cos 2π/λ (vt - x)
Where dy/dx represents the slope of the displacement curve. Therefore we may write,
v_particle = -v dy/dx
Hence particle velocity at any instant is the product of wave velocity and slope of displacement curve at that instant.
Example: 2.1
The displacement equation of a transverse plane wave at any instant is given by
y = 0.03 sin (3π t - 0.03π x) where x and t are in meter and seconds. Calculate wave length, frequency and velocity of the wave. Also calculate phase difference between two particles 0.05 m apart at same instant.
Solution:
The general displacement equation for a transverse plane wave equation is given by
y = a sin 2π/λ (vt - x) = a sin (2πvt/λ - 2πx/λ)
y = a sin (2πft - 2πx/λ)
Comparing it with given equation y = 0.03 sin (3 π t - 0.03 π x) we get
2πf = 3 π
or, f = 1.5 Hz
and 2π/λ = 0.03 π
or, λ = 2π / 0.03 π = 200 / 3 = 66.67 m.
Also velocity
v = fλ = 3/2 * 200/3 = 100 m/s.
For the phase difference between two particles 0.05 m apart is
Φ = 2π/λ x = 0.03 π x 0.05
= 0.0015 π
Example: 2.2
A simple harmonic wave is represented by y = 10 sin (2πt/T) The time period is 30 s. At time t = 0, the displacement is 5 cm. Calculate
(a) phase angle at t = 7.5 s and
(b) phase difference between two positions at a time interval of 6 s.
Solution:
The given equation
y = 10 sin (2πt/T + Φ)
T = 30 sec
(a) At t = 0, y = 5 cm so
5 = 10 sin Φ
i.e. Φ = sin⁻¹ (0.5) = π/6 radian.
At t = 7.5 s, the phase angle is
Φ' = 2π/30 * 7.5 + π/6

Wave Motion
37


[Page 21]
38 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
or, φ' = 2π/3 radian.
(b) Now phase difference for a time interval of 6 sec,
2π/T * 6 = 2π/30 * 6 = 2π/5 radians.

2.5. Energy of a Progressive Wave
In a progressive wave there is continuous transfer of energy in the direction of propagation of the wave. The energy is supplied from the source. Part of the energy is kinetic and part is potential. For a particle executing SHM, the velocity is maximum at equilibrium position and will be zero at extreme positions. Since kinetic energy is proportional to square of velocity, it is maximum at mean position and zero at extreme points. Consequently the potential energy at extreme position is maximum and will be zero at mean position.
To move the particle from its mean position to a distance y, work has to be done against the acceleration. For a small displacement dy work done is,
dW = F dy
If ρ is density of the medium, work done per unit volume (dw) for small displacement dy will be,
dw = ρ a_p dy
or, dw = ρ (4π² av² / λ²) sin (2π/λ (vt-x)) dy
Now the total work done for a displacement y is given by
w = ∫ dw = ∫ 4π² ρv² / λ² a sin (2π/λ (vt-x)) dy,
Work done per unit volume is stored as potential energy per unit volume u, which is given by,
u = 4π² ρv² a² / λ² * 1/2 sin² (2π/λ (vt-x))
= 2π² ρa² v² / λ² sin² [2π/λ (vt-x)]

Again kinetic energy per unit volume will become
k = 1/2 ρ [2πav/λ cos 2π/λ (vt-x)]² = 2π² ρa² v² / λ² cos² [2π/λ (vt-x)]

Thus the total energy per unit volume is,
E = k + u
= 2π² ρa² v² / λ² [sin² 2π/λ (vt-x) + cos² 2π/λ (vt-x)]
= 2π² ρa² v² / λ² ... 2.15
Since v = fλ
E = 2π² ρa² f²λ² / λ² = 2π² ρ a² f² ... 2.16

This shows that the average kinetic energy per unit volume and potential energy per unit volume are equal and each is equal to half the total energy per unit volume. Hence the average kinetic energy per unit volume and potential energy per unit volume = π² ρ a² f².
The quantity u and k of every particle is changing in time. However the average kinetic energy per unit volume and average potential energy per unit volume remains constant.

2.6. Power and Intensity of a Progressive Wave
Equation 2.16 is the total energy per unit volume and hence total energy will become,
E = 2π² ρ V a² f²
= 2π² ρ A v t a² f² ... 2.17
Here V is the volume of the medium which is V = A . l where A is the cross sectional area through which the wave travels, l is the distance the wave travels. But l = v t, v being the speed of the wave.
From equation 2.17 we can say the energy transported by a wave is proportional to square of amplitude (a²). The rate of energy transfer, P will thus be,
P = E/t = 2π² ρ A v a² f² ... 2.18
Again intensity of a wave, I, is the average power transferred across unit area perpendicular to the direction of energy flow will come out to be
I = P/A = 2π² ρ v a² f² ... 2.19
Both power and intensity of a travelling wave is directly proportional to square of amplitude.
Intensity is related to sound intensity level or sound pressure level which is defined by the equation I_L = 10 log (I/I_0) where I_0 is reference intensity or threshold of hearing (10⁻¹² W/m²). Sound intensity levels are expressed in decibel unit (dB).

Wave Motion 39


[Page 22]
40 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Example: 2.3
If the intensity of a seismic wave is 1.0 x 10^6 W/m^2 at 100 km from the source, what is the intensity 400 km from the source?

Solution:
The intensity decreases as square of the distance from the source. Therefore at 400 km, the intensity should be
I2/I1 = [r1/r2]^2
or, I2 = [100/400]^2 I1
= 1/16 * 1.0 x 10^6 = 6.2 x 10^4 W/m^2.

Example: 2.4
A source of sound has a frequency of 256 Hz and amplitude of 0.50 cm. Calculate the energy flow across a square cm per sec. The velocity of sound in air is 330 m/s and density of air is 1.29 kg/m^3.

Solution:
The total energy transferred in a square cm per unit time is given by,
I = E/At = 2 π^2 ρ f^2 a^2 v
= 2 π^2 x 1.29 x (256)^2 x (0.50 x 10^-2)^2 x 330
= 1.38 x 10^4 J/m^2.sec

2.7. Transverse Waves along a Stretched String:
Equation and Speed of a Pulse on a String

A string remains straight in its equilibrium position under tension. Let a string under tension F be considered which is so long that the effect of gravitational forces are negligible. A small length ab of the string be considered and is displaced in y direction perpendicular to x where the string is along undisturbed equilibrium position along x axis. The tensions F acting as tangent at a and b makes angles θx and θx+Δx with x axis. If these angles are very small, their cosines are very nearly equal to one and string cannot move in x direction, i.e. cosine components of F are cancelled out, so the total y component of the tension or resultant force on the segment ab
= F sin θx+Δx - F sin θx
= F [tan θx+Δx - tan θx]
= F [(∂y/∂x)x+Δx - (∂y/∂x)x]
= F ∂^2y/∂x^2 Δx ... 2.20

Wave Motion 41
If μ be the mass per unit length of the string, then mass of the segment ab = μ Δx.
Hence by using Newton's second law of motion in equation 2.20 we get,
μ Δx ∂^2y/∂t^2 = F ∂^2y/∂x^2 Δx
∂^2y/∂x^2 = 1/v^2 ∂^2y/∂t^2 ... 2.21
This is the one dimensional equation of transverse wave along a stretched string and it is similar to equation 2.12. So comparing them we get the velocity of transverse wave along a stretched string i.e.
v = sqrt(F/μ) ... 2.22
Hence the velocity of transverse wave depends on applied tension and the mass per unit length of the stretched string.

Speed of a Pulse on a String (alternative method):
The speed at which a pulse propagates depends on the properties of the medium. Consider an idealized uniform string which is perfectly flexible. Assume the pulse height is so small that the tension on the string is not changed by it. The pulse moves to the right with speed v as shown in figure 2.4. Any small segment AB may be treated as a circular arc of some radius R. Let θ be the angle between the vertical and radial line to A (or B). Thus the length of the arc AB is given by
2θ = Arc AB / R
or, Arc AB = R (2θ) ... 2.23
Suppose μ is the linear mass density or mass per unit length of the material of the string. The mass of the segment AB will become
m = 2 μ R θ ... 2.24
The tension F in the string must provide the centripetal force needed for circular motion. The tangents at these points A and B make the same angle as the normal to them cancels each other because of opposite directions. Therefore the net force in the segment is F sin θ + F sin θ = 2F sin θ vertically downward. This force balances the centripetal force. Hence,
2F sin θ = mv^2 / R ... 2.25
As we have assumed the pulse height is small, therefore θ is small. Using small angle approximation we may write,
2F θ = mv^2 / R
or, 2F θ = (2μ R θ)v^2 / R


[Page 23]
42 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

... 2.26
v = [square root of F/μ]
This equation is valid for any shaped pulse having small amplitude. The tension
explains how strongly the string tries to restore itself to its mean position.
Example: 2.5
One end of a string is fixed. It hangs over a pulley and has a block of mass 2 kg attached to the
other end. The horizontal part has a length of 1.6 m and mass 20 g. What is the speed of transverse
pulse on the string?
Solution:
Here the tension in the string is the weight of the block
F = mg = 2 x 9.8 = 19.6 N.
Using
v = [square root of F/μ] = [square root of 19.6 / (20 x 10^-3)] = 39.6 m/s.

2.8 Velocity of Longitudinal Waves in a Solid Rod and in a Fluid.
In longitudinal mechanical waves, the line
of vibration of the particles of the medium and
line along which the wave travels on the source.
There is no phenomenon like polarization.
Let a longitudinal wave be travelling
along an elastic solid rod, where two close planes
P and Q perpendicular to the rod and distance x
and (x + Δx) measured along the rod from some
arbitrary point be considered. Suppose the particles originally in the plane P moves to the
plane P' and the particles from Q moves to Q'. Then the displacements are
X = PP' at x
X + ΔX = QQ' at x + Δx
The original length
PQ = Δx
∴ the increase in length = P'Q' - PQ = ΔX
Hence the Young's Modulus Y = Tensile Stress / Longitudinal Strain
∴ Tensile force = AY ΔX/Δx = AY dX/dx ... 2.27
Where A is the cross-sectional area of the rod. As dX/dx is different in different parts of
the rod, the tensil force also vary along the rod. i.e. the tensile force at P is AY (dX/dx)x
the past PQ of the rod towards left and AY (dX/dx)x+Δx pulls PQ towards right.
So the resultant force acting on PQ is
F = AY [(dX/dx)x+Δx - (dX/dx)x]

Wave Motion 43

= AY Δx d²X/dx² ... 2.28
If ρ be the density of the rod, then the mass of the portion PQ is
M = ρ A Δx
So from equation 2.28 using Newton's second law,
M d²X/dt² = AY Δx d²X/dx²
or, ρ A Δx d²X/dt² = AY Δx d²X/dx²
∴ d²X/dx² = 1/(Y/ρ) d²X/dt² ... 2.29
on comparison with equation 2.12, the general differential equation for waves, we find the
velocity of longitudinal waves
v = [square root of Y/ρ] ... 2.30
only longitudinal waves can be propagated in a fluid. If we consider all particles originally
lying in a plane P within a fluid displaced by same amount X in a direction perpendicular to
the plane, so that they occupy the plane P'. On x axis choosing along a direction
perpendicular to the planes P, the position of P be x and the particles in a close parallel to the
planes Q at position (x + Δx) then in the same way, we get for fluid
v = [square root of B/ρ] ... 2.31
This result was first deduced by Newton, assuming isothermal elasticity and later
assuming adiabatic elasticity by Laplace, the velocity of sound was corrected by
v = [square root of γP/ρ] = [square root of γRT/M]
which is independent of pressure and directly proportional to the square root of absolute
temperature, where γ is ratio of two specific heat capacities at constant pressure and constant
volume for air.

2.9. Additional Examples:
1. A wave travelling along a string is described by y (x,t) = 0.00327 sin (72.1x – 2.72t) in which numerical constants are in SI units (0.00327 m, 72.1 rad/m, and 2.72 rad/s).
(a) What is the amplitude of this wave? (b) What are the wavelength, period, and frequency of the wave? (c) What is the velocity of this wave? (d) What is the displacement y at x = 22.5 cm and t = 18.9 s?

Solution:
(a) Given equation is of the same form as,
y = ym sin (kx – ωt)
so we have a sinusoidal wave. By comparing the two equations, we can find the amplitude as:
ym = 0.00327 m = 3.27 mm.

(b) By comparing the equations, we see that the angular wave number and angular frequency are
k = 72.1 rad/m and ω = 2.72 rad/s.
but λ = 2π / k = 2π / 72.1 rad/m = 0.0871 m = 8.71 cm.
Also, T = 2π / ω = 2π / 2.72 rad/s = 2.31 s.
For frequency of the wave
f = 1 / T = 1 / 2.31 s = 0.433 Hz.

(c) We calculate velocity by:
v = ω / k = 2.72 rad/s / 72.1 rad/m = 0.0377 m/s = 3.77 cm/s.

Because the phase in given equation contains the position variable x, the wave is moving along the x axis. Also, because the wave equation is written in the form of y = ym sin (kx – ωt) the minus sign in front of the ωt term indicates that the wave is moving in the positive direction of the x axis. (Note that the quantities calculated in (b) and (c) are independent of the amplitude of the wave.)

(d) The given displacement is a function of position x and time t. Substituting the given values into the equation yields
y = 0.00327 sin (72.1 x 0.225 – 2.72 x 18.9) = (0.00327 m) sin (-35.1855 rad)
= (0.00327 m)(0.588) = 0.00192 m = 1.92 mm.
Thus, the displacement is positive.

2. In Figure, two strings have been tied together with a knot and then stretched between two rigid supports. The strings have linear densities μ1 = 1.4 x 10^-4 kg/m and μ2 = 2.8 x 10^-4 kg/m. Their lengths are L1 = 3.0 m and L2 = 2.0 m, and string 1 is under a tension of 400 N. simultaneously, on each string a pulse is sent from the rigid support end, toward the knot. Which pulse reaches the knot first?

[Diagram: String with L1 and L2 sections, connected by a Knot. Labeled Figure AE 2]

Solution:
The time for the pulse on string 1 to reach the knot is,
t1 = L1 / v1 = L1 / sqrt(τ / μ1) = (3.0 m) / sqrt(400 N / 1.4 x 10^-4 kg/m) = 1.77 x 10^-3 s.

In figure two strings, of lengths L1 and L2, tied together with a knot and stretched between two rigid supports. Similarly, the data for the pulse on string 2 give us
t2 = L2 / v2 = sqrt(μ2 / τ) = 1.67 x 10^-3 s.
Thus, the pulse on string 2 reaches the knot first.

In another way the linear density of string 2 is greater than that of string 1, so the pulse on string 2 must be slower than that on string 1. Could we have guessed the answer from that fact alone? No, because from the first key idea we see that the distance travelled by a pulse also matters.

3. A string has linear density μ = 525 g/m and tension τ = 45 N. We send a sinusoidal wave with frequency f = 120 Hz and amplitude ym = 8.5 mm along the string. At what average rate does rate does the wave transport energy?

Solution:
We have,
ω = 2 π f = (2π)(120 Hz) = 754 rad/s.
v = sqrt(τ / μ) = sqrt(45 N / 0.525 kg/m) = 9.26 m/s.
Also,
Hence average power transferred is,
Pavg = ½ μ v ω^2 ym^2 = ½ (0.525 kg/m)(9.26 m/s)(754 rad/s)^2 (0.0085 m)^2 ≈ 100 W.

4. A sinusoidal wave travels along a string. The time for a particular point to move from maximum displacement to zero is 0.170 s. What are the (a) period and (b) frequency? (c) The wavelength is 1.40 m; what is the wave speed?

Solution:
(a) The motion from maximum displacement to zero is one-fourth of a cycle so 0.170 s is one-fourth of a period. The period is T = 4 (0.170 s) = 0.680 s.

(b) The frequency is the reciprocal of the period:
f = 1 / T = 1 / 0.680 s = 1.47 Hz.

(c) A sinusoidal wave travels one wavelength in one period:
v = λ / T = 1.40 m / 0.680 s = 2.06 m/s.

5. A sinusoidal wave frequency 500 Hz has a speed of 350 m/s. (a) How far apart are two points that differ in phase by π/3 rad? (b) What is the phase difference between two displacements at a certain point at times 1.00 ms part?

Solution:
Using v = fλ, we find the length of one cycle of the wave is
λ = 350 / 500 = 0.700 m = 700 mm.
From f = 1/T, we find the time for one cycle of oscillation is T = 1 / 500 = 2.00 x 10^-3 s = 2.00 ms.

(a) A cycle is equivalent to 2π radians, so that π/3 rad corresponds to one-sixth of a cycle. The corresponding length, therefore, is λ / 6 = 700 / 6 = 117 mm.

(b) The interval 1.00 ms is half of T and thus corresponds to half of one cycle, or half of 2π rad. Thus, the phase difference is (½) 2π = π rad.

6. The equation of a transverse wave travelling along a very long string is y = 6.0 sin (0.020 πx + 4.0 πt), where x and y are expressed in centimetres and t is in seconds. Determine (a) the amplitude, (b) the wavelength, (c) the frequency, (d) the speed, (e) the direction of propagation of the wave, and (f) the...


[Page 25]
46
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
the maximum transverse speed of a particle in the string. (g) What is the transverse displacement at x
= 3.5 cm when t = 0.26 s?
Solution:
(a) The amplitude is ym = 6.0 cm.
(b) We find λ from 2π/λ = 0.020π ⇒ λ = 1.0 x 10² cm.
(c) Solving 2πf = ω = 4.0π, we obtain f = 2.0 Hz.
(d) The wave speed is v = λf = (100 cm)(2.0 Hz) = 2.0 x 10² cm/s.
(e) The wave propagates in the -x direction, since function has kx + ωt instead of kx - ωt
(f) the maximum transverse speed (found from the time derivative of y) is
Umax = 2πfym = (4.0π s⁻¹)(6.0 cm) = 75 cm/s.
(g) y(3.5 cm, 0.26 s) = (6.0 cm) sin [0.020π(3.5) + 4.0π(0.26)] = -2.0 cm.

7. The function y(x, t) = (15.0 cm) cos (πx - 15πt), with x in meters and t in seconds, describes a wave
on a taut string. What is the transverse speed for a point on the string at an instant when that point
has the displacement y = + 12.0 cm?
Solution:
With length in centimeters and time in seconds, we have
u = dy/dt = 225π sin (πx - 15πt).
Squaring this and adding it to the square of 15πy, we have
u² + (15πy)² = (225π)² [sin² (πx - 15πt) + cos² (πx - 15πt)]
So that, u = √(225π)² - (15πy)² = 15π√15² - y².
Therefore, where y = 12, u must be ± 135π. Consequently, the speed there is 424 cm/s = 4.24 m/s.

8. The speed of a transverse wave on a string is 170 m/s when the string tension is 120 N. To
what value must the tension be changed to raise the wave speed to 180 m/s?
Solution:
We use v = √F/μ ∝ √F to obtain
F₂ = F₁ (v₂/v₁)² = (120 N) (180 m/s / 170 m/s)² = 135 N.

9. A sinusoidal wave is travelling on a string with speed 40 cm/s. The displacement of the particles of
the string at x = 10 cm is found to vary with time according to the equation y = (5.0 cm) sin [1.0 - (4.0
s⁻¹)t]. The linear density of the string is 4.0 g/m. What are (a) the frequency and (b) the wavelength of
the wave? If the wave equation is of the form y (x, t) = ym sin (kx ± ωt), what are (c) ym (d) k, (e) ω,
and (f) the correct choice of sign in front of ω? (g) What is the tension in the string?
Solution:
(a) The general expression for y (x, t) for the wave is y (x, t) = ym sin (kx - ωt), which, at x = 10 cm,
becomes y (x = 10 cm, t) = ym sin [k(10 cm - ωt)]. Comparing this with the expression given, we find ω = 4.0
rad/s, or f = ω/2π = 0.64 Hz.

Wave Motion
47
(b) Since k (10 cm) = 1.0, the wave number is k = 0.10/cm. Consequently, the wavelength is
λ = 2π/k = 63 cm.
(c) The amplitude is ym = 5.0 cm.
(d) In part (b), we have shown that the angular wave number is k = 0.10/cm.
(e) The angular frequency is ω = 4.0 rad/s.
(f) The sign is minus since the wave is travelling in the +x direction.
Summarizing the results obtained above by substituting the values of k and ω into the general
expression for y (x, t), with centimeters and seconds understood, we obtain
y (x, t) = 5.0 sin (0.10x - 4.0t).
(g) Since v = ω/k = √F/μ, the tension is
F = ω²μ/k² = (4.0 g/cm)(4.0 s⁻¹)² / (0.10 cm⁻¹)² = 6400 g cm/s² = 0.064 N.

10. A uniform rope of mass m and length L hangs from a ceiling. (a) Show that the speed of a
transverse wave on the rope is a function of y, the distance from the lower end, and is given by v =
√gy. (b) Show that the time a transverse wave takes to travel the length of the rope is given by t =
2 √(L/g).
Solution:
(a) The wave speed at any point on the rope is given by v = √F/μ, where F is the tension at that point
and μ is the linear mass density. Because the rope is hanging the tension varies from point to point. Consider a
point on the rope a distance y from the bottom end. The forces acting on it are the weight of the rope below it,
pulling down, and the tension, pulling up. Since the rope is in equilibrium, these forces balance. The weight of
the rope below is μgy, so the tension is F = μgy. The wave speed is v = √μgy/μ = √gy.
(b) The time dt for the wave to move past a length dy, a distance y from the bottom end, is
dt = dy/v = dy/√gy and the total time for the wave to move the entire length of the rope is
t = ∫[0, L] dy/√gy = 2/√g [√y][0, L] = 2 √(L/g).

11. Use the wave equation to find the speed of a wave given by
y (x, t) = (2.00 mm) [(20 m⁻¹)x - (4.0 s⁻¹)t]⁰.⁵
Solution:
The wave y (x, t) = (2.00 mm) [(20 m⁻¹)x - (4.0 s⁻¹)t]⁰.⁵ is of the form h(kx - ωt) with angular wave
number k = 20 m⁻¹ and angular frequency ω = 4.0 rad/s. Thus, the speed of the wave is
v = ω/k = 4.0 rad/s / 20 m⁻¹ = 0.20 m/s.


[Page 26]
48 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Wave Motion 49

12. Use the wave equation to find the speed of a wave given in terms of general function h(x, t):
y(x, t) = (4.00 mm) h(30 m⁻¹)x + (6.0 s⁻¹)t.

Solution:
The wave y(x, t) = (4.00 mm) h[(30 m⁻¹)x + (6.0 s⁻¹)t] is of the form h(kx – ωt) with angular wave number k = 30 m⁻¹ and angular frequency ω = 6.0 rad/s. Thus, the speed of the wave is
v = ω/k = 6.0 rad/s / 30 m⁻¹ = 0.20 m/s.
