3 Acoustics
Chapter
3.1 Acoustics of Buildings
The acoustics of building is a branch of sound Engineering. It has an important role in civil and architectural engineering. Before 1900 AD, there was no basis of the design of rooms and halls for huge gatherings because the causes of bad hearing were not known to them.
Acoustics of buildings is connected with the hearing to speakers and musicians in halls and auditoriums. It is found that some auditoriums are acoustically good and some bad. In good halls, the sounds produced are distinctly audible while in others the sound lack in distinctness. In 1895, Sabine made the quantitative measurement on it and the maze of superstition concerning the subject began to be replaced by a form of foundation of scientific knowledge. It is sometimes observed that a speech made in a certain hall or sound produced in a theatre is not audible at certain places. In some areas there is so much interference that it is sometimes difficult to understand what is being said. Therefore it is in fact necessary to bear certain points in mind while a place of public speaking is designed so that one can follow the utterances from every point clearly without interference. In a good auditorium the following conditions should be satisfied,
a) The sound must sufficiently be loud everywhere.
b) Successive syllables must be clear and distinct i.e. each syllable should die away sufficiently quickly to give place to the next syllable.
c) There should be no echoes or distortion of the original sound.
d) There should neither be any focusing of sound nor any zone of silence in any part of the hall.
e) The quality of sound must not change. The relative intensities of the several components of a complex sound must be maintained.

3.2 Reverberation
A common defect in large rooms is the undue prolongation of the sound of the speaker or music. This arises due to the successive reflection of the sound from the walls, ceilings or floors. of the room. A sound produced in the room reaches a listener not only by the direct path but also by other paths after successive reflections. The listener, therefore


[Page 28]
52 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

receives a series of sounds of diminishing
intensity (after each reflection). Instead of
single sound the observer hears a roll of
sound. This prolongation of sound is called
reverberation.

In the same way for a continuous
source of sound, some of the sound waves
reach the listener directly while the other
reach successively after reflection and
added to the direct waves still reaching
there. Consequently the listener receives increasing sound energy. In the course of time the
energy is partly absorbed by the walls and other materials in the room and partly escapes
through the open windows and ventilators. After all a balance is reached between the energy
emitted from the source and the energy lost. Then the sound intensity acquires a steady value.
If the source is stopped, the sound does not stop instantaneously but the listener continues to
pickup the successive reflections until they fall below the minimum audibility. The louder in
the original sound, the longer will this process take. This gradual decay of sound is known as
reverberation. Figure 3.1 shows a typical case of the rise and fall of sound intensity level.

3.3 Time of Reverberation

As discussed earlier even if the
sound is cut off at the source, it will not
stop instantaneously at the receiver.
Louder the sound, the larger will this
process take. This gradual dying out of
sound is known as reverberation and is
the single factor in deciding whether a
room is acoustically good or not.

The duration for which the
sound can be heard after the source has
ceased to produce the sound is called
the reverberation time. It is found that
the source becomes inaudible when its
intensity falls to one millionth of the
average intensity. This duration between the production of sound and its inaudibility is called
time of reverberation.

In other words the time taken by sound to fall to one millionth of its intensity just
before the source is cut off is time of reverberation or reverberation time. It is same as to say
the time to fall the loudness by 60 dB as illustrated in figure 3.2.

3.4 Absorption of Sound

One can manipulate the reverberation time in an auditorium or hall by varying the
absorption of sound. The amount of absorption of sound depends upon the nature of the
materials and their surface area.

Acoustics 53

The absorption coefficient of sound is the ratio of sound energy absorbed by the given
surface to the sound energy absorbed by an equal area of a perfect absorber. It can also be
defined as the reciprocal of the area which absorbs the same sound energy as absorbed by a
unit area of a perfect absorber. An open window is an example of a perfect absorber since
there is no reflection and the sound simply passes through. Sabine is an absorption unit
representing a surface whose absorption is equal to 1 square feet of the perfectly absorbing
surface.

Consider a certain material of area 10 m² absorb the same amount of energy as
absorbed by 1 m² of an open window. The absorption coefficient in such case is 1/10 = 0.1. It
is experimentally observed that a normal person is equivalent to 4.7 square feet or 0.4367 m²
of an open window unit.

Let a1, a2, a3, ... are the absorption coefficients of the absorbing surface of area s1, s2,
s3, ... made up of different materials forming the interior surface of the room. The total
absorption is therefore, A = Σ ai si. The average absorption is given by,
a̅ = Σ ai si / Σ si ... 3.1

Example: 3.1
Calculate the total absorption in a hall with the following data when the hall is empty and
with full capacity of 120 audience.

Surface | Area | Coefficient of absorption in open window unit
--- | --- | ---
Plastered wall | 112 m² | 0.0300
Wooden floor | 130 m² | 0.0600
Plastered ceiling | 170 m² | 0.0400
Wooden doors | 20 m² | 0.0600
Cushioned chairs | 120 m² | 0.5000
Audience | 120 | 0.4367

Solution:
Let's calculate the absorption of different units.
Absorption due to plastered wall = 112 x 0.03 = 3.36 m² Sabine.
Absorption due to wooden floor = 130 x 0.06 = 7.80 m² Sabine.
Absorption due to plastered ceiling = 170 x 0.04 = 6.80 m² Sabine.
Absorption due to wooden doors = 20 x 0.06 = 1.20 m² Sabine.
Absorption due to cushion chairs = 120 x 0.50 = 60.0 m² Sabine.
Now total absorption in a hall when it is empty is 79.16 m² Sabine
When the hall is full capacity of 120 persons,
then absorption due to 120 persons = 120 x 0.4367 = 52.404 m² Sabine.
Thus total absorption in this case = 79.16 + 52.404 = 131.564 m² Sabine.

3.5 Sabine's Relation

Sabine arrived at an empirical relation between the
reverberation time, the volume and dimensions of the hall to
express the rise and fall of sound in an auditorium. For this
he made the following assumptions.


[Page 29]
54 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

i) There is a uniform distribution of sound energy inside the room. This is a reasonable assumption because when a source of sound starts producing sound in a hall, where the absorption by walls is quite small, reflections at the walls produce a sound energy distribution that becomes more and more uniform with increasing time. Except in close proximity to the source, the energy distribution may be assumed to be completely uniform and to have direction of flow that is essentially random.
ii) The energy is not lost in the auditorium. The energy lost is only due to the absorption of the material of the walls and ceiling and also due to escape through the windows and ventilations. These factors are included in absorption of energy.

Consider a source which is producing sound continuously. The sound is propagated in all direction. Consider σ be the energy contained in a unit volume. The energy that is contained in a solid angle dφ will be σ dφ/4π

Let this energy be incident on a unit surface area of the wall at an angle θ (figure 3.3). Let v be the velocity of sound, the total energy falling per second on a unit surface area of the wall will become, (as in figure 3.3).
= σ dφ/4π cos θ . v

Thus the total energy falling per second within a hemisphere will become
= σv/4π ∫ cosθ dφ ... 3.2

But φ = 2 π (1 - cos θ)
∴ dφ = 2 π sin θ dθ

So, equation 3.2 be rearranged for total energy per second within a hemisphere as
σv/4π ∫_0^π/2 2 π sin θ cos θ dθ = σv/4π * 2π [cos^2 θ / 2]_0^π/2
= σv/4 ... 3.3

The reverberation time is directly proportional to the volume of auditorium, inversely proportional to the area of ceilings, walls etc and inversely proportional to the absorption plus transmission through open surfaces. It has been observed experimentally that suitable reverberation time for a hall of approximate volume less than 350 cubic meter is 1.03.

In order to decrease the reverberation time, the walls in the auditorium are covered with high absorption coefficient materials. The area of the surfaces of the walls is also increased to decrease the reverberation time in good acoustic halls.

Example: 3.2
A reverberation time of 2.3 seconds is observed in a hall of volume 5500 cubic meter. The sound absorbing surface of the hall has an area of 750 square meter. Calculate the average absorption coefficient.

Solution:
Given V = 5500 m^3
S = 750 m^2
T = 2.3 sec
α = ?

We have,
T = 0.158V / α S
α = 0.158 * 5500 / 750 * 2.3 = 0.504

3.6 Optimum Reverberation

Sabine established that there is an optimum time of reverberation which is larger for larger halls and also greater for music than for speech. Below this value the intensity of sound is weak. Above the optimum value the distinctness of syllables is hard. It is observed that a reverberation time from 1.3 to 1.5 sec is best in a room of 400,000 cu ft. The lower range is for speech and the upper value is for music.

Since reverberation is due to repeated reflections it may be reduced by increasing absorption of sound in the room. Some of the methods to increase the absorption include

• providing a few open windows,
• using heavy curtains, tapestries etc,
• hanging maps and pictures in the walls,
• covering the walls and ceilings with absorbing materials like celotex, felt, mineral wools and the floors with carpets,
• having a good audience,
• arranging seats having cushions at their backs which provide a high degree of absorption in themselves.

3.7 Reverberation in Daily Life

Speaking in a room is far less exhausting than speaking in the open space. This is because in the room the sound is reflected from the walls, ceiling and floor. These reflections help the audience to assist the voice of the speaker.

A layer of fresh snow covers, decreases noise considerably. As a result, a strange quietness appears. This is because the fresh snow has a large absorption coefficient. In the course of time the snow hardens and the coefficient decreases returning to the normal life.


[Page 32]
60 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

The speech or music made by people in boats on a lake can be heard over a considerable distance. In such case the smooth water surface is poor absorber and a good reflector of sound.

3.8 Ultrasonic Waves
Ultrasonic waves are waves of frequency greater than that of audible sound. The frequency of such wave is thus greater than 20,000 Hz. Since they travel with the velocity of sound the marginal wavelength comes out to be 331/20,000 = 1.65 cm at room temperature. These waves exhibit the properties of audible sound waves and also show some acoustic phenomena.

The sound waves of frequency lower than the audible limit are called infrasonic, Supersonic refers to the velocities higher than the velocity of sound.

The unique properties of ultrasound have been usefully applied in many area of science. Industrial testing, stimulation of plant growth, destruction of bacteria, depth sounding, underwater signalling are some of the field where we use ultrasonic. These waves can produce a highly directive and focussed beam of vibration and hence can be used in locating distance inaccessible objects.

3.9 Production of Ultrasonic Waves
A Galton's whistle or a tuning fork of sufficiently small dimensions is a simple device to produce ultrasonic, however these methods are not commonly used nowadays. There are two different methods in use for the generation of ultrasound based on piezo-electric effect and magnetostriction.

Piezo-electric Generation
Certain crystals like Quartz, Tourmaline or Rochelle salt exhibit electrical changes when heated or cooled. This phenomenon is called piezoelectricity.

(Figure 3.4 Production methods for the piezoelectric generation ultrasonic.)

Curie brothers in 1880 discovered that if such crystals are subjected to compression or tension, opposite charges of electricity appears at the ends of the crystals. It was also found that the amount of charge produced at each end of such crystal is proportional to the total force applied to the crystal not pressure.

Converse of the above phenomenon is also true. If electric field is applied across the two faces of the crystal, compression or extension will occur across the other pair of faces. The magnitude of compression or extension is proportional to the potential difference between the faces. This effect is used to produce high frequency oscillations in quartz. Tin foil sheets cover the opposite faces of a slab of quartz crystal. The application of alternating potential difference of same frequency to foils produces oscillations in quartz that can be communicated to liquid. If natural frequency of mechanical vibration of the quartz crystal coincides with that of the applied potential difference, resonance occurs and vibrations of large amplitude are set up in the crystal. One should remember that the faces of the crystal should be cut perpendicular to the optic axis.

The variable capacitor is adjusted so that the frequency of the oscillatory circuit is equal to the natural frequency of one of the modes of vibration of the crystal. This will ensure resonant mechanical vibration in the crystal due to the linear expansion and contraction.

When the frequency of the alternating voltage is equal to the natural frequency of vibration of the crystal or its simple higher multiplies, the crystal is thrown in resonant vibrations and amplitude will be large. These vibrations are longitudinal in nature. The frequency of vibration is,
f = k/2l * sqrt(Y/p) ... 3.22

Where k = 1, 2, 3, ... etc (mode of vibration)
Y = Young's modulus of elasticity
p = density of the crystal.

and
The velocity of longitudinal waves in crystal is given by
v = sqrt(Y/p) ... 3.23

In case of quartz the velocity v = 5.5x10^3 m/s. Thus for a crystal of length 0.05 m, the frequency for the first mode of vibration will be
f = 1*5.5x10^3 / 2*0.05 = 5.5x10^4 Hz.

Hence the other modes of frequency are integral multiplies of 5.5x10^4 Hz.

Magnetostriction Generation
In 1847 Joule discovered that when a rod of ferromagnetic substance like nickel is magnetized, it increases in length. This phenomenon is known as magnetostriction and can be used to produce ultrasonic. The variation in length is greater in nickel than any other ferromagnetic substances like iron and cobalt. Thus if an alternating field is applied parallel to the length of the nickel rod, each time field grows, its length varies resulting in setting up vibrations in it. The frequency of vibrations being

(Figure 3.5 Production of ultrasonic with magnetostriction method.)


[Page 33]
62 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

double to that of the applied alternating field. If the natural frequency of the vibration of the rod is nearly equal to the applied frequency, resonance occurs and amplitude of vibration becomes large. If the applied frequency is of the order of ultrasonic frequency, the rod can be made to send out ultrasonic waves. The frequency of the oscillator can be adjusted with the help of variable capacitor C.

The rod, being free at both ends, has a natural frequency given by,

f = 1 / 2l * sqrt(Y/p)

Where l = length of the rod
Y = young's modulus and
p = density of nickel rod.

By adjusting the length of the rod and capacitor of the condenser for resonance, high frequency oscillations of different frequencies can be obtained. A nickel rod, 10 cm long can procedure frequency of about 25 kHz.

3.10 Application of Ultrasound

The applications of ultrasonic are not limited to any branch of science. The major application of ultrasonic are listed below.

i) Directional signalling:
The wavelength of ultrasonic wave is very small in comparison to audible sound. Therefore it is possible to produce sharp beam of ultrasonic wave which does not spread out laterally by diffraction. This is the reason that ultrasonic waves have been used for signalling in any particular direction.

ii) Detection of submarine, aircraft:
Since ultrasonic waves have directional signalling properly they can be used in detecting submarine, aircraft etc. When the incident wave incidents with such objects they are reflected back and the reflected beam can be detected with the help of quartz receiver which is similar to the Radar.

iii) Depth of sea investigation:
Ultrasonic waves are used for determining the depth of sea, lakes etc. This is done by recording the time between sending a signal downward from the surface and receiving the echo. A special quartz crystal is made to send a series of ultrasonic wave train at intervals controlled by a motor. The reflected waves are received on the same quartz once it receives the reflected wave, it vibrates and emf is generated. The delay of this emf on that producing the waves is registered with a suitable oscillograph from which the depth can be measured.

iv) Structure of matter investigation:
First the velocity of ultrasonic in liquid and gases is determined. Later its variation with frequency, temperature etc provides the information about a number of properties of the medium like concentration, chemical structure, comparability, specific heat, absorption, atomic etc.

Acoustics 63

v) Detection of flaws in metals:
a. Vibrations are set up in the material to be tested with the help of quartz crystal generator attached at one end of the specimen. The other end a Rochelle crystal detector which is connected to a cathode ray oscilloscope after passing through amplifier and rectifier. The oscillator is modulated with some audio-frequency pulse. If there is no flows in the material, series of reflected pulses at regular intervals are observed. But if there is some flaws, then extra pulses will be observed.
b. These waves can also be used to detect cracks in metals. Cracks of thickness up to 0.0005 cm can be detected easily in a small time using x-ray technique.

vi) Elastic symmetries of crystals:
When ultrasonic waves are applied to certain crystals, they give rise to diffraction images which reveal the elastic symmetries of crystals.

vii) Mechanical Engineering:
A glass rod vibrating with ultrasonic frequency can bore a hole in a piece of steel or glass plate. It can make a hole through hard metals or alloy and also to diamond. High frequency ultrasonic waves of frequency 60 kHz are capable of shaking off the oily substances from metals. Therefore these waves are used for removing dust or grease etc. Similarly these waves are used to clear the cloths. Ultrasonic waves can be used to cure neuralgic and rheumatic pains and to restore contracted fingers. These waves are also used for loosing up scar tissues in various parts of the body. Nowadays ultrasonic waves are used in performing bloodless brain surgery.

viii) Chemical use:
a. A beam of ultrasonic wave keeps the constituents of alloys mixed uniformly that have different densities. Therefore these waves are used for making alloys of uniform composition. Iodine and sulphur can be liberated from a solution of potassium iodide and hydrogen sulphide respectively. Water is decomposed into Hydrogen ions by the action of ultrasonic waves. Ultrasonic wave reduce mercuric chloride into mercurous chloride.
b. Water and oil are immiscible. An emulsion of water and oil is obtained when the mixture is subjected to ultrasonic waves. Similarly an emulsion of water and mercury can be prepared.

ix) Biological use:
Ultrasonic waves are used to lame smaller animals like rat, fish, and frog and kill bacteria. The reproductory power of yeast is loosed if they are exposed to ultrasonic wave.

x) Medical use:
a. The ultrasonic waves have been widely used for examining the shape and movement of organs within the body. The image of ultrasonic waves that are reflected from the boundaries of the organs are obtained and is known as ultrasound scanning The ultrasonic waves reflected from moving objects like RBC exhibit a Doppler shift frequency that is used to measure the rate of flow of blood.
b. Ultrasonic waves are used by dentists for the proper extraction of broken teeth.

xi) Soldering:
a. Aluminium cannot be soldered by ordinary soldering method. To solder aluminium, ultrasonic waves are used in addition to the electric soldering iron. These waves remove the oxide film and facilitate soldering.


[Page 34]
64 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

3.11 Additional Examples:
1. A hall of floors is 15 × 30 m² along with height of 6 m, in which 500 people occupy upholstered seat and remainder seat on wooden chairs. Optimum reverberation time for orchestral music is 1.36 sec and absorption coefficient per person is 0.44. (a) Calculate the coefficient of absorption to be provided by the walls, floor and ceiling when the hall is fully occupied. (b) Calculate the reverberation time if only the half upholstered seats are occupied.

Solution:
(a) The optimum reverberation time is t = 1.36 sec.
Using Sabine's formula,
T = 0.158V / aS
Or, 1.36 = 0.158 × (15 × 30 × 6) / aS
Or, aS = 313.68 SI units
Absorption due to audience = 500 × 0.44 = 220 SI units
Hence the absorption provided by the walls, floor, and ceiling is
313.68 - 220 = 93.68 SI unit

(b) When the hall is only half filled the absorption will also be provided by vacant wooden seats in addition to the absorption by the audience.
250 × 0.44 = 110 SI unit.
The absorption by vacant wooden seats
= 250 × 0.02 = 5 SI unit.
So the total absorption of the hall
= 93.68 + 110 + 5 = 208.68 SI unit.
Here reverberation time, given by Sabine's formula, is now
T = 0.158V / aS = 0.158(15 × 30 × 6) / 208.68 = 158 × 15 × 30 × 6 / 213 = 2.04 sec

2. Calculate the reverberation time for a hall of volume 1400 m³, which has seating capacity of 110 persons with full capacity of audience and when audience are occupying only cushioned seats.
Relevant data can be taken from the following table.
Surface | Area (m²) | Coefficient of absorption
Plastered Wall | 98 | 0.03
Plastered Ceiling | 144 | 0.04
Wooden Door | 15 | 0.06
Cushioned Chairs | 88 | 1.00
Audience (each person) | 150 | 4.70

Solution:
We have total absorption in hall based on the given data,
The absorption by plastered wall = 98 × 0.03 = 2.94
Plastered Ceiling = 144 × 0.04 = 5.76
Wooden Door = 15 × 0.06 = 0.90
Cushioned Chairs = 88 × 1.0 = 88.0
Total absorption = 97.6
When the hall is with full capacity of 110 person, the absorption due to them
= 110 × 4.7 = 517
Now, total absorption
= 97.6 + 517 = 614.6
Reverberation time
T = 0.158V / aS = 0.158 × 1400 / 614.6
T = 0.36 sec

Acoustics 65
3. The volume of a room is 980 m³. The wall area of the room is 150 m², ceiling area 95 m² and floor area is 90 m². The average sound absorption coefficient (1) for wall is 0.03, (II) for ceiling is 0.80 and (iii) for the floor is 0.06. Calculate the average sound absorption coefficient and reverberation time.

Solution:
The average sound absorption coefficient
a = (a1S1 + a2S2 + a3S3) / (S1 + S2 + S3)
a = (0.03×150 + 0.80×95 + 0.06×90) / (150 + 95 + 90) = 0.256
And total area
S = 150 + 95 + 90 = 335
Now total absorption of the room
aS = 0.256 × 335 = 85.76 metric Sabines.
Reverberation time
T = 0.158V / aS = 0.158 × 980 / 85.76 = 1.80 sec

4. How much acoustic power enters the window of area 1.58 m², via the sound wave (standard intensity level = 10⁻¹² W/m²). The window opens on a street where the street noise results in an intensity level at the window of 60 dB.

Solution:
Given the intensity level at window = 60 dB
Area of the window = 1.58 m²
Standard intensity level Io = 10⁻¹² W/cm² = 10⁻¹² W/m²
We know that intensity level = 10 log10(I/Io) dB
Therefore 60 = 10 log10(I/10⁻¹²) dB
I = 10⁻⁶ W/m²
Acoustic Power = intensity × area = 1.58 × 10⁻⁶ W

5. A room has dimensions 6 × 4 × 5 m. Find (i) the mean free path of the sound wave in the room. (ii) Number of reflections made per sec by sound waves with the walls of the room. Given velocity of sound in air = 350 ms⁻¹.

Solution:
(i) The mean free path of sound waves may be defined as the average distance covered by a sound wave through air between two consecutive reflections with the walls of the room.
Mean free path σ = 4 × Volume / Area
In this case volume of the room = 6 × 4 × 5 = 120 m³
Total surface area = 2(6 × 4 + 4 × 5 + 6 × 5) = 148 m²
σ = 4 × 120 / 148 = 3.242 m
(ii) Number of reflection/sec,
n = velocity / σ = 350 / 3.243 = 108

4.1 Refraction at a Spherical Surface

In preparation of treatment of eye, we first examine the refraction of rays at the spherical surface of a transparent material. Such a surface could be one of the faces of a lens.

Figure 4.1 Ray diagram of refraction at a concave spherical surface.

Consider a point object O in a rarer medium having refractive index n1. Let a concave refracting surface of radius of curvature R separates the other medium that has refractive index n2 (figure 4.1). P is the pole of the concave surface such that OP is the principle axis of the refracting surface.

The ray OA is the incident ray on the spherical surface. From the laws of refraction, the refracting ray deviate towards the normal N along AB. If ray AB is extended backwards, it intersects the principle axis at I, forming the image at I. AN is the normal at A such that it meets the centre of curvature C in the principle axis. By geometry ∠OAC is incident angle (i) and ∠IAC is the angle of refraction (r). From figure 4.1 we may calculate the area of the triangles so formed.

Area of Δ OAI = Area of ΔOAC – Area of ΔIAC

But area of a triangle is given by half the product of sides and sine of angle between the sides. Therefore (from figure 4.1):


[Page 37]
70 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Lenses 71

or, 1/2 OA . AI sin (i - r) = 1/2 OA . AC sin i - 1/2 AI . AC sin r
or, OA . AI (sin i . cos r - cos i . sin r) = OA . AC sin i - AI . AC sin r
or, OA . AI (n2 sin r / n1 * cos r - cos i sin r) = OA . AC n2 sin r / n1 - AI . AC sin r [:: n1 sin i = n2 sin r]
or, n2 cos r . cos i / n1 . AC = n2 / n1 . 1 / OA
or, n2 cos r - n1 cos i / AC = n2 / n1 . 1 / OA ... 4.1
If we consider only rays that make small angles with the axis, such rays are called paraxial rays. Hence for paraxial rays
cos i = cos r = 1
OA = OP = object distance = u
AI = IP = image distance = v
AC = CP = radius of curvature = R
Then equation 4.1 reduces to,
n2 - n1 / R = n2 / v - n1 / u
Since object, image and centre of curvature all lie in the left hand side of the spherical surface, all distances are taken as negative.
or, n2 - n1 / -R = n2 / -v - n1 / -u
or, n2 - n1 / R = n2 / v - n1 / u ... 4.2
This is the general relation for refraction through spherical surfaces. If the spherical surface is kept in air (or vacuum); n1 = 1 and n2 = n. In such case the equation reduces to,
n / v - 1 / u = n - 1 / R ... 4.3

Example: 4.1
A concave spherical surface of R = 50 cm separates two media having refractive indices 3/2 and 4/3. A point object is kept at a distance of 20 cm having refractive index 3/2 from the surface. Determine the image distance.

Solution:
Here n1 = 3/2, n2 = 4/3
R = -50 cm, u = -20 cm [ These are on the left side of the surface.]
So, using n2 - n1 / R = n2 / v - n1 / u

or, 4/3 - 3/2 / 2(-20) = 4/3v - 3/2(-50)
or, 4/3 - 3/2 / 40 = 8-9 / 6(-50)
or, 4/3v - 1/300 = 3/40
or, 4/3v = 2-45 / 600
or, v = - 18.60 cm.
The image will form in the left side at a distance 18.60 cm from the surface.

4.2 Principal Foci
We have from the Equation 4.2 of refraction at a spherical surface
n2 / v - n1 / u = n2 - n1 / R
If the object is at infinity then
n2 / v - n1 / infinity = n2 - n1 / R
or, v = n2 R / n2 - n1 = f2 ... 4.4
This value of image distance when the image it at infinity is referred as second principal focus length, f2. The image position when the object is kept at infinity is called the second principal focus of the refracting surface.

In the same manner, if the object is kept in such a way that the emergent ray becomes parallel to the principal axis (v = infinity), the value of object distance for such happening is referred as first principal focal length of the refracting surface. In other words the position of axial point object whose image after refraction through the surface is formed at infinity is termed as first principal focus of the refracting surface. Mathematically
n2 / infinity - n1 / u = n2 - n1 / R
or, u = n1 R / n2 - n1 = f1 ... 4.5
Using equation 4.4 and 4.5 we may write,
f1 / n1 + f2 / n2 = 0
or, f1 / f2 = - n1 / n2 ... 4.6
Since refractive indices are always positive quantities and are not equal, the two focal lengths are also unequal and have opposite signs.
The two foci so obtained are referred as Principal Foci.


[Page 38]
72
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

4.3 Refraction Through a Lens

The most important, simple optical device is the thin lens. A lens is a portion of a transparent refracted medium. A thin lens is usually round and its two faces are each a portion of a sphere. Although cylindrical surfaces are also possible, we will concentrate on spherical ones. Such lens is built up from a series of small angled prism whose refracting angle changes continuously.

Consider a thin lens of material of refractive index n2 separates a medium of refractive index n1 on its two sides. The radius of curvature of the coaxial refracting surfaces is R1 and R2. Consider a point object O, be kept on the principal axis at distance u from the first refracting surface as shown in figure 4.2

Figure 4.2 Refraction through a thin lens.

When light from the object O falls on the first refracting surface of the lens the image I1 is formed at a distance v1 from the first refracting surface. So
n2/v1 - n1/u = (n2 - n1) / R1 ... 4.7

This image I1 acts as virtual object in medium of refracting index n2 for the second refracting surface and forms the final image I at distance v from the second refracting surface in medium of refractive index n1. Again
n1/v - n2/v1 = (n1 - n2) / R2 ... 4.8

Upon adding equation 4.7 and 4.8 we may write;
n1/v - n1/u = (n2 - n1) (1/R1 - 1/R2)

or,
1/v - 1/u = (n2/n1 - 1) (1/R1 - 1/R2) ... 4.9

When the lens is in air,
1/v - 1/u = (n-1) (1/R1 - 1/R2) ... 4.10

Where n is the refractive index of the material of the lens with respect to air. The equation 4.10 so obtained is lens maker formula. This relation holds true for paraxial rays for which the angles made by the rays are very small. However, the relation is true for thin lens where the thickness is negligibly small compared to the object distance, image distance and radii of curvatures.

As explained earlier in case of a lens also, we can obtain the two principal foci with focal lengths f2 and f1 as,
1/f2 = (n-1) (1/R1 - 1/R2) ... 4.11
1/f1 = -(n-1) (1/R1 - 1/R2) ... 4.12

Lenses 73
For lens placed in air, it has air on both sides. In such case f1 = f2 but of opposite signs. The above relation is also called lens maker's formula.

Example: 4.2
A Plano convex lens has one flat surface and the other surface has R = 18.4 cm. What is the focal length? (n=1.51)

Solution:
A plane surface has infinite radius of curvature, we call this as R1 (where light enters),
So 1/R1 = 0
1/f = (n-1) (1/R1 - 1/R2) = (1.51 - 1.00) (0 - 1/18.4)
= -36 cm.
Thus the lens is diverging with a focal length of 36 cm.

Figure 4.3 A real image formed after refraction through a lens.

4.4 Least Possible Distance between an Object and its Real Image in a Convex Lens

Consider a thin convex lens. Let the focal length of the lens be f. O is the point object on the principal axis and after refraction the image I is formed. Let the distance between O and I be d and the distance of image from the lens be x. Here u = object distance is negative and v = image distance is positive. Thus;
v = x
u = -(d-x)

We know,
1/v - 1/u = 1/f
or, 1/x - 1/-(d-x) = 1/f
or, d-x+x / x(d-x) = 1/f
or, xd-x^2 = fd
or, x^2 - xd + fd = 0

This is a quadratic equation and its solution is given by
x = d +- sqrt(d^2 - 4fd) / 2 ... 4.13
For image to be real, the distance x should be real. This condition is satisfied if
d^2 - 4fd >= 0


[Page 39]
74 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

d^2 >= 4fd
or, d >= 4f ... 4.14

Thus d should be greater than 4f, or at least should be equal to 4f for the image to be real. Therefore the minimum distance between real object and real image is 4f.

4.5 Deviation by a Lens

Consider a monochromatic light ray incident at a height h, in a thin lens parallel to the principal axis. Let the focal length of the lens be f. After refraction, the ray will pass through the second principal focus F of the lens as shown in figure 4.4.

Figure 4.4 Refraction of a parallel beam of light.

Here the deviation produced be δ and
tan δ = h/f

The deviation produced by the paraxial rays is small indicating tan δ ≅ δ. Therefore,
δ = h/f ... 4.15

Similarly let O be a point object in the principal axis and I is the point image of it but the ray strikes at the same height as in figure 4.5. The deviation here is also δ.

From figure 4.5 the deviation suffered by the ray is
δ = α + β

Figure 4.5 Refraction of a point object to produce the image I

Since the rays are paraxial (δ ≅ tan δ; α ≅ tan α; β ≅ tan β)
δ = tan α + tan β
or, δ = h/-u + h/v = h(1/v - 1/-u) = h/f ... 4.16

It is clear from equation 4.15 and 4.16 that the deviation produced by a lens is independent of the object position.

4.6 Combination of Two Thin Lenses Separated by a Finite Distance

Consider two thin lenses L1 and L2 having focal lengths f1 and f2 are placed coaxially as shown in figure 4.6 and are separated by a finite distance d. Consider a monochromatic ray AB, parallel to principal to principal axis is incident on the lens L1 at a height h1. This ray is refracted towards BF1 (assume in absence of F2). Here F1 is the second principal focus of the first lens. Therefore the deviation produced by the first lens, δ1, becomes,

δ1 = h1/f1 ... 4.17

Figure 4.6 Refraction through a combination of lenses.

The ray BF1 meets the second lens L2 at C, at a height h2 from the principal axis and gets emerged from this lens towards CF. F is the second principal focus of the combination. Let the deviation by the second lens be δ2 and we have,
δ2 = h2/f2 ... 4.18

Where, f2 is the focal length of the second lens. If we produce the first incident ray AB and final emergent ray CF, they will intersect at a point E as indicated in figure 4.6. Therefore if a thin lens is placed at E rather at P2, it will produce the same deviation as the lenses L1, and L2 together. This imaginary lens of focal length P2F (= f) is called an equivalent lens. As indicated in the figure, the deviation produced can be replaced as,
δ = h1/f ... 4.19

Now from geometry we can write,
δ = δ1 + δ2
or, h1/f = h1/f1 + h2/f2 ... 4.20

Since Δ BL1F1 and Δ CL2F1 are similar. This provides the sides of these triangles have proportionate relation.
BL1 / L1F1 = CL2 / L2F1
or, h1 / f1 = h2 / (f1 - d)
or, h2 = h1(f1 - d) / f1 ... 4.21

Using equation 4.20 and 4.21 we can write,
h1/f = h1/f1 + h1(f1 - d) / f1f2
or, 1/f = 1/f1 + (f1 - d) / f1f2
or, 1/f = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - d / f1f2 ... 4.22

This relation provides the equivalent focal length of two thin lenses separated by a distance d. We may further solve equation 4.22 to yield,
1/f = (f2 + f1 - d) / f1f2


[Page 40]
or,
f = - f1f2 / (d - (f1 + f2)) ... 4.23
The quantity d - (f1 + f2) is referred as optical separation between two lenses. Numerically it is the distance between second principal focus of first lens (L1) and first principal focus of the second lens (L2). Further let the distance between the imaginary equivalent lens and the second lens be β (= P2L2). The similar triangles EP2F and CL2F yields;
P2F / L2F = EP2 / CL2
or, f / (f - β) = h1 / h2
Since distance β is in the left side of L2, following the sign convention one can rewrite.
f / (f + β) = h1 / h2 = h1 f1 / (h1(f1 - d)) (From equation 4.21)
or, f / (f + β) = f1 / (f1 - d)
or, ff1 - fd = ff1 + f1β
or, β = - fd / f1 ... 4.24
Considering in the similar fashion for the rays as indicated in figure 4.7, triangles EP1F and AL1F are similar.

Therefore,
EP / P1F = AL1 / L1F
or, h2 / f = h1 / (f - α) ... 4.25
The other pairs of similar triangles BL2F2 and AL1F2 yields
BL2 / L2F2 = AL1 / L1F2
or, h2 / f2 = h1 / (f2 - d) ... 4.26
Solving from equation 4.25 and 4.26,

h1 f2 / (f2 - d) = h1 / (f - α)
ff2 - f2α = ff2 - fd
or, α = + fd / f2 ... 4.27
In case if the two lenses are in contact, distance between them, d = 0 which will provide the equivalent focal length of the lenses in contact as
1/f = 1/f1 + 1/f2 from equation 4.22
The points P1 and P2 in figure 4.7 and 4.6 are called the principal points.

4.7 Power of a Lens
Optometrists and ophthalmologists, instead of using the focal length, use the reciprocal of the focal length to specify the strength of lenses. Power of a lens is its magnifying (or diverging) capacity. It is defined as the reciprocal of focal length expressed in meter. Thus,
Power of lens (P) = 1 / (focal length (in m)) ... 4.28
The unit of lens power is diopter (D) which is an inverse meter: 1 D = 1 m^-1.
A 20 cm focal length lens, for example, has a power P = 1 / 0.20m = 5 D. The power of a converging lens is positive, whereas that for a diverging lens is negative. For the combination of lenses separated by a distance we use the following relation for its focal length
1/f = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - d / (f1 f2)
∴ P = P1 + P2 - d P1 P2 ... 4.29

Example: 4.3
Two thin convex lenses having focal lengths 10 cm and 4 cm are coaxially separated by a distance of 5 cm. Find the equivalent focal length of the combination. Determine also the position of the principal points.

Solution:
Given: f1 = 10 cm, f2 = 4 cm, d = 5 cm
We know, 1/f = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - d / (f1 f2)
or, 1/f = 1/10 + 1/4 - 5 / (10 * 4)
or, 1/f = 9/40
∴ f = 4.4 cm
Again α = df/f2 = 5 * 4.4 / 4 = 5.5 cm


[Page 41]
78 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Lenses 79
β = - df/f1 = - (5*4.4)/10 = - 2.2 cm
The first principal point is at 5.5 cm from first lens on its right and second principal point is at 2.2 cm from second lens on its left.

Example: 4.4
Two thin lenses of focal lengths 8 cm each are identical and coaxially separated by 4 cm. Determine the equivalent focal length of this lens combination and illustrate the principal points in figure 4.9. If the image is formed at infinity at a particular position of the object, find the object distance.

Solution:
Given, f1 = f2 = 8 cm, d = 4 cm.
So, 1/f = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - d/(f1 f2)
or, 1/f = 1/8 + 1/8 - 4/(8*8)
∴ f = 64/12 = 5.33 cm.
And α = - df/f2 = - (4*5.33)/8 = - 2.67 cm
β = - df/f1 = - (4*5.33)/8 = - 2.67 cm
Again for the image to be formed at infinity, replace v = ∞, u = ? and f = 5.33 in the following equation.
1/v - 1/u = 1/f
or, - 1/u = 1/5.33
or, u = - 5.33 cm
This is the distance of object from the first principal point in left side so the object is at 2.66 cm away on the left side of first lens or, 5.33 cm on the left side of first principal point.

4.8 Cardinal Points
While formulating the refraction through lenses, the thickness of the lens has been neglected. We refer the object distance, image distance and focal length as the distance from the lens to object, image and focus respectively. But from which point or surface is not defined clearly. This provides more confusion while solving the equations while considering the thick lenses. In 1841, Gauss defined two theoretical planes as reference points to measure the various distances to solve the formulae.

There are six different points which are considered as reference points to measure various distances in the refraction through a thick lens and in a system of coaxial lenses. These six points of reference are called cardinal points of an optical system. The cardinal points are composed of the following points.
a) two principal focal points
b) two principal points
c) two nodal points

a) Two Principal Focal Points
Consider a thick lens optical system as shown in figure 4.8. Consider parallel rays from infinity incident on the lens system. These rays after refraction, converges or appear to diverge from a point F2 in the principal axis. This is referred as the second principal focal point of the optical system. In the same way, rays from a point, or directed towards an axial point F1, becomes parallel to the principal axis after refraction. This particular axial point corresponds to first principal focal point of the system. These two sets of axial points (F1 and F2) are called principal foci. The planes passing through F1 and F2 and perpendicular to principal axis are called the focal planes.

b) Two Principal Points
Consider a thick lens system or two coaxial lens system as shown in figure 4.9. The system has two principal foci F1 and F2. The parallel ray of incident light (one arrowhead) after refraction intersect the principal axis at F2 as shown.

When the incident ray and final emergent rays are produced they intersect at a point H2. A plane passing through H2 and perpendicular to principal axis intersects the principal axis at P2. This plane is called second principal plane and point P2 is referred as second principal point. Similarly ray from F1, after refraction passes parallel to the principal axis (two arrowhead rays). The incident ray from F1 and final emergent ray, if produced meet at H1. A plane containing H1 and perpendicular to principal axis is termed as first principal plane. The point P1 in the principal axis is referred as first principal point.

The object distance is generally measured from P1 whereas the image distance from P2. Moreover, the focal distance, F1 and F2, are measured from P1 and P2 respectively.


[Page 42]
80 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

c) Two Nodal Points

Nodal points are a pair of conjugate points on the principal axis which have unit positive angular magnification. If a ray of light is directed towards one of the Nodal points, after refraction, it appears to proceed from the second conjugate point in the parallel direction. In figure 4.10, N1 and N2 are the nodal points.

Figure 4.12 Graphical representations of nodal points.

The angle made by incident ray and principal axis is θ1 and that by the refracted ray is θ2. Then angular magnification, m is
m = (angle made by refracted ray) / (angle made by incident ray) = θ2 / θ1 = 1 (for nodal points)

This means at nodal points, the angles subtended are equal. In case if a lens is placed in air, or other medium, such that the medium on both sides of a lens are optically same, the focal length f1 and f2 are equal. In such situation the nodal points coincides with the principal points.

4.9 Chromatic Aberration

A lens is composed of a large number of prisms of varying refracting angles placed one after other. The refracting angle of the prism goes on decreasing at a uniform rate from its centre to outwards in convex lens. If a ray, consisting of various colours is allowed to fall on a lens, it is dispersed into its constituent colours. The deviation for different colours is different. Therefore the dispersed rays focus at different points in the axis and there will be coloured fringes in the image. This phenomenon of formation of different images (different foci for colours), is referred as chromatic aberration.

Figure 4.13 Chromatic aberration produced by a lens.

The refractive index for violet rays is greater than that for red light in visible light. Thus the focal length of a lens for violet rays is less than that for the red rays. This causes the violet rays to focus closer than the red rays. The images (focal lengths) of all other intermediate colours lie in between these extreme colours accordingly. The complete image would become a linear spectrum. Since the concentration of blue and red colour in white light is more than other colours, for simplicity, we prefer the focal lengths of these colours only, so focal length of blue colour is less compared to that for red colour.

Consider a chromatic object O in the principal axis of a lens as shown in figure 4.13. The rays after refracting through the lens disperse into the constituent colours. Let Fr be focus for red and Fb be that for blue colour with focal lengths fr and fb. fr - fb is a measure of longitudinal chromatic aberration. Since the focal length depends upon the colour or

Lenses 81

wavelength of light, the images of different colours are magnified to different extents. This defect is called lateral chromatic aberration. The combined effect causes a blurred image. This defect in colour produced by a lens is known as chromatic aberration.

The focal length of a lens is given by,
1/f = (n-1) (1/R1 - 1/R2)
or, (1/R1 - 1/R2) = 1 / (f(n-1)) ... 4.30
∴ 1/fb = (nb-1) (1/R1 - 1/R2)
or, 1/fb = (nb-1) / ((n-1)f) ... 4.31
and 1/fr = (nr-1) / ((n-1)f) ... 4.32

Figure 4.14 Figure showing the circle of least confusion.

Where fs are focal lengths and n's are the refractive indices of respective colours. f and n are those for mean colour. From equations 4.31 and 4.32 we can write,
1/fb - 1/fr = (nb-nr) / ((n-1)f)
or, (fr-fb) / (fbfr) = ω/f
Where, ω is the dispersive power of the material of the lens.

Now, fbfr ≈ f^2 and fr-fb = longitudinal chromatic aberration. So,
fr-fb = ωf ... 4.33
Therefore the longitudinal chromatic aberration = dispersive power × mean focal length.

It should be noted here that a single lens cannot form an image free from chromatic aberration.

4.10 Circle of Least Confusion

Consider a white light illuminated point object O in the principal axis. Rays from this point object enters a lens of focal length f. After refraction through the lens, blue and red image will form at B and R respectively. If an XY screen is placed between these images blurred images of different colours will be observed. If XY screen is placed as shown, the image of least chromatic aberration is observed in the screen. Let the object distance be u, the blue and red image distances be vb and vr respectively. Then we can write:


[Page 43]
82 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

1/v_b - 1/u = 1/f_b ... 4.34
and 1/v_r - 1/u = 1/f_r ... 4.35
or, 1/v_b - 1/v_r = 1/f_b - 1/f_r
or, (v_r - v_b) / (v_b v_r) = (f_r - f_b) / (f_b f_r)
but, v_b v_r ≈ v^2 and f_b f_r ≈ f^2
so, (v_r - v_b) / v^2 = (f_r - f_b) / f^2
or, v_r - v_b = v^2 (f_r - f_b) / f^2 ... 4.36
From geometry, Δ PQR is similar to Δ MNR
SR / PQ = ZR / MN ... 4.37
and Δ PBQ is similar to MBN.
SB / PQ = ZB / MN ... 4.38
From equation 4.37 and 4.38 we may write,
(SR + SB) / PQ = (ZR + ZB) / MN
or, (v_r + v_b) / D = (v_r - v_b) / d ... 4.39
Where MN = d, is diameter of circle of least confusion and PQ = D, is the diameter of the lens. Since v_r + v_b ≈ 2v, Eq. 4.39 will become
2v / D = (v_r - v_b) / d
or, 2vd = (v_r - v_b) D
or, d = [(v_r - v_b) / 2v] * D ... 4.40
Eliminate v_r - v_b from equation 4.36 and 4.40 to produce,
d = [v^2 (f_r - f_b) / f^2 * 2v] * D = (1/2) * D * ω * (v/f) ... 4.41
for a parallel beam of incident light v = f. So in this particular case,
d = (1/2) * D * ω ... 4.42
Therefore when a parallel beam of light is incident on the lens, diameter of circle of least confusion depends on the diameter of lens (or lens aperture) and the dispersive power of the material of the lens but is independent of the focal length of the lens.

4.11 Achromatism
Chromatic aberration discussed earlier can be eliminated for any two colours and can be reduced greatly for all colours by the use of two lenses made of different materials. This phenomenon of elimination of the defect is referred as achromatism. The lenses used for achromatism, in general, have different indices of refraction. Normally one lens is converging and other is diverging. In many cases they are cemented together. Such type of combination is called an achromatic doublet or colour corrected lens.

4.12 Achromatism of Two Lenses in Contact
The achromatic combination is made by placing two lenses of different materials and suitable focal lengths in contact, so that the focal length of the combination is the same for both the colours.

Consider the two lenses have the mean focal lengths f and f' and mean refractive index of their materials as n and n' respectively f_b, f_r and f'_b, f'_r are the corresponding colour focal lengths of the lenses. Now for the first lens for blue rays, we have
1/f_b = (n_b - 1) * (1/R_1 - 1/R_2)
or, 1/f_b = (n_b - 1) / (n - 1)f ... 4.43
since, 1/f = (n - 1) * (1/R_1 - 1/R_2)
Similarly for red rays,
1/f_r = (n_r - 1) / (n - 1)f ... 4.44
For second lens of focal length f' and refractive index n', we have
1/f'_b = (n'_b - 1) / (n' - 1)f' ... 4.45
1/f'_r = (n'_r - 1) / (n' - 1)f' ... 4.46

Consider F_b and F_r be the focal lengths of the combination of the two lenses for blue and red rays respectively, then
1/F_b = 1/f_b + 1/f'_b = (n_b - 1) / ((n - 1)f) + (n'_b - 1) / ((n' - 1)f') ... 4.47
and 1/F_r = 1/f_r + 1/f'_r = (n_r - 1) / ((n - 1)f) + (n'_r - 1) / ((n' - 1)f') ... 4.48

For no chromatic aberration, the combined focal lengths for blue and red colours should be equal. So that


[Page 44]
84 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Fb = Fr
or, 1/Fb = 1/Fr
. (nb-1)/(n-1)f + (nb'-1)/(n'-1)f' = (nr-1)/(n-1)f + (nr'-1)/(n'-1)f'
or, (nb-1)/(n-1)f - (nr-1)/(n-1)f = - (nb'-1)/(n'-1)f' + (nr'-1)/(n'-1)f'
or, (nb-nr)/(n-1)f = - (nb'-nr')/(n'-1)f'
or, ω/f = - ω'/f' ... 4.49

Here ω and ω' are the dispersive powers of the materials of the two lenses. Their values are always positive. Equation 4.49 gives the condition for achromatism and indicates that f and f' must have opposite signs. Thus the achromatic doublet consists of one convex lens and the other concave lens of different materials.

Alternative Method
The above result can be deduced by the use of differential calculus. We know the Lens maker's formula for a lens is,
1/f = (n-1)(1/R1 - 1/R2)

Differentiating,
- df/f² = dn(1/R1 - 1/R2)
or, - df/f² = dn/(n-1)f ... 4.50

If nb and nr are the refractive indices for the blue and red rays, then dn is the change in refractive index (nb-nr).
Hence, Dispersive power (ω) = dn/(n-1)
. - df/f² = ω/f ... 4.51

When two lenses of focal lengths f and f' are placed in contact to form an achromatic combination, then focal length F of the combination is given by,
1/F = 1/f + 1/f'

Upon differentiating,
- dF/F² = - df/f² - df'/f'²

To bring different colours into focus at a point; dF, the change in focal length of the combination should be zero.

So, df/f² + df'/f'² = 0, dF = 0
or, ω/f + ω'/f' = 0

Where ω and ω' are the dispersive powers of the materials of the two lenses and are positive quantity. Which gives condition for achromatism.

4.13 Achromatism of Two Lenses Separated by a Finite Distance
Consider f1 and f2 be the mean focal lengths of two lenses separated by a distance d. For simplicity assume the lenses are made up of same material. Let n, nb and nr be refractive indices for mean, blue and red rays for both the lenses. Consider fr, fr' and fb, fb' be focal lengths of two lenses for red and blue rays.

Now we know,
1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - d/f1f2
1/Fr = 1/fr + 1/fr' - d/frfr' ... 4.52
1/Fb = 1/fb + 1/fb' - d/fbfb'

Where F, Fr and Fb are combined focal lengths for mean, red and blue colours respectively. In similar way as in equations 4.43 and 4.44 we may write,
1/fr = (nr-1)/(n-1)f1 + (nr-1)/(n-1)f2
1/fb = (nb-1)/(n-1)f1 + (nb-1)/(n-1)f2

Thus from Eq.4.52 we may deduce,
1/Fr = (nr-1)/(n-1)f1 + (nr-1)/(n-1)f2 - (nr-1)²/(n-1)² d/f1f2
and
1/Fb = (nb-1)/(n-1)f1 + (nb-1)/(n-1)f2 - (nb-1)²/(n-1)² d/f1f2

But for achromatism, Fr = Fb
or
1/Fr = 1/Fb => (nr-1)/(n-1)f1 + (nr-1)/(n-1)f2 - (nr-1)²/(n-1)² d/f1f2
= (nb-1)/(n-1)f1 + (nb-1)/(n-1)f2 - (nb-1)²/(n-1)² d/f1f2

Lenses 85


[Page 45]
86 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Lenses 87

or, (n-1)[1/f1 + 1/f2] - (n-1)^2 d / (n-1)^2 f1f2
= -(n-1)[1/f1 + 1/f2] - (n-1)^2 d / (n-1)^2 f1f2

or, (nr-nb)[1/f1 + 1/f2] = d / (n-1)^2 f1f2 [(nr-1)^2 - (nb-1)^2]

or, (nb-nr)[1/f1 + 1/f2] = d / (n-1)f1f2 [(nb-nr)(nb+nr-2)]

or, 1/f1 + 1/f2 = d/(n-1)f1f2 (2n-2) nb+nr=2n
or, 1/f1 + 1/f2 = 2d / f1f2
:. d = (f1+f2)/2 ... 4.53
Hence for achromatic combination of two lenses separated by a distance, the distance between them should be equal to the mean focal length of the two lenses.

Alternative Method
We know the equivalent focal length for the combination of two lenses of focal lengths f1 and f2 is
1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - x/f1f2 ... 4.54
Where, x is the distance of separation of two coaxial lenses.
Differentiating equation 4.54 we will have,
-dF/F^2 = -df1/f1^2 - df2/f2^2 - x(df1/f1^2f2 - df2/f1f2^2) ... 4.55
But for achromatism
or, dF = 0.
also -df1/f1 = -df2/f2 = w for lenses of same materials
Where, w is the dispersive power of the lens material.
Now equation 4.55 reduces to,

w/f1 + w/f2 - x(w/f1f2 + w/f1f2) = 0
or, 1/f1 + 1/f2 = 2x / f1f2
or, x = (f1+f2)/2 ... 4.56

Example: 4.5
A lens made of crown glass has focal length 50 cm. Calculate the longitudinal chromatic aberration. Given refractive indices of crown and flint glasses are 1.5164 and 1.5249.
Solution:
The refractive index of mean colour light is not given therefore we use mean
n = 1.5206.
Then, w = delta n / (n-1) = (n1-n2)/(n-1) = 0.0085/0.5206 = 0.0164
Thus, longitudinal chromatic aberration = wf = 0.0164 * 50 = 8.82 cm.

Example: 4.6
Dispersive powers of two different glass types are 0.0163 and 0.0243 respectively. How can you design an achromatic contact doublet of focal length 50 cm.
Solution:
For achromatic doublet,
w/f + w'/f' = 0
or, 0.0163/f + 0.0243/f' = 0 ... i
Also, 1/f + 1/f' = 1/F
or, 1/f + 1/f' = 1/50 ... ii
Solving these two equations we get,
f = 4000/243 = 16.5 cm
f' = 4000/-163 = -24.5 cm
So we can form an achromat by cementing two lenses of focal lengths 16.5 cm and 24.5 cm of those two materials. Also one of the lenses will be a convex lens and other a concave lens.

4.14 Additional Examples:
1. A coaxial lens system placed in air has two lenses of equal focal lengths f are separated by a distance 2f/3. Find the positions of the cardinal points. The lens system is shown in figure AE 1.
Solution:
Here
f1=f2=f, d=2f/3
The equivalent focal length is
feq = f1f2 / (f1+f2-d) = f^2 / (f+f-2f/3) = 3f/4
Distance of the first principal point P' from the first lens L1,
a = O1P' = feq*d / f2
= (3f/4 * 2f/3) / f
= (f/2), towards right
Distance of the first focal point F' from the first lens L1,
O1F' = feq - a = f' P' - O1P'
= 3f/4 - f/2
= f/4, towards left
Distance of the second principal point P from the second lens L2,
beta = O2P = feq * d / f1 = (3f/4 * 2f/3) / f
= f/2, towards left
Distance of the second focal point F from the second lens L2,
O2F = feq - beta = FP - O2P
= 3f/4 - f/2
= f/4, towards right
The nodal points coincide with the corresponding principal points as the medium is the same on both sides of the lens system.
2. The dispersive powers of crown and flint glasses are 0.015 and 0.030, respectively. Their refractive indices for the mean rays are 1.52 and 1.65, respectively. If one of the surfaces of the flint glass lens is plane, calculate the radii of curvature of the other surface of the flint glass lens and both the surfaces of the crown glass lens, so that they combine to form an achromatic doublet of focal length 80 cm.
Solution:
An achromatic doublet consists of a convex lens and a concave lens, and the combination is converging. Let the focal lengths of the two be f1 and f2 respectively. Then f1 < |f2|.
Further f1/f2 = -w1/w2 for achromatism.
The above condition shows that w1 < w2. Therefore, the crown glass lens must be convex and the flint glass lens must be concave. We have
f1/f2 = 0.015 / 0.030 = 1/2
Or, f2 = -2f1
Now, F = f1f2 / (f1+f2) = f1(-2f1) / (f1-2f1) = 2f1
This provides f1 = F/2 = 80/2 = 40 cm
And f2 = -2f1 = -80 cm
Let the radii of curvature of the two surfaces of the flint glass (concave) lens be R1 and R2. Here, R2 = infinity. Therefore,
1/f2 = (n2 - 1) (1/R1 - 1/R2)
Or, R1 = (n2 - 1)f2
= (1.65 - 1)(-80) = -52cm
Let radii of curvature of the two surfaces of the crown glass (convex) lens be R'1 and R'2. Then R'2 = R'2 = -52.0cm
Now, 1/40 = (1.52 - 1) (1/R'1 + 1/52)
Which yields R'1 = 34.7 cm.
Thus, the radii of curvature of the crown glass lens are 34.7cm and 52 cm, and those for the flint glass lens are 52.0 cm and infinite.
3. A coaxial lens system placed in air has two lenses of focal lengths 3f and f, separated by a distance 2f. Find the positions of the cardinal points.
Solution:
Here f1 = 3f, f2 = f, d = 2f
The equivalent focal length is,
feq = f1f2 / (f1+f2-d) = (3f)(f) / (3f+f-2f) = 3f/2
Distance of the first principal point P' from the first lens L1,
P' = O1P' = feq*d / f2 = (3f/2 * 2f) / f = 3f, towards right
Distance of the first focal point F' from the first lens L1,
O1F' = p' - feq = O1P' - F'P'
= 3f - 3f/2
= 3f/2, towards right
Distance of the second principal point P from the second lens L2,
p = O2P = feq*d / f1 = (3f/2 * 2f) / 3f = f, towards left


[Page 47]
90 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Distance of the second focal point F from the second lens L2,
O2F = feq - p = FP - O2P
= 3/2 - f = -1/2, towards right.

4. Two thin converging lenses of focal lengths 3 cm and 4 cm are placed coaxially in air separated by a distance of 2 cm. An object is placed 4 cm in front of the first lens. Find the position and nature of the image.

Solution:
Given f1 = 3 cm, f2 = 4 cm, d = 2 cm
Equivalent focal length is
feq = (f1 f2) / (f1 + f2 - d) = (3 * 4) / (3 + 4 - 2) = 2.4 cm
Distance of the first principal point from the first lens,
a = (f d) / f2 = (2.4 * 2) / 4 = 1.2 cm, towards right
Distance of the second principal point from the second lens
b = (f d) / f1 = (2.4 * 2) / 3 = 1.6 cm, towards left
Object distance u = -(4 + 1.2) = - 5.2 cm
Using the formula,
1/v - 1/u = 1/f
We have,
1/v = 1/f + 1/u = 1/2.4 + 1/-5.2
Or,
v = 4.46 cm
Distance of the image from the second lens = 4.46 - 1.6 = 2.86 cm.
The image is real.

5. An achromatic doublet of focal length 90 cm is to be made of two lenses, the material of one having 1.5 times the dispersive power of the other. The doublet is a converging type. Find the focal length of each lens.

Solution:
An achromatic doublet consists of a converging lens and a diverging lens. Let the focal lengths of the two be f1 and f2 respectively. According to the sign convention, the value of f2 is negative. Since the doublet is converging type, f1 < |f2|.
Now,
f2 / f1 = - w1 / w2 = - 1.5 / 1 = -3/2 f1
Further,
F = (f1 f2) / (f1 + f2) provides
90 = (f1 * (-3/2) f1) / (f1 - 3/2 f1) = (-3/2 f1^2) / (-1/2 f1) = 3 f1
f1 = 30 cm (convex)
f2 = - (3/2) f1 = -45 cm (concave)

6. The focal lengths of a thin convex lens are 1 m and 0.968 m for red and blue rays respectively. Calculate the chromatic aberration and dispersive power of the material of the lens.

Solution:
Chromatic aberration = fr - fb = 1 m - 0.968 m = 0.032 m

Lenses 91
We have dispersive power, w = (fr - fb) / f = (fr - fb) / sqrt(fr fb) = 0.032 / sqrt(1 * 0.968) = 0.0325

7. An achromatic converging combination of focal length 0.60 m is formed by placing a convex lens of crown glass and a concave lens of flint glass in contact with each other. Calculate their focal lengths, if dispersive power of crown glass is 0.03 and that of flint glass is 0.05.

Solution:
Focal length for a combination of lenses in contact is given by,
1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2
For achromatism,
w1/f1 + w2/f2 = 0
Since the focal length of a converging lens is +ve and that of a divergent lens is -ve, according to convention,
or,
1/0.60 = 1/f1 + 1/-f2 and 0.03/f1 + 0.05/-f2 = 0
Solving we get, f1 = 0.24 m and f2 = 0.40 m

8. It is required to form a converging achromatic system of focal length 0.30 m consisting of two lenses made of the same material. If one of the lenses be convex and of focal length 0.20 m, find (a) the focal length of the other, and (b) the distance between the lenses.

Solution:
We know the equivalent focal length of the combination of two lenses separated by a distance x is
1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - x / (f1 f2)
1/0.3 = 1/0.2 - 1/f2 - x / (0.2 * f2)
And,
Or,
0.2 = f2 - 2x = 0
2x = 0.2 + f2
Solving the above equations, we get,
xf2 = +0.6m
Or,
x = 0.4 m
Thus the other lens is convergent and is of focal length 0.6 m at a distance 0.4 m from the first lens.

9. The object glass of a telescope is an achromat of focal length 90 cm. If the magnitudes of the dispersive powers of the two lenses are 0.024 and 0.036, calculate their focal lengths.

Solution:
Here, w1 = 0.024, w2 = 0.036, F = 90 cm
We have, for achromatism,
w1/f1 + w2/f2 = 0
Or,
1/f2 = - (w1 / w2) * (1/f1) = - 0.024 / 0.036f1 = -2 / 3f1
Also,
1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2
Or,
1/90 = 1/f1 - 2 / 3f1 = 1 / 3f1
.:
f1 = 30 cm, f2 = -3f1 / 2 = -45cm


[Page 48]
92 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Lenses 93
10. It is desired to make a converging achromatic lens of mean focal length 30 cm by using two lenses of materials A and B. If the dispersive powers of A and B are in the ratio 1:2, find the focal length of each lens.
Solution:
Here w1/w2 = 1/2
Now, for achromatism
w1/f1 + w2/f2 = 0, provides 1/f2 = -w1/(w2 f1)
Or, 1/f2 = -1/(2 f1)
Also, 1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2 = 1/30
Or, 1/f1 - 1/(2 f1) = 1/30
Or, 1/(2 f1) = 1/30 => f1 = 15cm
Thus from above equation we may write,
f2 = -2 x 15 = -30cm.

11. A converging achromat of 40 cm focal length is to be constructed out of a thin crown glass lens and a thin flint glass lens, the surface in contact having a common radius of curvature of 25 cm. Calculate the radius of curvature of the second surface of each lens, given that the values of the dispersive powers and mean refractive indices are 0.017 and 1.5 for crown glass, and 0.034 and 1.7 for flint glass.
Solution:
Given, w1 = 0.017, w2 = 0.034, n1 = 1.5, n2 = 1.7
We know for achromatism,
w1/f1 + w2/f2 = 0
Or, 1/f2 = -w1/(w2 f1) = -1/(2 f1)
But, 1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2 = 1/40
Or, 1/f1 - 1/(2 f1) = 1/40
∴ f1 = 20cm and f2 = -2f1 = -40cm
For the crown glass lens
1/f1 = (n1-1)(1/R1 - 1/R2)
Or, 1/20 = (1.5-1)(1/R1 + 1/25)
∴ R1 = 50/3 = 16.67cm
For the flint glass lens
1/f2 = (n2-1)(1/R2 - 1/R2')
1/40 = (1.7-1)(1/25 - 1/R2')
Or, 1/40 = 0.7(1/25 - 1/R2')
R2' = -233.33cm
For crown glass lens,
R1 = 16.67cm, R2 = -25cm
For flint glass lens,
R1 = -25cm, R2' = -233.33cm

12. Two thin lenses of focal length f1 and f2 separated by a distance d have an equivalent focal length 50 cm. The combination satisfies the conditions for no chromatic aberration and minimum spherical aberration. Find the values of f1, f2 and d. Assume that both the lenses are of the same material.
Solution:
We know the equivalent focal length of the lens combination is given by,
1/F = 1/f1 + 1/f2 - d/(f1 f2) = (f1 + f2 - d)/(f1 f2)
And for no chromatic aberration the lenses should be separated by
d = (f1 + f2)/2
For minimum spherical aberration
d = f1 - f2
From above relations we write
f1 = 3d/2, f2 = d/2
Substituting these values of f1 and f2 in above relations,
1/50 = (3d/2 + d/2 - d)/((3d/2) * (d/2))
Or, d = 50 * (3/4) = 66.67cm
∴ f1 = (3 * 66.67)/2 = 100cm
And f2 = 66.67/2 = 33.33cm

13. (a) Prove that a ray of light incident on the surface of a sheet of plate glass of thickness t emerges from the opposite face parallel to its initial direction but displaced sideways as in figure AE7 (b) Show that, for small angles of incidence θ, this displacement is given by x = θ(n-1)/n * t, where n is the index of refraction of the glass and θ is measured in radians.
Solution:
Let θ be the angle of incidence and θ2 be the angle of refraction at the left face of the plate. Let n be the index of refraction of the glass. Then, the law of refraction yields
sin θ = n sin θ2
The angle of incidence at the right face is also θ2. If θ3 is the angle of emergence there, then n sin θ2 = sin θ3. Thus sin θ3 = sin θ and θ3 = θ.


[Page 49]
94 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Lenses 95

The emerging ray is parallel to the incident ray. We wish to derive an expression for x in terms of θ. If D is the
length of the ray in the glass, then D cos θ₂ = t and D = t / cos θ₂. Then angle α in the diagram equals θ - θ₂ and
x = D sin α = D sin (θ - θ₂).
x = t sin(θ - θ₂) / cos θ₂
If all the angles θ, θ₂, θ₃, and θ - θ₂ are small and measured in radians, then sin θ ≈ θ, sin θ₂ ≈ θ₂, sin (θ - θ₂) ≈ (θ - θ₂), and cos θ₂ ≈ 1. Thus x ≈ t(θ - θ₂). The law of refraction applied to the point of incidence at the left face of the plate is now θ ≈ nθ₂, so θ₂ ≈ θ / n and
x ≈ t(θ - θ/n) = t(n-1)θ / n
