5
Chapter
Fiber Optics

An optical fiber is a cylindrical system that operates at optical frequencies. The central part of the system is referred as core and the seal outside the core is referred as cladding. The refractive index of the material of the core is always greater than that of the cladding. The coating outside the cladding is often termed as buffer coating or sometimes referred as jackets as well. In fact the core and cladding both are made up of glass material. A cross sectional view of an optical fiber is as shown in figure 5.1.

Figure 5.1: A cross sectional view of an optical fiber (n1 > n2)

Lasers, in general, can generate coherent electromagnetic waves at optical frequencies. These electromagnetic waves can be modulated by an electrical signal to be transmitted. Such signal can be transmitted through an optical fiber. Nowadays, optical fibers are used to transmit voice, television signals and digital signals by light waves over hair- thin threads of glass or specially made plastic.

The typical length of an optical fiber can be as long as 1 km. They are then joined one after the other using suitable connectors. The diameter of core ranges from 5 to 600 microns and that of cladding lies in the range of 125 to 750 microns. It should be noted that the cladding should have a thickness of one or two wavelength of light to keep the light within the core. The diameter of buffer coating or jacket is as much as 100 microns. The optical fibers are strong wires and can be easily bent in rolls.

5.1 Total Internal Reflection

The electromagnetic wave transmission through optical fiber is, in general, based on the phenomenon of total internal reflection. When light ray travels from one medium to other, by Snell's law we can write,

n1 sin i = n2 sin r ... 5.1


[Page 52]
100 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Fiber Optics 101

Where n₁ and n₂ are index of refraction of the medium in which light enters and gets
refracted with angles i and r respectively.

When a light ray travels from a denser medium to rarer medium, the ray will not get
refracted in the second medium if the incidence angle is equal to or greater than a particular
angle called critical angle. The ray instead will be totally reflected in the incident medium.
Now the angle of incidence i is critical angle C when angle of refraction r is a right angle.
From equation 5.1 we can write:

n₁ sin C = n₂ sin 90°

or, sin C = n₂ / n₁ ...5.2

Here n₁ > n₂.

5.2 Propagation of Light Wave

Consider a ray incident on an optical fiber making an angle i with the axis from
outside as shown in figure 5.2. The refractive index of outside air is n₀, that of core is n₁ and
cladding is n₂ respectively.

[Diagram: Light propagation in optical fiber]

Again from Snell's law, for ray from air to core

n₀ sin i = n₁ sin θ ...5.3

for ray from core to cladding

n₁ sin C = n₂ sin 90 ...5.4

Dividing equation 5.3 by n₁ one may write

n₀ / n₁ sin i = sin θ = sin (90 - C) = cos C

cos C = n₀ / n₁ sin i ... 5.5

Squaring and adding equations 5.2 and 5.5

sin² C + cos² C = 1

or, n₂²/n₁² + n₀²/n₁² sin² i = 1

or, n₀²/n₁² sin² i = 1 - n₂²/n₁²

or, sin² i = (n₁² - n₂²) / n₀²

or, sin i = ± sqrt((n₁² - n₂²) / n₀²) ...5.6

Now if (n₁² - n₂²) >= n₀², total internal reflection will occur for all values of i. Let n₀ =
1, in such case sin i can have a maximum value for a ray to be guided is:

sin iₘ = sqrt(n₁² - n₂²) when n₁² - n₂² < 1
 = 1 when n₁² - n₂² > 1

The quantity sqrt(n₁² - n₂²) is referred as Numerical Aperture (NA) of the optical fiber and
is a measure of the light gathering power of the fiber

Again NA = sqrt(n₁² - n₂²) = n₁ sqrt(2Δ)

Where Δ = (n₁² - n₂²) / 2n₁² = (n₁ - n₂) / n₂ when n₁ ≈ n₂

- Thus the refractive index n₁ is nearly equal to n₂. In practical fibers i is called the
acceptance angle.

Optical fiber may be characterized by one more parameter called V- number or
normalized frequency which is determined using numerical aperture as

V = (2πa / λ) (NA) = (2πa / λ) n₁ sqrt(2Δ)

Where a is radius of fiber and λ is its wavelength of signal.

Example: 5.1
A silica glass optical fiber has core of refractive index 1.50 and the cladding of refractive
index 1.45. Calculate the critical angle for core cladding interface, the acceptance angle in air,
corresponding angle of obliqueness, the numerical aperture of the fiber and percentage of light
collected by the fiber with respect to the incident light.

Solution:
We know,
 n₁ sin C = n₂ sin 90°
or, sin C = n₂ / n₁ = 1.45 / 1.50


[Page 53]
102 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

or, C = 75°
From air to core Snell’s law provides,
n0 sin i = n1 sin 0
or, sin i = 1.5 x sin 0 = 1.5 sin (90° - 75°)
= 1.5 sin 15° = 0.39
i = acceptance angle = sin⁻¹ (0.39) = 23°.
The corresponding angle of obliqueness and acceptance angle are complimentary angles. Thus
corresponding obliqueness angle is 90° - 75° = 15°.
The numerical aperture is,
NA = √n1² - n2² = √(n1 + n2)(n1 - n2)
= √(1.5 + 1.45)(1.5 - 1.45) = 0.3841
Percentage of light collected by fiber is (NA)² x 100 = 0.1475 = 14.75%

Example: 5.2
Calculate the refractive indices of the core and cladding materials of a fiber from following
data: NA = 0.22 and fractional refractive index change Δ = 0.012.

Solution:
We have
Δ = n1 - n2 / n2 = 0.012
NA = n1 √2Δ
and
n1 = NA / √2Δ = 0.22 / √2 x 0.012 = 1.42
Hence we have
1.42 - n2 / 1.42 = 0.012
or, n2 = 1.40.

Example: 5.3
A step index fiber in air has a NA of 0.16, a core refractive index of 1.45 and a core diameter
of 60 µm. Determine the normalized frequency for the fiber when light at a wavelength of 0.9 µm is
transmitted.

Solution:
An optical fiber may be characterized by one or more parameters called normalized frequency or V-
number of the fiber, which is
V = 2πa / λ √n1² - n2² = 2πa / λ (NA) = 2πa / λ n1 √2Δ
= 2 x 3.143 x 0.3 / 9 x 10⁻⁷ x 0.16 = 3.35 x 10⁵

5.3 Classification of Fibers
The structure of the optical fiber is shown in figure 5.1. The material of the core can
be compared in two different ways. Hence there are two different kinds of optical Fiber. In
such fibers the light propagates either by reflection or refraction phenomenon.

a) Step index optical fiber:
In step index optical fiber, the refractive index of the core (n1) is fixed and that of the
cladding (n2) is also fixed. The refractive index of the core is always higher than that of the
cladding. Therefore there is a noticeable boundary between the core and the cladding (figure

Fiber Optics 103
5.3). In such fiber there is high transmission loss due to splitting of light signals. The step
index optical fiber transmit light by reflection in general.

b) Graded index optical fiber
The refractive index of the core in
graded index fiber is a
function of radial
distance from the centre
of the fiber. In other
words the refractive
index of the material
decreases smoothly
from the middle to the
outer surface. Therefore, there is no noticeable boundary between core and cladding. The
continuous decrease in refractive index bend the light back towards the core. In this class of
optical fiber there is low transmission loss due to self focusing. The variation in refractive
index in step index and graded index optical fiber are shown in figure 5.4.

5.4 Self Focusing
In a graded index fiber,
light rays travel at different speeds
in different parts of the fiber
because the refractive index
changes throughout the fiber (see
figure 5.5). Near the outer edge the
refractive index is lower. As a
result, rays near the outer edge
travel faster than rays in the center
of the core. Because of this all the
rays (signals) arrive at the end of the fiber at approximately the same time. Consequently
light rays in these fibers are continuously refocused as they travel down the fiber. This
reduces signal distortion. This whole phenomenon is called self focusing.

5.5 Fiber Transmission
The light is guided either by reflection or refraction in optical fiber, based on the type
of fiber used. The light travels in zigzag path in the reflective type of fiber transmission
whereas it travels in continuous curve in refractive type of transmission. In both the cases the
similarity is that the rays are confined to the core. Generally the reflective type optical fibers
are
(i) single mode step index fiber and


[Page 54]
104 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics Fiber Optics 105

(ii) multimode step index fiber.
The refractive type of optical fiber is the multimode graded index optical fiber.
Mode is a concept that describes the propagation of electromagnetic waves. In simple words mode is the path that ray or light can take in a fiber. Therefore in single mode, there is only one path but in multimode there are various paths of light ray (signal).

The single mode fiber has higher band width capacity and is not affected by modal dispersion Single or monomode optical fiber has relatively larger diameter of cladding. In multimode transmission or multimode fiber, the core is relatively larger in size. Moreover in multimode fiber several paths for light are possible. The larger radii of the multimode fibers make it easier to launch the optical power into the fiber. In multimode fiber, the light can be launched with help of light emitting diodes (LED's) However in monomode it can be done with a laser diode. LED's are easier to manufacture, less expensive, requires less complex electronics and have a longer lifetime than the laser diodes. Therefore LED transmission in fiber is more suitable than laser transmission in single mode.

5.6 Fiber Loss
The most important characteristics of an optical fiber is its low transmission loss or attenuation. This loss is primarily from absorption and scattering. The attenuation is a function of wavelength and is measured in decibels/km. The transmission loss or attenuation is comparatively less in optical fiber than coaxial cables.

The attenuation or transmission loss of an optical signal in an optical fiber is one of the most important factors widely used in the study of fiber communication. The transmission distance before the signal restoration is measured by the relative loss of the signal (attenuation). The transmission loss in an optical fiber communication is much less than that in a metallic conductor.

Signal attenuation or transmission loss is the ratio of input to output optical power. The ratio is a function of operating wavelength. The formula for attenuation in decibels per km is

O dB = [1/L] 10 log [Pin/Pout]

Where Pin and Pout are input and output powers and L is length of optical fiber in km.
If Pin and Pout are equal, such an optical fiber is ideal since there is no loss in signals. In ideal fiber there is a signal attenuation of O dB.
The attenuation of signals can occur due to scattering, absorption and the radiative losses of the optical energy. Absorption is due to the fiber material.

Example:5.4
The average optical power of a fiber is 100 MW. An average output power in a fiber of length 10 km is 2.5 MW. Calculate the signal attenuation through the fiber. If there are no connectors, find the signal attenuation per km of the fiber. If there is an optical link of 11 km with 3 splices (each of attenuation 0.8 dB) what is the overall signal attenuation. Find also the ratio of input to output power.

Solution:
Here Pin = 100 x 10^-6 W
Pout = 2.5 x 10^-6 W
L = 10 km
Signal attenuation,
O dB = [1/L] [10 log [Pin/Pout]]
= [1/10] [10 log10 [100/2.5]] = 1.6 dB per km.
Hence total attenuation = 1.6 x 10 = 16 dB.
For 11 km attenuation = 1.6 x 11 = 17.6 dB
Since there are 3 splices (connector); loss by them = 3 x 0.8 = 2.4 dB.
Total attenuation = 17.6 + 2.4 = 20 dB.
Again Pin / Pout = 10^(dB/10) = 10^(20/10) = 10^2 = 100

5.7 Dispersion
In the transmission of optical signal it is expected that the signal is observed at the other end unchanged in shape and width. However, the amplitude is reduced by losses. If the losses are extremely large the pulse amplitude at the receiving end will be too small to be detected. In such case a repeater has to be included to boost up the signal level prior of entering the other section.

In some cases a pulse at the output is wider than the pulse at input i.e. pulse gets distorted as it moves through the fiber (see figure 5.6).

[Input] [Output]
Figure 5.6

This distortion of pulse is due to dispersion effect which is measured in terms of nanoseconds per km (ns/km). The dispersion is determined using the fractional refractive index change for a certain length.

D = [n1/c] [Δ / (1-Δ)]

This term is also called intermodal factor for the cable. Here c is the speed of light, 3x10^8 m/s. The intermodal factor also represents the time delay between highest and lowest order modes of the fiber and is independent of wavelength of light. It is noted that this type of


[Page 55]
106 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
delay is absent in single mode fiber having one mode of propagation. However, in case of
GRAIN fiber, the time delay due to intermodal dispersion is
D = n₁Δ²
8c
Several dispersion effects are encountered by the pulse of light propagating through
the fiber and these act to spread out the pulse in time, changing the pulse shape. In such case
the pulse may get merged in the proceeding and succeeding pulses and becomes
indistinguishable. Those phenomena are fiber dispersion. There are three kinds of dispersion.
i) Intermodal dispersion or Multipath dispersion
ii) Material or chromatic dispersion
iii) Waveguide dispersion.

5.8 Applications
Optical fibers are used in many fields.
Fiber optics essentially deals with the communication including voice signals, video
signals or digital data. This is done by transmission of light through optical fiber. A fiber
optic system consists of three basic parts like a light source, an optical fiber and a light
detector. The source may be light emitting diodes (LED's). The optical fiber transmits the
light waves. Finally the detector can be an avalanche photo diode (APD) or a positive
intrinsic negative (PIN) diode. In general a fiber optics system converts an electric signal to
an infrared (IR) light signal which is transmitted through an optical fiber. At the other end it
is reconverted into an electric signal.

The fiber optics are used in the fabrication of fiberscope in endoscopy in medical
sciences. Such fibersopes are used in the visualization of internal organs of human body. The
fiber optics in combination of laser probe help to visualization of internal portion of human
body and cauterization of tissues.

The fiber optics is also useful in industry. It can be used to examine welds, nozzles
and combustion chambers inside aircraft engines which are inaccessible for observation
otherwise.

5.9 Advantages of Optical Fiber Communication
There are many advantages of communication with optical fiber. They are listed
below
a) Greater volume of information can be carried over in a fiber optics. The rate at
which the information can be transmitted is directly related to signal frequency.
Since light has a frequency in the range of 10¹⁴ Hz and that of radio frequency in
10⁶ Hz. Thus a transmission system that operates at light frequency can transmit at
a higher rate than system that operate at radio frequency.

b) Fiber optics are light in weight and flexible. So they can easily be handled than
copper cables.

Fiber Optics 107
c) In early days the communication were made with circuits in which signals from
one circuit may become stray to another. This causes other calls being heard in the
background. This defect in termed as cross talk. The cross talk is negligible in
fiber optics even for a numbers of fibers are cabled together.
d) There is less interference of waves.
e) Fiber cables transmit only light waves not electricity therefore they are safer than
copper cables.
f) The life span of fiber cables is 20-30 years greater than copper cables (10 - 12
years).
g) There is no corrosion and tolerability over higher temperatures.
h) Optical fibers are easy to maintain than copper cables and are reliable.


[Page 56]
108 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

5.10 Additional Examples:

1. Calculate the numerical aperture and the acceptance angle of optical fibre, given ncore = 1.62 and
ncladding = 1.52.

Solution:
Numerical aperture NA = sin imax = √n²core - n²cladding
sin imax = √(1.62)² - (1.52)² = 0.5603
Acceptance angle
imax = sin⁻¹(0.5603)
imax = 34.1°

2. A light ray enters from air to a fiber. The refractive index of air is 1.0. The fiber has refractive
index of core equal to 1.5 and that of cladding equal to 1.48. Calculate the critical angle, the fraction
refractive index change, the acceptance angle and the numerical aperture.

Solution:
Critical angle
θc = sin⁻¹(ncladding / ncore) = sin⁻¹(1.48 / 1.5) = sin⁻¹(0.9867) = 80.63°
Fractional refractive index change
Δ = (ncore - ncladding) / ncore = (1.5 - 1.48) / 1.5 = 0.02 / 1.5 = 0.013
Numerical Aperture NA = √n²core - n²cladding
= √(1.5)² - (1.48)² = 0.2441
Acceptance angle
imax = sin⁻¹(0.2441) = 14.13°

3. An optical fiber has a fiber loss of 3.5 dB/km. If 0.5 mW of optical power is initially launched into
the fiber, what is the power level after 4 km?

Solution:
We have
α = (10 / L) log (Pi / Po)
3.5 dB / km = (10 / 4km) log (0.5mW / Po)
Or, 3.5 × 0.4dB = log (0.5mW / Po)
Or, 1.4dB = log (0.5mW / Po)
Or, 10¹⁴ = (0.5mW / Po)
Or, Po = 19.9 μW

Interference

Chapter 6

In 1801, Thomas Young (1773-1829) obtained convincing evidence for the wave nature of light. He was able to determine the wavelength of light wave from his double slit experiment. Using the principle of superposition and Huygens's wave concept, Young explained the interference effects observed in various instances, such as the colours observed on oil slicks exposed to sunlight and Newton's rings.

6.1 Principle of Superposition

The principle of super position states: When two or more waves pass through the same region of space at the same time, the resultant displacement is the vector (or algebraic) sum of the separate displacements of individual waves. This means for the two unidirectional propagating waves of same phase, the crest of one wave falls exactly on the crest of other wave and so do the troughs (figure 6.1). If A₁, A₂, ... are the amplitudes of waves that are superimposed then the resultant amplitude A is

A = A₁ + A₂ + ...

[Diagram shows waves with Amplitude A1 and A2 resulting in a wave with Resultant Amplitude A]

Figure 6.1: Superposition of waves of equal phase and frequency.

The resultant intensity is the square of the sum of amplitudes of each waves

I = (A₁ + A₂ + .....)²


[Page 58]
112 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

6.2 Coherence

Light waves from a sodium lamp, for example, are due to energy changes in the sodium atoms. The emitted waves occur in bursts lasting about 10^-8 second. The light waves produced by the different atoms are out of phase with each other, since they are emitted randomly and rapidly. We call sources of light waves as coherent sources on account of continual change of phase. Coherent sources are those which emit light waves of the same frequency and have constant phase relationship. Two loud speakers driven by the same amplifier, two radio antennas powered by the same transmitter or two small apertures in an opaque screen, illuminated by the same monochromatic light source are the examples of coherent source. Hence a real source and its virtual source or two virtual images of single source can act as coherent sources. An interference pattern is observed only when the sources are coherent. The observations of interference are facilitated by reducing the separation between the sources of light producing interference.

There are two independent concepts of coherence i.e. temporal coherence and spatial coherence. Temporal type of coherence refers to the correlation between the field at a point and the field at the same point at a later time. If the phase difference between the two fields is constant during the period normally covered by observation, the wave is set to have temporal coherence. Second type of coherence is spatial coherence in which waves at different points in space preserve a constant phase difference over any time. Hence temporal or time coherence is a characteristic of a single beam of light where as special coherence concern the relationship between two separate beams of light. Interference is the manifestation of coherence.

6.3 Phase Difference, Path Difference and Optical Path

Consider the wavelength of light used for interference be λ. This implies that the change of phase that occurs over a distance λ will be 2π. Let the phase difference of two waves arriving at a point be δ and the corresponding path difference be x. Therefore for a path difference of x phase difference is given by,

Phase difference = δ = 2π / λ * x = 2π / λ * Path difference.

Optical path is defined as the distance with the light travels in vacuum during the same time for which it travels in the medium. If d be the distance traveled in medium of refractive index n, which is also called geometrical path, then the distance travelled by light at the same time in vacuum is L = n d. Hence the optical path is the product of geometrical distance and refractive index of the medium. For various medium the relation will be

n1 d1 = n2 d2

6.4 Interference

The phenomenon of non-uniform distribution of energy in the medium due to the superposition of two light waves is called interference of light. Figure 6.2 shows two pulses of a rope approaching each other. They have the same shape and amplitude. When a crest and a trough overlap, they momentarily cancel to produce zero displacement, as in figure 6.2 (a)

Interference 113

Figure 6.2 (a) Destructive interference between two pulses (b) Constructive interference between two pulses.

This is called destructive interference. It occurs because of two equal but opposite disturbances. When two crests overlap, as in figure 6.2 (b), they reinforce each other so that the amplitude of the resultant is double that of either pulse. This is constructive interference.

For destructive interference the interfering wave should have path difference equal to integer multiple of λ/2 and permanent dark band is formed.

When two crest or two trough overlap as in figure 6.2 (b), they reinforce each other, so that the amplitude of the resultant is double that of either pulse. This is constructive interference which is possible if the two waves have path difference equal to the integer multiple of λ. Generally permanent bright band is obtained at any point.

The phenomenon of interference is also divided into two classes on the basis of mode of production of interference:

i) Interference produced by the division of wavefront: In this case incident wavefront is divided into two parts by making use of the phenomenon of reflection refraction or diffraction. The two parts of the wavefront travel unequal distances and reunite to produce interference fringes: Young's double slit experiment, Fresnel's biprism are its examples.

ii) Interference by the division of amplitude: The amplitude of the incident wave is divided into two parts either by parallel reflection or refraction. These light waves with divided amplitude reinforce after travelling different distances and produce interference. The Newton's ring and Michelson's interferometer are examples of this type.

6.5 Analytical Treatment of Interference

In figure 6.3 S is a monochromatic source of light. It emits light of wavelength λ. Two small pinholes a and b, very close to each other and the source S, are at equidistance from S. Thus a and b pinholes act as two virtual coherent sources because they emit light waves of the same frequency, same amplitude and are in phase with each other. Let the amplitude of the waves be A and the phase difference between the two waves reaching the point P (figure 6.3) be δ. The displacements of waves from a and b are denoted by y1 and y2. The displacements can be written as,

y1 = A sin ωt

y2 = A sin (ωt + δ)

From the principle of superposition of waves, the resultant displacement of the interfered wave is given by


[Page 59]
114 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Figure 6.3 Interference of light waves passing through two slits.

y = y1+y2 = A sin ωt + A sin (ωt + δ)
= A sin ωt + A sin ωt cos δ + A cos ωt sin δ
= A sin ωt (1+cosδ) + A cos ωt sin δ

Let, A (1 + cosδ) = R cos θ ... 6.1
and A sin δ = R sin θ ... 6.2

∴ y = R cos θ sin ωt + R sin θ cos ωt
= R sin (ωt +θ)

This implies the resultant wave is simple harmonic with an amplitude R. Squaring and adding equations 6.1 and 6.2 we have,

R² sin² θ + R² cos² 0 = A² sin² δ + A² (1 + cos δ)²
or, R² = 2 A² + 2A² cos δ
or, R² = 2 A² (1 + cos δ)
or, R² = 2 A² × 2 cos² δ/2 = 4 A² cos² δ/2

Since the intensity at a point is given by the square of the amplitude,
I = R² = 4 A² cos² δ/2 ... 6.3

When the phase difference δ = 0, 2π, 2(2π) 3(3π) ... n (2π) or the path difference = 0, λ, 2λ, 3λ ... nλ, in this case,
I = 4 A² ... 6.4

When the phase difference δ = π, 3π, 5π....(2n + 1) π
or, the path difference = λ/2, 3λ/2, 5λ/2 ... (2n + 1) λ/2
I = 0 ... 6.5

Interference 115

Hence, intensity is maximum when the phase difference is a whole number multiple of 2π or path difference is a whole number multiple of wave length. Similarly the intensity is minimum when the path difference is an odd number multiple of half wavelength. From equation 6.5, it follows that the intensity at bright points is 4 A² and at dark points it is zero. From the conservation of energy, energy cannot be destroyed. Therefore the energy at dark points is not destroyed but transferred from the points of minimum intensity to the points of maximum intensity.

Figure 6.4 Energy distribution in interference.

Figure 6.4 shows the variation of intensity as a function of phase angle δ. It is seen from the plot that the intensity varies from zero at the fringe minima to 4 A² at the fringe maxima.

Furthermore, figure 6.4, also shows that the light distribution from the superposition of waves consists of alternative bright and dark bands. These bands are called interference fringes. These fringes can be observed practically if they are projected in a screen.

6.6 Young's Double Slit Experiment

In 1801, Thomas Young found a convincing fact for the wave nature of light and was able to measure its wavelength. A sketch of Young's famous double slit experiment is shown in figure 6.5. Light from a source passes through a slit (to make a narrow beam of parallel rays) and afterwards falls on a second screen containing two very closely spaced slits. If light consists of particles one will observe two bright lines on a viewing screen kept behind the slits (figure 6.5 b). But Young observed a series of bright lines (figure 6.5 c).

Figure 6.5: Arrangement of young's double slit experiment


[Page 60]
116 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Figure 6.5c: Arrangement of young's double slit experiment

He explained this result as wave interference phenomenon. The theory of this interference pattern is explained with the help of figure 6.6. Waves of equal wavelength λ enters the slits S1 & S2 which are a distance d apart. The slit separation d which is very small compared to the slit screen distance D. The wave from these slits spreads in all possible directions. Since D >> d, the rays from each slit will be essentially parallel.

Figure 6.6 Path difference in young's double slit experiment.

Let P be any arbitrary point on screen and θ be the angle between MP and horizontal line MO. Let S1T be a normal on to the line S2P. The distance S1P and TP are equal. The waves emitted by the slits S1 and S2 are initially in phase with each other. The difference in the path lengths of these two waves is

S2P - S1P = S2T ... 6.6

From right angled Δ S1S2T

S2T = d sin θ ... 6.7

Interference 117

The nature of the interference of the two waves at P depends simply on how many waves are contained in the length of the path difference S2T. Moreover, the additional path travelled by the lower ray S2P is d sinθ. This we call as the path difference. If this path difference contained a whole number of wavelength constructive interference (bright spot at P) will be observed, i.e.

d sinθ = nλ n = 0, 1, 2 ... 6.8

The value of n is called the order of interference fringe. The orders are the consecutive numbers on each side of the central one (at θ = 0). If path difference is odd number of half wave length, then the destructive interference (dark spot at P) will occur, i.e.

d sin θ = (n + 1/2)λ n = 0,1,2 ... 6.9

Now consider a case for constructive interference at P. The path difference between two waves reaching P is.

d sin θ = nλ

or, sin θ = nλ/d ... 6.10

Let y be distance of point P from O (central spot).

We have, from Δ PMO

y = D tanθ ... 6.11

For small angles we can write,

y = D tanθ = D sinθ

or, y = Dnλ/d using equation 6.8

or, λ = dy/Dn ... 6.12

We define fringe width as the distance between consecutive constructive-constructive (or destructive - destructive) interference and is denoted by β. It is obvious that

β = yn+1 - yn = Dλ/d ... 6.13

Using this equation Young was able to determine the wavelength of light used in the experiment. It should be noted that the width of bright fringe is equal to the width of the dark fringe. Therefore, the alternate bright and dark fringes are parallel. Furthermore, the fringe width β is independent of the order of the fringe and directly proportional to distance between the slits and screen. Moreover, the closer are the slits the wider will be the fringes.


[Page 61]
118 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Example: 6.1
A screen containing two slits 0.1 mm apart is 1 m from the viewing screen. Light of wavelength λ 500 nm falls on the slits from a distant source. Approximately how far apart will the bright interference fringes be seen on the screen?

Solution:
Here d = 0.1 mm = 1 x 10^-4 m
D = 1 m
For n = 1 (first order)
d sin θ = nλ
or, sin θ = (1 x 500 x 10^-9) / (1 x 10^-4) = 5 x 10^-3

since θ is small angle,
We can take sin θ = θ1. Let the first order fringe will occur at a distance x1 above (or below) the center of the screen and is given by
x1 / D = θ
So, x1 = D θ1 = (1.0 m) x 5 x 10^-3 = 5 mm
Similarly, x2 = D θ2 = 1 x (2λ / d) = (2 x 500 x 10^-9) / (0.1 x 10^-3) = 10 mm
Therefore fringes are 5 mm apart.

6.7 Interference in Thin Films
The colours in soap bubbles, on thin oil film and on water are due to the interference of light waves that have been reflected from the two surfaces of a thin film. When a ray of light encounters a reflector, the light gets reflected and the light pulse suffers a phase change of 180°. The refractive index of the material of the reflecting medium determines whether or not a phase inversion occurs at a boundary. When light encounters a medium that has a higher refractive index, the reflected light or wave suffers a phase change (π).

Consider a thin film of oil lying on the top of water (figure 6.7). A part of the incident light is reflected from A on the top surface of oil film of thickness t. Part of the ray is transmitted to the oil film and part of that is reflected at B on the lower surface of the film. The reflected part at the lower surface must travel an additional distance ABC assuming A and C are very close apart.
Figure 6.7: Reflected light from upper and lower surfaces of thin oil film on water.

If this path difference is equal to whole number multiples of wavelengths, the two waves, which are coherent as they originate from the same source, interfere constructively. Similarly the two waves interfere destructively if the path difference ABC equals to odd number multiples of half wavelengths. We shall derive the path difference between the rays reflected at A and C and find the conditions of interference in the next subsection.

Interference 119

6.8 Interference in plane parallel films due to reflected light
Consider a plane parallel film of uniform thickness t as shown in figure 6.8. The film is surrounded by air on both sides. Suppose a ray OA be incident on its upper surface at an angle of incident i. Part of the incident light is reflected towards AB and the other part is refracted into the medium of refractive index µ toward AC. At C, the incident ray AC also gets partly reflected toward CD and part of that emerges out as ray 2 at D. From geometrical optics, we know ray 1 and ray 2 are parallel to each other. Let r be angle of refraction of the ray at A.

Figure 6.8 interference in plane parallel films by reflection

Now we calculate the path difference between the reflected ray 1 and 2. Draw DB normal from point D on ray 1.

Equivalent air path of ray AC + CD will become µ (AC + CD) since µ is the refractive index of the film
µ = Velocity of light in vacum / Velocity of light in medium = c / v = Distance in vacum / Distance in medium

The optical path difference between ray 1 and 2, will therefore becomes.
x = µ [AC + CD] – AD sin i
= µ [CE/cosr + CE/cosr] – AD sin i
= 2µt / cosr – 2 AE sin i
= 2µt / cosr – 2t tan r . µ sin r
= 2µt / cosr – 2µt sin²r / cosr
= 2µt cos r
... 6.4

This is the optical path difference and not the total true optical path difference. Ray AB and CD suffers reflection in two different medium. As discussed in earlier section when light encounters a medium of higher refractive index, the reflected wave suffers a phase.


[Page 62]
120 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

change of π i.e. a path change of λ/2. So this has to be compensated in our expression.
Therefore the net or true optical path difference Δ in our case will become.
Δ = 2μt cos r + λ/2 ... 6.15

Hence the condition for constructive interference in the air film to appear bright is,
2μt cos r + λ/2 = nλ
or, 2μt cos r = (2n-1) λ/2 where n = 1, 2, 3 ...

Similarly the film will appear dark in the reflected light when
2μt cos r + λ/2 = (2n+1) λ/2
or, 2μt cos r = nλ where n = 0, 1, 2 ... ... 6.16

6.9 Interference in Plane Parallel Films Due to Transmitted Light
Consider a thin transparent film of thickness t as shown in figure 6.9 in air. Ray AB gets refracted along BC. This ray gets partly reflected along CD and partly refracted along CT at C. Similarly the ray CD also get reflection and refraction at D. In this way we will have transmitted rays CT and EU which are derived from the same point source A. These two transmitted rays interfere. However, the rays at C and D encounters a medium that has lower refractive index than that in which they are travelling. Hence they do not suffer a phase change. Let ∠i and ∠r be angle of incidence and refraction at B.

Draw CQ perpendicular to DE and EP perpendicular to CT.
The effective optical path difference between the two transmitted rays is,
x = μ(CD + DE) - CP.
Since μ = sin i / sin r = CP/CE / QE/CE = CP/QE
or, CP = μ QE
Thus path difference.

Interference 121

μ (CD + DQ + QE) - μ QE
= μ (CD + DQ)
= μ (ID + DQ)
= μ (IQ)

But in triangle CIQ
cos r = IQ / IC But IC = 2 t
or, IQ = 2 t cos r
∴ path difference = 2 μ t cos r ... 6.17

From the general theory of interference, the maxima is obtained when the path difference is equal to integral multiple of wavelength i.e.
For maxima,
2 μ t cos r = nλ n = 0, 1, 2 ...

Similarly for minima,
2 μ t cos r = (2n - 1) λ/2 n = 1, 2, 3 ... ... 6.18

Hence the conditions of maxima and minima in transmitted light are just reverse of the condition for reflected light.

6.10 Fringes in Wedge Shaped Thin Films
Consider two plane surfaces GH and GH1 inclined at an angle α encloses a wedge shaped film as shown in figure 6.10. The thickness of the film increases from G to H. Consider the material enclosed in the wedge has a refractive index μ. The thin film if illuminated, interference occurs between two system of rays, one reflected form the front surface and the other obtained by internal reflection at the back surface.

Figure 6.10: Interference in wedge shaped film by reflection.

Now, assume the nth bright fringe is obtained when the thickness of the air film (μ = 1) is Hn H and the monochromatic incident ray is nearly normal to the surface (cos r ≅ 1). So for a bright fringe, in this case we have,


[Page 63]
122 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

2μt cos r = (2n-1)λ/2 ... 6.19
or, 2tn = (2n-1)λ/2
The consecutive-next bright in fringe (n+1) will occur at a thickness of tn+1 providing
2 tn+1 = [2 (n + 1) - 1] λ/2 ... 6.20
2 tn+1 = (2n + 1) λ/2
From (6.17) and (6.18) we can solve for
tn+1 - tn = λ/2

This indicates the next bright fringe will occur at the point where the thickness of the
fringe increases by λ/2. In generalizing the result, mth bright fringe from nth bright fringe will
be observed with a thickness of mλ/2. If x is the horizontal distance from the nth to n+mth
bright fringe then
α = (mλ/2) / x = mλ/2x
x = mλ/2α ... 6.21
Here x corresponds the distance to m number of fringes providing the fringe spacing β
= mλ/2x where α is small and measured in radians.

This wedge shaped film and interference in it, is the basics for Newton's rings and
Haidinger fringes. The result is applicable to calculate the wavelength of incident light.

Example: 6.2
A wedge shaped film of air (Fig 6.11) is
produced by placing a fine wire of diameter D
between the ends of two flat glass plates of length L
= 30 cm. When the film is illuminated with light of
wavelength λ = 550 nm, there are 12 dark fringes
per centimeter. Find D.

[Figure 6.11 Wedge shaped film]

Interference 123
Solution:
We known in this case only one of the reflected rays suffers a phase inversion. At the end of the wedge,
where the thickness is less than λ/4, the two rays interfere destructively. This region is dark in the reflected light.
The condition for destructive interference in the reflected light is.
2t = mλ m = 0, 1, 2.....
The change in thickness between adjacent dark fringes is Δt = λ/2. The horizontal spacing between
fringes
d = 1/12 cm
= 8.3 x 10^-4 m
Also, D/L = Δt/d
D = L Δt/d = Lλ/2d
= (0.2 m)(5.5 x 10^-7 m) / (2 * 8.3 x 10^-4 m)
D = 6.6 x 10^-5 m.

6.11 Colours of Thin Films
The discussion in previous sections for the interference due to a parallel film and at a
wedge shape helps us to understand why the films appear coloured in sunlight. The incident
light is split by reflection at the top and bottom of the film. These rays do interfere and is
responsible for the colours. Since the interference is a function of the thickness of the film,
the wavelength, and the angle of refraction, colours are observed at different positions of the
eye. Only the colours that satisfies the condition of maxima (constructive interference) will
be observed; other will be absent. The colours observed in reflected and transmitted light
are complimentary because the conditions for maxima and minima in transmitted and
reflected light are opposite.

6.12 Fringes of Equal Thickness
In general, soap bubbles and oil films on a flat surface do not have uniform thickness.
The thickness determines whether the reflected light has a maximum or minimum intensity.
Using white light, in which each wavelength (colour) provides its own fringe pattern. So at a
reference point on the film, one colour may be enhanced and other colour may be suppressed.
In wedge shaped film with flat plates, one may observe a series of bright and dark bands
(equal inclination). If the plates forming the wedge shape are not flat, the fringes are not
straight. However the fringe is a locus of points with the same thickness. If one of the plates
is flat, the fringes display the irregularities of the other. Therefore this is an important optical
application to determine the plainness of surfaces.

6.13 Newton's Rings
When a lens or curved glass surface is placed in contact with a flat glass surface, as in
figure 6.12, a thin air film is formed. When such film is exposed by monochromatic light,
circular fringes, called Newton's Rings - a series of concentric rings is seen. Therefore


[Page 64]
124 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Newton's rings are concentric rings formed when light falls on a uniform wedge shaped thin film.

These fringes are due to the interference between rays reflected from top and bottom surfaces of the air gap between the two pieces of glass. An important feature of Newton's rings is the central dark spot. Newton tried to polish the surfaces to get rid of it. The dark spot indicated that light suffers a phase inversion upon reflecting from a medium with a higher refractive index. Young later tested it by placing oil of sassafras between a lens of crown glass and a plate of flint glass. The refractive index of this oil is in between that of the two glass materials. Since both reflections occur at a medium with a higher refractive index, they should both suffer a phase inversion and should therefore be in phase. This is precisely what happened by becoming the central spot bright.

6.14 Interference in Newton's Ring.

When monochromatic light is allowed in the experimental set up as shown in figure 6.13, Newton's rings are observed. The path difference between the rays reflected on the upper and lower surface of the film is
2 μ t cos r + λ/2
For almost normal incident of light in air film, r ≈ 0 and μ = 1. So the path difference will be
2t + λ/2
At the point of contact t = 0, so the path difference is λ/2. Hence central spot is dark.

The condition for bright rings (maxima) is
2t + λ/2 = nλ
or, 2t = (2n-1)λ / 2 ... 6.22

Where, n = 1, 2... and the condition for minima is,
2t + λ/2 = (2n+1)λ/2
or, 2t = nλ ... 6.23
where, n = 0, 1, 2......

Interference 125

Now let's find the diameter of the Newton's rings. Let LOL' be the lens placed on the plane glass plate AOB. The curved surface LOL' is a part of the spherical surface having radius of curvature R. At this particular case r is the radius of Newton's ring corresponding to constant film thickness t. From the property of circle in geometry,
r × r = (2 R - t) × t
r^2 = 2 R t [since t is very small r^2 can be neglected.]
or, t = r^2 / 2R

Thus for bright ring
2t = (2n-1)λ / 2 ... 6.24
or, 2r^2 / 2R = (2n-1)λ / 2
or, r^2 = (2n-1)λR / 2 ... 6.25

But if Dn is the diameter of the nth bright ring
Dn^2 = 2 (2n-1) λ R
or, Dn = sqrt(2λR) × sqrt(2n-1) where n = 1,2,3,... ... 6.26

And similarly for a dark fringe,
2r^2 / 2R = nλ
or, r^2 = nλR


[Page 65]
126 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
or, Dn = 2√nλR where n = 0,1,2... ... 6.27
Thus the diameters of the rings are proportional to the square roots of natural numbers. Also it is to be noted that the central spot, n = 0 and Dn = 0 i.e. the center will be dark. In the case of Newton's rings by transmitted light, in a similar fashion we can derive the diameters of bright and dark rings. However the relations for diameters will be complimentary and centre of the rings is brght.
Example: 6.3
In an experiment on Newton's rings the light has a wavelength of 600 nm. The lens has a refractive index of 1.5 and a radius of curvature of 2.5 m. Find the radius of 5th bright fringe.
Solution:
If R is the radius of curvature of the lens, then from figure 6.14 we see that
r² = R² - (R - t)²
Where r is the radius of a fringe and t is the thickness of the film. Since t is very small, we may drop terms in t² to obtain,
r² = 2 Rt
The condition for a bright fringe is
2t = (2n - 1) λ/2 [ μ = 1 for the air film]
Again, for fifth bright fringe,
t = (4.5) * (6 * 10⁻⁷) / 2
= 1.35 * 10⁻⁶ m
So, r = √2 Rt = 2.6 * 10⁻³ m.

6.15 Uses of Newton's ring
Determination of Wavelength of light
The experimental arrangement for the determination of wavelength of light using Newton's rings is shown in figure 6.16 in which S is a source of light. A parallel beam of light from the lens system is reflected by a glass plate P which is inclined at an angle 45° with the horizontal. A Plano convex lens of large focal length is exposed to the reflected light from the glass plate P which encloses an air film as shown.

[Diagram description: Eye piece, Collimating lens, S, P, Plano convex lens, air film, glass plate]
Figure 6.16: Experimental set up for the determination of λ by Newton's rings

Interference 127
The interference of the reflected light is observed using an eye piece of a travelling microscope. Using the same microscope we measure the diameters of dark rings.
Let the diameter of nth dark ring is Dn which is related with the wavelength of light and Radius of curvature of the Plano convex lens as given by equation 6.25, we have,
Dn² = 4 n λ R
Similarly the diameter of (n + m)th dark fringe is given by
Dn+m² = 4(n + m) λ R
Subtracting these relations,
Dn+m² - Dn² = 4 m λ R
or, λ = (Dn+m² - Dn²) / 4mR ... 6.28
Hence knowing the diameters of various dark rings and radius of curvature of the planoconvex lens, the wavelength of a given monochromatic source of light can be determined.

Determination of Refractive Index of Liquid
We can determine the refractive index of a liquid by using Newton's rings. Gently pour the liquid into the air film space without disturbing the entire arrangement. Measure the diameters of nth and (n + m)th dark rings in the same way as done in determination of wave length of light. Let D'n and D'n+m be diameter of nth and (n + m)th Newton's ring. Here we can rewrite the equation. For normal incidence and with film of refractive index μ as,
Dn+m² - Dn² = 4mλR / μ ... 6.29
Now using relations 6.26 and 6.27 we have
μ = (Dn+m² - Dn²) / (D'n+m² - D'n²) ... 6.30
With this expression we can find the refractive index of the liquid. Hence refractive index is the ratio of slope of D² and m curve for air and liquid enclosed.

6.16 Haidinger Fringes.
In thin films interference, fringes are produced due to path difference 2u t cos r between the overlapping rays. For a given film the path difference may arises due to (i) the angle of refraction r inside the film or (ii) the change in thickness. We can express the change in path difference δ (D) by differentiating the expression 2μ t cos r i.e.
δ (D) = 2 μ t . δ (cos r) + 2 μ cos r (δ t)
If t = constant


[Page 66]
128 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

δ (D) = 2 μ t δ (cos r) very
small
If t = large
δ (D) > high for small r

If the thickness of the film is large in thin film experiment, a very small change in r will change the path difference appreciably.
Here the ray through a plate appears as parallel beam and generally it is viewed by the eye (figure 6.17). Fringes are produced in this case due to superposition of rays, which are equally inclined to the normal. These inferring fringes pattern are known as fringes of equal inclination and are different from the Newton's rings. These fringes since were first observed by Haidinger are called Haidinger fringes.

As shown in figure 6.17, light rays fall on the plates forming the fringe from an extended source. The rays incident and refracted at same angle form a parallel beam and are observed by eye. The pattern is series of concentric circles whose centre is two principle focuses.

Figure 6.17: Experimental set up for the formation of Haidinger fringes

Interference 129

6.17 Additional Examples:

1. In a double-slit experiment, the distance between slits is 5 mm and the slits are 1 m from the screen. Two interference patterns can be seen on the screen: one due to light of wavelength 480 nm, and the other due to light of wavelength 600 nm. What is the separation on the screen between the third-order (m = 3) bright fringes of two interference patterns?

Solution:
The maxima of a two-slit interference pattern are at angles θ given by d sin θ = mλ, where d is the slit separation, λ is the wavelength, and m is an integer. If θ is small, sin θ may be replaced by θ in radians. Then, dθ = mλ. The angular separation of two maxima associated with different wavelengths but the same value of m is
Δθ = (m/d)(λ2 – λ1),
and their separation on a screen a distance D away is
Δy = D tan Δθ ≈ D Δθ = [mD/d](λ2 – λ1)
= [3(1m) / 5x10^-3m] (600 x 10^-9 m – 480 x 10^-9 m) = 7.2 x 10^-5 m.
The small angle approximation tan Δθ ≈ Δθ (in radians) is made.

2. A thin flake of mica (n = 1.58) is used to cover one slit of a double-slit interference arrangement. The central point on the viewing screen is now occupied by what had been the seventh bright side fringe (m = 7). If λ = 550 nm, what is the thickness of the mica?

Solution:
Consider the two waves, one from each slit, that produce the seventh bright fringe in the absence of the mica. They are in phase at the slits and travel different distances to the seventh bright fringe, where they have a phase difference of 2πm = 14π. Now a piece of mica with thickness x is placed in front of one of the slits, and an additional phase difference between the waves develops. Specifically, their phase at the slits differ by
2πx/λm - 2πx/λ = 2πx/λ (n - 1)
where λm is the wavelength in the mica and n is the index of refraction of the mica. The relationship λm = λ/n is used to substitute for λm. Since the waves are now in phase at the screen,
2πx/λ (n – 1) = 14 π
or, x = 7λ / n-1 = 7(550x10^-9m) / 1.58-1 = 6.64 x 10^-6 m.

3. Add the quantities y1 = 10 sin ωt, y2 = 15 sin (ωt + 30°), and y3 = 5 sin (ωt - 45°) using the phasor method.

Solution:
In adding these with the phasor method (as opposed to, say, trig identities), we may set t = 0 and add them as vectors:
yh = 10 cos 0° + 15 cos 30° + 5 cos (- 45°) = 26.5
yv = 10 sin 0° + 15 sin 30° + 5 sin (-45°) = 4

so that
yR = √yh² + yv² = 26.8 = 27
β = tan^-1 (yv/yh) = - 8.5°.
Thus, y = y1 + y2 + y3 = yR sin (ωt + β) = 27 sin (ωt + 8.5°).


[Page 67]
130 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

4. We wish to coat flat glass (n = 1.5) with a transparent material (n = 1.25) so that reflection of light at wavelength 600 nm is eliminated by interference. What minimum thickness can the coating have to do this?

Solution:
For complete destructive interference, we want the waves reflected from the front and back of the coating to differ in phase by an odd multiple of π rad. Each wave is incident on a medium of higher index of refraction from a medium of lower index, so both suffer phase changes of π rad on reflection. If x is the thickness of the coating, the wave reflected from the back surface travels a distance 2x farther than the wave reflected from the front. The phase difference is 2t(2π/λn), where λn is the wavelength in the coating. If n is the index of refraction of the coating, λn = λ/n, where λ is the wavelength in vacuum, and the phase difference is 2nx(2π/λ). We solve
2nx(2π/λ) = (2m + 1)π
for x. Here m is an integer. The result is
x = (2m + 1)λ / 4n
To find the least thickness for which destructive interference occurs, we take m = 0. Then,
x = λ/4n = 600 x 10⁻⁹ m / 4(1.25) = 1.2 x 10⁻⁷ m.

5. A thin film of acetone (n = 1.25) coats a thick glass (n = 1.5). White light is incident normally to the film. In the reflection, fully destructive interference occurs at 600 nm and fully constructive interference at 700 nm. Calculate the thickness of the acetone film.

Solution:
The incident light is in a low index medium, the thin film of acetone has somewhat higher n = n₂, and the last layer (the glass plate) has the highest refractive index. To see very little or no reflection, the condition
2x = (m + ½) λ/n₂ where m = 0, 1, 2, ...
must hold. Which was developed for the opposite situation (constructive interference) regarding a thin film surrounded on both sides by air n₂ = 1.25 and λ = 700 nm yields
x = 0, 280 nm, 560 nm, 840 nm, 1120 nm, ...
for the first several m values. And the equation shows above gives, with λ = 600 nm,
x = 120 nm, 360 nm, 600 nm, 840 nm, 1080 nm, ...
for the first several m values. The lowest number these lists have in common is x = 840 nm.

6. In a Newton’s rings experiment, the radius of curvature R of the lens is 5 m and the lens diameter is 20 mm. (a) How many bright rings are produced? Assume that λ = 589 nm. (b) How many bright rings would be produced if the arrangement were immersed in water (n = 1.33)?

Solution:
(a) We find m from the theory of interference through thin film in Newton’s ring:
m = r²/Rλ - 1/2 = (10x10⁻³ m)² / (5m)(589x10⁻⁹ m) - 1/2
which (rounding down) yields m = 33. Since the first bright fringe corresponds to m = 0, m = 33 corresponds to the thirty-fourth bright fringe.

(b) We now replace λ by λn = λ/n. Thus,
m = r²/R(λ/n) - 1/2 = r²n/Rλ - 1/2 = (1.33)(10x10⁻³ m)² / (5m)(589x10⁻⁹ m) - 1/2 = 45.
This corresponds to the forty-sixth bright fringe (see remark at the end of our solution in part (a)).

Interference 131

7. A thin film suspended in air is 0.41 μm thick and is illuminated with white light incident perpendicularly on its surface. The index of refraction of the film is 1.5. At what wavelength will visible light that is reflected from the two surfaces of the film undergo fully constructive interference?

Solution:
We use formula for constructive interference:
2nx = (m + ½)λn in thin film
λ = 2nx / (m + ½) = 2(1.5)(410nm) / (m + ½) = 1230nm / (m + ½)
where m = 0, 1, 2, .... The only value of m which, when substituted into the equation above, would yield a wavelength which falls within the visible light range is m = 1.
Therefore,
λ = 1230nm / (1 + ½) = 492 nm.

8. Sodium light (λ = 589 nm) illuminates two slits separated by d = 2 mm. The slit-screen distance D is 40 mm. What percentage error is made to locate the m = 10 bright fringe on the screen rather than using the exact path length difference?

Solution:
Let the m = 10 bright fringes on the screen be a distance y from the central maximum. Then from figure AE 8,
r₁ - r₂ = √(y + d/2)² + D² - √(y - d/2)² + D² = 10λ,
from which we may solve for y. To the order of (d/D)² we find
y = y₀ + (y₀² + d²/4) / 2D²
where y₀ = 10Dλ/d. Thus, we find the percent error as follows:
y₀² + d²/4 / 2y₀D² = ½ (10λ/d)² + 1/8 (d/D)² = ½ (8.89μm / 2000μm)² + 1/8 (2mm / 40mm)²
which yields 0.032%.

9. (a) Show that, in a Newton’s rings experiment, the difference in radius between adjacent bright rings (maxima) is given by Δr = rm+1 - rm ≈ λR / 2rm, assuming m >> 1. (b) Now show that the area between adjacent bright rings is given by A = πλR, assuming m >> 1. Note that this area is independent of m.

Solution:
(a) The binomial theorem allows us to write
√(1+x) = √1 + x/2 + 3x²/48... = √1 + x/2
for x << 1. Thus, the end result from the theory of Newtou’s ring and above relation gives
rm = √Rλm (1 + 1/2m) = √Rλm + 1/4 √Rλ/m
and
rm+1 = √Rλm (1 + 3/2m) = √Rλm + 3/4 √Rλ/m
for very large values of m. Subtracting these, we obtain
Δr = 3/4 √Rλ/m - 1/4 √Rλ/m = 1/2 √Rλ/m


[Page 68]
132 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

(b) We take the differential of the area: dA = d(πr²) = 2πr dr, and replace dr with Δr in anticipation of
using the result from part (a). Thus, the area between adjacent rings for large value of m is
2πrₘ (Δr) ≈ 2π (√Rλm + 1/4m √Rλm) (1/2√R/m) = 2π (√Rλm) (1/2√R/m)
which simplifies to the desired result (πλR). i.e.
A = πλR for m >> 1.

10. Light of wavelength 600 nm falls normally on a thin wedge-shaped film of refractive index 1.4
forming fringes that are 2 mm apart. Find the angle of wedge.

Solution:
We have fringe width in case of wedge-shaped film for normal incidence is
β = λ / 2µα
∴ angle of wedge
α = λ / 2µβ = 600 × 10⁻⁹ / 2 × 1.4 × 2 × 10⁻³ = 1.07 × 10⁻⁴ rad.

Chapter

Diffraction

Thomas Young's double slit experiments put the wave theory of light in a firm footing. A few years later, a French scientist, J.A. Fresnel, independently performed Young's experiment and developed the mathematical theory of interference, diffraction, and other similar phenomena.

[Diagram: Aperture, Plane wave, Screen]
Figure 7.1: From the geometrical optics a sharp shadow is cased if a plane wave of an aperture d is incident.

He showed that the size of the aperture or obstacle relative to the wavelength is the determining factor for diffraction phenomena. Consider a plane wave incident on a narrow slit of thickness d as in figure 7.1. From geometrical optics, region x y in the screen will be illuminated by light. The other portion in the screen (above x and below y) will be dark. However if this region is carefully observed with the aperture size not very large compared to wavelength of light used, the intensity of light in the region xy is not uniform and there is some light even inside the geometrical shadow. Moreover, if the slit (aperture) is made even smaller, the light energy, in large reaches the geometrical shadow.

The phenomenon of spreading or bending of wave when it passes through a narrow opening is known as diffraction. The intensity distribution of light on the screen is called the diffraction pattern. Diffraction phenomena are part of our common experience. The luminous border that surrounds the profile of a mountain just before the sun rise behind it is the example of diffraction effect. Radio wave is detectable inside a closed room but not the light wave, is also due to the diffraction.


[Page 71]
138 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

7.1 Fresnel and Fraunhofer Diffraction

Diffraction patterns are usually classified into two categories depending on where the source and screen are placed. When the source or the screen is near the aperture or obstacle, the wave fronts are spherical and the pattern is quite complex. This is called near-field diffraction or Fresnel diffraction. Some light enters the region of the geometrical shadow, and fringes are observed near the edges of obstruction. If the source, obstacle and screen are far enough away that all lines from the source to the obstacle can be considered parallel, the phenomenon is called far-field diffraction or Fraunhofer diffraction. The diffraction pattern in this type of diffraction is simpler to analyze. Therefore in the latter type of diffraction, lenses and optical systems are used to make the light beam parallel.

The interpretation of diffraction phenomenon was provided by Fresnel. According to Fresnel, the diffraction phenomenon is due to mutual interference of secondary wavelets originating from various points of the wavefront which are not blocked off by the obstacle. In interference, interaction takes place between two separate wavefronts originating from two coherent sources. While in diffraction interaction takes place between the secondary wavelets originating from different points of the same wavefront. The regions of minimum intensity are usually almost perfectly dark in interference. However this is not the case in diffraction. Furthermore, all the maxima are of some intensity in interference but in diffraction they are of varying intensity. The fringe widths in diffraction pattern are never equal but could be equal in interference.

7.2 Fraunhoffer's Single Slit Diffraction

Figure 7.2 shows plane wavefronts (parallel rays) incident on a slit of width D. Since the screen is assumed to be far away, the rays heading for any point are essentially parallel. Consider rays that pass straight through in which θ = 0. These rays are all in phase so there will be a central bright spot on the screen. Now consider rays moving at an angle of θ in such a way that the ray from the top of the slit travels one wavelength nearer than the ray from the bottom edge. In this case the ray passing through the center of the slit will travel one half wavelengths further than the ray at the bottom of the slit. Therefore these two rays will be exactly out of phase with one another and so will destructively interfere. Similarly, other rays lightly above the bottom one will cancel a ray that is the same distance above the central one. Now in general each ray passing through the upper half of the slit will cancel with the corresponding ray from the lower half of the slit. So, all the rays interfere in pairs and the screen will be dark at this particular angle. The path difference between the rays from the top and bottom edge of the slit is D sin θ. Setting this path difference equal to λ, our condition for the first dark fringe we have D sin θ = λ and provides a condition for minima or minimum,

D sin θ = λ

Diffraction 139

By continuing this process of dividing the slit we find that there is complete destructive interference whenever

D sin θ = mλ m = 1,2,3 ... ... 7.1

Figure 7.3: Intensity in diffraction pattern of a single slit as a function of sin θ

Note that m = 0 is not included in Equation 7.1, since θ = 0 corresponds to the central maximum, not to a minimum. Consider a larger angle θ such that the bottom ray travels 3λ/2 farther than the top ray. Here the rays from top third of the slit will cancel in pairs with those from the middle third because they are out of phase by λ/2. However the rays from the bottom third portion of the slit will still reach the screen. Therefore there will be a bright spot in the screen which is not as bright as the central spot (θ = 0). Therefore for constructive interference to occur the following equation for path difference is to be satisfied.

D sin θ = (m + 1/2)λ m = 1,2,3... ... 7.2

Increase the angle θ such that the bottommost ray travels 2λ farther than the topmost ray. In this case the rays from corresponding quarters will cancel since the lengths differ by λ/2. Hence at this particular angle there will be a minimum or zero intensity in the diffraction pattern.

When D >> λ, we observe the usual uniform illumination in the shape of the slit. As the slit width is reduced, the illumination starts to spread out and dark bands become visible. When the slit width is comparable to the wavelength, the central maximum becomes very wide and the screen kept far away will be uniformly illuminated. A plot of the intensity as a function of angle is shown in figure 7.3.

tan θ = x / L
or,

= L . 2λ / D

7.3 Intensity in Single Slit Diffraction
In figure 7.5 a slit of width D is divided into N numbers of very thin tiny strips of width Δy. Each strip passes light in all direction towards the screen. Consider the screen is very far compared to D so that the rays heading for any particular point on the screen are parallel making an angle θ with the horizontal. Light from a given strip are in phase because Δy is much smaller than λ. Let the wave amplitude from one strip be ΔE₀ and is equal to that from other strips since the strips are of equal size and the slit (D) is uniformly illuminated. But the separate amplitudes from the different strips will have a phase difference Δβ, and is equal to

Δβ = 2π / λ Δy sin θ ... 7.3
[ phase difference = 2π / λ x path difference]

The net amplitude on the screen will be sum of amplitudes due to each strip. The phase diagram for the amplitude can be represented as given in figure 7.6. At the center of the screen, θ = 0, all the waves from each strip are in phase, Δβ = 0, so the arrows representing ΔE₀-line up as given in figure 7.6 (i). Therefore the net amplitude of the light arriving at the center of the screen is E₀ = N ΔE₀. Again at a small angle θ, the wavelets of amplitude ΔE₀ add up to give total amplitude on the screen at angle θ as shown in figure 7.6 (ii). The phase difference between the adjacent strips is Δβ. Hence the net phase difference between the wavelets from the top and bottom edge of the slit is given by,

(i) At centre, θ = 0
(ii) Between centre and first minimum.
Figure 7.6: Phaser diagram for single slit diffraction pattern. E₀ is total amplitude at different angles θ.

β = N Δβ = N . 2π / λ . Δy sin θ = 2π / λ D sin θ ... 7.4

Since D = N Δy = slit width. There is phase difference between waves from each strips. Therefore it is represented by chord in the figure 7.7. The arc in figure 7.6 (ii) has


[Page 73]
142 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

length N ΔE0 = E0. However the amplitude of the total wave E0 is the vector sum of amplitude of each wave and is equal to the length of the chord. Note that E0 > E0.

Now to obtain a quantitative value of intensity (or amplitude) for any point on the screen at any angle 0, we consider a limit N → ∞ providing Δy to become infinitesimal width dy. This can be represented by smooth curves as shown in figure 7.7.

If r is the radius of curvature of the arc, from figure 7.7 we obtain
E0 / 2 = r sin(β / 2)

From the circular measurement we can write,
β = E0 / r

Thus,
E0 = 2 r sin(β / 2) = E0 * sin(β/2) / (β/2) ... 7.5

It can clearly be seen from figure 7.7 that β is the phase difference between the waves from the top and bottom edges of the slit. So,
β = (2π / λ) D sin θ

Since intensity is proportional to the square of amplitude, the intensity I0 at any angle 0 is
Iθ = E0^2 = E0^2 * [sin(β/2) / (β/2)]^2 = I0 * [sin(β/2) / (β/2)]^2 ... 7.6

Where I0 (∝ E0^2) is the intensity at θ = 0, the central maximum
Now, Iθ = I0 [sin(π D sin θ / λ) / (π D sin θ / λ)]^2 ... 7.7

Because β = (2π / λ) D sin θ.

It is here to be noted that the minima Iθ = 0 occurs when sin(π D sin θ / λ) = 0 that is (π D sin θ / λ) is an integral multiple of π or (π D sin θ / λ) = π, 2π, 3π .... This indicates the minima will be obtained when
π D sin θ = m π λ
or, D sin θ = m λ m = ±1, ±2, ±3 ...

Diffraction 143

This is what we had obtained in previous unit. Note that m can not be zero because if (π D sin θ / λ) = 0, both the numerator and denominator of Equation 7.7 vanishes. For very small angles, sin(β / 2) / (β / 2) → 1 and Iθ = I0. This is the maximum at the center of the pattern. The Figure 7.3 shows the intensity is diffraction pattern of a single slit as a function of sin θ. In order to determine the positions of maxima, we differentiate equation 7.6 with respect to β and set it equal to zero i. e.
dIθ / dβ = I0 [ (8 sin(β/2) * cos(β/2) / β^2) - (16 sin^2(β/2) / β^3) ] = 0

or 8 sin(β/2) / β^3 [β - 2 tan(β/2)] = 0

The condition β = 0 corresponds to central maxima. The condition for other maxima are roots of the following transcendental equation tan(β/2) = β/2 (maxima).

The root β = 0, corresponds to central maximum. The other roots can be found by determining the points of intersection of the curve y = β/2 and y = tan(β/2). The intersection occurs at β/2 = 0, ± 3π/2, ± 5π/2, ± 7π/2 ... as shown in figure 7.8 and are known as the first maxima, second maxima etc. The first value β = 0 gives the position of principal maxima while β = ± 3π/2, ± 5π/2 etc give the positions of first secondary, second secondary maxima and so on. The intensity of first maxima is
I1 = I0 [sin(3π/2) / (3π/2)]^2 = I0 / 22

Similarly, the intensity of second secondary maxima is
I2 = I0 [sin(5π/2) / (5π/2)]^2 = I0 / 62


[Page 74]
144 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Figure 7.8 A plot of y = tan (πDsin θ/λ). A curve y = πDsin θ/λ is also shown.

Thus the intensity of the first maxima is about 4.96% of the central maxima. Similarly the intensity of the second and third maxima are about 1.68% and 0.83% of the central maxima respectively. Figure 7.8 shows the relation between tangent of (πDsin θ/λ).

Example: 7.3
Light of wavelength 600 nm is incident normally on a slit of width 0.1 mm. What is the intensity at θ = 0.2°?

Solution:
We have,
Iθ = I0 [sin (πDsin θ/λ) / (πDsin θ/λ)]^2 or, Iθ = I0 [sin (β/2) / (β/2)]^2

But,
β = 2π/λ D sin θ
= [2π * (0.1 * 10^-3 m) * sin 0.2] / (600 * 10^-9 m)
= 3.67 radian

Iθ = I0 [sin (3.67/2) / (3.67/2)]^2 = 0.27 I0

7.4 Diffraction Gratings

A diffraction grating consists of thousands of very fine equally spaced parallel slits. Gratings are often made by ruling fine lines on glass plate with a diamond tip. The untouched parts act as the slits. We assume the slits are so fine that the single slit diffraction pattern illuminates the screen uniformly. In single slit diffraction, we imagined the opening to consist of a large indefinite number of point (line) sources. The point (line) sources in the grating are a small, but finite, distance d apart.

Diffraction 145

A diffraction grating containing slits is called a transmission grating. There is another type of grating as well, reflection gratings. Reflection gratings can be made by ruling the lines on a metallic or glass surface from which light is reflected and examined. However the analysis is basically same as for transmission grating and we discuss that for plane transmission grating here.

The analysis of a diffraction grating is much like that of Young's double slit experiment. Let's assume parallel rays of light are incident on the grating as shown in figure 7.9. Consider the slits are narrow enough so that diffraction by each of them spreads light over a very wide angle on a distant screen behind the grating. Therefore interference of light can occur with light from all the other slits. We should note that the light rays that pass through each slit without deviation, θ = 0°, interfere constructively and will produce a bright spot at the center.

When the path difference between rays 1 and 2 in figure 7.9 is λ, they interfere constructively. The same holds for rays 2 and 3, and so on. Any path difference equal to an integral multiple of wavelengths would lead to constructive interference. Here the path difference between rays from adjacent slits is d sin θ where d is the slit separation. Therefore the positions of the principal maxima are given by
d sin θ = m λ, m = 0, 1, 2, 3 ... ... 7.8

These are called principal maxima since the waves from all this slits are in phase. However the bright maxima are much sharper and narrower for a grating. Figure 7.10 shows how the pattern changes as the number of slits increases. Eight slits lead to principal maxima in the same positions as in the double slit pattern, but there are six secondary peaks. For a grating with large number of slits, the principal maxima are sharp lines and the secondary peaks are not visible.

Figure 7.10: Changes in the interference pattern as the number of slits is increased. In a grating with thousands of slits, the principal maxima are very sharp and secondary maxima are not visible.

A small change in θ away from the condition in Equation 7.8 is associated with a small path difference between adjacent slits. For two slits the resultant amplitude changes only slowly with θ. In the case of grating with the same value of d, the path difference


[Page 75]



[Page 76]
148 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Δθ0 = λ/Nd ... 7.13

Thus it is clear from this equation that the larger N is, the narrower will be the central peak. Thus the peaks become narrower for larger N as shown in figure 7.10. Equation 7.13 gives the half width of the central (m = 0) peak. To determine the half width of higher order peaks, Δθm for order m, we differentiate Equation 7.10 so as to relate the change Δδ in δ, to the change Δθ in the angle θ.

dδ = dθ (dδ/dθ) = (2πd/λ) cos θ Δθ

Δθm = (dθ/dδ) Δδ

= (λ / 2πd cos θ) . Δδ

If Δθm represents the half width of a peak of order m (m = 1,2,3....). This is the angle between the peak maximum and minimum to either side. Thus Δδ = 2π/N as discussed above.

In this case.

Δθm = λ / (Nd cos θm) = λ / (mN dθ) ... 7.14

Where θm is the angular position of the mth peak as given by d sinθ = mλ. This relation is valid for small Δδ (= 2π/N) as in case of gratings where N is in high orders.

An important property of a grating is its ability to resolve nearly equal wavelengths. This depends on the width of each principal maximum and the difference between the wavelengths.

The resolving power of a grating refers the power of distinguishing two nearby spectral lines defined by the following equation.

Resolving power (R) = λ/dλ

Where dλ is the separation of two wavelengths which a grating can resolve. One can note that the smaller the value of dλ, the larger is the resolving power.

From Rayleigh's criterion if the principal maximum wavelength λ + dλ falls on the first minimum of wavelength λ, then the wavelengths are said to be resolved. Let this common diffraction angle be represented by θ. So for mth order spectrum, the two wavelengths λ and λ + dλ will be joint resolved if the following conditions are satisfied

d sinθ = m(λ + dλ) ... 7.15

d sinθ = mλ + λ/N ... 7.16

Since the direction (θ + dθ) of the nearby ray of wavelength (λ + dλ) = mλ + λ/N

or, m dλ = λ/N

Diffraction 149

or, R = λ/dλ = m N ... 7.17

Where N = no. of rulings in the grating. Thus the resolving power depends on the total number of lines in grating. Furthermore one should note that the resolving power is proportional to the order of the spectrum. Now from equation 7.14 and 7.17 we can relate dispersive power and resolving power through Rayleigh criteria as

dθ/dλ = (Δθm / λ) R ... 7.18

Now dispersive power refers to side separation of the spectral lines whereas high resolving power refers to the ability of the instrument to show nearby spectral lines as separate ones.

Example: 7.6
A diffraction grating that has 4000 lines to a cm is used d = 6.35 x 10^-6 m at normal incidence. Calculate the dispersive power of the grating in the third order spectrum in wavelength region 5000 A.

Solution:
Again, d cosθ dθ = m dλ
or, dθ/dλ = m / (d cos θ) = 3 x 4000 / 0.8 = 15,000.

Example: 7.7
a) What is the resolving power required to resolve the two sodium lines at 589 nm and 589.6 nm?
b) If a grating is 2 cm side, how many lines per millimeter are needed to resolve these wavelengths in 3rd order?

Solution:
a) The difference in wavelengths in dλ = 0.6nm and one take the average wavelength for λ. Thus the resolving power needed is,
R = λavg / dλ = 589.3 nm / 0.6 nm = 982.

b) The number of slits N is related to the width of the grating by w = Nd, here d is the slit separation.
Thus,
R = mN = m (w/d)
d = mw / R = 3 x 2 cm / 982 = 0.0061 cm.

.. The number of lines per millimeter is 1 / 0.061 mm = 16.4 lines per mm.


[Page 77]
150 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Example: 7.8
Yellow sodium light, which consists of two wavelength, λ1 = 589 nm and λ2 = 589.59 nm, falls on a 7500 lines/cm diffraction grating. Determine (a) the maximum order in that will be present for sodium light, (b) the angular dispersion of the grating, (c) the width of grating necessary to resolve the two sodium lines, (d) the angular width of each sodium line.

Solutions:
a) Here d = 1cm / 7500 = 1.33 x 10^-4 m
The maximum value of m for λ = 589 nm can be found from d sin θ = mλ with sin θ ≤ 1 as
m = d / λ sin θ ≤ 1.33 x 10^-6 / 589 x 10^-9 = 2.25
So m = 2 is the maximum order present because it takes a whole number.

b) The angular dispersion is,
dθ / dλ = m / d cos θ
The sodium lines in order 2 occur for sin θ = 2λ / d = 2 x 589 x 10^-9 / 1.33 x 10^-6 = 0.886
and
cos θ = sqrt(1 - sin^2 θ) = 0.464
:.
dθ / dλ = 2 / (1.33 x 10^-6 m) x 0.464 = 3.24 x 10^3 rad / nm
= 0.186 deg / nm.

a) The resolving power needed is,
R = λavg / Δλ = 589 / 0.59 nm = 1000.
and since R = mN, the number of lines N = R / m = 1000 / 2 = 500.
So the grating will be, 500 / 7500 cm^-1 = 0.0667 cm wide.

b) For a grating with the minimum 500 lines, the angular width given by
Δθ = λ / Nd cos θ = 589 x 10^-9 m / (500) x (1.33 x 10^-6) x (0.464) = 0.0019 rad = 0.11°.

7.6 X-ray diffraction and Material Testing
In 1895, W.C. Roentgen discovered that when electrons were accelerated by a high voltage in a vacuum tube and allowed to strike a glass (or metal) surface, fluorescent minerals some distance away would flow, and film would become exposed. Roentgen explained these effects to a new type of radiation different from cathode rays. They were given the name X-rays after the algebraic symbol X meaning an unknown quantity. Later on he found that X-rays penetrated though some materials better than through others.

The complicated technique of X-ray diffraction (or crystallography) has proved very effective to examine the microscopic world of atoms and molecules. In a simple crystal such as NaCl, the atoms are arranged in an orderly cubical fashion, figure 7.11, with atoms spaced a distance d apart. Consider that a beam of X-rays is incident on the crystal at an angle θ to the surface. Two rays, one reflected as shown from two subsequent planes of atoms. The two rays will interfere constructively if the I - ray travels an extra distance than that by II - ray, is a whole number multiple of wavelengths.

Thus for constructive interference
2 d sin θ = mλ, m = 1, 2, 3 ... 7.19

Where, m is an integer. This equation is called Bragg's diffraction equation. Therefore, if the X-ray wavelength is known and the angle θ at which constructive interference occurs is measured, the atomic spacing d can be obtained. This is the basis of x-ray crystallography.

Rearranged the above equation we get,
sin θ = mλ / 2d ... 7.20

Examining this equation we see that, we will only get appreciable diffraction (θ > 0) when wavelength is less than 2d. For example if λ = 2.1 d, then sin θ = 1.05 for m = 1
Since sin θ cannot exceed one, diffraction will not occur under this condition.

A material with crystalline structure has a regular arrangement of atoms. The distance between each atoms will of course, depend on the arrangement of atoms but this distance will be constant through the lattice for particular structure. The distance between consecutive parallel atomic planes is similar to the spacing (d) between slits found in the light diffraction experiment. The wavelength of light is in the region of 400 nm to 700 nm while the interplanner spacing is about 2 Å. It may be seen that no diffraction of light by crystal of the materials will ever take place. So d is the important factor for materials characterization.

A cubic crystal of density ρ and molar mass M having n ions of diffracting centers (n = 2 N atoms for NaCl) is considered. If d be the crystal spacing then mass associated with each ion be
ρd^3 = M / n
or, d = [M / 2 Np]^(1/3) ... 7.21


[Page 78]
150
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Example: 7.8
Yellow sodium light, which consists of two wavelength, λ1 = 589 nm and λ2 = 589.59 nm, falls on a 7500 lines/m diffraction grating. Determine (a) the maximum order in that will be present for sodium light, (b) the angular dispersion of the grating, (c) the width of grating necessary to resolve the two sodium lines, (d) the angular width of each sodium line.

Solutions:
a) Here d = 1cm / 7500 = 1.33 x 10^-6 m
The maximum value of m for λ = 589 nm can be found from d sin θ = mλ, with sin θ ≤ 1 as
m = d / λ sin θ ≤ (1.33 x 10^-6) / (589 x 10^-9) = 2.25
So m = 2 is the maximum order present because it takes a whole number.

b) The angular dispersion is,
dθ / dλ = m / d cos θ
The sodium lines in order 2 occur for sin θ = 2λ / d = (2 x 589 x 10^-9) / 1.33 x 10^-6 = 0.886
and cos θ = √1 - sin² θ = 0.464
∴ dθ / dλ = 2 / (1.33 x 10^-6) x 0.464 = 3.24 x 10³ rad / nm
= 0.186 deg / nm.

a) The resolving power needed is,
R = λavg / Δλ = 589 / 0.59 nm = 1000.
and since R = mN, the number of lines N = R / m = 1000 / 2 = 500.
So the grating will be, 500 / 7500cm^-1 = 0.0667cm wide.

b) For a grating with the minimum 500 lines, the angular width given by
Δθ = λ / Nd cos θ = 589 x 10^-9 m / (500) x (1.33 x 10^-6) x (0.464) = 0.0019 rad = 0.11°.

ρd³ = M / n
or, d = [ M / 2 Nρ ]^1/3 ... 7.21

The distance from the centre of the pattern to this second minimum is
y2 = D tan θ2 = (3 m) tan (1.178 × 10⁻³ rad) = 3.534 × 10⁻³ m.
