4. Monochromatic light with wavelength 538 nm is incident on a slit with width 0.025 mm. The distance from the slit to a screen is 3.5 m. Consider a point on the screen 1.1 cm from the central maximum. Calculate (a) θ for that point, (b) α, and (c) the ratio of the intensity at that point to the intensity at the central maximum.
Solution:
(a) We have the angle of diffraction, θ = sin⁻¹ (0.011 m/ 3.5 m) = 0.18°.
(b) Using the relation of intensity distribution of diffraction fringes due to single slit, we get,
α = (πa/λ) sin θ = (π(0.025mm)sin 0.18)/ 538×10⁻⁶ mm = 0.46 rad.
(c) Hence, the ratio of intensities at that point to the central maximum from theory is
I(θ)/Im = (sin α / α)² = 0.93.

5. A diffraction grating 20 mm wide has 6000 rulings. Light of wavelength 589 nm is incident perpendicularly on the grating. What are the (a) largest, (b) second largest, and (c) third largest values of θ at which maxima appear on a distance viewing screen?
Solution:
The distance between adjacent rulings is
d = 20 mm/6000 = 0.00333 mm = 3.33 µm.
(a) Let d sin θ = mλ (m = 0, ± 1, ± 2, ...). Since |mλ/d| > 1 for |m| ≥ 6, the largest value of θ corresponds to |m| = 5, which yields
θ = sin⁻¹ |mλ/d| = sin⁻¹ (5(0.589µm)/ 3.33µm) = 62.1°.
(b) The second largest value of θ corresponds to |m| = 4, which yields
θ = sin⁻¹ |mλ/d| = sin⁻¹ (4(0.589µm)/ 3.33µm) = 45°.
(c) The third largest value of θ corresponds to |m| = 3, which yields
θ = sin⁻¹ |mλ/d| = sin⁻¹ (3(0.589µm)/ 3.33µm) = 32°.

6. Light of wavelength 600 nm is incident normally on a diffraction grating. Two adjacent maxima occur at angles given by sin θ = 0.2 and sin θ = 0.3. The fourth-order maxima are missing. (a) What is the separation between adjacent slits? (b) What is the smallest slit width this grating can have? For that slit width, what are the (c) largest, (d) second largest, and (e) third largest values of the order number m of the maxima produced by the grating?
Solution:
(a) Maxima of a diffraction grating pattern occur at angles θ given by d sin θ = mλ, where d is the slit separation, λ is the wavelength, and m is an integer. The two lines are adjacent, so their order numbers differ by unity. Let m be the order number for the line with sin θ = 0.2 and m + 1 be the order number for the line with sin θ = 0.3. Then, 0.2 d = mλ, and 0.3 d = (m + 1)λ. We subtract the first equation from the second to obtain 0.1 d = λ.
d = λ/0.1 = (600 × 10⁻⁹ m)/0.1 = 6 × 10⁻⁶ m.

Diffraction 155
(b) Minima of the single-slit diffraction pattern occur at angles θ given by a sin θ = mλ, where a is the slit width. Since the fourth-order interference maximum is missing, it must fall at one of these angles. If a is the smallest slit width for which this order is missing, the angle must be given by a sin θ = λ. It is also given by d sin θ = 4λ, so
a = d/4 = (6 × 10⁻⁶ m)/4 = 1.5 × 10⁻⁶ m.
(c) First, we set θ = 90° and find the largest value of m for which mλ < d sin θ. This is the highest order that is diffracted toward the screen. The condition is the same as m < d/λ and since
d/λ = (6 × 10⁻⁶ m)/(600 × 10⁻⁹ m) = 10.
The highest order seen is the m = 9 order. The fourth and eight orders are missing, so the observable orders are m = 0, 1, 2, 3, 5, 6, 7, and 9. Thus, the largest value of the order number is m = 9.
(d) Using the result obtained in (c), the second largest value of the order number is m = 7.
(e) Similarly, the third largest value of the order number is m = 6.

7. Assume that the limits of the visible spectrum are arbitrarily chosen as 430 and 680 nm. Calculate the number of rulings per millimeter of a grating that will spread the first-order spectrum through an angle of 20°.
Solution:
The angular positions of the first-order diffraction lines are given by d sin θ = λ. Let λ₁ be the shorter wavelength (430 nm) and θ be the angular position of the line associated with it. Let λ₂ be the longer wavelength (680 nm), and let θ + Δθ be the angular position of the line associated with it. Here Δθ = 20°. Then,
λ₁ = d sin θ,
λ₂ = d sin (θ + Δθ).
So we have,
sin (θ + Δθ) = sin θ cos Δθ + cos θ sin Δθ,
then use the equation for the first line to replace sin θ with λ₁/d, and cos θ with sqrt(1 - λ₁²/d²). After multiplying by d, we obtain
λ₂ = λ₁ cos Δθ + sqrt(d² - λ₁²) sin Δθ = λ₂.
Solving for d, we find
d = sqrt(((λ₂ - λ₁ cos Δθ)² + (λ₁ sin Δθ)²)/ sin² Δθ)
d = sqrt(((680nm) - (430nm)cos 20)² + (430nm)sin 20)² / sin² 20)
= 914 nm = 9.14 × 10⁻⁴ mm.
So, there are 1/d = 1/(9.14 × 10⁻⁴ mm) = 1.09 × 10³ rulings per mm.

8. Prove the expression for the intensity pattern for a three-slit 'grating':
I = 1/9 Im (1 + 4 cos Φ + 4 cos² Φ),
where Φ = (2πd sin θ)/λ and a << λ.
Solution:
The slit width is much less than the wavelength of the light, the central peak of the single-slit diffraction pattern is spread across the screen and the diffraction envelope can be ignored. Consider three waves, one from each slit. Since the slits are evenly spaced, the phase difference for waves from the first and second slits is the same as the phase difference for waves from the second and third slits. The electric fields of the waves at the screen can be written as
E₁ = E₀ sin (ωt)
E₂ = E₀ sin (ωt + Φ)
Figure AE 8


[Page 81]
156 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
E = E0 sin (ωt + 2φ)
where φ = (2πd/λ) sin θ. Here d is the separation of adjacent slits and λ is the wavelength. The vector diagram is shown on the right. It yields
E = E1 + E2 + E3 = E0 (1 + 2 cos φ) sin (ωt + φ)
where E = E0 (1 + 2 cos φ)
is the amplitude of the resultant wave. Since the intensity of a wave is proportional to the square of the electric field, we may write I = AE0^2 (1 + 2 cos φ)^2, where A is a constant of proportionality. If I_m is the intensity at the centre of the pattern, for which φ = 0, then I_m = 9 AE0^2. We take A to be I_m / 9 E0^2 and then we get
I = I_m / 9 (1 + 2 cos φ)^2 = I_m / 9 (1 + 4 cos φ + 4 cos^2 φ).

9. A diffraction grating 3 cm wide produces the second order at 33° with light of wavelength 600 nm. What is the total number of lines on the grating?
Solution:
We solve equation d sin θ = mλ for d:
d = mλ / sin θ = 2(600×10^-9 m) / sin 33 = 2.203 × 10^-6 m = 2.203 × 10^-4 cm
which is typically expressed in reciprocal form as the 'number of lines per centimeter' (or per millimeter, or per inch):
1/d = 4539 lines/cm.
The full width is 3 cm, so the number of lines (4539/cm)(3 cm) = 1.36 × 10^4.

10. Show that the dispersion of a grating is D = tan θ / λ
Solution:
The dispersion of a grating is given by D = dθ/dλ, where θ is the angular position of a line associated with wavelength λ. The angular position and wavelength are related by d sin θ = mλ, where d is the slit separation (which we made boldfaced in order not to confuse it with the d used in the derivative, below) and m is an integer. We differentiate this expression with respect to θ to obtain
dθ/dλ d cos θ = m,
or, D = dθ/dλ = m / d cos θ
Now m = (d/λ) sin θ, so
D = d sin θ / dλ cos θ = tan θ / λ

11. Show that a grating made up of alternately transparent and opaque strips of equal width eliminates all the even orders of maxima (except m = 0).
Solution:
The trigonometric identity used in this proof is sin (2θ) = 2 sin θ cos θ, and the intensity distribution relation for a grating width a + b = 2a (i.e. a = b) is similar to that of a single slit of width 2a then, we get
I(θ) = I0 [ (sin 2πa sin (θ/λ)) / (2πa sin (θ/λ)) ]^2, instead of

Diffraction 157
I(θ) = I0 [ (sin πa sin (θ/λ)) / (πa sin (θ/λ)) ]^2 = I0 [ sin(β)/β ]^2
In case of single slit Equation 7.7, we get the maxima at β = 0 ± 3π/2 ± 5π/2 ± 7π/2 ... But for grating
β = πa/λ * 2 = 0 ± 3π, ± 5π, ± 7π, ... i.e. all even orders of maxima except (m = 0) are eliminated and became all odds.

12. What do you understand by missing of order diffraction spectra? What particular spectra would be absent if the width of transparences and opacities of the grating are equal?
Solution:
The direction of principal maxima due to grating according to theory is (a + b) sin θ = nλ
The direction of minima due to single slit is given by
a sin θ = mλ,
where n = m = 1, 2, 3, ... i.e. order of principal maxima.
Dividing this relation we get,
(a + b) / a = n / m
or, n = (a + b) / a * m.
This is the condition for nth order to be absent in grating spectra. Now if a = b then for grating n = 2m.

1. Discuss the diffraction pattern obtained by a narrow wire illuminated by monochromatic light from a narrow slit parallel to the wire. How do you use the pattern to measure the thickness of the wire?

7. How many completer orders are formed for the visible range 400 - 700 nm by a grating that has 6000 lines per cm?

9. Light of wavelength 633 nm is incident on a narrow slit. The angle between the first diffraction minimum on one side of the central maxima and the first minimum on the other side is 1.2°. What is the width of the slit?
10. What must be the ratio of the slit width to the wavelength for a single slit to have the first diffraction minimum at θ = 45°?
11. A 0.1 mm wide slit is illuminated by light of wavelength 589 nm. Consider a point P on a viewing screen on which the diffraction pattern of the slit is viewed; the point is at 30° from the central axis of the slit. What is the phase difference between the Huygens wavelets arriving at point P from the top and midpoint of the slit?
12. If superman really had x ray vision at 0.1 nm wavelength and a 4 mm pupil diameter, at what maximum altitude could he distinguish villains from heroes, assuming that he needs to resolve points separated by 5 cm to do this?

(= 5.1 m) telescope at Mount Palomar, assuming that this separation is determined by diffraction effects. The distance from Earth to the Moon is 3.8 x 10⁵ km. Polarization
8
Chapter

The phenomena of interference and diffraction provide evidence that light has wave characteristics. Initially both Young and Fresnel thought light in terms of longitudinal waves. The realization that light is a transverse wave arose from the study of its polarization. To understand the meaning of this term, consider a mechanical example. Figure 8.1 (a) shows a rope vibrating in a vertical plane and in figure 8.1 (b) it vibrates in a horizontal plane. In each case the wave is said to be plane polarized that is the oscillations are in a plane.

Figure 8.1 A linearly polarized wave.

Now place an obstacle in the path of the wave that contains a vertical slit (figure 8.2). In such case we can observe that a wave that is in vertical direction will pass through, but horizontally polarized wave will not.

Figure 8.2 Vertically polarized wave passing through a vertical slit.

In contrast if horizontal slit is used, the vertically polarized wave will be stopped. In addition if both types of slits are used, one after other, neither waves will pass through. Here it should be noted that the polarization can exist only for transverse waves, not for longitudinal waves. The longitudinal wave vibrates only along the direction of motion so neither orientation of the slit would stop them. The phenomenon of polarization of light was recognized in the nineteenth century. However earlier, even in Newton's time, a phenomena depending upon the polarization was already realized. In 1969, E. Bartholinius looked at a small object through a crystal of Iceland spar (calcite) and discovered two refracted images. Certain other crystals such as tourmaline would not transmit one or the other of these two


[Page 84]
162 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
rays, depending on the orientation of the crystal. After the polarization phenomenon, we recognize that the two rays in a doubly refracting crystal are plane polarized in mutually perpendicular directions. The tourmaline crystal acts as a 'slit' to eliminate one or the other if properly oriented (see figure 8.2).

Hence polarization of light is the phenomena due to which the vibration of light are restricted in a particular plane. Such a light is called plane polarized light. The plane in which the vibration of polarized light takes place is called the plane of vibration. The plane perpendicular to the plane of vibration where no vibration occurs is plane of polarization.

The material which polarizes the transverse wave is called Polaroid. Tourmaline crystal, Nichol prism, Calcite crystal are some of the examples of Polaroid.

8.1 Representation of a Polarized and Unpolarized Light
Light is not necessarily polarized. Light can be unpolarized. That means the source has vibrations in many planes at once as shown in figure 8.3. An ordinary light bulb emits unpolarized light as the sun does. Plane polarized light can be obtained from unpolarized light using crystal like tourmaline. Nowadays with modern technology polarized light can be obtained with polarized sheet in which there are complicated long molecules arranged with their axes parallel. This Polaroid act as a series of parallel slits to allow one orientation of polarization to pass through whereas a perpendicular polarization is almost completely absorbed.

Unpolarized light can be polarized by reflection, when incident on a polaroid medium. The angle of incidence at which the light is polarized is called angle of polarization or Brewster's angle (ip). The unpolarized light from a transparent medium is polarized if the angle made by reflected ray and transmitted ray is right angle such that the refractive index of the medium from Snell's law will be

n = sin ip / sin r = sin ip / sin(90-ip) = tan ip ... 8.1

This is also called Brewster's law of polarization.

If a beam of plane polarized light passes through a Polaroid whose axis is at an angle 0 to the incident polarization direction, the beam will emerge plane polarized light parallel to the Polaroid axis. The amplitude of the light will be reduced by cos θ as shown in figure 8.4. Since the intensity of a light beam is intensity of the transmitted beam from the polarizer is
I = I0 cos² θ ... 8.2

Polarization 163
Where θ is the angle between the polarizer axis and the plane of polarization of the incoming wave and I0 is the incoming intensity. Equation 8.2 is often referred as Malus law after E. Malus.

As shown in figure 8.5, in case of unpolarized light, the orientation of the electric field is not fixed. In other words any orientation is equally probable. Therefore the electric field in unpolarized light will be vibrating in all directions. For simplicity we assume half the number of waves to be vibrating in vertical direction and another half in horizontal direction. In figure 8.5 the vibration of vertical polarized light are represented as parallel (arrows heads) and that of horizontal polarized light are represented as perpendicular (dots) to the plane of paper.

Example: 8.1
Unpolarized light passes through two Polaroid; the axis of one is vertical and that of the other is at 60° to the vertical. What is the orientation and intensity of the transmitted light?

Solution:
The first Polaroid eliminates half the light so the intensity is reduced by half
I1 = 1/2 I0
The light reaching the second polarizer is vertically polarized and so is reduced intensity by the following relation.
I = I0 cos² θ
or, I2 = I1 cos² 60 = 1/4 I1
.: I2 = 1/8 I0
The transmitted light has intensity one eighths that of original and is plane polarized at a 60° angle to the vertical.


[Page 85]
8.2 Double Refraction

Liquids and amorphous solids (like glass) are called isotropic because their properties do not depend on direction. The speed of light is the same in all directions in such substances. However in some crystals, the speed of light is different in different directions. Such materials are referred as anisotropic. These substances are said to be doubly refracting or birefringent as they give rise to an unusual phenomena. When a small object is viewed through a crystal of Iceland spar (calcite, CaCO3) or quartz (SiO2) there will be two refracted images. Obviously this is because of an unusual two types of refractions or there are two refracted rays. One refracted ray, called the ordinary ray (O) passes in a normal way. But the other ray, extraordinary ray (E), is refracted with a different angle. The O ray obeys Snell's law whereas the E ray does not. Sugar solution, ice are other examples of doubly refracting objects.

The E and O rays are found to be plane polarized in mutually perpendicular directions and are indicated in figure 8.6. The dots on the O ray indicate the vibrations are perpendicular to the plane of paper whereas E ray is indicated by the short arrows. Along the so called 'optic axis', the O and E rays have the same speed and therefore they possesses same refractive index. In other words optic axis is the direction in which there is no double refraction. Since the O ray is perpendicular to the optic axis, the speed of O ray will be same in all directions. However E ray, on the other hand, travels with different speed in different directions. It travels with the same speed on the O ray if its polarization vector is perpendicular to the optic axis. At other angles E ray has a higher speed (or in some crystals, a lower speed) which reaches a maximum (or minimum) when its polarization is parallel to the optic axis. Therefore the refractive index for ordinary light no is the same for all directions whereas that for extraordinary ray, ne, depends on the angle. In some crystals the velocity of O light is greater than that of E light. Since n = c/v, no < ne in these crystals. Such crystals are referred as positive crystals. Whereas crystals in which velocity of E ray is greater than O ray and hence ne > no, are referred as negative crystals.

As stated above both the rays are plane polarized. The vibrations of O ray are perpendicular to the principal section of the crystal but vibration of E ray is in the plane of principal section of the crystal. Therefore the two different rays are plane polarized. The vibrations of these two specific rays are perpendicular to each other.

Some birefringent crystals, such as tourmaline, absorb one of the polarized components more strongly than the other. Such a crystal is said to show dictroism. For sufficiently thick crystals, one component of unpolarized light will be completely eliminated by dicroism producing the emerging light as plane polarized. This is the basics of polaroid sheet.

8.3 Nicol Prism

Nicol prism is a convenient optical device for producing and analyzing the plane polarized light. It was invented by William Nicol in 1826. It is based upon the principle of double refraction.

The Ordinary and Extraordinary rays are polarized and are perpendicular to each other. A Nicol prism can separate the O and E rays from an unpolarized light source. It is an optical device made from a natural crystal of calcite (CaCO3 hydrates) sometimes also called Iceland spar. Nicol prism is often used in different optical instruments to produce and to analyze polarized light.

Nicol prism is made by cutting a calcite crystal along a diagonal as shown in figure 8.7. They are cemented back together with a layer of Canada balsam. Canada balsam is a transparent medium that is optically denser than calcite for E ray and less dense for O ray in light (e.g. sodium light). In other words calcite is a negative crystal with no > ne and Canada balsam has refractive index between no and ne. [no = 1.65836; ncb = 1.55 and ne = 1.48641].

When an ordinary unpolarized ray incidents on the first calcite crystal surface (prism), it breaks into O and E rays. Once these rays reach the cemented layer they behave in two ways. For the O ray, the cemented layer satisfies the condition for total internal reflection since light is travelling from a denser medium to rarer one. Therefore the rays are refracted at the first crystal surface; the O ray is totally internally reflected whereas the E ray passes on to emerge parallel to the incident rays as shown in figure 8.7. In this way Nicol prism can transmit only plane polarized light from the unpolarized light.

To study the optical properties of crystals two Nicol prisms (N1, N2) can be placed one after the other as shown in figure 8.8. The prism N1 is used to produce plane polarized light and is called Polarizer. The other prism N2 is used to analyze the light and this is called an analyzer. When N1 and N2 are parallelly oriented, the light from the polarizer passes on through.

the analyzer, (figure 8.8 upper). If we rotate the analyzer the intensity of the light observed afterwards will keep on decreasing and no light is transmitted through the analyzer when the


[Page 86]
166 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
analyzer is rotated through 90°. In this particular condition the E ray in prism N2 is totally
internally reflected.

8.4 Phase Retardation Plates
Retardation plates are plates of uniaxial crystals cut with optic axis parallel to the
faces of the plate. When a plane polarized light is incident normally on such a plate, the
vibrations in the beam break at the first face into one vibration along the optic axis and other
perpendicular to it. These components travel the plate in same direction but with different
velocities (vo and ve). If t is the thickness of the plate, time taken by the two beams in
traversing the plate will differ by

|time difference| = t (1/vo - 1/ve)

The relative retardation in terms of equivalent length in vacuum is given by

Δ distance = c × (time difference) = t |no - ne|

Therefore a relative phase retardation of 2π/λ × (Δ distance) is created between the two
mutually perpendicular vibrations. This is why these plates are called phase retardation
plates. The retardation in path can be made selective. If the retardation in path is equal to λ/4,
the plate is referred as quarter wave plate and if it is λ/2, the plate is half wave plate
respectively.

Quarter - wave plate:
It is made of a doubly refracting crystal like Calcite or Quartz. The refracting faces of
the crystal are cut parallel to the optic axes and its thickness is such that it introduces a phase
difference of π/2 or path difference of λ/4 between the emerging ordinary and extraordinary
rays.

For negative crystal the thickness of the quarter wave plate
t = λ / 4(no - ne)
and for positive crystal
t = λ / 4(ne - no)

This plate is used to produce circularly and elliptically polarized light when
introduced on the path of a plane polarized light.

Polarization 167

Half - wave plate:
The half wave plate is made of doubly refracting crystal. Its refracting faces are cut
parallel to optic axes and its thickness is such that it produces a phase difference of π or path
difference λ/2 between ordinary and extraordinary rays.

Hence the thickness of the negative half wave plate is
t = λ / 2(no - ne)
and for positive crystal
t = λ / 2(ne - no)

This type of plate is used to produce plane polarized light.

Example: 8.2
To make a quarter wave plate of calcite for light of wavelength 589 nm, how thick it should
be? (no = 1.658; ne = 1.486)

Solution:
To make a quarter wave plate in calcite the path difference should be λ/4
and is also equal to (no - ne)t
or (no - ne)t = λ/4
or, t = λ / 4(no - ne) = 589×10^-9 / 4(1.658 - 1.486) = 856 nm.

8.5 Linearly, Circularly and Elliptically Polarized Light.
Consider
monochromatic light incidents
on a Nicol Prism. The emerging
light is plane polarized. Let this
emergent light falls normally on
a doubly refracting crystal
whose faces are cut parallel to
the optic axis. The plane
polarized light is splitted into O
and E components. Both of
these O and E ray travel in the
same direction however they have different velocities. Let δ be the phase difference between
the two rays after the crystal be represented by two SHM perpendicular to each other as
shown in figure 8.9. Let the amplitude of the incidental plane polarized light be A. It makes


[Page 87]
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
an angle 0 with the optic axis. The amplitude of the O ray which is vibrating along a direction
PO is given by A sin 0 while that of E ray along PE by
A cos 0.

Also these rays are represented as
y = A sin 0 sin ωt and E ray
x = A cos 0 sin (ωt + δ)
where δ = phase difference between the rays
traversing a distance d.

Let's represent A cos 0 = a; A sin 0 = b.
Therefore x = a sin (ωt + δ); y = b sin ωt
or, sin ωt = y/b and
cos ωt = √1 - sin^2 ωt = √1 - y^2/b^2
again x = a sin ωt cos δ + cos ωt sin δ
or, x/a = y/b cos δ + √1 - y^2/b^2 sin δ
or, (x/a - y/b cos δ)^2 = (1 - y^2/b^2) sin^2 δ
or, x^2/a^2 - 2xy/ab cos δ + y^2/b^2 = sin^2 δ ... 8.3

Special Cases:
(A) When phase difference between the two waves is 0°; δ = 0; equation 8.3 provides.
x^2/a^2 - 2xy/ab + y^2/b^2 = 0
(x/a - y/b)^2 = 0
or, y = b/a x.

Polarization 169
This is the equation of a straight line. Therefore if the phase difference between the
two rays is zero, the emergent light will be linearly polarized, figure 8.10 (a).

(B) When the phase difference is π/2 equation (8.3) provides
x^2/a^2 + y^2/b^2 = 1
Which is the equation of a ellipse. Hence if the phase difference between the two rays
is π/2, the emergent light will be elliptically polarized, figure 8.10 (b).

(C) When the phase difference is π/2 and a = b i.e.
A cos 0 = A sin 0; equation 8.2 provides
x^2/a^2 + y^2/a^2 = 1
i.e. x^2 + y^2 = a^2
Which is the equation of a circle of radius a. So if the phase difference between the
two rays is π/2 and a = b, in such case the emergent light will be circularly polarized, figure
8.10 (c).

When an unpolarized light is passed through a Nicol prism, the emergent light is
plane polarized. The emergent ray once passed through retardation plate (a quarter plate in
general). Such final emergent ray will be elliptically polarized. Similarly if the angle between
the optic axis and diagonal of Nicol Prism is 45° of 135°, the emergent ray is circularly
polarized.

8.6 Optical Activity
Consider a set of Nicol Prism placed in such a way that there is no emergent light
coming out from the analyzer, as in figure 8.11. But if a quartz plate which is cut with its face


[Page 88]
170 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

parallel to the optic axis is kept between the Prism in such a way that the emergent ray from N1 falls at angle 90° with the plate there is light emerging out from N2 as well. The plane of vibration of plane polarized light from N1 entering the quartz plate is gradually rotated. The amount of rotation of the plane of vibration is turned depends mainly on the thickness of the quartz plate and wavelength of light. This property of rotating the plane vibration by crystals is referred as Optical Activity. Optical activity is performed in materials which are optically active. Some examples of optical active substances are sugar crystals, sugar solution in water, sodium chloride, turpentine etc.

Figure 8.11(a) Rotation of plane of vibration when a quartz plate is introduced.

Figure 8.11 (b) Rotation of plane vibration inside optically active substances

There are two types of optically active substances. In the first type of substances they rotate the plane vibration to the right of the plane vibration is rotated in clockwise direction. Such substances are termed as right handed or dextro-rotatory. In the second type of substances, they rotate the plane of vibration to the left or the plane vibration is rotated in anticlockwise direction. These second type of substances are termed as left handed or laevo-rotatory.

The use of optical activity is to find the amount or concentration of solute in a given solution such as sugar solution.

8.7 Specific Rotation

Solution of optically active substances rotates the plane of linearly polarized light. The angle through which the plane polarized light is rotated depends on the thickness of the medium, concentration of the solute in the solution, the wavelength of the light used and temperature of the substance.

The specific rotation is the rotation produced by a decimeter long solution containing 1 gm of optically active substance in one cubic centimeter of the solution.

S = 10 θ / lC

Where θ is the angle of rotation, l is the length of the solution in cm, C is the concentration of active substance in gm/cm³ in the solution and S is the specific rotation at a given temperature for a given wavelength of light.

Polarization 171

The angle of rotation of plane polarization made by an optically active substance is measured by a device called Polarimeter. Laurent's half - shade polarimeter, Bi - Quartz polarimeter are its example.

8.8 Laurent's Half Shade Polarimeter

A polarimeter is a device for measuring the angle through which the plane of polarization rotates by an optically active substance. A Laurent half shade polarimeter consists two Nicol Prisms as an analyzer and polarizer rotating about a common axis. It also consists a circular half - shade plate, objective, eyepiece and a 10 cm long tube. Figure 8.12 (a) represents the arrangement of different parts of the polarimeter.

Figure 8.12 (a)

The incident light from source enters into the Nicol prism through objective lens. The plane of vibration of the polarized light will come out of the polarizer and fall on the half - shade plat. The half - shade plate is a semicircular quartz plate cemented with the glass plate. The quartz plate is half wave plate and thickness of the glass plate is so chosen that it absorbs the same amount of light as is done by quartz plate. Let the incident vibration on the half shade be along OP (figure 8.12 b). On passing light through the glass plate, the vibrations remains along AP but on passing through quartz half these will split up into extraordinary AE and ordinary components AO. On passing through the quartz plate x is these vibrations. Thus AO suffers a phase difference by π vibration will advance in phase by π and will take along AO'. So the resultant vibration will be along AQ with angle θ as shown in figure 8.12 b.

If the principal section of analyzing Nicol is parallel to OAO', both halves of the field of view will be equally illuminated.

A slight rotation of the analysis from this setting either in clockwise or anti clockwise direction causes one component greater than other and then either the quartz appears brighter than the glass or vice-versa.

Example: 8.3
If the plane polarization of a given solution is turned through 6.6°, calculate the specific rotation of the sample. The length of the 20% solution is 10 cm.

Solution:
Here, θ = 6.6° C = 20% l = 10 cm
So, S = 10 θ / lC = 10 × 6.6 / 10 × 0.2 = 33°.

Example: 8.4
A 20 cm long tube having a sugar solution rotates the plane of polarization by 10°. If the specific rotation of sugar is 66°, calculate the strength of the solution.

Solution:
Given; l = 20 cm θ = 10°
S = 66° C = ?


[Page 89]
172 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
We know,
S = 10.0 / 16 = 10x10 / 66x20 = 0.075 gm/cc.

8.9 Scattering of Light by the Atmosphere
Unpolarized light from sun falls on a molecule of the earth's atmosphere as shown in figure 8.13. The electric field of the electromagnetic wave sets the electric charges inside the molecule into motion. The molecules also absorb a part of incident radiation. However the molecule re-emits the light since the charges are oscillating. In figure 8.13 the motion of charges is resolved into two components. An observer who is at right angle to the direction of sunlight will see plane polarized light, since there is no light emitted along the line of other component of the oscillation.

Scattering of sunlight by earth's atmosphere depends on the wavelength of incident light (λ). For smaller particles than wavelength of light, the particles will be less of an obstruction to long wavelength than to short ones. The scattering decreases as 1/λ^4. Red light is thus scattered less than blue or violet. This is the reason behind the blue colour of the sky. If human eye could have detected the violet light, the sky would have appeared violet. At sunrise and sunset, the sunlight has to pass through a maximum length of the atmosphere. Much of the blue light have been taken out by scattering. The light that reaches the surface of the earth is thus lacking in blue. This is why sunset (or sunrise) appear red. Here we have used red and blue colours of the sunlight since the intensity of these colours are high in sunlight.

The inverse fourth power law of wavelength (∝ 1/λ^4) is valid if the scattering objects are much smaller than the wavelength of light. This is valid for most of the molecules in air. Clouds in other hand contain water droplets or crystals whose size is larger than λ, they scatter all colours nearly uniformly. This is the region why clouds appear white or gray.

Polarization 173

8.10 Additional Examples:
1. In figure AE 1, beam of unpolarized light, with intensity 43 W/m^2, is sent into a system of two polarizing sheets with polarizing directions at angles θ_1 = 70° and θ_2 = 90° to the y axis. What is the intensity of the light transmitted by the system?
Solution:
In this case, we replace I_o cos 70° by ½ I_o as the intensity of the light after passing through the first polarizer. Therefore,
I_t = ½ cos^2 (90° - 70°) = ½ (43 W/m^2) (cos^2 20°) = 19 W/m^2.

2. At a beach the light is generally partially polarized due to reflections off sand and water. At a particular beach on a particular day near sundown, the horizontal component of the electric field vector is 2.3 times the vertical component. A standing sunbather puts on polarizing sunglasses; the glasses eliminate the horizontal field component. (a) What fraction of the light intensity received before the glasses were put on now reaches the sunbather's eyes? (b) The sunbather, still wearing the glasses, lies on his side. What fraction of the light intensity received before the glasses were put on now reaches his eyes?
Solution:
(a) The fraction of light which is transmitted by the glasses is
I_f / I_o = E_v^2 / E_v^2 + E_h^2 = E_v^2 / E_v^2 + (2.3 E_v)^2 = 0.16.

(b) Since now the horizontal component of E will pass through the glass,
I_f / I_o = E_h^2 / E_v^2 + E_h^2 = (2.3 E_v)^2 / E_v^2 + (2.3 E_v)^2 = 0.84.

3. A beam of polarized light is sent into a system of two polarizing sheets. Relative to the polarization direction of that incident light, the polarizing directions of the sheets are at angles θ for the first sheet and 90° for the second sheet. If 0.1 of the incident intensity is transmitted by the two sheets, what is θ?
Solution:
According to Malus law, as the polarized beam of intensity I_o passes the first polarizer, its intensity is reduced to I_o cos^2 θ. After passing through the second polarizer which makes a 90° angle with the first filter, the intensity is
I = I_o cos^2 θ * cos^2 (90° - θ)
= (I_o cos^2 θ) sin^2 θ = I_o / 10
which implies sin^2 θ cos^2 θ = 1/10
or, sin θ cos θ = sin 2θ / 2 = 1 / √10
This leads to θ = 70° or 20°.

4. An unpolarized light is sent into a system of three polarizing sheets, which transmits 0.03 of the initial light intensity. The polarizing directions of the first and third sheets are at angles θ_1 = 0° and θ_3 = 90°. What are the (a) smaller and (b) larger possible values of angle θ_2 ( < 90°) for the polarizing direction of sheet 2?
Solution:
We apply Malus law for polarizing sheet to obtain


[Page 90]
174 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

I = ½ I₀ cos² θ₁ cos (90° - θ₂).
We rewrite this as I/I₀ = ⅛ sin² (2θ₂).

(a) Therefore we find θ₂ = ½ sin⁻¹ √0.4 = 19.6°.
(b) Since the first expression we wrote is symmetric under the exchange: θ₂ ↔ 90° - θ₂, then we see that the angle's complement, 70.4°, is also a solution.

5. We want to rotate the direction of polarization of a beam of polarized light through 90° by sending the beam through one or more polarizing sheets. (a) What is the minimum number of sheets required? (b) What is the minimum number of sheets required if the transmitted intensity is to be more than 60% of the original intensity?

Solution:
(a) The rotation cannot be done with a single sheet. If a sheet is placed with its polarizing direction at an angle of 90° to the direction of polarization of the incident radiation, no radiation is transmitted. It can be done with two sheets. We place the first sheet with its polarizing direction at some angle θ, between 0 and 90°, to the direction of polarization of the incident radiation. Place the second sheet with its polarizing direction at 90° to the polarization direction of the incident radiation. The transmitted radiation is then polarized at 90° to the incident polarization direction. The intensity according to Malus law is
I = I₀ cos² θ cos² (90° - θ) = I₀ cos² θ sin² θ,
where I₀ is the incident radiation. If θ is not 0 or 90°, the transmitted intensity is not zero.

(b) Consider n sheets, with the polarizing direction of the first sheet making an angle of θ = 90°/n relative to the direction of polarization of the incident radiation. The polarizing direction of each successive sheet is rotated 90°/n in the same sense from the polarizing direction of the previous sheet. The transmitted radiation is polarized, with its direction of polarization making an angle of 90° with the direction of polarization of the incident radiation. The intensity is
I = I₀ cos²ⁿ (90°/n).
We want the smallest integer value of n for which this is greater than 0.6 I₀. We start with n = 2 and calculate cos²ⁿ (90°/n). If the result is greater than 0.6, we have obtained the solution. If it is less, increase n by 1 and try again. We repeat this process, increasing n by 1 each time, until we have a value for which cos²ⁿ (90°/n) is greater than 0.6. The first one will be n = 5.

6. Light travelling in water of refractive index 1.33 is incident on a plate of glass with index of refraction 1.53. At what angle of incidence is the reflected light fully polarized?

Solution:
The angle of incident iₚ for which reflected light is fully polarized is given by iₚ + r = 90°. If n₁ is the index of refraction for the medium of incidence and n₂ is the index of refraction for the second medium, then from Brewster's law,
iₚ = tan⁻¹ (n₁ / n₂) = tan⁻¹ (1.53 / 1.33) = 49°.

7. Calculate the (a) upper and (b) lower limit of the Brewster angle for white light incident on fused quartz Assume that the wavelength limits of the light are 400 and 700 nm for which refractive indices are 1.47 and 1.456 respectively.

Solution:
We have nₘₐₓ = 1.47 for λ = 400 nm and nₘᵢₙ = 1.456 for λ = 700 nm.

(a) The corresponding Brewster's angles are
iₚ,ₘₐₓ = tan⁻¹ nₘₐₓ = tan⁻¹ (1.47) = 55.8°.

(b) and iₚ,ₘᵢₙ = tan⁻¹ nₘᵢₙ = tan⁻¹ (1.456) = 55.5°.

Polarization 175
8. Three polarizing sheets are stacked. The first and third are crossed; the one between has its polarizing direction at 45° to the polarizing directions of the other two. What fraction of the intensity of an originally unpolarized beam is transmitted by the stack?

Solution:
Using Snell's law and Malus law, we obtain
(Iₜᵣₐₙₛ / I₀) = (1/2) (cos² 45) (cos² 45) = 1/8 = 0.125.

9. In figure AE 9, where n₁ = 1.7, n₂ = 1.5, and n₃ = 1.3, light refracts from material 1 into material 2. If it is incident at point A at the critical angle for the interface between materials 2 and 3, what are (a) the angle of refraction at point B and (b) the initial angle θ? If, instead, light is incident at B at the critical angle for the interface between materials 2 and 3, what are (c) the angle of refraction at point A and (d) the initial angle θ? If, instead of all that, light is incident at point A at Brewster's angle for the interface between materials 2 and 3, what are (e) the angle of refraction at point B and (f) the initial angle θ?

Solution:
(a) The angle of incidence θₐ,₁ at B is the complement of the critical angle at A; its sine is
sin θₐ,₁ = cos θ꜀ = √(1 - (n₃/n₂)² )
so that the angle of refraction θₐ,₂ at B becomes
θₐ,₂ = sin⁻¹ ( (n₂/n₁) √(1 - (n₃/n₂)² ) ) = sin⁻¹ ( (n₂/n₁) √( (n₂²/n₂²) - 1 ) ) = 35.1°.

(b) From n₁ sin θ = n₂ sin θ꜀ = n₂ (n₃/n₂), we find
θ = sin⁻¹ (n₃ / n₁) = 49.9°.

(c) The angle of incidence θₐ,₁ at A is the complement of the critical angle at B, its sine is
sin θₐ,₁ = cos θ꜀ = √(1 - (n₃/n₂)² )
so that the angle of refraction θₐ,₂ at A becomes
θₐ,₂ = sin⁻¹ ( (n₂/n₁) √(1 - (n₃/n₂)² ) ) = sin⁻¹ ( (n₂/n₁) √( (n₂²/n₂²) - 1 ) ) = 35.1°.

(d) From the relation
n₁ sin θ = n₂ sin θₐ,₁ = n₂ √(1 - (n₃/n₂)² ) = √(n₂² - n₃²),
then we find,
θ = sin⁻¹ ( √(n₂² - n₃²) / n₁ ) = 26.1°.


[Page 91]
176
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
(e) The angle of incidence 0B,1 at B is the complement of the Brewster angle at A; its sine is
sin 0B,1 = n2 / (n1^2 + n2^2)^(1/2)
so that the angle of refraction 0B,2 at B becomes
0B,2 = sin^-1 (n2^2 / (n2 * (n1^2 + n2^2)^(1/2))) = 60.7°.
(f) From the relation,
n1 sin 0 = n2 sin 0Brewster = n2 * (n2 / (n1^2 + n2^2)^(1/2)),
then we find,
0 = sin^-1 (n2 * n2 / (n1 * (n1^2 + n2^2)^(1/2))) = 35.3°.

LASER
9
Chapter
LASER stands for Light Amplification by Stimulated Emission of Radiation. The stimulated emission is used for amplifying light waves. Laser light is coherent source. An atom can emit radiation by two different methods: spontaneous and stimulated emission. T.H. Maimam was the first person to demonstrate the laser in 1960. After this laser action has been demonstrated with gases, solids and semiconductors etc.
9.1 Characteristics of laser light
The following are the main characteristics of laser light.
all waves in same phase.
a) Coherence – The laser beam is coherent both spatially and temporally to a light degree. From two independent laser beams there is possibility to observe the phenomenon of interference.
b) Directionality – A laser beam is highly directional. A laser light of 10 cm diameter if passed from earth to moon, it will not have a width of 5 km.
c) Monochromaticity – Laser can produce light in a very narrow wavelength beam.
d) Highly intense – The laser light has a high brightness compared to an ordinary light. Due to its high intensity, it can also be used for welding i.e. at a high temperature.
9.2 Spontaneous and stimulated emission
Consider two atomic states of energies E1 and E2 as shown in figure 9.1. The number of electrons in the levels at an absolute temperature T is given by Boltzmann factor
N1 = No e^-E1/kT and N2 = No e^-E2/kT
Figure 9.1 The relative number of particles in the two energy levels is given by Boltzmann factor.


[Page 93]
180
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
LASER
181
Hence,
N2 / N1 = e^{-(E2-E1)/kT} = e^{-hf/kT}
... 9.1
In other case let the atom is in the state 2 as shown in figure 9.3. A photon of appropriate frequency "resonate" in some fashion and induces it to drop to level one.
Where k is Boltzmann constant. In thermal equilibrium, the higher states is less populated i.e. N2 < N1. Where N1 and N2 are the electron numbers in the ground and higher states respectively.
Even at thermodynamic equilibrium, there is an ongoing absorption and emission of radiation. Consider an atom in state one. If an incoming photon has the correct frequency, (hf = E2 - E1), the photon will be absorbed such that the atom will be raised to level two as shown in figure 9.2. This phenomenon is called absorption.
The absorption process depends on the energy density ρ of the radiation at this frequency as well as N1. The number of such transition from state one to state two per unit time is given by N1 B12 ρ, where B12 is the probability of the transition. B12 is also called Einstein's coefficient for absorption of radiation. The probability that an atom from the upper level E2 drop to lower one E1 as shown in figure 9.2 is A21. Here the phenomenon is called spontaneous emission and the constant A21 is called Einstein's coefficient of spontaneous emission of radiation. Number of such spontaneous emission depends on N2 but on the presence of the external radiation. The transition from N2 to N1 per unit time is A21 N2.
Figure 9.2 A particle in the lower state absorbs a photon and have a transition to the higher state (left). The particle falls from the higher to the lower state during spontaneous emission (right).
In thermodynamic equilibrium N2 A21 = N1 B12 ρ. This shows the rate of upward transition is equal to the rate of downward transition. Hence,
ρ = (N2 A21) / (N1 B12)
... 9.2
Substituting N2 / N1 from equation 9.1, the function resembles Wien's radiation law rather than Planck's radiation law, describing the energy density of cavity radiation.
This difficulty caused Einstein to propose another mechanism by which an atom can interact with radiation.
Figure 9.3 The stimulated emission. The incoming photon causes a particle in the upper level to fall to lower level. The emitted photon is coherent in the original photon and both photons move in same direction.
There are two outgoing photons of same frequency. The stimulated photon moves off in the same direction as the incoming photon. This phenomenon is called stimulated emission. The stimulated emission has a probability B21. Therefore the number of such transitions per unit time depends on the radiation density and on N2 and is given by N2 B21 ρ.
Hence in stimulated emission the balanced rate of absorption and rate of emission provides,
N1 B12 ρ = N2 (A21 + B21 ρ)
... 9.3
ρ = A21 / ( (N1 / N2) B12 - B21 )
... 9.4
Substituting N2 / N1 from equation 9.1, ρ is of the form of Plank's law provided B21 = B12 and A21 is related to B in a simple way. This simple derivation of Plank's law showed that the process of stimulated emission is a compulsion to attain the thermodynamic equilibrium. Under normal condition the absorption process dominates because there are a larger number of atoms in the lower level.
ρ = A21 / B12 * ( 1 / (e^{hf/kT} - B21 / B12) )
... 9.5
According to Planck's law of radiation, the energy density
ρ = (8π h f^3 / c^3) * ( 1 / (e^{hf/kT} - 1) )
... 9.6
Comparing 9.5 and 9.6 we get
B21 / B12 = 1
... 9.7
And we have
A21 = B12 * (8π h f^3 / c^3)
... 9.8
And A21 = B21 * (8π h f^3 / c^3)
... 9.9


[Page 94]
182 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
These equations are Einstein's equations. A21, B12 and B21 are called Einstein's coefficients since B21=B12. So the probability of stimulated emission is same as that of induced absorption.

9.3 Population Inversion
It is clear from equation 9.1 that population is maximum in the ground state and decreases exponentially as one goes to higher energy states. It has also been discussed that N2 < N1 since E1 < E2. Therefore as N1 > N2, whenever an electromagnetic wave is incident, there is net absorption of the radiation. For lasing action, it is necessary that stimulated emission predominate over spontaneous emission. This happens only if N2 > N1. The situation in which the upper levels are more populated than the lower levels is called population inversion. Consider a three level system. It has three energy states E1, E2 and E3. The highest energy state E3 is least populated and the lowest state E1 is mostly populated (figure 9.4).

Figure 9.4 Population of three level system under thermal equilibrium (left). The three level system showing population inversion of E2 with respect to E1 (right).

The curve in figure 9.4 shows the Boltzmann distribution. Here N1 > N2 > N3, so the system is external energy, it is possible that N2 exceeds N1. This situation occurs if E2 happens to be a metastable state. It is a state in which the electrons remain longer than usual so that the transition to the lower state occurs by stimulated emission rather than spontaneous. In such situation the transition probability between level 3 and 2 is very high. Therefore the stage of population inversion is achieved.

9.4 Pumping
The state of population inversion cannot be achieved thermally. Generally population inversion is achieved by exciting the medium with suitable form of energy. Such a phenomenon of population inversion occurs with a process called pumping. There are different methods of pumping a laser to produce population inversion which is a must for stimulated emission. They are for example
i) Optical pumping
ii) Chemical reactions
iii) Electric discharge
iv) Direct conversion
v) Inelastic atom atom collision etc.

A light source is used to supply luminous energy in optical pumping. Usually this energy comes in the form of light flashes. This method was used in ruby laser first by T.H. Maimam and widely used in solid state lasers. In such case the lasing material is placed inside a helical xenon flash lamp.

LASER 183
Pumping can also be done by direct electron excitation as in an electric discharge. In gas lasers this method is used. A high electric field (several kV/m) emits electrons from the cathode and is accelerated towards anode. Some of the excited electrons collide with atoms of the active medium. This ionise the medium and raise it to the higher level and acquires the population inversion.

In some lasers, the excitation by electrical discharge provides the initial excitation, raising one type of atoms to their excited states. Then it inelastically collides with another type of atoms. It is those latter atoms that provide the population inversion needed for the laser emission. This method of pumping is used He - Ne gas laser.

A direct conversion of electrical energy into radiation occurs in light emitting diodes. Semiconductor lasers use this method for pumping. The energy comes from a chemical reaction without other energy sources in a chemical laser. Hydrogen combine with fluorine and the reaction liberates heat energy to pump a CO2 laser.

9.5 He - Ne Laser
The first gas laser to be operated successfully was the He - Ne laser. A Javan in 1960, operated the first continuous wave laser using the mixture of these gases in a discharge tube.

Figure 9.5 The four energy levels involved in the He-Ne laser. The metastable state E3 of the Neon atoms is populated by collisions into the electrons in the state E1 of the Helium atoms.

The He - Ne laser consists of a long and narrow discharge tube of diameter about 2-8 mm and length 10-100 cm. The lasing material is the mixture of the gases with a concentration of about 15 percent Helium and 85 percent Neon. The mixture works as lasing material because of same compatible properties of the two gases. The electrodes in the discharge tube are connected to a high voltage source of few kV d.c. So an electric discharge takes place within the gas. With this high voltage some of the He atoms are raised to a metastable state at E1 = 20.61 eV above the ground state as shown in figure 9.5. It so happens that Ne has a metastable state at nearly the same energy, E2 = 20.66 eV. The He atoms do not quickly return to the ground state by spontaneous emission. Rather it transfers the energy to Ne - atoms during collision. With such collision the energy of excited He atoms will be transferred and it drops to ground state. However, getting the excess energy Ne atom is excited to the state E3. The small difference of 0.05 eV is supplied the kinetic energy of the atoms. In this way the higher state E3 of Neon, becomes the metastable state, than E2. Therefore the population for inversion is achieved for Ne atoms. Hence the lasing action takes place by stimulated emission between the E3 and E2 states of Neon. The laser light emitted is of about 632.8 nm.


[Page 95]
184 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
LASER 185

Example: 9.1
A laser beam of wavelength 6000 Å on the earth is focused by a lens of diameter 2 m on a crater on the moon. The distance of the moon is 4×10⁸ m. How big is the spot on the moon if the effect of the earth's atmosphere is neglected?
Solution:
Angular spread (dθ) = λ/d = 6×10⁻⁷ / 2 = 3×10⁻⁷ rad
Area spread, i. e. area of the spot on the moon is
A = (D × dθ)² = (4×10⁸ × 3×10⁻⁷)²
= 1.44×10⁴ m²

Example: 9.2
A laser beam has a power of 50 mW. It has an aperture of 5×10⁻³ m and it emits light of wavelength 7200 Å. The beam is focused with a lens of focal length 0.12 m Calculate the area and the intensity of the image.
Solution:
We have dθ = λ/d = 7200×10⁻¹⁰ / 5×10⁻³ = 1.44×10⁻⁴ rad
Area spread or the swfit area for the angle dθ is
A = (dθ × f)² = (1.44×10⁻⁴ × 0.1)²
= 2.074×10⁻¹⁰ m²
And the intensity I = P/A = 50×10⁻³ / 2.074×10⁻¹⁰ = 2.41×10⁸ W/m²

9.6 Semiconductor Laser
Lasing action can also be produced in semiconductors. Semiconductor lasers are highly efficient and they are for implication for optical communication.
A semiconductor laser is made by forming a p - n junction between p type and n type materials in the lattice (figure 9.6). However the semiconductor is heavily doped. The p-n junction so formed is forward biased. The light emission is due to the recombination of electrons and holes. More and more photons become stronger.

When we apply electric field to solid materials, light is produced. This phenomenon is called electroluminescence. The light emitted by this method is incoherent. As the current across p-n junction is gradually increased from zero, spontaneous emission starts. Increasing the current, a time is reached when spontaneous emission charges out to laser beam. This process is enhanced by polishing the faces of the p-n junction. This type of laser light was first obtained in GaAs. Later InP, InAs, InSb etc have proved to be useful materials for laser action.

9.7 Uses of Laser
1. Scientific uses - Laser offer a wide opportunity to investigate the basic laws of interaction of atoms and molecules with electromagnetic wave of high intensity.
2. Distance measurement - Laser light is coherent so is used for measurement of distances based on interferometric techniques. It can be used for small distance measurement also. For large distance the time taken for a laser pulse to travel from the source to target and back again is measured. The velocity of light is used to calculate the distance between source and target.
3. Communication - Laser light can be used in different communication system. The rate at which information can be transmitted is proportional to the band width of the information carrier. Bandwidth is proportional to the carrier frequency. The step from microwaves to optical region expands by a factor of 10,000. So by modulating, a large numbers of messages can be sent simultaneously. The modulation is achieved in different ways e.g. amplitude modulation, frequency modulation or pulse code modulation.
4. Industry - Laser is highly intense. Therefore it is used in welding, cutting of materials. The laser can be focussed into a fine spot. High energy density is possible at a fine spot. Therefore laser is used in welding fine wire. Laser can also be used for drilling fine holes due to its high energy density.
5. Medicine - Lasers are used in eye surgery for the treatment of detached retina. Laser is widely used for treatment of dental decay, destruction of malignant tumours and for treatment of skin diseases.
6. Space technology and research - Lasers are used for automatic control of rockets and satellites. It can also be used for detection and destroy airplanes, missiles and tanks.
7. Research - Laser can be used in laser induced fusion process. It can also be used in isotope separation. In atmospheric science it is used to measure the cloud optical depth using LIDAR technology.

9.8 Holography
Laser light is used in the production of three dimensional images interestingly. Such images are called holograms. In general photography, the film records the light nearly at each point. When the film is viewed, light from it or passing through it gives a 2D picture. In holography, the images are formed by interference. After a hologram is made, a laser beam is splitted into two parts by a semisilvered mirror as shown in figure 9.7. This allows a part of the ray to go directly to the film and the rest passes to the object to be photographed and is reflected to the film separately. Light from each point on the object reaches each point on the film. The interference of the two beams allows the film to record both the intensity and the relative phase of the light at each point. Once the film is developed it is placed in laser beam and a 3D image of the object is observed. It looks like original object but not in reality.


[Page 96]
186 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

9.9 Additional Examples:

1. For an ordinary source of light the temperature is of the order of 10³ K. Show that for such a source the process of spontaneous emission dominates over stimulated emission.
Solution:
Let us take the average wavelength of visible light, λ = 6000 Å. The corresponding frequency is
f = 3x10⁸ / 6000x10⁻¹⁰ = 5x10¹⁴ Hz
Planck's constant h = 6.6x10⁻³⁴ Js
Boltzman constant k = 1.38x10⁻²³ JK⁻¹
Temperature T = 10³ K
We know Rate of spontaneous emission/rate of stimulated emission
N₂A₂₁ / N₂B₂₁u(v) = A₂₁ / B₂₁u(v) = exp(hv / kT) - 1
= exp[ (6.6x10⁻³⁴ Js x 5.0x10¹⁴ s⁻¹) / (1.38x10⁻²³ JK⁻¹ x 10³ K) ] - 1
= e²³ - 1 ≈ 10¹⁰
Thus, the rate of spontaneous emission is much, much greater than the rate of stimulated emission.

2. A three-level laser emits a light of wavelength of 5500 Å. What will be the ratio of populations of upper level (E₂) to the lower energy level (E₁) if the optical pumping mechanism is shut off (Assume T = 300 K). At what temperature for the above conditions would the ratio of populations be ½?
Solution:
Given λ = 5500 Å
We have E₂ - E₁ = hf = hc/λ
= (6.63x10⁻³⁴ J/sec)x(3x10⁸ m/s) / (5.5x10⁻⁷ m)x(1.6x10⁻¹⁹ J/eV) = 2.26eV
and kT can be calculated as
kT = (8.62x10⁻⁵ eV/K)x(300K) = 0.0259eV
The ratio of upper to the lower energy levels i.e.
E₂/E₁ = e^{-(E₂ - E₁)/kT} = e⁻²·²⁶/⁰·⁰²⁵⁹
= e⁻⁸⁷·³
= 1.3x10⁻³⁸
Again E₂/E₁ = 1/2
Then by using above equation
E₂/E₁ = 1/2 = e^{-(E₂-E₁)/kT}
e^{(E₂-E₁)/kT} = 2
(E₂ - E₁)/kT = loge 2
T = (E₂ - E₁)/k loge 2 = 2.26eV / (8.62x10⁻⁵ eV/K)x(0.693)
T = 37800 K
Or, This temperature is much hotter than the sun.

3. (a) A He-Ne laser of wavelength 6328 Å has an internal beam of radius 0.28 mm. What would be the beam divergence angle?
(b) What lower limit might be expected for the beam divergence, if we can control the beam-waist radius (r₀) by lower cavity design and selecting the wavelength? By what factor will the beam divergence decrease if we design a laser having a beam waist of 2.4 mm radius and wavelength 2000 Å?
Solution:
(a) Given λ = 5500 Å, r = 0.23 mm
We use the formula
θ = λ/πr₀ = 6328x10⁻¹⁰m / 3.14x2.3x10⁻⁴ m = 8.76x10⁻⁴ rad
i.e. beam radius increases about 8.76 cm at every 100 m distance.

(b) Given λ = 2000 Å, r = 2.4 mm
then θ = λ/πr₀ = 2000x10⁻¹⁰m / 3.14x2.4x10⁻³ m = 2.65x10⁻⁵ rad
i.e. about 33 fold decrease is beam spread over the He-Ne laser described in part (a) and in this case beam radius increases about 2.65 mm at every 100 m distance.

4. Calculate the power per unit area delivered by a laser pulse of energy 4.0 x 10⁻³ J and the pulse length in time as 10⁻⁹ sec when the pulse is focused on target to a very small spot of radius 1.5 x 10⁻⁵ m.
Solution:
Given P = 4.0 x 10⁻³ J, r = 1.5 x 10⁻⁵ m
Power delivered per unit area is given by
I = P/A, where P = 4.0x10⁻³ J / 10⁻⁹ sec = 4.0x10⁶ W
And A = πr² = 3.14x(1.5x10⁻⁵)² = 7.065x10⁻¹⁰ m²
So, I = P/A = 4.0x10⁶ W / 7.065x10⁻¹⁰ m² = 5.7x10¹⁵ Wm⁻²
Or, I = 5.7 x 10¹⁵ W/m²

5. A laser beam has wavelength of 7200 Å and aperture 5x 10⁻³ m. The laser beam is sent to moon at a distance of 4 x 10⁸ m from the earth. Determine (a) angular spread and (b) real spread when it reach the moon.
Solution:
Given λ = 7.2 x 10⁻⁷ m, radius r = d/2 = 2.5x 10⁻³ m, D = 4.0x 10⁸ m
(a) We know angular spread
θ = λ/πr = 0.637λ / r
θ = 0.637x7.2x10⁻⁷ / 2.5x10⁻³ = 1.834x10⁻⁴ radian
(c) Areal spread = (θD)²
= (4x10⁸ x 1.834x10⁻⁴)² = 53.85x10⁸ m²

10
Chapter
Electric Force and Field

When you put off your sweater entering into a dark room after travelling a long distance on a dry day, you may get shocked by an annoying spark of static electricity. When a used comb drops on the ground from hand, large numbers of tiny particles stick on it. Lightening is the same phenomenon on a vast scale which can destroy enormous building structure. When materials behave in this way, they are said to be electrified or to have become electrically charged. It is reasonable to conclude that the phenomenon includes some physical entity being transferred from one body to another or vice-versa. The physical entity is called electric charge.

10.1 Electric Charge
The discovery that a body could be made attractive by rubbing is attributed to ancient Greek Thales as early as 600 BC. First he observed the Greek's particle of spinning silk with an amber (English word) spindle (Greek word for amber is "Elektron"), a body made attractive by rubbing is said to be electrified or charged. Later, in 1600 AD Gilbert found that other substances besides amber could be electrified, such as glass, rubber, plastics etc. However he failed to electrify metals. In 1734, Du Fay found that a metal could be charged by rubbing with fur or silk, but only if it were held in a handle of plastic, rubber etc. This experiment followed the discovery by Gray in 1729, that electric charges would be transmitted through the human body, metal, water etc. Their experiments then concluded the important results;
* The transmission of electric charges to the ground is called "The Earthing", where the Earth is assured as the sea of charges.
* There are two types of materials on the basis of transmission of charges: which are "conductor" such as metal and "insulator" such as glass, amber, plastics etc.
* Two types of charges can be classified. It was further confirmed by pith-ball experiments. Such as electric field glass rubbed with fur produced "positive charge" and "negative charge" to that on ebonite rod rubbed with silk.

Benjamin Franklin in 1780 suggested calling these two kinds of charges and concluded that "like charges repel and unlike charges attract each other". After the discovery of "electron" in substances by J.J. Thomson in 1897, it was concluded that the "charge" is


[Page 98]
190 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
responsible to the loss or gain of electrons from the materials due to rubbing or by another
process.

10.2 Nature of Electric Charges
An electric charge is a scalar quantity. Addition of equal and opposite charges gives a
zero charge, which concludes that the electric charge is conserved. This is not surprising
because what has actually happened is the transfer of some electrons from our body to
another i.e. no charge is created or annihilated. On the other hand, a high energy gamma ray
photon can get converted to an electron and positron. Again an electron and a positron may
destroy each other producing two quanta of gamma rays. So the total charge in an isolated
system remains always conserved.

Another fundamental property of electric charge is its quantum nature. Millikan from
oil drop experiment in 1909, concluded that electric charges always occurs as some intergral
multiple of a fundamental amount of charge e i.e.

q = ne, n = 0, ± 1. ± 2, ± 3 ... ... 10.1

Where e is basic unit or elementary charge is one of the fundamental constants of
nature i.e. e = 1.6x10^-19 C. n is integer number represents the loss or gain of electrons. When
a physical property such as charge exists in discrete "packets" rather than in continuous
amounts, the property is said to be quantized.

According to Modern theory, the sizes of electric charges are identified with the sizes
of the fundamental charged particles. The radius of such charged particles is of the order of
10^-13 cm, which is so small that we can consider them as point charges. However, in a large
body, the charge can be measured by the number of atom (N) in the body and distribution of
basic unit of charge in the nucleus.

q = NZe = Ze [Na m / M] ... 10.2

Where NA = 6.02x10^23 atoms/mol, m = mass of the body, M = molar mass, Z = atomic
number. The charge is enormously high in the nucleus i.e. charge density is uniformly
distributed.

Example 10.1
Considering charge of one electron be equal to -1.6x10^-19 C, how many electrons must
be removed (lost) from a body to give it a positive charge of 8x10^-19 C?

Solution:
From equation 10.1 we get
n = q / e = 8x10^-19 C / 1.6x10^-19 C = 5
Hence the number of electrons is 5.

Example 10.2
If an atom of carbon has six electrons, what is the charge on the carbon nucleus?

Solution:
Now we have, n = 6, so

Electric Force and Field 191
q = ne = -6 x 1.6x10^-19 C = -9.6x10^-19 C
Hence the charge on the carbon nucleus is + 9.6x10^-19 C.

Example 10.3
If a copper coin has mass 3.11g, what is the total charge on the nucleus of the atoms in
the coin?

Solution:
For copper Z = 29, M = 63.5 g/mol,
NA = 6.02x10^23 atom/mol,
So from relation 10.2 we have;
q = Ze [NA m / M] = 29 x 1.6x10^-19 [6.02x10^23 x 3.11 / 63.5] = 1.37x10^5 C.
This charge is extremely high in comparison to the charges that we might get by rubbing a plastic rod
(10^C).

10.3 Electric Force: Coulomb's Law
Charles A. Coulomb in 1785 studied interaction on charged particles and measured
the magnitudes of the electric forces between them using the torsion balance. The operating
principle of the torsion balance similar to Cavendish device used to measure gravitational
constant. Basically the balance used to establish the inverse square law for electric force
between charges. The magnitude of force also depends on the quantity of charge on each
charged body. From the experiment Coulomb concluded that, the electrostatic force is,
* attractive for unlike charges and repulsive for like charges,
* proportional to the product of the charges q1 and q2,
* inversely proportional to the square of the distance or separation (r) between the
particles and directed along the line joining them,
* a conservative force.

Now we can express coulomb law as an equation giving the magnitude of the electric
force between two point charges;
F = K |q1 q2| / r^2 ... 10.3

Where, K is a constant called Coulomb constant. The Coulomb constant depends on
the choice of unit system and medium between charges. For SI system and air medium;

K = 1 / 4πε0
K = 8.9875x10^9 Nm^2/C^2 ≅ 9x10^9 N.m^2/C^2.

It can also be written in SI unit and material medium
K = 1 / 4πε = 1 / 4πε0εr ... 10.4


[Page 99]
192 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
Where ε is permittivity of medium, ε0 is permittivity of free space = 8.8542×10⁻¹² F/m
and εᵣ = ε/ε0 = relative permittivity or dielectric constant. Hence the force between the charges
in SI system in air medium is
F = 1/4πε0r² |q1q2| ... 10.5
Where εᵣ = 1.005 ≅ 1.000 for air
εᵣ is always greater than 1 for material medium. Force being a vector quantity, has a fixed direction. The direction is determined by the relative sign of the two electric charges as shown in figure 10.1. Hence for the vector distance r₁₂, equation 10.5 will be
F = F₁₂ = 1/4πε0 r₁₂² f̂₁₂ ... 10.6
Where f̂₁₂ is a unit vector in the direction of r₁₂ and is
f̂₁₂ = r₁₂ / |r₁₂|
The electric force obeys Newton's third law. So the force exerted by q₂ on q₁ is equal in magnitude to the force exerted by q₁ on q₂ is
F₂₁ = -F₁₂ ... 10.7
When more than two charges are present, the force between any pair of them is same as equation 10.6 and the resultant force on any one of them equals the vector sum of the forces exerted by the various individual charges,
i.e. F₁ = F₁₂ + F₁₃ + F₁₄ + ... = 1/4πε0 ∑ q₁qⱼ/rij² r̂ᵢⱼ ... 10.8
This is also called principle of superposition of electric forces.

Example 10.4
A charge of 60 nC is 84 cm from another charge of -80 nC as shown in figure 10.2. What forces in air will these charges exert on a charge of +0.21 μC placed half way between them?
Solution:
Let the repulsive force between q₁ and q₃ be F₁₃ which is given by
F₁₃ = K q₁q₃/r² = 9×10⁹ × 80×10⁻⁹ × 0.21×10⁻⁶ / (0.42)²
= 85.597×10⁻³ N.
Similarly the attractive force between q₂ and q₃ be F₂₃ which is given by
F₂₃ = K q₂q₃/r² = 9×10⁹ × 60×10⁻⁹ × 0.21×10⁻⁶ / (0.42)²
=
The direction of F₁₃ and F₂₃ is same so the resultant at O is
F = F₁₃ + F₂₃ = 1.498 × 10⁻³ N.

Example 10.5
At what distance the resultant force will be zero if two charges lie on the x-axis at a distance 2 cm as shown in figure 10.3 where q₁ = 15 μc and q₂ = 6 μc?
Solution:
Let a negative charge q₃ in between q₁ and q₂ be assume, then the forces between q₁q₃ and q₂q₃ should be equal and opposite, such that,
F₁₃ = F₂₃
K |q₁q₃|/(2-r₂)² = K |q₂q₃|/r₂²
or, 15×10⁻⁶×q₃ / (2-r₂)² = 6×10⁻⁶×q₃ / r₂²
i.e. r₂ = 0.775 m and -3.44 m. The distance -3.44 m is another location at which resultant force is zero which lies outside the two charges.

Example 10.6
If two similar balls of mass m are hung from silk threads of length l m and carry similar charges as shown in figure 10.4 assuming θ is very small, prove that
separation of the balls is x = [q²l / 2πε0mg]^(1/3) where q is the charge of each ball.
Solution:
Forces acting on the ball are: Weigh of the ball (mg), tension on the string (T) and electrical force Fe. Directions of these forces are as shown in figure 10.4. Resolving T into two rectangular components, at equilibrium we have,
T sin θ = Fe ... (i)
T cos θ = mg ... (ii)
Dividing equation (i) by (ii), we get
tan θ = Fe/mg ... (iii)
For small angle
tan θ ≈ sin θ = x/2l ... (iv)
And Fe = q² / 4πε0x²


[Page 100]
194 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
From equation (iii) and (iv), we have
mg x x/2l = q^2 / 4πε0x^2
x^3 = lq^2 / 2πε0mg
or, x = [lq^2 / 2πε0mg]^1/3

10.4 The Electric Field
If we consider a point charge, then in the region of space around it a test charge experiences an electric force. The effect or force produced by the point charge on the unit positive test charge is called electric field. Any electric charge sets up an electric field in the space through which it interacts with other charges. It is assumed that it does not distort the field of point charge by the presence of test charge i.e. test charge should be very small in magnitude. Further, it is assumed as unit positive charge. Hence a unit test charge is brought in the field of another point charge; the force produced between them per unit test charge is called electric field or electric intensity (similar to gravitational field).
i.e E = F / q0 ... 10.9
The electric force is a vector quantity, so electric field is also a vector quantity. If q0 is positive, the electric field is in the same direction as the force and if q0 is negative, then the force and field are in opposite direction. Hence the direction of E is the direction of force when a positive test charge experience in the field. Figure 10.5 shows the direction of electric field.

Electric field of a Point Charge
The electric field exists at a point if a test charge at that point experiences an electric force. For a point charge q, the electric field E when a unit test charge q0 is brought at a distance r (figure 10.6) is given as,
E = F / q0 = q / 4πε0r^2 r̂ ... 10.10

Electric Force and Field 195
To find total electric field E for a group of N point charge, we can follow the principle superposition of electric field,
i.e. E = E1 + E2 + E3 + ... = Σ En (N = 1, 2, 3, ...)
= 1 / 4πε0 Σ qi / ri^2 r̂i ... 10.11
The sum is a vector sum, to turn over all charges.

Field of Continuous charge distribution
In case of group of charges, the distances between the charges are much smaller than the distance from the point of interest. In such case, the system of charge can be explained in terms of continuous charge distribution. In such situation, charge distribution is divided into small elements of small charge in terms of linear charge density (λ), surface charge density (σ) and volume charge density (ρ) according to the dimensions of the charged body. The electric field at any point P due to one charge element Δq is
ΔE = 1 / 4πε0 Δq / r^2 r̂ ... 10.12
For continuous charge distribution, we have,
dE = 1 / 4πε0 lim Δq -> 0 Δq / r^2 r̂
The total electric field at P due to all elements in the charge distribution is
E = 1 / 4πε0 ∫ dq / r^2 r̂ ... 10.13
The integration is over the entire charge distribution.

10.5 The Electric dipole
One type of configuration of two equal and opposite charges separated by a distance with no net charge is called an electric dipole (figure 10.7). For example NaCl in vapour state, water molecule etc. The product of charge and separation of the charges is called electric dipole moment (p)
i.e. p = 2 qd ... 10.14
The quantity behaves like a vector, directed from the negative charge to positive charge along the line joining the two charges. The net electric field due to dipole at a point has certain value. The electric field is calculated for different points from the dipole.
Electric field along the axial line of a dipole


[Page 101]
196
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
Let E+ and E- be the electric fields at a point P along the axial line of the dipole AB with separation 2d (figure 10.8). Using the principle of superposition, the resultant electric field at P at a distance r from centre of the dipole is in our case E = E+ + E-
E = E+ - E- = [q / 4πε0(r - d)^2] - [q / 4πε0(r + d)^2] = [q / 4πε0] [4rd / (r^2 - d^2)^2]
= [2pr / 4πε0(r^2 - d^2)^2]
∴ E = [2pr / 4πε0(r^2 - d^2)^2] ... 10.15
Where, p is electric dipole moment. For a short dipole r >> d, then we get,
E = p / 2πε0r^3 ... 10.16
Hence electric field drops 1/r^3 for all distant points rapidly. Moreover the electric field of the two equal and opposite charges at distance points almost but not quite cancel each other.

Electric field across the axial Line of a dipole
The point P is equidistance from each charge +q and -q is. From figure 10.9, we have,
AP = BP = sqrt(r^2 + d^2)
Where OP = r, the +q and -q charges set up electric fields E+ and E-, respectively,
so |E+| = |E-|
The total electric fled at P, the vector sum of
|E| = |E+| + |E-|
and the magnitude is given by the total resultant field along the dipole axis as
|E| = |E+| cos θ + |E-| cos θ
Where |E+| = [q / 4πε0(r^2 + d^2)] and cos θ = d / sqrt(r^2 + d^2)

Electric Force and Field
197
Hence we get the field across the dipole is
E = [2qd / 4πε0(r^2 + d^2)^(3/2)] = [p / 4πε0(r^2 + d^2)^(3/2)] ... 10.17
For short dipole r >> d, then we get,
E = p / 4πε0r^3 ... 10.18
The variation of electric field to 1/r^3 is a characteristic result for electric dipole.

A Dipole in an Electric Field
Let an electric dipole AB of separation 2d and magnitude of charge q be placed in an electric field of strength E. The dipole has a rigid structure due to internal electrostatics force. The dipole moment p makes an angle θ with E at equilibrium condition as shown in figure 10.10. At the charged ends of the dipole, electrostatics forces FA and FB act in opposite direction with the same magnitude qE. Thus the net force exerted on the dipole by the field is zero; however these forces exert a net torque τ on the dipole about its centre of mass, which we can take to be midway along the line connecting the charged ends. The total torque acted on the dipole is
τ = F x perpendicular distance = qE x d sin θ + qE x d sin θ
= 2 qdE sin θ

In terms of electric dipole moment p, we get the torque
τ = pE sin θ ... 10.19
Generalizing the equation to vector form as
τ = p x E ... 10.20
The torque is greatest when p and E are perpendicular to each other and is zero when they are parallel or anti-parallel. Figure 10.11 shows direction of τ, p and E.
When a dipole changes direction in a field, the electric torque does work on it; with a corresponding change in potential energy. The work done or potential energy is minimum
