201
The limit of integration in equation 10.27 depends on the geometry of the charged rod.
If Rod is Infinite long:
If the rod is infinitely long, the electric intensity from equation (10.27) is
E = (lambda / 4pi epsilon_0) * integral (cos theta dx / (x^2 + z^2))
From Figure 10.15 we have,
cos theta = z / sqrt(x^2 + z^2)
E = (lambda * z / 2pi epsilon_0) * integral_0^infinity (dx / (x^2 + z^2)^(3/2)) ... 10.28
Putting x = z tan theta and integrating we get
E = lambda / 2pi epsilon_0 * z ... 10.29
This relation is equivalent to the electric intensity of a charged cylinder.
If Rod is Finite long
If the charged rod is finite of length l then the relation 10.27 will be
E = (lambda * z / 2pi epsilon_0) * integral_0^(l/2) (dx / (x^2 + z^2)^(3/2))
Again putting x = z tan theta and dx = z sec^2 theta d theta, we get
E = (lambda * z / 2pi epsilon_0) * [ (l/2) / (z * sqrt((l/2)^2 + z^2)) ] = (q / 2pi epsilon_0 * z * sqrt(l^2 + 4z^2))
or,
E = q / (2pi epsilon_0 * z * sqrt(l^2 + 4z^2)) ... 10.30

E = E_A + E_B - E_0 = q / 4pi epsilon_0 (r + d)^2 + q / 4pi epsilon_0 (r - d)^2 - 2q / 4pi epsilon_0 r^2
= q / 4pi epsilon_0 [ 1/(r + d)^2 + 1/(r - d)^2 - 2/r^2 ]
Using equation 10.23 we get
E = Q / 4pi epsilon_0 * [ (3r^2 - d^2) / r^2(r^2 - d^2)^2 ] ... 10.24
This gives the magnitude of electric intensity of a quadrupole along its axis. For a short quadrupole r >> d, then
E = Q / 4pi epsilon_0 r^4 ... 10.25
Hence the electric intensity of a quadrupole varies inversely with fourth power of the distance from its centre.

10.7 Electric Field due to Line of Charge
Let us consider a long thin rod having uniformly distributed charges q with linear charge density lambda lies along x-axis (figure 10.15). To find the electric fled by the rod at a point located at the perpendicular bisector of the rod or at a distance z, the rod is divided into small elements each of length dx. Consider one of the element at a distance x from centre of the rod. It contains an element of charge given by,
dq = lambda dx
This produces an electric field at P at a distance r as
dE = dq / 4pi epsilon_0 r^2 = lambda dx / 4pi epsilon_0 (x^2 + z^2) ... 10.26
This electric field dE makes an angle theta with the vertical, so it can be resolved into two components, i.e. dE cos theta and dE sin theta. Taking similar charge elements on the other side of the rod we noticed that the electric field set up by this element also has magnitude dE. Resolving this we find that dE sin theta points opposite direction of previous one. For the whole contribution of the rod, the component dE sin theta is cancelled out. Thus the resultant electric field due to the charged rod is
E = integral dE cos theta = lambda / 4pi epsilon_0 * integral (cos theta dx / (x^2 + z^2)) ... 10.27


[Page 103]
202 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

This is the electric intensity at P due to a charged rod of length l. If the point P is very far, then the rod acts like a point charge and if the length l >> z, the above relation 10.30 reduces to equation 10.29.

Example 10.9
Calculate the electric field due to a uniformly charged rod of length l at a point along its long axis at a distance a from its nearest end.
Solution:
Let the rod be lying along x-axis and has uniform positive charge per unit length λ.
The rod be divided into elementary segment of length dx and the electric field intensity due to this segment which is at a distance x from far end of the rod is (figure 10.16)
dE = λdx / 4πε0(l+a-x)²
Total field at P due to all segments is
E = ∫dE
= ∫ [λdx / 4πε0(l+a-x)²] = λ / 4πε0 ∫ [dx / (l+a-x)²]
= λ / 4πε0 [1 / (l+a-x)] = λl / 4πε0a(l+a)
= q / 4πε0a(l+a)
Where, q is the total charge of the rod.

10.8 The electric Field of a Ring of Charge
The figure 10.17 shows a thin ring of radius a carrying a uniformly distributed positive charge q with linear charge density λ around its circumference. The ring is divided into elementary segments of length dl. The segment produces electric intensity at P. The electric intensity dE makes an angle θ with vertical, so it can be resolved into two components dE sin θ and dE cos θ. If we consider the contribution of the whole ring, then the dE sin θ components cancelled out and resultant field is
E = ∫dE cos θ
Where dE = dq / 4πε0r² = λdl / 4πε0(a² + z²) and cos θ = z / √(z² + a²)
Hence the resultant field is

Electric Force and Field 203
E = 2πλ / 4πε0(z²+a²) 3/2 ∫dl
E = qz / 4πε0(z²+a²) 3/2 ... 10.31
This is the electric field intensity at P due to positively charge ring. For negative charge, the only change is the direction of the field which is towards the ring. The electric field intensity will be maximum when,
z = ± 1/√2 a ... 10.32
In the expression 10.31 if z << a, then we get,
E = (q / 4πε0a³) z ... 10.33
Then if a charge -q' is placed near the centre of this charge ring, we get a force
F = -q'E = -(qq' / 4πε0a³) z ... 10.34
This force has a form of Hook's law (F = -kx) which is restoring force. So the motion of the charge -q' will be simple harmonic.

Example 10.10
A negatively charged particle -q' is placed at the centre of a uniformly charged ring, where the ring has a total positive charge q. The particle constrained to move along z-axis as in figure 10.17. It is displaced a small distance x along z-axis and released. Show that the motion of the particle will be simple harmonic with frequency f = 1/2π √(qq' / 4πε0a³)
Solution:
Using equation 10.34, we have,
m(d²z / dt²) + (qq' / 4πε0a³) z = 0
Comparing this equation with differential equation of simple harmonic motion or free-oscillation, we noticed that
d²z / dt² ∝ z
and d²z / dt² → -ve


[Page 104]
204 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
These are the characteristic of SHM. So motion of particle is simple harmonic and
frequency is given by
f = ω / 2π = 1 / 2π (qq' / 4πε0a^3)^1/2
This depends on charges and radius of the ring.

10.9 The Electric Field of Charged Disk
A disk of radius a has uniform surface charge density σ. To find the electric field at a point P along the centre perpendicular axis of the disk and distance z (figure 10.18) from the centre, we consider the disk as a set of concentric rings. We calculate electric field at P due to one ring. The vector sum of all those fields gives us the fields set up at P by the disk. Let the radius of the ring be x and width of the ring be dx. By symmetry, the field at an axial point must be along the central axis.

The ring of radius x and width dx has surface area 2πx dx. So the charge on the ring of width dx is
dq = 2 πσx dx
Now using the result of equation 10.31 we have for the ring
dE = qz(2πσx dx) / 4πε0(z^2 + x^2)^3/2
Figure 10.18 Electric field of charge disk

To obtain the total field at P, we integrate the above expression over the limit 0 to a, noting that z is constant
E = ∫ 2πσ z x dx / 4πε0(z^2 + x^2)^3/2
E = 2πσ / 4πε0 [1 - z / √(z^2 + a^2)]
Or, E = σ / 2ε0 [1 - z / √(z^2 + a^2)] ... 10.35
If we let a → ∞, which keeping z finite, equation 10.35 reduces to,
E = σ / 2ε0 ... 10.36

Electric Force and Field 205
This is the electric field produced by an infinite sheet of uniform charge located on one side of non conductor. Equation 10.35 is valid for all values of z > 0.

Example 10.11
At what distance along the central axis of a uniform charged plastic disk of radius a is the electric field strength equal to half the value of the field at the centre of the surface of the disk?

Solution:
We have from equation 10.35, the electric field along the centre axis of a uniform charged plastic disk of radius a at a distance z is
E = σ / 2ε0 [1 - z / √(z^2 + a^2)]
At the centre of the surface of the disk z = 0, so we have;
E0 = σ / 2ε0
From given condition E = 1/2 E0, so we have;
σ / 2ε0 [1 - z / √(z^2 + a^2)] = σ / 2ε0 * 1/2
or, z / √(z^2 + a^2) = 1/2
i.e. z = a / √3
Hence the condition is satisfied at the point from the disk which lies a / √3 from the centre of the dist

10.10 The Electric Field and Flux
The electric field as we explained by equations 10.10 and 10.11 at any point can also be represented by imaginary lines drawn around the charge which represent the strength of the electric field. These are called electric lines of force which are shown in figure 10.19. The lines are curves and tangent drawn at any point of the line represents the direction of the electric fled at that point. The lines are closer together near each charge where the electric field is stronger. We have a convention that the number of lines of force passing through a unit area, taken perpendicular to the direction of electric field is equal to the strength of the electric field.
(a) (b) (c) Dipole
Figure 10.19 Lines of forces

The number of lines of electric field is also called electric flux (φ). Lines of force originate or terminate in the space surrounding a charge. For convenience, we use lines of force start from positive charge and end to negative charge. Further no lines of force intersect to each other.


[Page 105]
206 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Electric flux is a property of any vector field; it refers to hypothetical surface in the field, which may be closed or open. For an electric field the flux is measured by the number of lines of force that cut through such surface. Suppose we have a small area dÃ which is not perpendicular to the lines but making an angle θ (figure 10.20), we have the flux of electric field is defined as

dΦ = (Electric field) . (Area)
= Normal Component of Ē . dÃ
= E cos θ dÃ
= Ē . dÃ ... 10.37

Thus the total flux passing through larger closed area is expressed by:
Φ = ∑ Ē . dÃ = ∮ Ē . dÃ ... 10.38

This surface integral indicates that the scalar quantity Ē ds can be evaluated by dividing surface into infinitesimal element of area dÃ. Within the dielectric medium the total number of lines of force is expressed in terms of electric displacement vector D.

i.e. Φ = ∮ D . dÃ ... 10.39

Where the electric displacement vector is defined as the free charges per unit area and is related by the relation
D = ε0 εr Ē
εr is relative permeability of dielectric medium.

10.11 Gauss' Law
The Gauss law states that: "the total electric flux of an electric field passing through any closed surface is directly proportional to the free charges enclosed by the surface"

So if q be the charge enclosed by the surface (called Gaussian surface), then electric flux of an electric field is
Φ ∝ q

or, Φ = q / ε0 ... 10.40

By substituting equation 10.38, the definition of flux, we can also write Gauss law as
ε0 ∮ Ē . dS = q ... 10.41

For non-uniform charge distribution, Gauss law can be written as
ε0 ∮ Ē . dS = ∫ ρ dv

10.12 Applications of Gauss law
Gauss law is valid for any distribution of charge and for any surface but in practice the system should have recognizable symmetrical properties that can be used to evaluate the flux integrals. The law is a two-way street; to calculate the field if we know the charge distribution with enough symmetry and / or to find the charge distribution if the field is known. Here we generally concentrated on the calculation of field due to conducting and non-conducting charged symmetry.

10.13 Field due to a Conducting Spherical Charge Distribution
Let a charge q be uniformly distributed over the surface of a conducting sphere of radius a (figure 10.21). For simplicity, we may suppose the sphere is surrounded by concentric layers of spherical shell or surface of radius r from its centre, called Gaussian surface.

For r > a, all the charges are on the surface of the sphere, so we can use the property of spherical symmetry. The direction of the electric field at any point outside the sphere is radial i.e. if we rotate the system, the field pattern of the rotated system must be identical to the original system. It concludes that the field cannot have component, so magnitude of E depends only on the distance r from the centre. The area of the Gaussian surface is 4 π r² and Ē . d ѕ is simply E ds, then Gauss law,

∮ Ē . d ѕ = q / ε0
becomes E (4 π r²) = q / ε0

∴ E = q / 4πε0 r² ... 10.42

Thus the uniformly charged shell or a charged conducting sphere behaves like a point charge for all points outside the shell.

For r < a, then no charge is enclosed by the Gaussian surface, the total flux passing through it must be zero, which leads to
E = 0 ... 10.43

Thus the electric field within a uniformly charged conducting spherical surface is always zero (see figure 10.22). It is because all excess charge placed on an isolated conductor or shell moves entirely to the outer surface of the conductor. None of the execs charge is


[Page 106]
208 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
found within the body of the conductor. If a conductor is charged, then the charges can be deposited anywhere with the interior of it. Initially there is an electric field in the interior of the conductor due to these charges, but very quickly, within 10^-9 seconds, due to electrostatic equilibrium electric field becomes zero and charges stop moving which are collected on the surface. If the field in the interior is not zero, the conduction electrons in the metal would experience a force and moving charges are observed with electric current, but in practice no such current has observed yet.

10.14 Field due to Non Conducting Spherically Symmetric Charge Distributions
Figure 10.23 shows a spherical distribution of charge of radius R. The charge density p (that is, the charge per unit volume) at any point depends only on the distance of the point from the centre and not on the direction, a condition called spherical symmetry. To find an expression for E for points (a) outside and (b) inside the charge distribution, we use Gauss law.

Figure 10.23 A Spherically symmetric charge distribution, showing two Gaussian surfaces.

(a) Electric field outside the sphere
Applying Gauss law to a spherical Gaussian surface of radius r (r>R) leads exactly to equation 10.42 i.e.
∮ E.ds = q / ε0
E 4πr² = q / ε0
E = 1 / 4πε0 * q / r² ... 10.44
Where, q is the total charge. Thus for points outside a spherically symmetric distribution of charge, the electric field has the value that it would have if the charge were concentrated as its centre.

(b) Electric field inside the sphere
Figure 10.23(b) shows a spherical Gaussian surface of radius r drawn inside the charge distribution. Gauss's law gives
ε0 ∮ E.ds = ε0 E (4πr²) = q'
E = 1 / 4πε0 * q' / r²
or,
in which q' is that part of q contained within the sphere of radius r. The part of q that lies outside this sphere makes no contribution to E at radius r.
An interesting special case of a spherically symmetric charge distribution is a uniform sphere of charge. For such a sphere, the charge density p would have a constant value for all points within a sphere of radius R and would be zero for all points outside this sphere. For points inside such a uniform sphere of charge we can put
q' = q * (4/3 πr³) / (4/3 πR³)
or,
q' = q (r/R)³
Where, 4/3 πR³ is the volume of the spherical charge distribution. The expression for E then becomes,
E = 1 / 4πε0 * qr / R³ ... 10.45

This equation becomes zero, as it should, for r = 0. Note that equation 10.45 and equation 10.44 gives the same result, as they must, for points on the surface of the charge distribution (that is, if r=R)
Figure 10.24 is a plot of E as a function of distance from the centre of the sphere, using equations 10.44 and 10.45 we see that E has its maximum value on the surface and decreases linearly to zero at the centre. Outside the sphere E decreases as the inverse square of the distance.

U = 91 [q1 + q2] / 4πε0 [r1 r2]
= 3x10^9 x 9x10^9 [2x10^-6 / 4 - 6x10^-6 / 5] = -1.89x10^2 J
Thus potential energy of the system being negative, positive work would have to be done by an external agent to remove the charge from point P back to infinity.

10.16 The Electric Potential
The amount of work done required to bring a unit test charge from infinity to a point is called electric potential. We may also define the potential V at any point in an electric field as the potential energy per unit test charge. If q0 be the test charge, then potential is given by
V = U / q0 = Wext / q0 ... 10.49
The unit of potential is J/C → Volt (V), using the relation 10.47 of the potential energy of a point charge, we get
V = q / 4πε0 r ... 10.50
This is the potential at any point at a distance r from q.
Suppose we have a collection of n point charges q1, q2, q3 ... qn located at various fixed points. The potential at an arbitrary point P due to the collection of charges is determined using superposition principle i.e.
V = V1 + V2 + V3 + ... + Vn
= 1 / 4πε0 [q1/r1 + q2/r2 + ... + qn/rn] = 1 / 4πε0 Σ (i=1 to n) (qi/ri) ... 10.51
Where qn is the value of nth charge and rn is the distance of nth charge from point P where we wish to find the potential.
In case of potential energy difference ΔU, for two finite points in the electric field of charge q we get potential difference ΔV, i.e.
ΔV = Vb - Va = ΔU / q0 = q / 4πε0 [1/rb - 1/ra] ... 10.52
Similar to equation 10.48 for the system of charges equation 10.52 can be written as
ΔV = q / 4πε0 Σ [1/ri - 1/ri] ... 10.53

Electric Force and Field 213
Here q is fixed charge in the field of which unit test charge is moved from positions J to
Example 10.15
What is the potential at a point P, located at the centre of the square of point charge as shown in figure 10.27 where a = 1.3 m and charges q1 = 12 nC, q2 = -24 nC, q3 = 31 nC and q4 = 17 nC?

V = Σ (i=1 to 4) Vi = 1 / 4πε0 [(q1 + q2 + q3 + q4) / r]
= 9x10^9 x (12x10^-9 - 24x10^-9 + 31x10^-9 + 17x10^-9) / 0.919
= 350 V

10.17 The Electric Potential and Electric Field
The field around a charged body can be described not only by electric field strength but also by the electric potential. As the temperature is deciding factor for the flow of heat from one body to another and the levels of liquid in inter-connected vessel is a deciding factors for the flow of liquid from one vessel to another, similarly electric potential is the decisive factor in determining the flow of electricity and not the quantity of electricity. So electric field strength are potential are interrelated.
In an electric field due to a charge at all points there will be field strength and if we place a unit positive charge at any point in the field it will experience a force along the direction of the electric field. If the unit charge is moved, its potential energy will be changed and certain amount of work is done. This amount of work done in moving the charge between any points is a measure of potential difference.
Let a unit positive charge q0 be brought from B to A through a differential displacement dr (figure 10.28). The differential work dw done on a particle by a force F = q0 E is
dw = F . dr = q0 E dr
From the definition of potential equation 10.49 we have
- q0 dV = q0 E dr
or, E = - dV / dr ... 10.54


[Page 108]
214 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

The quantity dV/dr is the rate at which potential rises with distance and is called potential gradient which is vector quantity. Equation 10.54 allows us to calculate the difference in potential between any two points in the field if the field is known through out a certain region. Because the electric force is conservative, all paths yield the same result.

In relation 10.54 for one dimensional calculation dr is parallel to x-axis, so component of E parallel to dr is just the x – components of E i.e. Ex. The component of E in any direction is the negative of the rate of change of the electric potential with distance in that direction. If we take the r – axis to be the x,y,z axes for three dimensions, then we have,

Ex = - ∂V/∂x, Ey = - ∂V/∂y, Ez = - ∂V/∂z

In terms of unit vectors we can have the electric field E as

E = i Ex + j Ey + k Ez
= - [i ∂/∂x + j ∂/∂y + k ∂/∂z] V = - ∇V ...10.55

In vector notion the quantity inside the bracket in above relation is called vector differential operator ∇ or "del". Thus in vector notion electric intensity is negative of potential gradient we note that the electric intensity is expressed in Volt/m and 1 V/m = 1 N/C.

Example 10.16
If the electric intensity of a charged disc of radius a at a point P at a distance x along its axis from centre (figure 10.29) is E = σ/2ε₀ [1 - x/√x²+a²]. Find the electric potential at P, where σ is surface charge density.

Solution:
From the relation between electric potential and electric field strength
E = - ∇ V
For one dimension we have
Ex = - ∂V/∂x = - dV/dx = E
∴ V = - ∫E.dx = - ∫ dx = - σ/2ε₀ ∫ [1 - x/√x²+a²] dx
= - σ/2ε₀ [∫dx + ∫ xdx/√x²+a²]
Using standard integration ∫ xdx/√x²+a² = √x²+a² we get
V = σ/2ε₀ [√x²+a² - x]

Electric Force and Field 215

The potential has maximum value at the surface of the disc (where x = 0) and decreases or we move along x – axis.

10.18 Potential due to an Electric Dipole
Let AB be an electric dipole with charge -q at B and +q at A with separation 2d and O is middle point of it. Electric potential at P is to be determined at a distance r from the centre of dipole O and ∠POA = θ and an arc with centre P and radius OP is drawn to meet PA and PB produced at N and M. But if the dipole is very short i.e. 2d << r, then MN is nearly a straight line perpendicular to both PB and PN. Hence we have,

MP = OP = NP = r
MB = NA and from ΔAON, we have
NA = OB sin (90° – θ) = d sin (90° – θ) = d cos θ

By definition the electric potential at P due to dipole is

V = 1/4πε₀ [q/PA - q/PB]
= q/4πε₀ [1/PN - AN - 1/PM + MB]
= q/4πε₀ [1/r - d cos θ - 1/r + d cos θ]
= q 2d cos θ / 4πε₀ (r² - d² cos² θ)

For r² >> d² we get

V = p cos θ / 4πε₀ r² ...10.56

Where, p is electric dipole moment equal to 2qd. The dipole moment is a vector whose direction is along BA from the negative to the positive side. Thus we have,

V = p.r/4πε₀r³ = p.r/4πε₀r² ...10.57

This is the potential at any point P of the dipole with angle θ made by r to the dipole axis. If θ = 90°, the expression 10.56 gives V = 0 i.e. at equatorial position V = 0 and if θ = 0°


[Page 109]
216 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

along axial line V = P / (4πε₀r²) which is maximum value. The electric field can also be determined using relation 10.56 with the help of equation 10.55.

Example 10.17
For a given short electric dipole, the electric potential any point at a distance r is given by:
V = (p cos θ) / r²
Where θ is the angle made by r to the dipole and p is its dipole moment. Using above relation, find an expression for resultant electric intensity at that point.

Solution.
To find the electric field at P, we chose coordinate system with the origin at P, x - axis along r, y - axis perpendicular to r and in the plane containing P = 2 qd and z - axis perpendicular to x and y - axes, such that ds = dr and dy = r dθ, so we get,
Er or Ex = - ∂V/∂x
= - ∂/∂r (p cosθ / r²)
= 2 p cosθ / r³
Eθ or Ey = - 1/r * ∂V/∂θ = - 1/r * ∂/∂θ (p cosθ / r²) = p sinθ / r³
and Ez = - ∂V/∂z = 0
It is because; the potential at P remains unchanged along z - axis, if the dipole is rotated through a small angle about AB axis. Now the resultant field will be
E = √(Er² + Eθ²) = (p/r³) √4cos²θ + sin²θ
E = (p/r³) √3cos²θ + 1
This field will make an angle φ with r called resultant direction which is
φ = tan⁻¹(Eθ/Er) = tan⁻¹(1/2 tanθ)
In this relation r, θ may be interpreted as the polar coordinates of P. For θ = 0 i.e. along the axis electric field will be
Er = 2p/r³
and for θ = 90°, i.e. along the equatorial line, we get
Eθ = p/r³
These relations give same values of fields as we already derived for a short dipole.

10.19 The Electric Potential due to linear Quadrupole
Let AB be a linear quadrupole of separation 2d (figure 10.32) and we have to determine the electric potential at P at a distance r from centre O of the quadrupole. PO is extended to R and BQ, RA are drawn perpendicular, so that ∠BOP = ∠AOR = 0,
OP = r, AP = r₁, PB = r₂,
RO = OQ = d cos θ
BQ = RA = d sin θ

Again we have,
r₁² = AR² + RP² = (d sin θ)² + (r + d cos θ)²
r₁ = √(d² + r² + 2dr cosθ)
Similarly
r₂ = √(d² + r² - 2dr cosθ)
Now the electric potential due to the quadrupole at P is
V = q/4πε₀ [1/r₂ + 1/r₁ - 2/r]
= q/4πε₀ [ 1/√(d² + r² + 2dr cosθ) + 1/√(d² + r² - 2dr cosθ) - 2/r]
= q/4πε₀ [ 1/r (1 + d²/r² + 2d/r cosθ)^(-1/2) + 1/r (1 + d²/r² - 2d/r cosθ)^(-1/2) - 2/r]

Using Binomial expression we get,
V = q/4πε₀r [ { 1 - 1/2 (d²/r² + 2d/r cosθ) + 3/8 (d²/r² + 2d/r cosθ)² + ... } + { 1 - 1/2 (d²/r² - 2d/r cosθ) + 3/8 (d²/r² - 2d/r cosθ)² + ... } - 2]
Neglecting higher terms we get,


[Page 110]
218 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

V = 1/4πε₀ [-d²/r² + 6/8(2d cosθ/r)²]
= q/4πε₀ [3 cos²θ - 1] d²/r² ...10.59

This is the electric potential at any point P at an angle θ at a distance r from the centre
of the quadrupole. For axial line, θ = 0, we have

V = 2qd²/4πε₀r³ = Q/4πε₀r³ ...10.60

Where Q = 2 qd² is the electric quadrupole moment of the charge assembly.

It is also noted that V varies as:
(i) 1/r for a point charge,
(ii) 1/r² for a dipole, and
(iii) 1/r³ for a quadrupole.

Similarly for Equatorial line, θ = 90°, we have,

V = Q/8πε₀r³ ...10.61

Which is half, that along axial line of the short quadrupole.

10.20 Electric Potential due to Continuous Charge Distribution

If the charge distribution is continuous, instead of consisting of separate point charges,
we must choose a typical differential element of charge dq, determine potential dV and then
integrate over the entire continuous charge distribution. Taking zero of potential to be at
infinity and dq as a point charge, then we can use equation 10.50 to express the potential dV
at point P due to dq.

dV = dq/4πε₀r

Here r is the distance between P and dq. Hence total potential V at P is given by

V = ∫dV = 1/4πε₀ ∫dq/r ...10.62

The integral is to be taken over the entire charge distribution.

Example 10.18
Calculate the potential of a uniform line of charge of length l at a distance d from its one end.

Solution:
A thin insulating rod of length l having positive charge of uniform linear density λ be considered, as shown in figure 10.33. Assuming zero potential at infinity, electric potential V is to be determining at P perpendicular distance d from one end of the rod. Let dz be the differential element of the rod having charge dq i.e.
dq = λ dz
The electric potential produced by this element at P is
dV = dq/4πε₀r = λdz/4πε₀(z² + d²)¹/²

The charge on the rod is positive and V = 0 at infinity, thus we have,
V = ∫dV = λ/4πε₀ ∫₀ˡ dz/(z² + d²)¹/²
= λ/4πε₀ ln [l + (l² + d²)¹/² - ln d]
= λ/4πε₀ ln [(l + √l² + d²)/d] ...10.63

Here V is sur of positive values of dV and should be positive.
The abov sults will slightly be modified if we take the point P, perpendicular distance from the centre of the rod. The result can be obtained as
V = λ/4πε₀ ∫₋ˡ/₂ˡ/₂ dz/(z² + d²)¹/²
= λ/4πε₀ ln [(l/2 + √(l/2)² + d²)/(-l/2 + √(l/2)² + d²)] ...10.64

Both result 10.63 and 10.64 give same and correct limiting value for
d >> l
i.e. V = q/4πε₀d
This is simply the expression for potential at a distance d from a point charge.

Example 10.19
Find an expression for electric potential due to ring of charge of radius 'a' at a distance y from the centre of the ring.

Solution:
Let a charged ring of radius a and linear charge density λ be considered (figure 10.34).
O is the centre and at P at a distance y from O, potential is to be determined. Because the ring consists of a continuous distribution of charge rather than a set of discrete charge, so an elementary charge dq is taken in an elementary section dx
i.e. dq = λ dx
Then the potential due to dq at a distance r is
dV = dq / 4πε0 r = λdx / 4πε0 sqrt(a^2 + y^2)
In this relation a, y, λ are constant for the system, thus integrating only for dx we get,
V = λ / 4πε0 sqrt(a^2 + y^2) integral dx
... 10.65
therefore V = q / 4πε0 sqrt(a^2 + y^2)

10. 21 Xerography
The electrostatics force that charged particles exerts on one another plays the central role in an office copier. The copying process is called Xerography, from Greek Xeros and graphos, meaning "dry writing." The heart of a copier is the xerographic drum, an aluminium cylinder coated with a layer of selenium (see figure 10.35) Aluminium is an excellent electric conductor. Selenium, on the other hand, is a photoconductor, it s an insulator in the dark but becomes a conductor when exposed to light. Consequently, a positive charge deposited on the selenium surface will remain there, provided the selenium is kept in the dark. When the drum is exposed to light, however, electrons from the aluminium pass through the conducting selenium and neutralize the positive charge.

Figure 10.35 (a) The cutaway view shows the essential elements of a copying machine
Figure 10.35 (b) The five steps in the xerography process.
The photoconductive property of selenium is critical to the xerographic process. First, an electrode called a corotron gives the entire selenium surface a positive charge in the dark. Second, a series of lenses and mirrors focuses an image of a document onto the revolving drum. The dark and light areas of the document produce corresponding areas on the drum. The dark areas retain their positive charge, but light areas become conducting and lose there positive charge, ending up neutralized. Thus, a positive charge image of the document remains on the selenium surface. In the third step, a special dry, black powder, called the tonner, is given a negative charge and then spread onto the drum, where it adheres selectively to the positively charged areas. The fourth step involves transferring the tonner onto a blank piece of paper. However, the attraction of the positive charge image holds the tonner to the drum. To transfer the toner, the paper is given a greater positive charge than that of the image, with the aid of another corotron. Lastly the paper and adhering toner pass through heated pressure rollers, which melt the toner into the fibres of the paper and produce the finished copy.

Figure 10.36 Laser printer drum

Laser Printer
A laser printer is used with computers to proved high quality copies of text and graphics. It is similar in operation to the xerographic machine, except the information to be reproduced is not on paper. Instead, the information is transfer from the computer's memory to the printer, and laser light is used to copy it onto the selenium – aluminium drum. A laser beam, focused to a fine point, is scanned rapidly from side to side across the rotating drum. While the light remains on, the positive charge on the drum is neutralized. As the laser beam moves, the computer turns the beam of at the right moments during each scan to produce the desired positive charge image, which is the letter "in figure 10.36".

10.22 Inkjet printer
And inkje printer is another type of printer at uses electric charges in its operation. While shuttling back and forth across the paper, the inkjet print head ejects a thin stream of ink. Figure 10.3 illustrates the elements of one type of print head. The ink is forced out of a small nozzle and beaks up into extremely small droplets; with diameters less than 1x10^-4 m. About 150000 droplets leave the nozzle each second and travel with a speed of approximately 18 m/s toward the paper. During their flight, the droplets pass through two electrical components, an electrode and the deflection plates (a parallel plate capacitor). When the print head moves over regions of the paper that are not to be inked, the charging control is turned on and an electric field is established between the print head and the electrode. As the drops pass through the electric field, they acquire a net charge by the process of induction. The deflection plates divert the charged droplets into a gutter and thus prevent them from reaching the paper. Whenever ink is to be placed on the paper, the charging control, responding to instructions from the computer, turns off the electric field. The on charged droplets fly straight through the deflection plates and strike the paper.

10.23 Electrostatic Precipitation
Electrostatic precipitators removes solids and/or liquid particles from effluent gases by imparting a charge to entrained particles which then are attracted to positively
Figure 10.37 Discharge and collection electrodes of an electrostatic precipitator


[Page 112]
222 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

charged collection plates. Specifically, a high DC voltage is applied to a dual-electrode system which produces a corona between the discharge electrode and the collection plates or electrodes.(figure 10.37).The corona produces negatively charged ions. Particles entrained in the gas stream become negatively charged. These negatively charged particles migrate towards the positively charged plates where they are deposited and held by electrical, mechanical, and molecular forces. Solid particles are usually removed by periodic rapping of collecting electrodes. Collected dust falls into a hopper, from which is manually removed. A typical plate-type electrostatic precipitator is illustrated in figure.

The electrostatic precipitator was first developed in 1907 to collect mist from sulfuric acid plants. Historical applications have been the collection of metal oxides and dust from a variety of metallurgical operations, including ferrous and nonferrous processes. It is commonly used in steelmaking to collect particles from blast, basic O₂ and open-hearth furnaces, sintering plants, and coke ovens. Presently, the largest single application of electrostatic precipitation is the collection of flyash from coal-fired electric power plants.

Collection efficiency is primarily influenced by particles retention time in the electrical field and particle resistivity. Retention time is determined by gas path length (distance gas travels across the electrical fields) and gas velocity. Highest collection efficiencies are achieved in precipitators employing long gas path lengths and relatively low gas velocity.

The resistivity of particles to accepting an electrical charge is one of the most important factors affecting collection efficiency. When dust resistivity is high as in the case of flyash from low-sulfur western coals, the charge is not neutralized at the collection plates; as a result, an electrical potential builds up on collected dusts. As the potential increases, the incoming particles do not receive a maximum charge, and the collection efficiency decreased. If this condition continues, a corona discharge may occur at the collection plate, causing the collector to malfunction. Paradoxically, low-sulfur coals, which are widely used to comply with SiO₂ emission standards in many coal-fired utility boilers, produce flyash of high resistivity. High sulfur coals produce flash with low resistivity, and thus collection efficiency is high.

Resistivity is a direct function of coal sulfur content with low-sulfur coals producing flash with highest resistivity. Control methods employed for high-resistivity particles/dusts require a high level of technical sophistication and, of course, cost. As a result of the resistivity problem, fabric filtration is used as an alternative to the electrostatic precipitators for utility boilers using low-sulfur coal.

On the other end of the spectrum are dusts with low resistivity, such as carbon blocks. In such cases collection efficiency is markedly decreased because carbon particles are highly conductive and lose charges too readily. Because the collected particle loses its negative charge, it may reenter the waste stream, where it becomes negatively charged and is again attracted to the collection plate. The process is repeated until particles reach the precipitators outlet where they are discharged to the atmosphere. Because of this phenomenon, low resistivity also results in excessive dust re-entrainment. Electrostatic precipitation is a widely used particle/dust collection method because it has high collection efficiency for all particle sizes, including sub micrometer particles. Collection efficiencies in excess of 99% can achieved even on very large gas flows.

Electric Force and Field 223

Electrostatic precipitators have low operating and power requirements because energy is only necessary to act on particles collected and not the entire waste stream. Consequently, energy costs are low compared to other particle/dust collection systems.

Although electrostatic precipitation has advantages that make it well suited to many particle/dust collection applications, it also has the disadvantages of high capital costs and space requirements.


[Page 113]
224 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 225 Electric Force and Field

10.24 Additional Examples:

1. A charged nonconducting rod, with a length of 2.00 m and a cross-sectional area of 4.00 cm², lies along the positive side of an x axis with one end at the origin. The volume charge density p is charge per unit volume in coulombs per cubic meter. How many excess electrons are on the rod if p is (a) uniform, with a value of -4.00 µC/m³, and (b) nonuniform, with a value given by p = bx², where b = -2.00 µC/m⁵?

Solution:
The charge dq within a thin section of the rod (of thickness dx) is p A dx where A = 4.00 x 10⁻⁴ m² and p is the charge per unit volume. The number of (excess) electrons in the rod (of length L = 2.00 m) is n = q/-e.

(a) In the case where p = -4.00 x 10⁻⁶ C/m³, we have
n = q/-e = pA / -e [integral of dx from 0 to L] = |pAL| / e = 2.00 x 10¹³

(b) With p = bx² (b = -2.00 x 10⁻⁶ C/m⁵) we obtain
n = [integral of bAx² dx from 0 to L] / -e = |bAL³/3| / e = 1.33 x 10¹³

2. Two small, positively charged spheres have a combined charge of 5.0 x 10⁻⁵ C. If each sphere is repelled from the other by an electrostatic force of 1.0 N when the spheres are 2.0 m apart, what is the charge on the sphere with the smaller charge?

Solution:
Let the two charges be q₁ and q₂. Then q₁ + q₂ = Q = 5.0 x 10⁻⁵ C. Using Coulomb's law:
1.0 = (8.99 x 10⁹) q₁q₂ / (2.0)²
Substitute q₂ = Q - q₁ and simplifying he have
q₁² - Qq₁ + 4.45 x 10⁻¹⁰ = 0
Solving this quadratic equation we get
q₁ = 1.2 x 10⁻⁵ C and q₂ = 3.8 x 10⁻⁵ C.
Thus, the charge on the sphere with the smaller charge is 1.2 x 10⁻⁵ C.

3. Figure AE 3 (a) shows a plastic rod having a uniformly distributed charge -Q. The rod has been bent in a 60° circular arc of radius r. In terms of Q and r, what is the electric field E due to the rod at the centre of curvature of the rod?

Solution:
We place coordinate axes such that the axis of symmetry of the rod lies along the x - axis and the origin is at the centre of curvature P of the rod [see figure AE 3 (a)].
Consider a differential element having arc length ds and located an angle θ above the axis.
Electric field dE of this element at P is given by
dE = λds / 4πε₀r² ... I
Where λ represent the linear charge density of the rod. The direction of dE is toward ds, because charge element is negative.

The element ds has a symmetrically located (mirror image) element ds' in the bottom half of the rod. The electric field dE' set up at P by ds' also has the magnitude equal to dE but field vector points towards ds'.
If we resolve the electric field vectors of ds and ds' into x and y components as shown in figure AE 3 (b), we see that their y components cancel.
From figure AE 3 (b) we can write the component dEx set up by ds as
dEx = dE cos θ
= 1 / 4πε₀r² (λ cos θ ds)
But from figure AE 3 (c) we have
ds = r dθ
dEx = λr / 4πε₀r² cos θ dθ = λ / 4πε₀r cos θ dθ
Thus,
Total electric field at P is given by
E = [integral of dEx] = λ / 4πε₀r [integral of cos θ dθ from -30° to 30°]
= λ / 4πε₀r [sin θ] from -30° to 30°
According to question θ = 30°, so
E = λ / 4πε₀r [sin 30° - sin(-30°)]
E = λ / 4πε₀r ... II
To evaluate λ, we note that the rod has an angle of 60° and so is one - sixth of a full circle. Its arc length is then 2πr / 6, and its linear charge density must be
λ = charge / length = Q / 2πr/6 = 0.954 Q / r
Substituting this into equation II and simplifying gives us
E = 0.954 Q / 4πε₀r²

4. An electric field E with an average magnitude of about 150 NC points downward in the atmosphere near Earth's surface. We wish to 'float' a sulfur sphere weighing 4.4 N in this field by charging the sphere. (a) What charge (both sign and magnitude) must be used? (b) Why is the experiment impractical?

Solution:
(a) Since E points down and we need an upward electric force (to cancel the downward pull of gravity), then we require the charge of the sphere to be negative. The magnitude of the charge is found by working with the absolute value of F = qE:
|q| = F / E = 4.4 N / 150 N/C = 0.029 C,
or, q = -0.029 C.


[Page 114]
226
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
(b) The feasibility of this experiment may be studied by using electric field due to a point charge (using k for 1/4πε0). We have E= kq/r^2 with
ρ_sulfur (4/3 π r^3) = m_sphere
Since the mass of the sphere is 4.4/9.8 = 0.45 kg and the density of sulfur is about 2.1 x 10^3 kg/m³, then we obtain
r = (3m_sphere/4πρ_sulfur)^(1/3) = 0.037 m
∴ Electric field intensity E = 9 x 10^9 x (q/r^2) = (9x10^9 x 0.029) / (0.037)^2 = 2 x 10^11 N/C
which is much too large a field to maintain in air.

5. Two long, charged, thin-walled, concentric cylindrical shells have radii of 3.0 and 6.0 cm. The charge per unit length is 5.0 x 10^-6 C/m on the inner shell and -7.0 x 10^-6 C/m on the outer shell. What are the (a) magnitude and (b) direction (radially inward or outward) of the electric field at radial distance r = 4.0 cm? What are (c) magnitude and (d) the direction at r = 8.0 cm?
Figure AE 5

Solution:
We denote the inner and outer cylinders with subscripts i and o, respectively.
(a) Since ri < r = 4.0 cm < ro, using Gauss law, electric field intensity is given by
E(r) = λ_i / 2πε0r = (5.0x10^-6 C/m) / (2π(8.85x10^-12 C²/N·m²)4.0x10^-2m) = 2.3 x 10^6 N/C.
(b) The electric field E(r) points radially outward.
(c) Since r > ro, using Gauss law we have
E(r = 8.0 cm) = (λ_i + λ_o) / 2πε0r = (5.0x10^-6 C/m - 7.0x10^-6 C/m) / (2π(8.85x10^-12 C²/N·m²)8.0x10^-2m) = - 4.5 x 10^5 N/C,
or, |E(r=8.0cm)| = 4.5 x 10^5 N/C.
(d) The minus sign indicates that E(r) points radially inward.

6. Charge of uniform volume density ρ = 3.2 µC/m³ fills a nonconducting solid sphere of radius 5.0 cm. What is the magnitude of the electric field (a) 3.5 cm and (b) 8.0 cm from the sphere’s centre?
Solution:
Since the charge distribution is uniform, we can find the total cahrge q by multiplying ρ by the spherical volume (4/3 πr³) with r = R = 0.050 m. This gives q = 1.68 nC.
(a) Applying equation 10.45 with r = 0.035 m, we have electric field inside the sphere is
E_inside = |kq/R³| r = (9x10^9)(1.68x10^-9)(0.035) / (0.050)³ = 4.23 x 10³ N/C.
(b) Applying equation 10.44 with r = 0.080 m, we have electric field outside the sphere is
E_outside = (9x10^9)(1.68x10^-9) / (0.080)² = 2.4 x 10³ N/C.

Electric Force and Field
227
7. An infinite nonconducting sheet has a surface charge density σ = 5.80 pC/m². (a) How much work is done by the electric field due to the sheet if a particle of charge q = 1.60 x 10^-19 C is moved from the sheet to a point P at distance d = 3.56 cm from the sheet? (b) If the electric potential V is defined to be zero on the sheet, what is V at P?
Solution:
(a) The work done by the electric field is
W = ∫q E · d(z)
Here E = σ/2ε0 and d = 0.0356 m, we have
W = (qσd)/2ε0 = (1.60x10^-19 C)(5.80x10^-12 C/m²)(0.0356m) / 2(8.85x10^-12 C²/N·m²)
= 1.87 x 10^-21 J.
(b) Since V - V0 = - W/q0 = - σd/2ε0, with V0 set to be zero on the sheet, the electric potential at P is
V = - σd/2ε0 = - (5.80x10^-12 C/m²)(0.0356m) / 2(8.85x10^-12 C²/N·m²)
= - 1.17 x 10^-2 V.

8. A spherical drop of water carrying a charge of 30 pC has a potential of 500 V at its surface (with V = 0 at infinity). (a) What is the radius of the drop? (b) If two such drops of the same charge and radius combine to form a single spherical drop, what is the potential at the surface of the new drop?
Solution:
(a) The electric potential V at the surface of the drop, the charge q on the drop, and the radius R of the drop are related by V = q/4πε0R. Thus
R = q/4πε0V = (8.99x10^9 N·m²/C²)(30x10^-12 C) / 500V
= 5.4 x 10^-4 m.
(b) After the drops combine the total volume is twice the volume of an original drop, so the radius R' of the cobmined drop is given by (R')³ = 2 R³ and R' = 2^1/3 R. The charge is twice the charge of original drop: q' = 2q. Thus,
V' = 1/4πε0 (q'/R') = 1/4πε0 (2q / 2^1/3 R) = 2^2/3 V = 2^2/3 (500 V)
≈ 790 V.

9. What is the magnitude of the electric field at the point (3.00 i - 2.00 j + 4.00 k) m if the electric potential is given by V = 2xyz², where V is in volts and x, y, and z are in meters?
Solution:
We have
Ex = - ∂V/∂x = - 2.00 yz²


[Page 115]
228 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Ey = - ∂V/∂y = - 2.00 xz
Ez = - ∂V/∂z = - 4.00 xyz
which, at (x, y, z) = (3.00 m, - 2.00 m, 4.00 m), gives
(Ex, Ey, Ez) = (64.0 V/m, - 96.0 V/m, 96.0 V/m).
The magnitude of the field is therefore
|E| = √Ex² + Ey² + Ez² = 150 V/m = 150 N/C.

Chapter

Capacitors

Capacitors have a tremendous number of practical applications in devices such as electronic flash units for photography, pulsed lasers, air bag sensors for cars, radio and television receivers. Tube light has starter which is one type of capacitor, water pump, energy storage devices, AC filtering devices etc also consists varieties of capacitors. We'll encounter many of these applications in later chapters. In this chapter, however, our emphasis is on the fundamental properties of capacitors.

11.1 Capacitance and capacitance

A capacitor is a device that stores electric potential energy and electric charge. Any two conducting plates separated by an insulator or vacuum form a capacitor. When charges are supplied to the capacitor, one plate acquires positive charge and other has an equal amount of negative charge. This process is called charging the capacitor. In this case the net charge as a whole remains zero. But work must be done to move the charges through the resulting potential difference between the conductors and the net work stored as electric potential energy. A capacitor having charge q means that plate at higher potential has + q charge and the conductor at lower potential has - q charge.

If a capacitor is connected to a battery, then the battery serves as a pump for the electric charge i.e. charges move from positive plate of the capacitor to the negative plate and maintains charge q at a plate after certain time. The charge appeared on the capacitor plates is always directly proportional to the potential difference between the plates due to the battery.
Hence
q ∝ V
or,
q = C V
... 11.1

The constant of the geometry of the capacitor is called capacitance; which is defined as the ratio of the magnitude of the charge on either plate to the magnitude of the potential difference between the conductors. It is also defined as the measure of the ability or capacity to store charge. The SI unit of capacitance is coulomb / volt or Farad (F) named in honors of Michael Faraday.


[Page 118]
234 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

.. 1 F = 1 C/V

In practice, typical devices have capacitance ranging from 1 µ F to 1 p F. We will see the value of capacitance depends mainly on shape and size of the conductor and the nature of insulating material between them. The insulating material is also called dielectric material. A capacitor consisting a pair of parallel plates as shown in figure 11.1 is represented either by the symbols shown in lower part of the figure.

Figure 11.1 Parallel plate capacitor

11.2 Types of Capacitors

Large numbers of capacitors are available in practice. Practical capacitors have a variety of forms but basically they are all forms of parallel plate capacitors. A capacitor in which the effective area of the plates and separation between the plates can be adjusted is called variable capacitor. The variable capacitor is used in radio receivers for tuning to the different wavelengths of stations. Multiple capacitors consists different dielectrics, such as air, mica, paper, glass, polystyrene etc. Electrolytic capacitors are widely used in electronic circuits. The capacitance of such capacitor is measured mainly by the separation of the plates and nature of electrolytic materials used. Here we mainly concentrate on the calculation of capacitance of basic types of capacitors.

Calculating the capacitance: General method

General methods to calculate capacitance of a capacitor of different geometry are as follows:
a) Assume a charge q on the plates
b) Calculate the electric field E between the plates in terms of this charge, using Gauss' law i.e.
∫ E.dA = q / ε0
Here q is the charge enclosed by a Gaussian surface. Gaussian surface will be such that it will enclosed only one plates.
c) Calculate the potential difference V between the plates, by using equation
V = ∫ E.ds
Here V represents the difference Vf - Vi and we shall always choose a path that follows as electric field line from the negative to the positive plate. For this path, the vector E and ds will have opposite directions, so the dot product E . ds will be equal to - E ds.
d) Calculate capacitance C from equation
C = q / V

11.3 Parallel Plate Capacitor

The arrangement of two similar metal plates at a certain separation to store charge is called parallel plate capacitor. It is the simplest form of capacitor with two conducting plates each with area A, separated by a distance d (figure 11.2) which is very small in comparison with their dimensions. When completely localized in the region between the plates, we can

Capacitors 235

neglect the fringing of the electric field that occurs near the edges of the plates and assume that the electric field has same magnitude and direction everywhere. If the capacitor plates are very large than the net electric field due to two plates according to Gauss law is,

E = σ / ε0 = q / ε0 A

Figure 11.2 A Parallel plate capacitor with plate area A

Where σ is surface charge density = q/A. The direction of the uniform electric field is shown in figure 11.3. So the corresponding electric field is,

V = ∫ E.dr = ε0 ∫ dr = qd / ε0 A
0 0

Capacitance of the capacitor is

C = q/V = ε0 A / d ... 11.2

Figure 11.3 A parallel plate capacitor

So the capacitance does indeed depend only on geometrical factors i.e. area of the plate A and the plate separation. The capacitance of equation 11.2 is for air or vacuum. In this case C depends only on the shapes, dimensions and separation of the conductors that makes up the capacitor. If the conductor shapes are more complex than those of parallel plate capacitor, the expression will be more complicated.

Example: 11.1
If a parallel plate capacitor has capacitance 1 F and separation of the plates is 1 mm, what will be the area of the plates?

Solution:
We have the area of the plate is
A = Cd / ε0 = 1x1x10^-3 / 8.85x10^-12 = 1.1x10^8 m^2.
This area is very large and not very practical design for a capacitor. If the plate size is square, then to achieve 1 F, the side of the square should be 10 km.

11.4 A Spherical Capacitor

A charged sphere is used as an isolated capacitor. If r be the radius of the sphere and is charged by q then the potential with respect to infinity is,

V = q / 4πε0r

Then its capacitance is,

Figure 11.4 A spherical capacitor. The dashed circle is a Gaussian sphere of radius r


[Page 119]
236 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

C = q/V = 4πε₀r ... 11.3

In this case the charged sphere itself acts as a positive plate and the earth is used as a negative plate.

The spherical capacitor in practice consists of two metallic concentric spheres A and B of radii a and b respectively and insulated from each other. The inner conductor A is solid sphere and outer B is hollow spherical shell of radius b. As a Gaussian surface, we draw a sphere of radius r concentric with the two shells. Figure 11.4 shows spherical capacitor.

In the region a < r < b, using Gauss law, we get the electric field,

E = q / 4πε₀r²

Using this expression for the determination of potential

V = ∫E·dr = ∫E·dr = q/4πε₀ ∫dr/r² = q/4πε₀ [b-a / ab]

Hence the capacitance of the spherical capacitor is

C = 4πε₀ ab / b-a ... 11.4

It is noted that only geometrical factors appear in the relation and that the capacitance has the form of 4πε₀ times a quantity with the dimension of length.

Special cases: If b -> ∞, in equation 11.4 then, C = 4πε₀a.

Example: 11.2
Prove that the capacitance of a concentric spherical capacitor of radii a and b is C = 4πε₀ [b² / b-a]. If outer plate is charged positively and inner sphere is earthed.

Solution:
Let +q charge be given to the outer sphere B. Some of these charges will remain on the outer surface and the remaining charge will be distributed over the inner surface. Thus the total charge q = q₁ + q₂
Due to charge q₂ on the inner surface A induces a charge -q₂ on the outer surface of A and +q₂ on the inner surface of the inner sphere which goes to earth being free.
So two capacitors C₁ and C₂ are formed connected in parallel.
C₁ = isolated capacitor of radius b formed by the earth and outer surface of sphere B = 4πε₀b.
and C₂ = capacitor consisting of inner surface of B and outer surface of A = 4πε₀ ab / b-a

Capacitors 237

∴ Total capacitance due in parallel combination is C = C₁ + C₂ = 4πε₀ [b²/b-a]
This value is greater than the value when outer sphere is earthed.

11.5 A Cylindrical Capacitor
The Figure 11.6 represents a section of a cylindrical capacitor of length l, inner radius a and outer radius b. The inner cylinder is a solid rod carrying a charge -q uniformly distributed over its surface and outer conductor is a co-axial cylindrical shell carrying charge +q which is also uniformly distributed. If we assume l >> b, we can neglect the fringing effect. As a Gaussian surface, we choose a cylinder of length l and radius r, closed by the end caps, and placed as shown in figure 11.6. From Gauss' law i.e.

∮E·dA = q/ε₀

yields E 2πrl = q/ε₀

or, E = q / 2πε₀lr

Hence the potential difference between a and b is,

V = Vab = + ∫E·dr = q/2πε₀l ∫dr/r = q/2πε₀l ln(b/a)

Now from definition of the capacitance, we get,

C = q/V = 2πε₀l / ln(b/a) ... 11.5

Hence the capacitance of the cylinder is proportional to the length of the cylinder. It also depends on the radii of the two cylinders.

Example: 11.3
A parallel plate capacitor is formed by two long parallel and oppositely charged wires of the length l and radius r with thin centers separated by a distance d (figure 11.7). Assuming the charge is uniformly distributed on the surface of wire, derive the expression of the capacitance of the capacitor.

Solution:
P and Q are two parallel wires of cylindrical shape having length l and separation d. If the radius of the cylindrical


[Page 120]
238 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
wire is r then the electric field is,
E = E+ + E- = 2 x q / 2πε0lr = q / πε0lr
The potential difference between the plates is,
V = ∫ d-r r E.dr = ∫ d-r r q / πε0l r dr
V = q / πε0l ln [d-r / r]
Hence the capacitance of the arrangement is
C = πε0l / ln(d-r/r)
This also depends mainly on its dimension.

11.6 Combinations of Capacitors
In the combination of capacitors in a circuit, a single value of capacitance is obtained which is called equivalent capacitor, with such a capacitor; we can simplify the circuit to solve for unknown quantities of the circuit more easily. Basically two types of combinations are used in practice.

11.7 Parallel Combination
The connection of two or more than two capacitor at a common point is said to be parallel combination, such that (i) potential difference of each capacitor is same and (ii) total charge of the system is the sum of charges of each capacitor. Figure 11.8 shows parallel combination of three capacitors.
In this combination all similar terminals of the capacitors are connected at a common point. The equivalent capacitance C will be the sum of individual capacitance. So that it is always greater than each capacitance. So
C = C1 + C2 + C3 + ... = Σ Cn ... 11.6
This combination thus stores more charges than any one of the capacitor.

11.8 Series Combination
The connection of two or more than two capacitors end to end is said to be series combination, so that (i) the charges on each capacitor is same and (ii) total potential difference is the sum of the potential differences across the individual capacitors. Figure 11.9 shows series combination of three capacitors.
In this combination positive plate of a capacitor is connected to negative plate of another capacitor i.e. connected end to end as shown in figure 11.9.
For capacitance C1, C2, C3 etc, the equivalent capacitance will be,

Capacitors 239
1/C = 1/C1 + 1/C2 + 1/C3 + ... = Σ 1/Cn ... 11.7
Hence the inverse of the equivalent capacitance is the algebraic sum of the inverse of the individual capacitances and C is always less than any individual capacitance in the series combination.

11.9 Energy Stored in an Electric Field
When a capacitor is connected to a cell or battery, charges accumulate on the capacitor plates and large amount of work is required to add more charges on the plates. The amount of work done required to transfer electrons from one plate of the capacitor into another plate one at a time is equivalent to energy stored by a capacitor.
Let us visualize the work required to charge a capacitor as stored in the form of electric potential energy in the electric field between the plates. This energy similar to electric energy is covered in the discharging the capacitor in the circuit. Suppose at any instant a charge dq be transferred from one plate to another due to applied potential difference V, then the amount of work done required is,
dW = V dq
The total work done to store charge q is,
W = ∫0 q V dq = ∫0 q q/C dq
W = 1/2 q^2 / C ... 11.8
Using q = C V, we have different forms.
W = 1/2 qV = 1/2 CV^2 = U
Where U is potential energy stored.
These relations hold no matter what the geometry of the capacitor is. The relation also represents the electric potential energy stored in the electric field between the plates.
In parallel plate capacitor, the electric field has nearly same value for all points between the plates. Thus the energy density i.e. the potential energy per unit volume between the plates should be uniform. Hence we have,
u = U / Ad = CV^2 / 2Ad
Using C = ε0 A / d we get


[Page 121]
240 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
u = 1/2 ε₀ E² ... 11.9
Although, we used for the special case of parallel plate to find the energy density, it holds good for every type of capacitor, what ever may be the source of electric field.
It is important to note that the energy in the capacitor is gained from battery. The battery supplies qV amount of energy during charging process. Half of the energy i.e. 1/2 qV goes to the capacitor and the other half is transferred to heat in the circuit resistance.
Example: 11.4
Two capacitors are charged separately by potential difference V₁ and V₂. If the charged capacitors are removed from the battery and are connected in parallel combination, prove that there is loss of energy due to combination (or after contact).
Solution:
Let the energy before the parallel combination (or before contact)
Wb = 1/2 (C₁ V₁² + C₂ V₂²)
Here V₁ and V₂ are charging potentials to the capacitors C₁ and C₂ by the battery before parallel combination.
After combination, a common potential difference is set up, which is,
V = q/C = (q₁ + q₂)/(C₁ + C₂) = (C₁ V₁ + C₂ V₂)/(C₁ + C₂)
Wa = 1/2 (C₁ + C₂) V²
= 1/2 (C₁ + C₂) [(C₁ V₁ + C₂ V₂)/(C₁ + C₂)]² = (C₁ V₁ + C₂ V₂)² / [2(C₁ + C₂)]
Hence the difference of energy between Wb and Wa is
Wb - Wa = 1/2 C₁ V₁² + 1/2 C₂ V₂² - (C₁ V₁ + C₂ V₂)² / [2(C₁ + C₂)]
ΔW = 1/2 [C₁ C₂ / (C₁ + C₂)] [V₁ - V₂]²
This relation shows that Wb always greater than Wa because ΔW is always positive. So there is always loss of energy due to contact.

11.10 Charging and Discharging a Capacitor
Let a capacitor having capacitance C be connected in an electric circuit with a resistance R, ammeter A, battery B with emf E and two way key (X, Y). Figure 11.10 show RC circuits for charging and discharging the capacitor. A capacitor is charged when a battery is connected to it and stores charges. Large values of C and R in the circuit help to slow the current flow. In such case electrons from the negative terminal of the battery to plate of the capacitor connecting to it and at the same rate, electrons flow from other plate of the capacitor towards the positive terminal of the battery. Then positive and negative charges appear on the plates and oppose the flow electrons. As the charges accumulate, the potential difference between the plates increases and the charging current falls to zero, when the potential difference reaches saturated value.

Capacitors 241
When the battery is disconnected and the plates are joined together by a wire, electrons flow back from one plate to another until the positive charge is completely neutralized.
In case if alternating voltage (AC) is applied, due to alternating nature continuous charging and discharging occurs then current flows in the circuit. The charging and discharging process occur rapidly with the frequency of AC. The flow of current through the capacitor is thus,
I = fq
Where f is frequency of AC and q is charge on the capacitor each time it is charged.

11.11 Charging a capacitor
The capacitor C is initially uncharged. When switch X is on, it starts charging because loop is complete. Applying Kirchhoff's loop rule to the circuit after switch is closed, we get
E = Vc + VR = q/C + iR
= q/C + dq/dt R
or, R dq/dt + q/C = E
or, dq / (EC - q) = dt / RC
Integrating both sides, we obtain.
∫ dq / (EC - q) = 1/RC ∫ dt
or, q = EC (1 - e^(-t/RC)) = q₀ (1 - e^(-t/RC)) ... 11.10
Where q₀ = EC = maximum charges stored in the capacitor.
Differentiating 11.10 with respect to time, we find the current during charging is
i = dq/dt = i₀ e^(-t/RC) ... 11.11
The potential across the capacitor also varies exponentially similar to q and is given by,


[Page 122]
242
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Vc = q/C = E(1 - e^-t/RC)

In the same way, the potential difference across the resistor is,

VR = i R = E e^-t/RC

The figure 11.11 shows the nature of plot of q, i, Vc and VR with respect to time.

The product RC which appears in above equations has dimensions of time, called capacitive time constant (τc) of the circuit. It represents the time interval during which the current decreases to 1/e of its initial values i.e.

I = e^-1 Io = 0.37 i0 and q = q0 (1 - e^-1) = 0.63 q0.

11.12 Discharging a Capacitor

When the capacitor is fully charged and switch Y is on discharging occurs in the capacitor through the resistor. At same time t during discharging, the current in the circuit is I and charge is q. Hence using Kirchhoff's rule we get,

0 = VR + VC = I R + q/C

or, R dq/dt + q/C = 0

or, dq/q = - 1/RC dt

∴ q = q0 e^-t/RC ... 11.12

Differentiate this expression with respect to time we get instantaneous current as a function of time i.e.

I = dq/dt = - Io e^-t/RC ... 11.13

The negative sign indicates that as the capacitor discharges, the current is opposite to that of charging. Equation 11.12 tells us that q decreases exponentially with time, at a rate that is set by the capacitive time constant τc = RC. At t = τc, the capacitor's charge has been reduced to q0 e^-1 or about 37 % of its initial values. Further, equations 11.11 and 11.13 show that magnitude of charging current is equal to discharging current.

Example: 11.5
A capacitor of a capacitance C is discharged through a resistor of resistance R. After how many time constants is the stored energy 1/4 of its initial value?

Solution:
The relation of energy stored in the capacitor at any time t is
U = ½ q^2/C = ½ q0^2/C e^-2t/RC = U0 e^-2t/RC
Where U0 = ½ q0^2/C = initial energy stored in the capacitor.
We have U = U0/4
U0/4 = U0 e^-2t/RC
or, t = ½ τc ln 4 = 0.693 τc

11.13 Capacitor with Dielectrics

A dielectric is a non conducting material, such as rubber, glass, or waxed paper etc. When a dielectric is inserted between the plates of a capacitor, it is found that the capacitance of the capacitor increases. If the dielectric completely fills the space between the plates, the capacitance increases by a dimensionless factor called the dielectric constant of the material. The dielectric constant varies from material to material. It is also defined as the ratio of the capacitance with and without the dielectric between the plates i.e.

k = C/Co
or, C = Co k


[Page 123]
244 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

It is also called relative permittivity.

As per molecular point of view, when the dielectric is in a charged capacitor, its molecules are in an electric field, the nuclei are urged in the direction of the field, and the electrons in opposite direction producing dipoles (figure 11.12b).

Thus each molecule is distorted or polarized. At the surface of dielectric, therefore opposite charges appear to that of plates, called surface charges or induces charge (figure 11.12c). Thus due to the surface charges induced electric field (Ei) is set up which reduces the supplied field i.e.

E = E0 - Ei

If the capacitor is connected to a battery, then its potential difference is constant, but the surface charges on the dielectric shell increases its capacitance. They do so because they offset the charges on the plates and so enable greater charges to accumulate these before the potential difference rises to the battery voltage.

In such case, some molecules of some dielectrics, like water have permanent electric dipole moment. Such dielectrics are called polar dielectrics. In such dielectrics, the dipoles tend to line up along the applied electric field, but the alignment is not complete due to constant thermal agitation. In case of non-polar dielectrics, dipoles are formed due to application of external field and are called induced dipoles. These induced dipoles tend to align with the external field. Thus we can polarize a dielectric with an external field regardless of whether the molecules are polar or non-polar.

The effect of dielectric in a capacitor can be summarized in more general terms.

• If a potential difference between the plates of a capacitor is maintained by battery, the effect of dielectric is to increase the charge on the plates.

• If the charge on the capacitor plates is maintained, the effect of a dielectric is to reduced the electric field between the plates i.e.

E = E0 - Ei = σ/kε0

• The dielectric inside the space of a capacitor always
C = k C0

Example: 11.6
If a dielectric is inserted into a space between charged capacitor having filed E0 and charge density σ, prove that the induced charge density on the dielectric will be
σi = (k-1/k)σ. Where k is dielectric constant.

Solution:
From Gauss law of electrostatics, the electric field within the charged plates before inserting dielectric is
E0 = σ/ε0

It is known that induced electric field is Ei = σi/ε0. We know the resultant field E = E0/k, so from relation,
E = E0 - Ei
or, E0/k = σ/ε0 - σi/ε0
or, σi/ε0 = σ/ε0 - σ/kε0
or, σi = (k-1/k)σ
Where k is always greater than one. So σ > σi.

Example: 11.7
Into a parallel plate capacitor of separation d and plate area A, an uncharged copper plate of thickness b is inserted, midway between the plates. Find the capacitance of the device.

Solution:
When an uncharged metallic plate is inserted into the space between the plates of a capacitor (figure 11.12), so that electrostatic induction occurs and net charge on the plate remains zero. Hence the capacitance is equivalent to two capacitors in series, each having a plate separation (d-b)/2. Thus the equivalent capacitance is
1/C = 1/C1 + 1/C2 = 1/(ε0 A / (d-b)/2) + 1/(ε0 A / (d-b)/2) = ε0 A / d-b

If the metallic plate is not midway between the plates in figure 11.13, same result will be obtained.

11.14 Dielectrics and Gauss' law

In earlier chapter Gauss law was used to study the relation between electric charge and field for air medium in the situation with no dielectrics. In this case we had the total flux

Φ = ∮ E. dA = q/ε0 ... 11.14

i.e. E0 = q / Aε0

Here, E0 is the electric field between the plates without dielectrics. Now consider the case of dielectric slab inserted in the space between the plates, we assume that the net charge or free charge q on the plates is same in both situation, but electric field is decreased from E0 to E as shown in figure 11.12 (c). Inside the dielectric dipole is produced and on the surface of it surface or induced charges qi are produced. In the figure 11.12 (c) the doted rectangular surfaces represents the Gaussian surface within which, the Gauss law is

∮ E. dA = EA = (q - qi)/ε0 ... 11.15

∴ E = q - qi / ε0 A ... 11.16

The effect of the dielectric is to weaken the original field E0 by a factor of dielectric constant (k), so we have


[Page 124]
246 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

E = E0 / k = q / kε0 A ... 11.17

From equation 11.16 and 11.17 we get,

and q' = q(1 - 1/k) ... 11.18

This shows that q' is always less than q and is equal to zero if no dielectric is present

i.e. k = 1.

Hence the Gauss law in dielectric is given by equations 11.15 and 11.18 as,

∮ E . d A = q / ε0 ... 11.19

Again we have from 11.16

ε0 E = q/A - q'/A

Where q/A is called electric displacement vector D which is the product of the dielectric constant and the electric field i.e.

D = k E

It is equivalent to free surface charge density. The term q'/A is called polarization (P).

It represents the capacity of dipole formation due to applied field and is equivalent to induced surface charge density. Hence we have

ε0 E = D - P

i.e. D = ε0 E + P ... 11.20

The important relation, although derived to a parallel plate capacitor can be generally applied for all dielectrics.

Example: 11.8
An air filled parallel plate capacitor has a capacitance of 1.32 pF. The separation of the plates is double and wax is inserted between them. The new capacitance is 2.57 pF. Find the dielectric constant of the wax.

Solution:
Let the capacitance without dielectric is
C0 = 1.32 pF
and with dielectric, but double separation is

247
Capacitors

C' = 2.57 pF
But we need the capacitance (C') for same separation to calculate dielectric constant. Thus for parallel plates

C'/C = 2d / d = 2

∴ The dielectric constant
k = C / C0 = 2x2.57 / 1.32 = 3.89


[Page 125]
248 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

11.15 Additional Examples:

1. Figure AE 1 shows a variable 'air gap' capacitor for manual tuning. Alternate plates are connected together; one group of plates is fixed in position, and the other group is capable of rotation. Consider of n = 8 plates of alternating polarity, each plate having area A = 1.25 cm² and separated from adjacent plates by distance d = 3.40 mm. What is the maximum capacitance of the device?

Solution:
For maximum capacitance the two groups of plates must face each other with maximum area. In this case the whole capacitor consists of (n - 1) identical single capacitors connected in parallel. Each capacitor has surface area A and plate separation d so its capacitance is given by C₀ = (ε₀A)/d. Thus, the total capacitance of the combination is
C = (n - 1) C₀ = ((n - 1)ε₀A)/d = ((8 - 1)8.85 × 10⁻¹² C²/N·m²)(1.25 × 10⁻⁴ m²)/(3.40 × 10⁻³ m)
= 2.28 × 10⁻¹² F.

2. The parallel plates in a capacitor, with a plate area of 8.50 cm² and an air-filled separation of 3.00 mm, are charged by a 6.00 V battery. They are then disconnected from the battery and pulled apart (without discharge) to a separation of 8 mm. Neglecting fringing, find (a) the potential difference between the plates, (b) the initial stored energy, (c) the final stored energy, and (d) the work required to separate the plates.

Solution:
(a) Let q be the charge on the positive plate.
Now, Cᵢ = (ε₀A)/dᵢ, then q = CV = (ε₀AVᵢ)/dᵢ.
After the plates pulled apart, their separation is d_f and the potential difference is V_f. The charge
q = (ε₀AV_f)/(2d_f) and
V_f = (d_f)/(ε₀A) q = (d_f ε₀A)/(ε₀Adᵢ) Vᵢ = (d_f)/(dᵢ) Vᵢ.
With dᵢ = 3.00 × 10⁻³ m, Vᵢ = 6.00 V and d_f = 8.00 × 10⁻³ m, we have V_f = 16.0 V.

(b) The initial energy stored in the capacitor is
Uᵢ = ½ CVᵢ² = (ε₀AVᵢ²)/(2dᵢ) = ((8.85 × 10⁻¹² C²/N·m²)(8.50 × 10⁻⁴ m²)(6.00V)²)/(2(3.00 × 10⁻³ m))
= 4.51 × 10⁻¹¹ J.

(c) The final energy stored is
U_f = ½ CV_f² = (ε₀AV_f²)/(2d_f) = ½ (ε₀A)/(d_f) (d_f/dᵢ Vᵢ)²
= (d_f/dᵢ) (ε₀AVᵢ²)/(2dᵢ) = (d_f/dᵢ) Uᵢ.
With d_f/dᵢ = 8/3, we have U_f = 1.20 × 10⁻¹⁰ J.

(d) The work done to pull the plates apart is the difference in the energy:
W = U_f - Uᵢ = 7.52 × 10⁻¹¹ J.

Capacitors 249

3. The space between two concentric conducting spherical shells of radii b = 1.70 cm and a = 1.20 cm is filled with a substance of dielectric constant k = 23.5. A potential difference V = 73 V is applied across the inner and outer shells. Determine (a) the capacitance of the device, (b) the free charge q on the inner shell, and (c) the charge q' induced along the surface of the inner shell.

Solution:
(a) The capacitance of an air-filled spherical capacitor is given by
C = 4πε₀ (ab)/(b - a)
When the dielectric is inserted between the plates the capacitance is greater by a factor of the dielectric constant k. Consequently, the new capacitance is
C = 4πε₀ k (ab)/(b - a) = 23.5 / (8.99 × 10⁹ N·m²/C²) * ((0.012m)(0.017m)) / (0.017m - 0.012m) = 0.107 nF.

(b) The charge on the positive plate is q = CV = (0.107 nF)(73 V) = 7.79 nC.

(c) Let the charge on the inner conductor be -q. Immediately adjacent to it is the induced charge q'. Since the electric field is less by a factor 1/k than the field when no dielectric is present, then -q + q' = -q/k. Thus,
q' = (k - 1)/k * q = 4πε₀(k - 1) (ab)/(b - a) V = ((23.5 - 1)/23.5)(7.79 nC) = 7.45 nC.


[Page 126]
