12 Direct current

Galvani, an Italian scientist, laid the foundation of the source of steady current in 1786. One day a few freshly skinned frogs were hanging from the brass hooks of an iron railing. He observed by chance, that the muscles of the frog contracted as it touched the railing. He attributed this motion to the presence of the vital fluid still present in the frog, while the railing served as a conductor. Volta, an Italian physicist contradicted this conclusion and put forward that the source of electricity was the contact between two dissimilar metals as the frog acted as the conductor. Volta turned out to be right and in 1799, he showed that the same effect could be produced by taking copper and zinc plates separated by a piece of cloth moistened with acidulated water. There was always a potential difference between the two different metals in contact. On the basis of this idea Volta designed voltaic cell to produce constant electric current..

Most practical applications of modern life deal with electric currents. The currents in the light bulbs, electric appliances through household wiring are familiar to all. Charged particles of both signs flow in the ionized gas of fluorescent lamps, in the batteries of transistor radios and in car batteries. Electric currents through semiconductors are to be found in pocket calculator, mobiles, IPod and in the chips that controls electric dishwashers, oven, computer, TV and electric heater. A beam of electrons move through an evacuated space in the picture tube of television set. The lightning strokes in the sky constitute electric current that may destroy our infrastructure also.

12.1 Electric Current
Charge is a basic property of some elementary particles of matter when they acquire the property of attraction of tiny particles due to the excess or deficit of electrons with respect to protons of the substance. When the charges are made to flow through the conductor maintaining potential difference electric current is said to be produced. There is an analogy


[Page 128]
254 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

between thermal conduction, air flow, water flow with electric current. Although an electric current is a stream of moving charges, not all moving charges constitute an electric current. To produce electric current there must be net flow of charge through a given surface; that are not in equilibrium.

The charges passing through a surface can be positive or negative or both. It is conventional to assign to the current the same direction as the flow of positive charge. When we speak of electric current in an ordinary conductor, such as copper or aluminium, the current is due to the motion of negatively charged electrons and direction is opposite to the flow of electrons. In metals mobile charge carriers are only free electrons.

Consider a section of a conductor having uniform cross - sectional area A and length Δx having n number of electrons per unit volume randomly moving with average velocity v as shown in Figure 12.1.

When electric field E is applied to the conductor, field exerts an electric force on the electrons producing a current. The electrons still do not move in a straight line along the conductor, rather they collides repeatedly with atom of the metal resulting zig – zag motion as shown in Figure 12.2. Due to the collisions, the electrons move slowly along the conductor at the drift velocity vd opposite to the direction of field.

The current is the rate at which charges flow through the conductor due to the applied field. If Δq be the amount of charges passes through the area A in a time interval Δt, the average current

I = Δq / Δt = (n A Δx)e / Δt = n Ae (Δx / Δt)

I = n e vd A ... 12.1

The SI unit for current is coulomb per second or the ampere (A). The current is a scalar quantity. So current arrows are not vectors; they show only a sense of flow of positive charge along a conductor. However, a charge carrier at a given point will flow in the direction of the electric field. To represent this flow current density J, a vector quantity is introduced. The current density J in a conductor is defined as the current per unit area, i.e.

J = I / A = n e vd ... 12.2

SI unit of J is Am^-2. This expression valid only if J is uniform and A is perpendicular to the direction of the current.

Direct Current 255

Example: 12.1
If a typical copper wire of household wiring has a cross-sectional area 3.31×10^-6 m^2 and carries a current of 10 A. What is the drift speed of the electrons? Density of copper is 8.95 gm/cm^3 and Avagadro's number NA = 6.02×10^23 mol^-1.

Solution:
There is one conducting electron per atom in copper and molar mass is 63.5 gm. We have the number of electron per unit volume of the conductor is
n = (NA ρ) / M = (6.02×10^23 × 8.95×10^3) / 63.5×10^-3 = 8.5×10^28 m^-3
Then we have,
vd = I / neA = 10 / (8.5×10^28 × 1.6×10^-19 × 3.30×10^-6) = 2.22×10^-4 m/s.

This shows a typical drift speed of electrons which is very slow as compared to the average velocity of electrons (~ 10^6 ms^-1) in an isolated atom. If current travels with this speed, then more than 2 hours requires turning on the light in Kathmandu after switching on at Kulekhani Hydropower. The flow of current is due to change in electric field configuration that drives the free electrons travel through the conductor with a speed close to that of light.

Example: 12.2
If the copper wire connected between car battery to the starting motor has a cross-sectional area 0.21 cm^2 and length 0.85 m carries a current of 300 A, calculate the time required for the electrons to get from the battery to the motor.

Solution:
The number of electrons of the copper per unit volume is 8.5×10^28 m^-3. The drift velocity is
vd = I / neA = 300 / (8.5×10^28 × 1.6×10^-19 × 0.21×10^-4) = 1.05×10^-3 ms^-1.
Hence the time required (t) = l / vd = 13 min.

12.2 Ohm's Law
A German secondary school teacher, George Simon Ohm first studied the flow of electric current through a conductor in 1826 and concluded "The potential difference across any two points on a conductor is directly proportional to the steady current through it, if physical conditions remain the same."

For a given conductor of length l and cross-sectional area A, if I be the current flowing through the conductor due to potential difference V (figure 12.3) then
V ∝ I
or, V = RI


[Page 129]
256 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Here the constant quantity R is called resistance of the conductor. It depends on nature, temperature, dimensions and physical state of the conductor. Moreover resistance,
R = V/I is independent of V and I. The unit of R is Volt/Amp or Ohm (Ω) in SI system.

Let a given resistor of resistance R be connected in an electric circuit with a battery E, Rheostat (Rh), ammeter (A) and voltmeter V as shown in figure 12.4 (a). Noting the readings I from ammeter and V from voltmeter for various values of resistance, we plot a graph between V and I for given value of resistance. The graph appears as shown in Figure. 12.4(b).

Metallic conductors obey Ohm's Law. These are also called Ohmic conductors. An electrolyte such as copper sulphate solution with copper electrodes also obeys Ohm's Law. Non-Ohmic conductors are those which do not obey Ohm's Law. Non-Ohmic characteristics may have a curve instead of straight line or may not pass through the origin as shown in Figure 12.4(c).

The equation (12.2) can also be expressed in terms of current density, and electric field i.e.
J = σE ... 12.3

σ is called conductivity of the conductor. This relation is also called modified Ohm's Law. Ohm's Law is not a fundamental law of nature but rather an empirical relationship valid only for certain materials. Equation 12.3 holds only for isotropic materials where electrical properties are the same in all direction.

12.3 Resistance and Resistivity

A metal consist large number of free electrons randomly distributed on its surface, according to the classical model of electrical conduction in metal proposed by P. Drude in 1900. This model is also called free electron model of metal. The randomness of the free electrons in the metal opposes the flow of electric current through it. The quantity which measures the randomness of the free electrons of the conductor is called resistance.

Consider a segment of straight wire of uniform cross-sectional area A and length l (see figure 12.3). If a potential difference V is maintained between the ends due to electric field E then we have
E = V/l

The modified Ohm's Law is
J = σE = σ V/l

We have J = I/A, so the potential difference across the conductor is
V = (Jl/σ) = (l/σA)I = RI ... 12.4

The quantity (l/σA) is called resistance (R) of the conductor. Resistance can be expressed in terms of resistivity ρ as;
R = (l/σA) = (ρl/A) ... 12.5

The inverse of conductivity is called resistivity (ρ). It is the measure of resistance in a given area per unit length of the conductor. Hence
ρ = 1/σ = RA/l ... 12.6

The unit of resistivity is ohm-m. The resistivity of conductor lies in the range 10^-8 to 10^-6 Ωm for semiconductor and above 10^7 Ωm for insulators.

Thus resistance depends as (i) dimensions of conductor (ii) nature of the material and (iii) temperature. The resistance is high for long wire having less cross-sectional area. Resistivity depends on nature of material and temperature but is independent of dimension of conductor. Thus resistance is a property of an object and resistivity is the property of a substance. It increases with the increase of temperature at a rate called temperature coefficient of resistance (α), defined by the relation


[Page 130]
258 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

α = 1/R₀ * ΔR/ΔT ... 12.7

i.e. R = R₀ [1 + α ΔT] = R₀ e^αΔT

Similarly ρ = ρ₀ (1 + α ΔT) = ρ₀ e^αΔT ... 12.8

Value of α depends on the nature of the substance. It is positive for metals and negative for non-metals and electrolyte.

Most electric circuit consist of resistor to control the current level in various parts of the circuit. Two common types of resistors are wire - wound resistor and composition resistor. Generally values of composition resistors are indicated by colour - coding (see appendix).

Example: 12.3
The space between two metallic coaxial cylinders of radii a and b (figure 12.5) is filled with a material of resistivity ρ. The cylinders shown in Figure 12.5, has length L. Show that the radial resistance between the cylinders is given by
R = ρ/2πl * ln(b/a)

Solution:
Equation 12.5 can not be used directly because the cross-section through which the charge trends varies from 2πal at the inner cylinder to 2πbl at the outer cylinder. Instead, we consider a cylindrical shell of inner radius r and thickness dr. The area A is then 2πrl, and the length of the current path through the shell is dr. Thus, the resistance dR of the shell is
dR = ρ dr / 2πrl

and total radial resistance between the cylinder is
R = ∫[a to b] dR = ρ/2πl ∫[a to b] dr/r = ρ/2πl ln(b/a)

Figure 12.5 Coaxial cable

Example: 12.4
A conductor of uniform radius 1.2 cm carries a current of 8 A due to potential gradient of 120 V/m. What is the specific resistance of the material?

Solution:
The cross-sectional area of the conductor
(A) = πr² = 22/7 * (1.2x10⁻² )² m² = 4.53x10⁻⁴ m²

Current density
J = I/A = 6.63x10³ A/m²

The resistivity or specific resistance is
ρ = E/J = 120 / 6.63x10³ = 1.8x10⁻² Ωm.

12.4 Microscopic view of Ohm's Law.
As discussed in section 12.2 the free electrons in metal move randomly with average speed on the order of 10⁶ ms⁻¹. The situation is similar to the motion of gas molecules confined in a vessel. Thus these conduction electrons in a metal are called electron gas. In such case drift velocity of the free electron is zero, so there is no current in the conductor in the absence of electric field; because many electrons move in one direction as in opposite direction and net flow is zero.

Figure 12.6(a) Random motion of free electrons.
Figure 12.6(b) Drift motion of free electrons.

When we apply an electric field to the metal, this situation will change. The electrons modify their random motion slightly and drift very slowly in a direction opposite to that of the field with an average drift speed vd, that is much smaller (~10⁻⁴ m/s) than their average speed (~10⁶ m/s). Figure 12.6(a) depicts the random motion of free electrons in the conductor in the absence of an electric field. When electric field is applied, random motion is modified and causes the electrons to drift in opposite direction to that of field, E (see figure 12.6 (b)). The motion of the electrons in an electric field is thus a combination of motion due to random collisions and that due to field. The drift speed is due to only the effect of the electric field on the electrons because their random motions average to zero.

Let an electron of mass m and charge e be considered in an electric field E, it experiences a force F = e E. The corresponding acceleration given by Newton's law is
a = F/m = eE/m ... 12.9

If τ be the average time between the collisions, the drift speed vd = a τ then
vd = aτ = eEτ/m ... 12.10

Using this value with J = n e vd we get
E = (m / ne²τ) J ... 12.11

and the resistivity is


[Page 131]
260
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

ρ = E/J = (m / ne²τ) ... 12.12

This relation shows that resistivity and conductivity are independent of applied field and depends on nature of the material.

Let us consider how the variables n and τ change as the temperature is increased. In a conductor, n is large but remains nearly constant with any change of temperature. The increase of resistivity with temperature for metals is due to increase in collision rate of the charge carriers. As temperature increases τ decreases, so ρ increases.

In a semiconductor, n is small but increases very rapidly with temperature as the increased thermal agitation makes more charge carriers available. This causes a decrease of resistivity with increase temperature. The same increase in collision rate that we noted for metals also occurs for semiconductors but its effect is swamped by the rapid increase in the number of charge carriers.

Moreover, the average time interval between the collisions is related to the average distance between the collisions (λ). The average time is also called mean free time and average distance is called mean free path. The mean free path and time are related by

λ = vdτ

Hence from (12.11) the resistivity in terms of λ is

ρ = (m vd) / (ne²λ) = (3KT) / (ne² vd λ) ... 12.13

Where k is Boltzmann constant T is absolute temperature.

This relation shows that the resistivity is directly proportional to absolute temperature where vd λ is independent of temperature because with the rise in temperature, vibration of atoms will increase in the crystal lattice decreasing the mean free path and increasing the drift velocity (vd ∝ √T as kinetic theory of gas). In some books you may find resistivity directly proportional to √T, instead of T, this is by assuming λ independent of temperature.

Example: 12.5
If the temperature of an iron filament changes from 25°C, what will be the fractional change in resistance? α = 6x10⁻³ K⁻¹.

Solution:
We have from (12.8)
α = (1/R₀) * (ΔR/ΔT)

The fractional change in resistance is

ΔR/R₀ = α ΔT = 5x10⁻³ x 25 = 0.125.

Example: 12.6
Calculate the (i) mean free time and (ii) mean free path between collisions for the conduction electrons in copper having electron density 8.5x10²⁸ m⁻³ and resistivity 1.7x10⁻⁸ Ωm. Charge of electron = 1.6x10⁻¹⁹ C, mass of electron = 9.1x10⁻³¹ kg, effective speed of electron = 1.6x10⁶ ms⁻¹.

Solution:
(i) From the relation (12.12) we have
τ = m / (ne²ρ) = 9.1x10⁻³¹ / (8.5x10²⁸ x (1.6x10⁻¹⁹)² x 1.7x10⁻⁸)
= 2.5x10⁻¹⁴ sec.
(ii) The mean free path
λ = τ veff = 2.5x10⁻¹⁴ x 1.6x10⁶ = 40 nm.

Example: 12.7
What will be the conductivity of sodium metal having electron density 2.5x10²⁸ m⁻³ and relaxation time 3x10⁻¹¹ sec?

Solution:
We have from the relation (12.12)
ρ = m / (ne²τ)
σ = 1/ρ = (ne²τ) / m = (2.5x10²⁸ x (1.6x10⁻¹⁹)² x 3x10⁻¹¹) / 9.1x10⁻³¹ = 2.1x10⁷ mho/m.

12.5 Semiconductors

Semiconductors in the present day world are one of the most important materials owing to their widespread uses. Almost all the modern sophisticated engineering creations employ semiconductor devices for their effective functioning. Twenty century has witnessed phenomenal growth and advancement in semiconductor technology. The IV group elements in modern periodic table such as Ge and Si having four electrons in the outermost orbit are semiconductors. Not all the group IV elements are semiconductor in nature. These semiconductors are also called pure or intrinsic semiconductors which show insulating behaviour at absolute zero.

In comparison to metal, semiconductor has (i) very high value of resistivity (ii) many fewer charge carriers and (iii) large and negative temperature coefficient of resistivity. The resistance of semiconductor decreases with the increase of temperature due to negative temperature coefficient of resistivity. The electrical properties of semiconductors are determined by (a) the presence of a gap in the energy states and (b) the presence of impurities. The existence of an energy gap between the highest filled band and the next highest band at absolute zero is a feature determined by band theory of solid. The highest

261


[Page 132]
262 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
filled band is called valence band and the next highest band is empty at T = 0 is called conduction band. Figure 12.7 (a, b) shows energy level diagram for typical semiconductor.

At finite temperatures electrons may be thermally exited across the gap between valence and conduction bands provided the gap is not too big. Two types of carriers of electricity are then created, electrons in the conduction band and holes in the valence band. The possibility of existence of both types of currents of holes and free electrons simultaneously in a solid and behaving almost independently gives rise to the interesting properties of semiconductors. When a voltage is applied to such an intrinsic semiconductor a current flows which is due to the motion of both holes and electrons. The drift velocities of the two types of carriers in the electric field are different because of their differing effective masses. If n and p are electron and hole densities; ve and vp drift velocities of electrons and holes of a given semiconductor at a temperature T, the total current is

J = Je + Jp = n e ve + p e vp

If ue and up are mobilities of electrons and holes, defined as the drift velocity per unit applied electric field E, then

J = n e ue E + p e up E

i.e. J = e E (n ue + p up)

or, 0 = J/E = e (n ue + p up)

In intrinsic semiconductor, the concentration of holes is equal to the concentration of electrons.

Figure 12.7 (a), (b) Energy level diagram for semiconductor

However, having very high resistivity or low conductivity of the intrinsic semiconductor, not used directly in device fabrications. It is possible to modulate the bulk conductivity of intrinsic semiconductors by (i) changing the concentrations of electrons or holes maintaining charge neutrality or (ii) increasing the temperature.

Impurities are introduced by substituting them for atoms of the host intrinsic semiconductor to increase its conductivity. This may be done either by in foreign atoms or by introducing them during diffusing crystal growth. The process of impurity addition in pure

Direct Current 263
semiconductor is called doping. Such semiconductor in which trivalent or pentavalent atoms are incorporated are called "extrinsic semiconductor" which have great practical application in semiconductor technology. Depending on dopent atoms there are two types of extrinsic semiconductors:
(i) P - Semiconductors are made by adding foreign atoms which are trivalent to pure Ge or Si. Examples are boron indium etc. In p-type, 'holes' are formed during covalent bond formations which are majority charge carriers and minority charge carriers, electrons are formed due to thermal agitation. The impurity atoms of this type are also called "acceptors".
(ii) N - Semiconductors are made by doping pentavalent elements such as arsenic, phosphorus to a Ge or Si. The four electrons of impurity atom share with neighbouring Ge atoms and the fifth electron becomes free to wander through the crystal. Thus majority charge carriers of N-type are free electrons and holes are minority charge carriers. The impurity atom of this kind is called "donor".

Some semiconductors are doped with both N - and P - type impurities which are called compensated semiconductors. P and N - type semiconductors are basic for fabrication of semiconductor devices. P-N junction diode, N-P-N, P-N-P transistors, light emitting diode (LED), zener diode, photovoltaic cell or solar cell, LASER diode are few examples.

12.6 Super conductors

The electrical resistance of metal and alloys decreases with the decrease of temperature. At lower temperature, the thermal vibrations of the atoms decrease. So due to decrease in randomness of free electrons resistance decreases. The phenomenon was first observed in 1911. The Dutch Physicist Kamerlingh Onnes discovered that the electrical resistances of mercury drops to zero see Fig 12.8. at temperature of about 4k. He found that below this temperature, the resistance remained effectively zero. Mercury has passed into a new state which on account of its extra ordinary properties may be called the superconductive state.

When a conductor is cooled below a certain temperature, there is not only an abrupt loss of electrical resistance as discovered by Onnes but there are also abrupt changes in many properties of the materials. All these changes takes place at a same temperature which is known as critical temperature (Tc). Below the critical temperature, the material is superconductive state and above the critical temperature, the material is normal state. Before 1986, the superconductivity was rather low temperature phenomenon and is a definite obstacle to its technological exploitation, since it means that a superconducting device has to be accompanied refrigeration equipment of some kind to keep the temperature sufficiently low. Even now a days only 164 k critical temperature has been obtained in the compound HgBaCaCuO (HBCC0) at high pressure. Many scientists of the world are working to obtain at room temperature.

In 1913, the superconductivity in lead at 7.2 K was discovered. It was observed in 1914 that superconductivity is destroyed by magnetic field. The maximum magnetic field above which the superconductive state is destroyed is called critical magnetic field. Later, towards the end of 1986, the existence of high temperature superconductor (HTSC), which can be called Neon superconductors was reliably proved in


[Page 133]
264 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

B = μ₀ (H + M) = 0

which is true for a perfect diamagnet.

Critical Magnetic Field.
A sufficiently strong magnetic field will destroy
superconductor. The threshold or critical value of the
applied magnetic field for the destruction of
superconductivity is represented by Hc (T) and is a
function of temperature. At critical temperature the
critical field is zero i.e. Hc (T) = 0

The figure 12.9 depicts the variation of critical
magnetic field with temperature when S is
superconducting state and N is normal state. The nature
of the curve is approximately parabolic and satisfies the
parabolic relation
Hc (T) = Hc (0) [1-(T/Tc)²] ... 12.15

where Hc (0) is the critical field at 0k. Thus we find that the superconducting state is
stable only in some definite ranges of magnetic fields and temperatures. For higher fields and
temperatures, normal state is more stable. The magnetic or current barrier was overcome by
the discovery of superconducting state in alloys in 1930s. Such types of superconductors are
called second type superconductors. For such materials, superconducting state 'vanishes only
above very high magnetic field of critical value Hc₂(T).

The Meissner Effect
If a superconductor is cooled in a magnetic field below the transition temperature then
at the transition temperature, the lines of induction of B are pushed out (see figure 12.10).

This phenomenon was first discovered by Meissner and Ochsenfeld in 1933, and is
It shows that a bulk superconductor behaves in an applied external

But B is constant in perfect conductor, which is contradiction to the Meissner effect.
The behaviour of superconductor is different from that of a perfect conductor. The
superconductors showing such behaviour are known as soft or type I superconductors.

The BCS theory
Theoretical study provides the causes of occurrence of superconducting state. There is
really no good microscopic theory of superconductivity except basic theory proposed by
Bardeen, Cooper and Schrieffer called BCS theory. What prevents from creating a new
theory? Do scientists lack of additional facts or information or are waiting for the advent of a
genius, a New Einstein?

It is more or less clear that superconductivity is the super fluidity of electrons in
metal. Then after Bose - Einstein condensation and super fluidity theories, it is concluded that
electrons in metals can be converted into bounded electron pairs with zero total spin. How
electrons usually repeal, can form a bound state? In 1956 Cooper introduced first pairing
called Cooper pair.

Cooper pair
If wave vector (k₁) of an electron is equal and opposite to another (-k₂), results an
attractive force with same energies, which causes the electrons to exits in pair. It is also
shown that the interaction is larger for the electrons with opposite spins because they have
lager probability of being close to each other. The coherence length is used to measure the
size of cooper pair. If BE be the binding energy of the pairs i.e. cooper pairs then the coherent
length will be
ξ = hv_f / B_E ...12.16

i.e. the size of pair depends on electron velocity and binding energy. It is of the order of 10
cm. All electrons in the metal tend to form such pairs k with -k


[Page 134]
Types of Superconductors
The superconductor in which the Meissner effect is complete up to critical field Hc is called type I superconductor. The magnetic behaviour of a typical type I superconductor is shown in figure 12.11.

These materials give away their superconductivity at lower field strength so these are referred to as soft superconductors. Pure specimens of various metals exhibits this type of behaviour. Surface energy of a material is a result of magnetic field which is positive for type I superconductor. The indium shows type I nature of superconductor, but when some amount of indium is doped to the lead, the behaviour will no longer type I. It shows the curve 2 type behaviour which is characteristics of type II superconductor as shown in figure 12.12.

These types of materials find to be alloys ceramic or hard transition metals with high value of electrical resistivity i.e. low value of mean free path in normal state. In such type of materials, superconducting state exists up to Hc2. Between the lower critical field Hc1 and upper critical field Hc2 B ≠ 0 i.e. Meissner effect is incomplete. The region between Hc1 and Hc2 is called Vortex State. The critical field of Pb is Hc = 4.8x10^4 A/m at 4k when 20% indium is added it becomes type II superconductor of which Hc1 = 3.6x10^3 A/m and Hc2 = 2.9x10^5 A/m. Surface energy of type II superconductor is negative so Vortex State or mixed state is stable. These superconductors are also called hard or impure superconductor.

Example: 12.8
A silicon sample has been doped with 10^17 arsenic atoms/cm^3. Calculate the conductivity of the sample at 300 K and 400 K. The mobilities of electron at 300 K and 400 K are 800 cm^2/Vs and 420 cm^2/Vs respectively.
Solution:
The electrical conductivity for Nd << ni and p << n is
σ = n e µe = 1.6x10^-19 x 10^17 x 800 = 12.8 Sm / cm. at 300 K
And at 400 K we have
σ = n e µe = 1.6x10^-19 x 10^17 x 420 = 6.72 Sm /cm.

Example 12.9
The super conducting state of a lead specimen has critical temperature 6.2 k at zero magnetic field and the critical field is 0.064 x 10^5 A/m at 0 k. Estimate the critical field at 5 k.
Solution:
We have
Hc (0) = 0.064x10^5 A/m, T = 5 k, Tc = 6.2 k
Now using
Hc = Hc (0) [1 - T^2/Tc^2]
= 0.064x10^5 [1 - (5/6.2)^2]
= 2.22x10^4 A/m
Hence the critical field of Pb at 5 k is 2.22x10^4 A/m.

12.7 Additional Examples:
1. The magnitude J of the current density in a certain wire with a circular cross section of radius R = 2 mm is given by J = (3 x 10^8) r^2, with J in amperes per square meter and radial distance r in meters. What is the current through the outer section bounded by r = 0.9 R and r = R?
Solution:
Assuming J is directed along the wire (with no radial flow) we integrate ∫ J dA, for i,
i = ∫ J dA = ∫ (kr^2) 2πr dr = ½ kπ (R^4 - 0.656 R^4)
where k = 3 x 10^8. Therefore, if R = 0.002 m, we obtain i = 2.59 x 10^-3 A.

2. A wire with a resistance of 6 Ω is drawn out through a die so that its new length is three times its original length. Find the resistance of the longer wire, assuming that the resistivity and density of the material are unchanged.
Solution:
Since the mass density of the material do not change, the volume remains the same. If Lo is the original length, L is the new length, A0 is the original cross-sectional area, and A is the new cross-sectional area, then
LoA0 = LA and A = LoA0 / L = LoA0 / 3Lo = A0 / 3
The new resistance is
R = ρL / A = ρ3Lo / (A0 / 3) = 9 ρLo / A0 = 9 Ro
where Ro is the original resistance.
Thus, R = 9 (6 Ω) = 54 Ω.

3. Two conductors are made of the same material and have the same length. Conductor A is a solid wire of diameter 1 mm. Conductor B is a hollow tube of outside diameter 2 mm and inside diameter 1 mm. What is the resistance ratio RA/RB, measured between their ends?
Solution:
The resistance of conductor A is given by
RA = ρL / (π ra^2)
where ra is the radius of the conductor.
If ro is the outside diameter of conductor B and ri is its inside diameter, then its cross-sectional area is π (ro^2 - ri^2), and its resistance is
RB = ρL / (π (ro^2 - ri^2))
Hence their ratio is
RA / RB = (ro^2 - ri^2) / ra^2 = ((1mm)^2 - (0.5mm)^2) / (0.5mm)^2 = 3.

4. A cylindrical resistor of radius 5 mm and length 2 cm is made of material that has a resistivity of 3.5 x 10^-5 Ω.m. What are (a) the magnitude of the current density and (b) the potential difference when the energy dissipation rate in the resistor is 1 W?


[Page 135]
268 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Solution:
(a) We have Power is
P = i²R = J²A²R
Now current density will become,
J = √P/AR = 1/A √P/R = √P/pLA = √1 W / √ (3.5x10⁻⁵ Ωm)(2x10⁻²m)((5x10⁻³m)²)
= 1.3 x 10⁵ A/m²

(b) Using P = iV = JAV we get,
V = P/AJ = P/πr²J = 1 W / π(5x10⁻³m)²(1.3x10⁵ A/m²) = 9.4x10⁻²V

5. At what temperature would the resistance of a copper conductor be double its resistance at 20° C?

Solution:
We know that resistance of a copper wire increase with increasing temperature. Increase in resistance is given by
R - Ro = Roα(T- To)
Let R = 2 Ro, We have
2 Ro - Ro = Roα(T- To)
And solving for temperature T, We have,
T = To + 1/α = 20° C + 1 / 4.3x10⁻³/K ≈ 250° C

Exercise: 12
1. Calculate the drift speed of electrons when 10 A current is supplied through a copper wire of cross-sectional area 1 mm² and electron density 10²⁸ m⁻³.
2. Two copper wires of same length l and cross-sectional areas A and 2A are connected to a battery. What will be the ratio of drift velocities when the wires are in
(i) series and (ii) parallel?
3. A copper wire of cross-sectional area 5x10⁻⁶ m² carries a steady current of 50 A. Assuming one free electron per atoms, calculate
(i) free electron density and
(ii) average drift velocity.
Density of Cu = 8.9x10³ kg/m³, Molar mass of Cu = 64 and Avogadro's number = 6.02x10²³ mol⁻¹.

13
Chapter
Electromagnetism

The word magnetism comes after the name of Magnesia; a place in Asia Minor, the stones which attracted iron were found. The stone is called magnet. By 121 AD Chinese invented magnetic compass. In 1269 AD, P. Mericourt found that magnetic effects were strongest near the ends of magnets which were called poles. It was also found that poles always occur in pair of equal and opposite strength, so that it is impossible to isolate one type of polarity. Dr. Gilbert in 1600 AD pointed out that the earth is itself a great magnet and has two poles. He found that "Like poles repel and unlike poles attract each other." Gilbert in his book "de Magnet" defined the magnetic field as the strength of a magnet at any point in vacuum. But within materials medium, there are strong local magnetic fields due to moving electrons, which are equivalent to electric current and there is practical difficulty of introducing magnetic poles without displacing the medium near it. In materials medium two types of magnetic fields are defined. The intensity of magnetic field (H) represents the strength of magnetic field at a point due to external electric currents or apparent poles and the magnetic induction field (B) represents the magnetic average value of strength of magnetic field. It is measured by lines of induction.

The magnetic induction field B is assumed as the more fundamental and more useful quantity within a material medium. So at present, the terms magnetic field or strength of magnetic field mean the field B. These fields are related by

B = μH = μ₀μr H

Where μ is called permeability of the medium. In air it is represented by μ₀ which is 4πx10^-7 H/m. The ratio of μ and μ₀ is called relative permeability μr.

For two hundred years or more after Gilbert there is no further advance in the development of the basic nature of magnetism. But at present we know magnetic poles do not exist. Magnetic fields are produced by current carrying loops, which was the discovery of Oersted in 1820. In the same year, Ampere suggested that magnetic properties of matter were the result of tiny atomic currents. Besides natural magnets, artificial magnets i.e. bar or hard steel piece of soft iorn, alloys etc are now widely used. Magnets made of steel of some alloys are used as permanent magnets whereas soft iron temporary magnet. In 1845, Faraday concluded that all substances were, to a greater or lesser extent affected by a magnet which results the classification of magnetic materials, such as dia, para, fero etc. Later after twelve


[Page 137]
272 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

months J. Henry had anticipated the Faraday discovery. Ferromagnetic materials are generally considered as magnetic material and dia, para are non-magnetic materials. One of the important properties of ferromagnetic materials is 'histeresis'. On this basis hard and soft magnetic materials are classified. Hard magnetic materials have broad and almost rectangular hysteresis in shape but soft has narrow loop.

From where these magnetic fields come? The scientist's experiments show that a charge set up an electric field whether the charge is at rest or in motion. A charge sets up a magnetic field, however, only if it is moving.

13.1 Magnetic Flux

The space around a magnet or current loop in which its influence is felt is called magnetic field. To represent the magnetic field of a magnet or current loop, large numbers of lines are drawn around it in such a way that tangent to the line at any point indicates the direction of the magnetic field at that point. Figure 13.1 shows magnetic field lines of a bar magnet. The imaginary lines in a magnetic field considered collectively are called magnetic flux. In other words, the total number of field lines emanating from a magnet is called magnetic flux. The field lines never intersect to each other. Closer the line of force stronger is the magnetic field. The magnetic flux is represented by ΦB. The unit of ΦB is Weber (Wb) in SI system. The number of these lines of induction or force per unit area normal to the direction is called magnetic flux density represented by B, i.e.

Magnetic flux Φ
B = ——————— = —
Area A

... 13.1

[Image of bar magnet] Figure 13.1 Magnetic field lines of a bar magnet
[Image of area element] Figure 13.2 The magnetic flux through an area element dA

We can divide any surface into elements of area d A. For each element, we determine B⊥ (perpendicular component). From figure 13.2 we have B⊥ = B cos θ, then

dΦ = B⊥ dA = B cos θ dA = B · d A

The total magnetic flux through the surface is

ΦB = ∫ B⊥ dA = ∫ B · d A

... 13.2

The unit of magnetic flux density or magnetic field B is Wb/m² i.e. Tesla (T). Another unit of B is Gauss (1 G = 10⁻⁴ T) is also in common use. The magnetic field of earth is of the order of 10⁻⁴ T. The magnetic fields of the order of 10 T occur in the interior of atoms which are used in the analysis of atomic spectra. The largest steady magnetic field that can be produced at present in laboratory is about 45 T. The magnetic field at the surface of neutron star is believed to be of the order of 10⁸ T.

Electromagnetism 273

Analogy to Gauss Law in electrostatics, the total flux through a closed surface is proportional to the total magnetic charge enclosed, but no magnetic monopole has ever been observed, despite intensive searches. So we conclude that the total magnetic flux through a closed surface is always zero; mathematically, it is

∫ B · d A = 0 ... 13.3

This equation is sometimes called Gauss's law for magnetism.

Example 13.1
A flat surface with area 3.0 cm² as shown in figure 13.3
is in a uniform magnetic field of flux 0.90x10⁻³ Wb. Calculate
the magnitude of the magnetic field and find the direction of
vector area.

Solution:
The area A is 3x10⁻⁴ m², the direction of A is perpendicular
to surface, so θ could be either
θ = 90° - 30° = 60°
or, θ = 90° + 30° = 120°
But ΦB and A are all positive, so cos θ must be positive. This rules out 120°, so θ = 60° and
magnitude of magnetic field is given by

ΦB 0.9x10⁻³
B = ———— = ———————— = 6.0 T.
A cos θ 3x10⁻⁴ x cos 60°

The are vector A is perpendicular to the surface.

13.2 Magnetic Field and Force

The magnetic field B is defined in term of magnetic flux lines in earlier unit. We can also define the magnetic field B at some point in space in terms of magnetic force that the field exerts on a moving charged particle, as that of electric field E was defined. The electric field is defined as

F E
E = ——— ... 13.4
q

However magnetic monopole having no existence, it cannot be define in a similar way. Again a charge sets up an electric field if it is at rest or is moving but magnetic field is set up only due to its motion. Hence magnetic field is said to exist as a point if a force is exerted on a moving charge at the point. Experiment shows that;
* The magnitude of the magnetic force (FB) exerted on the particle is directly proportional to the amount of charge particle q.
* The magnitude and direction of FB depends on the velocity of the particle (v)
* The magnitude of force acting on the particle depends on the magnitude and direction of magnetic field B.
* When the charge particle moves parallel to the magnetic field no force will be set up and is maximum it moves normal to B.
* The magnitude of magnetic force exerted on the moving particle is proportional to sin θ, where θ is the angle between particle velocity and magnetic field, as shown in figure 13.4.


[Page 138]
274 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Figure 13.4 (a) Magnetic force on moving +ve charged particle (b) Direction of B

In principle, we obtain these results by firing a test charge through a point where B is to be defined, using various directions and speeds for the test charge and determine the force. We summaries these observations as

FB = q v B sin θ

i.e. F_B = q (v x B) ... 13.5

This force is called magnetic force. The charge q may be either positive or negative. In comparing this FB with FE define by equation 13.4 we concluded that:
• The electric force is always directed to the electric field but magnetic force always acts perpendicular to the magnetic field.
• The electric force acts on a charged particle whether it is at rest or motion but magnetic force acts on a charged particle only when the particle is in motion.
• The electric force always does work on displacing a charge but magnetic force does no work even the particle is displaced. Hence no energy is gained by a charge when it enters magnetic field. The direction of FB is given by Fleming's right hand rule. The magnitude of the magnetic field is thus defined by the relation,

B = FB / qv sin θ ... 13.6

When FB is expressed in Newton, q in coulombs, v in m/s, then B is said to be in Weber per square meter. Thus one Weber/m² is the magnetic induction of a magnetic field in which one coulomb of charge moving with a component of velocity perpendicular to the field equal to one meter per second is acted on by a force of one Newton.

Moreover the SI unit for B that follows from equation 13.6 is the Newton per coulomb-meter per second. For convenience, this is called Tesla (T);

1 Tesla (T) = 1 N / Cm/s = 1 N / Am

When a charged particle moves through a region of space where both electric and magnetic field are present, both fields exert forces on the particle. The total force F is the vector sum of the electric and magnetic forces:

F = q (E + v x B) ... 13.7

This force is called Lorentz force.

Electromagnetism 275

Example 13.2
What will be the path of a particle of charge q and mass m travelling in a magnetic field of strength B with a velocity v: (i) in the direction of the field (ii) perpendicular to the field (iii) inclined at an angle to the field? (Figure 13.5)

Solution:
(i) Since v and B are parallel so v x B = 0 i.e. particle experiences no force and travels in a straight line.
(ii) If v and B are perpendicular to each other, the magnitude of the force q (v x B) will be q v B and direction will be perpendicular to v and B. The particle will move in a circular path in a plane perpendicular to B, and the magnetic force will act as the centripetal force i.e.
mv²/r = q v B
i.e. r = mv/qB ... 13.8

(iii) If v is inclined at angle θ to B, it can be resolved into components, V|| = v cos θ along B and perpendicular component V⊥ = v sin θ. The parallel component makes it move in a straight line along B and the perpendicular component makes it move in a circular path of radius
r = m v sin θ / q B ... 13.9

So the resultant path will be helix with its axis along B.

Example 13.3
An electron is projected into a magnetic field of flux density 10 T with a velocity of 3x10⁷ m/s in a direction at right angle to the field. Compute the magnetic force on the electron and compare with the weight of the electron.

Solution:
The magnetic force is
F_m = B q v = Bev [∴ q = N_e = e]
= 10 x 1.6x10⁻¹⁹ x 3x10⁷
= 4.8x10⁻¹¹ N
The gravitational force on the electron of mass 9.1x10⁻³¹ kg is
F_g = mg = 9.1x10⁻³¹ x 9.8 = 13.8x10⁻³⁰ N
Hence F_g is negligible in comparison to F_m.

13.3 Magnetic Force and Torque on a Conductor

Magnetic Force on a current - carrying conductor

When a current carrying conductor lies in the magnetic field, magnetic forces are set up on the moving electrons within the conductor. These forces are transmitted to the material of the conductor and hence the conductor as a whole experiences a force or torque or both. The electric motor, galvanometer, voltmeter, CD/DVD player etc depend for their operation on the torque exerted on a current carrying loop in a magnetic field.


[Page 139]
276 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Figure 13.6 shows a straight conductor of portion of a wire of length l and cross-sectional area A. The electric current I is flowing through it. The electrons in this section of wire will drift in the time l/v carrying a charge
q = N e = (n l A) e ... 13.10
The force on the conductor as a whole is the result of the force on all moving charges within it. Thus we have from equation 13.5:
F = N F_B = (n l A) (B e v) sin θ = B I l sin α
Hence F = I (l x B) ... 13.11

This equation gives the general relation of force that acts on a segment of a straight wire of length l carrying current I immersed in a magnetic field B. If a wire is not straight line, we can imagine it broken up into small straight segments. Thus in the differential limit, we have,
dF = I dl x B ... 13.12
Then we can integrate this expression along the wire to find the total force on the conductor of any shape. This integral is called a line integral.

Torque on a Current Loop
Let us take a rectangular current loop as a conductor in a uniform magnetic field. Figure 13.7 (a) shows a rectangular loop of a wire with side length a and b. A line portion to the plane of the loop makes an angle α with the direction of magnetic filed B and θ be the angle made by the loop with the direction of B as shown in figure 13.7 (b), where we have,
α + θ = 90°.
I be the current flowing through the loop. The force F, on the right side of the loop of length a i.e. on the vertical sides PS and QR, is given by
F1 = I aB
F1 and -F1 have some magnitude but opposite direction. The force on the side 'B' makes an angle θ with B of which magnitude is
F2 = I bB cos θ

The lines of action of both forces lie along same line. The total force on the loop is zero because the force on opposite sides cancel out in pairs. The force F2 and -F2 lie along the same line, so they have no net torque with respect to any point. The two forces F1 and -F1 form a couple. Hence the torque of a couple, with respect to any point is the magnitude of either force multiplied by the distance between the lines of action of the two forces. So the torque τ is
τ = F1 b cos θ
τ = I a B b cos θ
= B I (a b) cos θ
= B I A cos θ

Where A (= a b) is the area enclosed by the loop. Suppose we replace the single loop of current with a coil of N loops or turns, then total torque on coil is given by
τN = τ N
τN = B I N A cos θ
or, τN = B I N A sin α ... 13.13

The torque rotates the loop in the direction of decreasing α, since α is angle between A and B, so the convenient expression for the torque.
τN = N I (A x B) ... 13.14
The product N I A is called magnetic moment of the coil (μ) or magnetic dipole moment μ
μ = N I A ... 13.15

The magnetic moment is a vector quantity. The direction of μ is defined to be perpendicular to the plane of loop, with the sense determine by right hand rule. Hence from 13.13 we have the torque on the current loop
τN = μ x B ... 13.16

Magnetism is due to circulating and spinning electrons inside atoms. The moving charges are equivalent to electric currents. Consequently like a current carrying coil permanent magnets also have torque acting on them, when they are placed with their axis at an angle to a magnetic field like a coil, they turn and settle in equilibrium along the field direction with their axis.

From equation 13.15, dividing both sides by volume of the loop we get magnetic moment per unit volume also called intensity of magnetization (M) or simply Magnetietion.

Electromagnetism 277


[Page 140]
278 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

i.e. M = μ/V = NIA/V = nIA ... 13.17

Where n is number of turns per unit volume of the coil. This current loop treatment is equivalent to magnetic dipole and is valid for any orientation.

When magnetic dipole changes its orientation in a magnetic field, the field does work on it. In an infinitesimal angular displacement da the work dw is given by t da and there is corresponding change in potential energy. To find the expression for the potential energy U as a function of orientation, we can make use of interactions. The torque on an electric dipole in an electric field is t = P x E and corresponding potential energy is U = P . E. The torque on a magnetic dipole in a magnetic field is t = μ x B, so we can conclude immediately that the corresponding potential energy is

U = ∫τdα = - μ . B = -μ B cos α ... 13.18

with this definition, U is zero when magnetic dipole moment is perpendicular to the magnetic field.

This potential energy is work done required to rotate the dipole from 90° to θ. The energy is least when θ = 0 which is when μ and B are in the same direction and is maximum when θ = 180° i.e. when μ and B

Example: 13.4
A wire of length l, carries a current I. If the wire is formed into a circular coil, then the maximum torque in a given magnetic field B developed for a single turn is τ = (1/4π) L² I B

Solution:
We have for a wire carrying current I from equation 13.13 is
τ = B I A sin α
For maximum we have
τ = B I A
If the circular coil has radius r then we have
L = 2 πr
r = L/2π
...i
and area of the circular coil (A) = πr² = L²/4π . Thus from equation i, τ = (1/4π) L² I B.
Thus we have torque depends on the length, magnetic field and supplied current.

Example: 13.5
The magnetic dipole moment of the Earth is 8x10^22 J/T. Assume that this is produced by charges flowing in the molten outer core of the Earth. If the radius of their circular path is 3500 km, calculate the current they produce.

Solution:
We know the magnetic dipole moment
μ = IA = I x πr²
I = μ / πr² = 8x10^22 / π x (3.5x10^6)^2 = 2.08x10^9 A
= 2.08 GA
Which is extremely high current.

Electromagnetism 279

Example: 13.6
A coil of wire has an area of 2x10^-4 m², consists of 100 turns and contains a current of 0.045 A. The coil is placed in a uniform magnetic field of magnitude 0.15 T. (a) Determine the magnetic moment of the coil. (b) Find the maximum torque that the magnetic field can exert on the coil.

Solution:
(a) The magnetic moment of the coil is
μ = NIA = 100 x 0.045 x 2x10^-4
= 9x10^-4 Am²
(b) According to equation 13.13, the maximum torque occurs when α = 90°, thus
τmax = B I N A sin 90° = μ B = 9x10^-4 x 0.15
= 1.4x10^-4 Nm

Example: 13.7
A coil of magnetic moment 1.45 Am² is oriented initially with its magnetic moment anti parallel to a uniform 0.835 T magnetic field. What is the change in potential energy of the coil when it is rotated 180° so that its magnetic moment is parallel to the field.

Solution:
From equation the initial potential energy U1, is
U1 = - μ B cos 180°
and final potential energy U2 is
U2 = - μ B cos 0°
The change in potential energy
ΔU = U2 - U1 = - μ B cos 0° + μ B cos 180°
= - 2 μ B = - 2 x 1.45 x 0.835
= - 2.42 J

13.4 Hall Effect
When a sample of conductor carrying current is placed in a uniform magnetic field perpendicular to the direction of the current, a transverse field will be set up across the conductor. This was first experimentally observed by Edwik H. Hall in 1879. The field developed across the conductor is called Hall field and corresponding potential difference is called Hall Voltage and its value is found to depend on the magnetic field strength, nature of the materials and applied current. This type of effect is called Hall effect. The principle of Hall effect is based on the simple dynamics of charges moving in electromagnetic fields.

Figure 13.8 Hall Voltage
To explain Hall effect, consider a sample of a block of conductor of length l, width d and thickness t, through which electric current I is supplied along x- axis as shown in figure 13.8. The flow of electron is in the opposite direction to the conventional current. If the conductor is placed in a magnetic field B along z-axis perpendicular to the direction of current, a force Bev then acts on each electrons in the direction from top surface to the bottom of the sample. Thus electrons accumulate on the bottom surface of the conductor which will make the surface negatively charged and top surface will be charged positively. Hence a potential difference opposes the flow of electrons. The flow ceases when the potential difference across the conductor along y-axis reaches a particular value i.e. Hall Voltage (VH), which may be measured by using a high impedance voltmeter. If d be the width of the slab of


[Page 141]
the sample, then the electric field or the Hall Field ($E_H$) will be setup across the sample. Hence at equilibrium condition, the force directed downwards due to magnetic field will be equal to the upward electric force, i.e.

$eE_H = Bev$

$e(V_H/d) = Bev$

$V_H = Bvd$ ...13.19

Here $v$ is drift velocity, which can be expressed by the relation

$I = -nevA$ ...13.20

Where $n$ is number of electrons per unit volume and $A$ is the area of cross-section of the conductor. The area of the cross-section in the sample is $A=td$. So from equation 13.19 and 13.20 we get

$V_H = -\frac{Bi}{net}$ ...13.21

We can take some typical values for copper and silicone to see the order of magnitude of $V_H$. For copper $n = 10^{29} m^{-3}$ and for Si, $n = 10^{25} m^{-3}$. Hence the Hall voltages at $B = 1 T$ and $i = 10 A$ and $t = 1 mm$ for copper and Silicone are, 0.6 $\mu V$ and 6 $mV$ respectively. The Hall voltage is much more measurable in semiconductor than in metals i.e. Hall effect is more effective in semiconductor.

Recalling equation 13.21 and expressing in terms of current density and Hall field we get,

$\frac{E_H}{JB} = \frac{1}{ne}$

Where $\frac{E_H}{JB}$ is called Hall Coefficient ($R_H$). It is negative for free electron and positive for holes in semiconductors. In some cases, it has been found that $R_H$ is positive for metal. It also implies that the charge carriers are positive rather than negative. Hence we have

$R_H = -\frac{1}{ne}$ ...13.22

The Table below gives the Hall coefficients of a number of metals and semiconductors at room temperature with number of electrons per unit volume. The unit of $R_H$ is $m^3/Coulomb$.

Table 1
Hall Coefficients and Number of electrons per unit volume of Materials
Materials $n(10^{28} m^{-3})$ $R_H(10^{-11})$
Na 2.50 - 25.0
K 1.50 - 42.0
Cu 11.00 - 5.5
Ag 7.40 - 8.4
Al 21.00 - 3.0
Sb 0.31 +2300.0
Be 2.60 +244.0
Zn 19.00 +3.3
Si 1.50x10^{-7}

If the steady electric field $E$ is maintained in a conductor by applying a external voltage across it, the carriers of current attains a drift velocity $v$. The drift velocity acquired in unit applied electric field is known as the mobility of the carrier and is denoted by $\mu$ and is also called Hall mobility. So we have

$\mu_H = \frac{v}{E} = \frac{J}{neE} = \sigma R_H = \frac{R_H}{\rho}$ ...13.23

Thus by measuring the resistivity of the materials and knowing the Hall coefficient, electron mobility can be calculated.

Another variable which is often used to relate the Hall effect is the ratio of current density along y-axis and current density along x-axis. This ratio is called Hall angle and is denoted by $\Theta$. Hence we have,

$\Theta = \frac{J_y}{J_x} = \sigma \frac{E_y}{J_x} = \mu_H B_z = \sigma R_H B_z$ ...13.24

The Hall angle measures the average number of radians traversed by a particle between collisions.

Again from equation 13.21, we get

$R = \frac{V_H}{i} = \frac{B}{net}$ ...13.25

The quantity $R$ has dimension of resistance, though it is not resistance in conventional sense. It is commonly called Hall resistance. From this relation it is expected to increase Hall resistance linearly with the increase of magnetic field, however, German Physicist Klaus Von Klitzing in 1980 in his experiment showed that the Hall resistance did not increase linearly with the field, instead, the plot showed a series of stair steps as shown in figure 13.9. Such effect has become known as the quantized Hall effect and Klaus was

Figure 13.9 Quantized Hall effect


[Page 142]
282 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

awarded the 1985 Nobel Prize in Physics for his discovery.

The Hall effect has many applications. It is used to accurate measurement of magnetic field, Hall mobility etc. It is also used to determine the nature of materials . It is also used to determine whether the specimen is metal, semiconductor or insulator.

Example:13.8
A copper strip 2cm wide and 1.0 mm thick is placed in a magnetic field 1.5 T. If a current of 200 A is setup in the strip calculate (i) Hall voltage and (ii) Hall mobility if the number of electrons per unit volume is 8.4x10^28 m^-3 and resistivity is 1.72x10^-8 ohm-m.

Solution:
(i) We have,
VH = Bi / net = 200x1.5 / (1.6x10^-19 x 8.4x10^28 x 1x10^-3)
= 22uV
(ii) The Hall mobility (μH) = RH/ρ = 1 / nep = 1 / (8.4x10^28 x 1.6x10^-19 x 1.72x10^-8)
= 231.2 m^2V^-1S^-1

Example 13.9
A specimen of semiconductor has a Hall coefficient of 3.66x10^-4 m^3/C and resistivity of 8.93x10^-3 Ohm-m. In a Hall effect experiment if a magnetic flux density of 0.5 T is used, find the Hall angle.

Solution:
We have the Hall angle by definition is
Θ = Jy/Jx = Ey/Jx = RHB / ρ = (3.66x10^-4 x 0.5) / 8.93x10^-3
= 1.1°

Example 13.10
A rectangular copper strip 1.5 cm wide and 0.10 cm thick carries a current of 5.0 A. Find the Hall voltage for a 1.2 T magnetic field applied in a direction perpendicular to the strip.

Solution:
If we assume that one electron per atom is available for condition, we can take the charge carrier density to be 8.49x10^28 electrons/m^3. Substituting this value and the given data into the equation, gives
Δ VH = IB / nqt
= (5.0A)(1.2T) / (8.49x10^28 m^-3)(1.6x10^-19 C)(0.0010m)
Δ VH = 0.44 µ V
Such an extreme small Hall voltage is expected in good conductors. (Note that the width of the conductor is not needed in this calculation.)

What if the strip has the same dimensions but is made of a semiconductor? Will the Hall voltage be smaller or larger?

Solution:
In semiconductors, n is much smaller than it is in metals that contribute one electron per atom to the current; hence, the Hall voltage is usually larger because it varies as the inverse of n. Currents on the order of 0.1 mA are generally used for such materials. Consider a piece of silicon that has the same dimensions as the copper strip in this example and whose value for n is 1.0x10^20 electrons/m^3. Taking B = 1.2 T and I = 0.10 mA. we find that Δ VH = 7.5 mV. A potential difference of this magnitude is readily measured.

Electromagnetism 283

13.5 Cyclotron and Synchrotron

The cyclotron is a particle accelerator which is also known as Lawrence Cyclotron, As it was conceived by Lawrence in 1929. A cyclotron consists of two large dipole magnets designed to produce a semicircular region of uniform magnetic field, pointing uniformly downward. Because of their D-shape these are called D's. The two D's are placed back-to-back with their straight sides parallel but slightly separated, as shown in figure 13.10.

Now in order to produce an electric field across this gap, we apply an oscillating voltage. Particles, which are injected into the magnetic field region of a D, trace out a semicircular path until they reach the gap.

However, as the particles pass across-the gap they are accelerated by the applied electric field, After gaining energy, these particles follow a semicircular path in the next D with larger radius. Then they reach the gap again but with the opposite direction of motion. Hence, the electric field frequency must be such that the direction of the field is reversed by the time of arrival of the particles at the gap. This way the particles are accelerated by the field in the gap. After gaining energy, these particles enter the first D again. Because of larger speed they trace a larger arc and so they always take the same time to reach the gap. This way a constant frequency electric field oscillation continues to always accelerate the particles across the gap. However, there is a limitation on the energy that can be achieved in such a device. This depends on the size of the magnets that form the D's and the strength of their magnetic field.

The cyclotron uses electric and magnetic fields and the whole accelerator remains in a uniform magnetic field. In this accelerator, charged particles moving in the field feel a force acting at 90° to their direction of motion. Hence, they move in circle. Here the Lorentz force due to the magnetic field provides the necessary centripetal force for the circular motion with radius R. It means for a charged particle of charge q and mass m circulating with velocity v,

q vB = mv^2 / R

or, R = (m / qB)v ... 13.26

This equations says that the particles move in circles with radii proportional to their speeds. Hence the time period of this motion

T = 2πR / v = 2π/v (m / qB)v = 2πm / qB

Inverse of this time period gives the cyclotron frequency as


[Page 143]
284 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

f = qB / 2πm

Clearly, it is not the function of the velocity v. This expression also shows that similar particles of same charge and masses will all orbit at the same rate, regardless of their speed.

The key to the operation of the cyclotron is that the characteristic frequency f at which the ion circulates in the field must be equal to the fixed frequency f0 of the oscillator

or, f = f0 = qB / 2πm ... 13.27

This resonance condition says that if the energy of the circulating ion is to increase energy must be fed to it at a frequency f0 that is equal to the natural frequency f at which the ion circulates in the field.

The particles' orbits inside two metal semicircles are called Dees. These are connected to opposite terminals of an ac supply which is set to the cyclotron frequency calculated above. The particles are accelerated in bunches by the electric field between the plates. Since the cyclotron frequency is same for all the particles, they all will be accelerated by the field.

It is clear from R = mv / qB that the accelerated particles will move in larger and larger circles until reaching a deflector at the edge of the machine which directs them to the target.

The maximum speed of the particles corresponding to maximum radius of the circle would be vmax = qBRmax / m. Therefore, Maximum energy of the particles would be

1/2 mv^2 max = q^2 B^2 R^2 max / 2m

It is clear from this expression that for obtaining high energy, the strength of the magnetic field should be large and the radius of machine also be as large as possible. Since all the particles must orbit at the same frequency, whatever their speed, the basic design of this accelerator is the real limiting factor. It is seen that as particles approach the speed of light, they behave as if their mass is increasing. Therefore, their frequency becomes less hence they start to lag behind the oscillating electric field.

The cyclotrons fails to operate at high energies because one of its assumptions, that the frequency of rotation of an ion circulating in a magnetic field is independent of its speed, is true only for speeds much less than that of light. As the particle speed increases, we must use the relativistic mass m in equation 13.27. The relativistic mass increases with velocity so that at high enough speeds v decreases with velocity. Thus the ions get out of step with the electric oscillator, and eventually the energy of the circulating ion stops increasing.

Another difficulty associated with the acceleration of particles to high energies is that the size of the magnet that would be required to guide such particle in a circular path is very large. For a 30-Bev proton, for example, in a field of 15000 gauss the radius of curvature is 65 meters. The magnet of the cyclotron type of this size (about 430 ft in diameter) would be

Electromagnetism 285

prohibitively expensive. Incidentally, a 30-Bev proton has a speed equal to 0.99998 that of light.

Both the relativistic and the economic limitation have been removed by techniques that can be understood in terms of equation 13.27 in which m is now taken to be the relativistic mass, given by equation 13.27,

m = m0 / √(1 - v^2/c^2)

v being the speed of the particle and c being that of light.

As the particle speed increases, the relativistic mass m also increases. To maintain the equality in equation 13.27, and thus insure resonance, one may decrease the oscillator frequency f0 as the particle (assumed to be a proton) accelerates in such a way that the product fo m remains constant. Accelerators that used this technique are called synchrocyclotrons.

To remove the magnet cost limitation one can vary both B and f0 in a cyclic fashion in such a way that not only is equation 13.27 satisfied at all times but the particle orbit radius remains constant during the acceleration process. This permits the use of annular (or ring-shaped) magnet, rather than the conventional cyclotron type, at great saving in cost. With the two variables B and f0 at our disposal, it is possible to preserve two equalities during the acceleration process one being equation 13.27 and the other being the relation

f = v / (2π R0) = R0

in which R0 is the desire (fixed) orbit radius. Accelerators that use this technique are called synchrotrons.

Example: 13.11

What radius is needed in proton synchrotron to atom particle energy of 10 Gev, assuming that guide field of 1.8 wb/m^2 is available?

Solution:

The rest mass of proton is 1.00752 mu, so its corresponding energy.
M0 c^2 = M0 x 931 Mev
= M0 x 0.931 Gev
= (1.00752 x 0.931) Gev
= 0.938 Gev
Where M0 is in atomic mass unit (u).
Hence the total energy required = (10 + 0.938) Gev and equivalent mass of proton
= 11.75 unit = 11.75 x 1.66 x 10^-27 Kg

Hence, r = mv / Be = (11.75 x 1.66 x 10^-27 x 3 x 10^8) / (1.8 x 1.6 x 10^-19)
= 20.31 m.


[Page 144]
286 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

13.6 Sources of Magnetic Field

A basic fact of magnetism is that current generates magnetic field and magnetic field exerts force on current. Calculation of magnetic field due to currents is our aim. We use Biot and Savart law to calculate the magnetic field produced at some point by small current element. Using this formalism and principle of superposition, we then calculate total magnetic field due to various current distributions. Ampere's law is also used in calculating the field of a highly symmetric configuration carrying a steady current.

13.7 The Blot - Savart Law

Jean - Baptiste Biot and Felix Savart derived a mathematical expression after their experiment to calculate magnetic field due to a current element. Figure 13.11 shows a wire of arbitrary shape carrying current I. We want to find magnetic field B at a nearby point P. We first mentally divide the wire into differential elements dl whose length is dl and whose direction is the direction of current. The magnetic field dB due to small current element I dl at a point P is given by

dB = μ₀ / 4π * I dl sin θ / r²

or,

dB = μ₀ / 4π * (I dl × r̂) / r² ... 13.28

Where μ₀ is permeability of air = 4π × 10⁻⁷ H/m.

The total magnetic field B produced by the whole length of the conductor is

B = μ₀ / 4π * ∫ (I dl × r̂) / r² ... 13.29

Where θ is the angle between the direction of dl and r.
The Biot-Savart's law gives the magnetic field of an isolated electric current element at some point but such an isolated current element cannot exist the way an isolated electric charge. The direction of the magnetic field is determined using right – hand thumb rule, as shown in figure 13.12.

13.8 Magnetic Field on the Axis of a Circular Loop

Figure 13.13 shows a circular conductor with radius R that carries a current I. To find the magnetic field at a point P on the axis of the loop, at a distance x from the centre, we can use Biot-Savart law. Accordingly, consider an element dl of the coil at right angles to the plane of paper. This sets up of field dB at P, in the plane of the paper and right angles to the radius vector r. The magnitude dB of the field due to the element dl is

dB = μ₀ I dl sin(π/2) / 4πr²

= μ₀ I dl / 4π(x² + R²) ... 13.30

The components of the vector dB are: dBₓ = dB sin θ along axial line and dBᵧ = dB cos θ at right angles to the axis.

If we now consider to element dl' diametrically opposite to dl', we see that it sets up a field dB' equal in magnitude to dB. This also has a component dB' sin θ and therefore cancels it. By considering elements such as dl and dl' all round the circumference of the coil, we see that the field at P can have no component at right angles to the axis.

To obtain x-component of the total field B, we integrate equation 13.30 including all the dl all around the loop. Everything in this expression except dl is constant and can be taken outside the integral, and we have

Bx = ∫ dB sin θ = μ₀I / 4π * ∫₀²πR dl / (x² + R²) * R / √(x² + R²)

Bx = μ₀ I R² / 2(x² + R²)³/² ... 13.31

Now suppose that instead of the single loop in figure 13.13, we have a coil consisting of N loops, all with the same radius, then total magnetic field is given by;

Bₙₓ = μ₀ N I R² / 2(x² + R²)³/² ... 13.32


[Page 145]
288 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
Figure 13.14 shows a graph B, as a function of x. The maximum value of field is at x = 0, i.e. at the centre of loop Bmax will be,
B_0 = mu_0NI / 2R ... 13.33
We can express equation 13.32 in terms of dipole moment
mu = NIA
= NI pi R^2
so B_x = mu_0 mu / 2pi(x^2+R^2)^(3/2) ... 13.34
If x >> R, then
B_x = mu_0 mu / 2pi x^3 ... 13.35
This result shows that a circular current carrying coil behaves as a magnetic dipole for a large distance. In other words, if we look the circular coil from large distance, I appears as a dipole.

• 13.9 Magnetic Field due to a Curved Wire Segment
The wire shown in figure 13.15 carries a current I and consists of three segments; AA', BB' and AB where AA' and BB' are straight sections and AB is circular arc of radius R and constant angle pi/2 rad. Let B1, B2 and B3 be magnetic field at P due to section AA', BB' and AB respectively. The angle between dl and r is zero for straight sections AA' and BB' and hence the product dl x r = 0. Thus the magnetic fields due to these sections are zero, i.e.
B1 = B2 = 0
However at every point on AB, dl is perpendicular to r i.e. dl x r = dl. Hence using Biot-Savart law, the magnetic field at P due to segment AB is,
dB3 = mu_0 / 4pi * (I dl sin 90deg / R^2) = mu_0 I dl / 4pi R^2
For the arc AB we have dl = R dtheta
So we have B3 = mu_0 I / 4pi R^2 * integral_0^(pi/2) (R dtheta) = mu_0 I / 4pi R * (pi/2)
B3 = mu_0 I / 8 R ... 13.36

Electromagnetism 289
Thus the total magnetic field at P due to the wire is
B = B1 + B2 + B3 = mu_0 I / 8 R ... 13.37
The direction of the field is into the plane of the page which can be determined by using right-hand thumb rule to the section AB.

Example: 13.12
A long circular coil consisting of 100 turns with diameter 1.2 m carries a current of 5 A. (a) Find the magnetic field at a point along the axis 80 cm from the centre (b) At what distance from the centre, along the axis, is the field magnitude 1/8 as great as at the centre?
Solution:
(a) from equation 13.4 we have for N - turns
B_x = (mu_0 N I R^2) / (2(x^2 + R^2)^(3/2)) = (4pi x 10^-7 x 100 x 5 x (0.6)^2) / (2((0.8)^2 + (0.6)^2)^(3/2)) = 1.1 x 10^-4 T
This is very low field in comparison to the magnetic field of the earth.

(b) Let x be distance from the centre. From given condition we have.
B at x = 1/8 B at the centre
or, B_x = 1/8 B_0
or, (mu_0 N I R^2) / (2(x^2 + R^2)^(3/2)) = 1/8 * (mu_0 N I R^2) / (2R^3)
i.e. x = +- R sqrt(3)
= 1.73 R = 1.73 x 0.6 = 1.038
x = 1.04 m.

Example: 13.13
A straight wire segment of length l carries a current i. Show that the magnitude of magnetic field B produced by the segment at a distance y from it along a perpendicular bisector is
B = mu_0 / 2pi * I / y * l / sqrt(l^2 + 4y^2) ... 13.38
Solution:
The magnitude of the differential magnetic field produced at P by the current, length element I dx located a distance r from P is given by
dB = mu_0 I / 4pi * (dx sin theta) / r^2
The direction of dB in figure 13.16 is that of the vector dl x r namely, directed into page. Further dB at point P has this same direction for all the current length elements into which the wire can be divided. Thus
B = 2 integral_0^(l/2) dB

Figure 13.16 Magnetic field producing a current in straight wire
