B = μ0i/2π ∫ sin θ dx/r^2
and from figure 13.16
r = √(x^2 + y^2)
sin θ = sin (π - θ) = y/√(x^2 + y^2)
Thus B = μ0i/2π ∫ y dx / (x^2 + y^2)^3/2
= μ0i/2π [x / y(x^2 + y^2)^1/2] (limits) = μ0i/2πy [l / (l^2 + 4y^2)^1/2]
If l → ∞, the value reduces to long straight conductor.
i.e. B = μ0i / 2πy ... 13.39

13.10 Ampere's Law
In the calculation of magnetic flux density, another simple law called Ampere's law, analogous to that of Gauss law for electric fields is more useful. Biot-Savart law is relation between a current distribution and magnetic field that it generates. However, Ampere's law is more simple and compatible with other equations of electromagnetism. This law is applied to a closed loop called an Amperian loop similar to Gaussian loop in electrostatic. We can find net magnetic field due to any distribution of currents with Biot-Savart law and also with Ampere's law but we have to use a computer for a complicated distribution. However, if the distribution has some symmetry, we may be able to apply Ampere's law to find the magnetic field with considerably less effort. Ampere's law can be derived from the Biot-Savart law.

Ampere's law is formulated in terms of the line integral of magnetic flux density B around a closed path of a current - carrying conductor as shown in figure 13.17. We divide the Amperian loop into differential line segments of length dl. At this line element, the magnetic field due to the current will have particular value B. Because of symmetry B must lie in the plane of the loop making an angle θ with the direction of dl. Hence the quantity B.dl on the line integral is a scalar product and has the value
B.dl = B dl cosθ
The magnetic field of a long current carrying conductor at a distance r from Biot-Savart law is
B = μ0i / 2πr

Electromagnetism 291
and from figure 13.17 we have dl cos θ = r dφ, where dφ is the angle subtended by all at the position of the conductor and r. Thus the line integral is
∮ B.dl = ∮ B dl cosθ = ∮ μ0i/2πr * r dφ = μ0i/2π ∮ dφ
But ∮ dφ is just the total angle swept out by the radial line from the conductor to dl during a complete trip around the path and is equal to 2π, So we get
∮ B.dl = μ0 i ... 13.40

This result for the line integral is independent of the shape of the path or on the position of conductor inside it and also called Ampere's circuital law or Ampere's theorem. It is also noted that the net change in φ during the trip around the integration path is zero i.e. ∮ dφ = 0. For several long straight conductors, magnetic field at any point is vector sum of field produced by individual conductors and the line integral of total field is equal to μ0 times the algebraic sum of the current. The direction of the field is determined using right-hand thumb rule.

Thus Ampere's law can be stated as " line integral of B around a closed path is equal to μ0 times the current enclosed by the path". i.e.
∮ B.dl = μ0 Iencl ... 13.41

Where Iencl is the net current encircled by the loop. Equation 13.41 is valid only for steady current and uniform magnetic field invariable with time.

13.11 Magnetic Field Outside and Inside a Long Straight Wire Carrying Current
Figure 13.18 (a) shows a cylindrical conductor with radius a that carries a steady current i. The current is uniformly distributed over the cross-sectional area of the conductor. Because the wire has a high degree of symmetry, we can use Ampere's law to find magnetic field as a function of the distance r from the conductor axis for points both inside (r < a) and outside (r > a) the conductor.


[Page 147]
292 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

(1) Outside (r > a)
Figure 13.18 (b) shows cross-section of a cylindrical conductor that carries uniformly distributed current i, directed out of the page. To find magnetic field for the region r > a, we draw a circular Amperian loop of radius r (r > a) that encloses the conductor as shown in figure 13.18 (b). From symmetry, B must be constant in magnitude and parallel to dl at every point on this circle. If i be the total enclosed current inside the loop then Ampere's law gives,

∮B.dl = B ∮dl = B (2 πr) = µ₀ i

Then
B = µ₀i / 2πr for r > a ... 13.42

Which is identical to the relation (13.40) as obtained from Biot-Savart law.

Figure 13.18 (c) To find magnetic field in the region r < a, draw a circular Amperian loop.
Figure 13.18 (d) Magnitude of B as a function of r.

(2) Inside (r < a)
To find the magnetic field at a point inside the wire we can again use an Amperian loop of radius r as shown in figure 13.18 (c). Let i' be the current enclosed by the Amperian loop, then from Ampere's law

∮B.dl = µ₀ i' ... 13.43

We note current density, J is constant because the current is uniformly distributed over the conductor, Thus

J = i / πa² = i' / πr²

∴ i' = (r/a)² i

Hence from equation 13.43, we have,

B . 2 πr = µ₀ (r/a)² i

i.e. B = (µ₀i / 2πa²) r ... 13.44

B is zero at the centre and the maximum at the surface r = a. Figure 13.18 (d) shows a graph of B as a function of r, both inside and outside the conductor.

13.12 Gauss Law in Magnetism
According to Gauss law in electrostatics, the number of electric lines of force leaving the surface depends only on the net charge within it, i.e. the lines of force originate and terminate on the electric charge. But for magnetic fields situation is quite different i.e. they are continuous and form closed loop (they do not begin or end at any point). Hence for any closed surface, the number of lines entering the surface equals the number leaving the surface, so the net magnetic flux is zero. Hence Gauss law in magnetism states that:

∮B.ds = 0 ... 13.45

This law is based on the experimental fact that magnetic monopoles have never been detected.

Electromagnetism 293


[Page 148]
294 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

13.13 Additional Examples:

1. An electron moves through a uniform magnetic field given by B = Bx i + (3.0 Bx) j. At a particular instant, the electron has velocity v = (2.0 i + 4.0 j) m/s and the magnetic force acting on it is (6.4 × 10^-19 N) k. Find Bx.

Solution:
Using Force on a current carrying conductor in magnetic field, we obtain
F = q (vx By - vy Bx) k = q[vx(3Bx) - vyBx] k
Using By = 3 Bx
But the force (at the instant considered) is Fzk with Fz = 6.4 × 10^-19 N,
Hence, q (3vx - vy) Bx = Fz
or, Bx = Fz / q(3vx - vy)
Substituting vx = 2 m/s, vy = 4 m/s and q = -1.6 × 10^-19 C, we obtain
Bx = 6.4 × 10^-19 N / (-1.6 × 10^-19 C) [3(2 m/s) - 4 m/s]
= - 2 T.

2. An electron has an initial velocity of (12 i + 15 k) km/s and a constant acceleration of (2 × 10^11 m/s²) i in a region in which uniform electric and magnetic fields are present. If B = (100 µT) i, find the electric field E.

Solution:
Let's apply F = q (E + v × B) = m, a to solve for E:
E = (m a / q) - (v × B)
= (9.11 × 10^-31 kg (2 × 10^12 m/s²) / -1.6 × 10^-19 C) + (400 µT) i × [(12 j) km/s + (15 k) km/s)]
= (-11.4 i - 6 j + 4.8 k) V/m.

3. In a Hall-effect experiment, a current of 3 A sent lengthwise through a conductor 1 cm wide, 4 cm long, and 10 µm thick produces a transverse (across the width) Hall potential difference of 10 µV when a magnetic field of 1.5 T is passed perpendicularly through the thickness of the conductor. From these data, find (a) the drift velocity of the charge carriers and (b) the number density of charge carriers.

Solution:
(a) At equilibrium eE = B e vd
Drift velocity vd = E / B
= (10^-5 V) / (10^-2 m) (1.5 T) = 6.7 × 10^-4 m/s.
(b) According to equation 13.21 we have,
n = Bi / v e l
n = (1.5 T) (3 A) / (6.7 × 10^-4 m/s) (1.6 × 10^-19 C) (10^-5 m) = 2.8 × 10^29 /m³.

Electromagnetism 295
An electron follows a helical path in a uniform magnetic field of magnitude 0.3 T. The pitch of the path is 6 µm, and the magnitude of the magnetic force on the electron is 2 × 10^-15 N. What is the electron's speed?

Solution:
We knew that if the velocity of charged particle has a component parallel to the uniform magnetic field, the particle will move in a helical path about the direction of the field vector. Figure AE 4 (a) for example shows the velocity vector v result into two components; one parallel to B and one perpendicular to it:
Figure AE 4 (a)

v|| = v cosθ and v⊥ = v sinθ
The parallel component determines the pitch p of the helix - that is the distance between adjacent turns in figure AE 4 (b). From figure AE 4 (b),
p = v|| T ...I
where T is the time period which is obtained by equating magnetic force with centripetal force i.e.
B e v⊥ = mv²/r and T = 2πr / v⊥
T = 2πm / eB ...II
from equation I
v|| = p / T = peB / 2πm
= 6 × 10^-6 × 1.6 × 10^-19 × 0.3 / 2π × 9.1 × 10^-31
= 50.3 km/s.
Since magnetic force is F = B e v⊥
v⊥ = F / Be = 2 × 10^-15 / 0.3 × 1.6 × 10^-19 = 41.7 km/s.
The speed is therefore v = √v||² + v⊥² = 65.3 km/s.

5. In a certain cyclotron a proton moves in a circle of radius 0.5 m. The magnitude of the magnetic field is 1.2 T. (a) What is the oscillator frequency? (b) What is the kinetic energy of the proton, in electron-volts?

Solution:
(a) The frequency of oscillation of a cyclotron is given by equation 13.27,
fosc = qB / 2πmp = (1.6 × 10^-19 C) (1.2 T) / 2π (1.67 × 10^-27 kg) = 1.83 × 10^7 Hz
(b) Radius R of the circular path is given by equation 13.26, we have,
R = mp v / qB but kinetic energy of the proton is given by k = ½ mp v²
or, R = √2mp k / qB
k = (RqB)² / 2mp = [(0.5 m) (1.6 × 10^-19 C) (1.2 T)]² / 2 (1.67 × 10^-27 kg) (1.6 × 10^-19 J/eV) = 1.72 × 10^7 eV.


[Page 149]
296 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
6. A circular coil of 160 turns has a radius of 1.9 cm. (a) Calculate the current that results in a magnetic dipole moment of magnitude 2.3 A-m². (b) Find the maximum magnitude of the torque that the coil, carrying this current, can experience in a uniform 35 mT magnetic field.
Solution:
(a) The magnitude of the magnetic dipole moment is given by µ = NiA, where N is the number of turns, i is the current in each turn, and A is the area of a loop. In this case the loops are circular, so A = πr², where r is the radius of a turn. Thus
i = µ / Nπr² = 2.3 A·m² / (160)(π)(0.019m)² = 12.7 A.
(b) The maximum torque occurs when the dipole moment is perpendicular to the field and is given by
Tmax = µB = (2.3 A m²)(35 × 10⁻³ T) = 8.05 × 10⁻² N m.

7. In given figure AE 7, a wire forms a semicircle of radius R = 0.096 cm and two (radial) straight segments each of length L = 18.1 cm. The wire carries current i = 34 mA. What are the (a) magnitude and (b) direction of the net magnetic field at the semicircle's centre of curvature C?
Solution:
(a) Recalling the straight sections discussion in section 13.9, we see that the current in the straight segments collinear with C do not contribute to the field at that point. Equation 13.36 (with θ = π) indicates that the current in the semicircular arc contributes (µ₀i / 4R) to the field at C. Thus, the magnitude of the magnetic field is
B = µ₀i / 4R = (4π×10⁻⁷ T·m/A)(0.0348A) / 4(0.0926m) = 1.18 × 10⁻⁷ T.
(b) The right-hand rule shows that this field is into the page.

8. A solenoid 1.3 m long and 2.6 cm in diameter carries a current of 18 A. The magnetic field inside the solenoid is 23 mT. Find the length of the wire forming the solenoid.
Solution:
Magnetic field inside the solenoid is given by B = µ₀i n = µ₀iN / l
∴ N = Bl / µ₀i
Thus, the total length of wire used in making the solenoid is
2πrN = 2πrBl / µ₀i = 2π(2.6×10⁻² m)(23×10⁻³ T)(1.3m) / 2(4π×10⁻⁷ T·m/A)(18A) = 108m.

9. A long wire is known to have a radius greater than 4 mm and to carry a current that is uniformly distributed over its cross section. The magnitude of the magnetic field due to that current is 0.28 mT at a point 4 mm from the axis of the wire and 0.20 mT of a point 10 mm from the axis of the wire. What is the radius of the wire?
Solution:
Let B₁ and B₂ be magnetic fields at distance 4 mm (say point 1) and 10 mm (say point 2) respectively.
According to the question |B₂| < |B₁|. So point 2 is known to be external to the wire. Hence radius of the wire must lie in between 4 mm and 10 mm. Using equation 13.44 magnetic field inside the wire is given by
B₁ = (µ₀i / 2πa²)r ... I
and from equation 13.42, magnetic field outside the wire is given by
B = (µ₀i / 2πr) ... II

Electromagnetism 297
From equation II current
I = 2πrB₂ / µ₀ = 2π×0.010×2×10⁻⁴ / 4π×10⁻⁷ = 10A.
From equation I
a = √µ₀ir / 2πB₁ = √4π×10⁻⁷ × 10 × 0.004 / 2π×2.8×10⁻⁴ = 5.3 × 10⁻³ m.
Thus radius of the wire is 5.3 mm.

14 Chapter
Electromagnetic Induction

After Ampere's investigation of magnetic effect of a current and motor effect, Faraday, a British Physicist attempted to find its converse effect. He tried to produce an electric current by a magnetic field or mechanical energy. He started working on this problem in 1825 and did not succeed until 1831 and independently by Joseph Henry in United States showed than an emf can be induced in a circuit by a changing magnetic field. The results of the experiments led to a very basic and important law of electromagnetism.

When a magnet is moved to a loop, as shown in figure 14.1, the galvanometer needled deflects in one direction, and if the magnet is moved away from the loop, the needle deflects in opposite direction. No deflection in the galvanometer needle is observed if the magnet kept at rest.

Figure 14.1 Induction experiments
Figure 14.2 Induction Experiments

In another way, if battery B and switch (k) are connected to primary coil and galvanometer in secondary coil through a rod figure 14.2, secondary coil detects electric currents even primary and secondary coils are not connected electrically.

These experiments result that an electric current is set up even through no batteries on present in the circuit.

From these experiments it can be concluded that:
(i) The galvanometer shows deflection until there is change in magnetic field or relative motion between coil and the magnet.
(ii) The emf induced in the circuit is directly proportional to the time rate of change of magnetic flux through the circuit.


[Page 152]
302 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

These conclusions are termed as Faraday's laws of electromagnetic induction.

The process of production of such electric current is called electromagnetic induction. The current produced in the coil or secondary coil is called induced current and corresponding emf is called induced emf.

14.1 Faraday's Law of Induction

The common element in the experiments is changing magnetic flux through the circuit and electric current is set up even though no batteries are present in the circuit. It is the reverse effect of electric motor and gives the basic concept for production of electric current or invention of electric generator. It converts mechanical energy into an electric energy.

If a conductor be moving with velocity v in a magnetic field as shown in figure 14.3 then the force experienced by the charge particle q of the conductor is

F = q (v x B)

or, F/q = v x B

The non-electrostatic force per unit charge is called non-electrostatic field, E. So,

E = v x B

From definition the induced emf ε = - ∮ E.dr = - ∮ (v x B).dr

ε = - ∮ (dr x B) dr = - d/dt ∮ (r x dr) B

Where r x dr gives the surface area ds swept out by the conductor, so

ε = - d/dt ∮ B.ds = - dΦB / dt ... 14.1

Hence the Faraday's law of electromagnetic induction states that "the induced emf in the circuit equals the negative of the time rate of change of magnetic flux through the circuit." This is the principle of electric generator, the direction of mechanical force, electric current and magnetic field is determined by Fleming's Left Hand Rule. The minus sign in 14.1 shows that a decrease in the flux linkage results in an emf that sets up an induced current. It also concludes that the unit of flux is related to that if emf in the way;

|ΔΦ| = |εΔt| = Volt x sec

1 Wb = 1 V. s

Electromagnetic Induction 303

Example 14.1
A loop of wire of radius r is placed in a uniform magnetic field perpendicular to its plane. The magnitude of the field varies with time according to B = Bo e^(-t/τ). Where Bo and τ are constant. Find the emf in the loop as a function of time.

Solution:
The plane of the loop being perpendicular to the magnetic field B, the magnetic flux through the loop at t > 0 is

ΦB = AB cos 0 = A Bo e^(-t/τ)
Hence from Faraday's law of electromagnetic induction we get

ε = - dΦB / dt = - A Bo e^(-t/τ) / τ
ε = A Bo / τ e^(-t/τ) ... 14.2

It shows that the induced emf decays exponentially in time. The maximum emf at t = 0 is

εmax = A Bo / τ

14.2 Lenz's law

In 1835, just after 4 years of Faraday's discovery of electromagnetic induction, H.F. Lenz put forward a rule to determine the direction of induced current or the meaning of the minus sign in Faraday's law. This rule is called Lenz's law which states that:
"The induced current flows always in such a direction as to oppose the change causing it."

The minus sign of Faraday's law represents the direction of emf, but Lenz's law refers to induced current which means that we can apply it only to the closed loop directed in such a way that its magnetic field acts against the cause of the induced emf. If a conductor does not form a closed circuit, then we mentally complete the circuit between the ends of the conductor and use of the Lenz's law to determine the direction of current. When using Lenz's law to find the direction of induced current, one should proceed as:

(i) Find the cause of the induced current.
(ii) assuming the induced current to opposite this cause, find the direction of the currents' magnetic field.
(iii) find the direction of the induced current from the direction of its magnetic field.

Figure 14.4 Direction of induced current

To understand Lenz's law, let us apply it first to the Faraday's experiment


[Page 153]
304 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Let a bar magnet is moved towards a stationary conducting loop as shown in figure 14.4 (I). As the magnet moves to the right towards the loop, the external magnetic flux through the loop increases with time. The induced current flows in the loop. To counter act the increase in flux due to a field toward the right, the induced current produces its own magnetic field to left as shown in figure 14.4 (II), hence, the induced current is in the direction shown. If the magnet moves to the left, (figure 14.4 (III)) its flux through the area enclosed by the loop decreases in time and induced current is produced in opposite to the first case, figure 14.4 (IV). In this case the left face of the loop is a south pole and the right face is a north pole.

Thus whether the magnet is pushed or pulled, the motion will always be opposite and the direction of induced current is also reversed.

Let a bar AB in the magnetic field moves on the rail to the left, figure 14.5 (I), the magnetic flux through the area enclosed by the circuit increases with time, because the area increases. Hence the induced current must be directed anticlockwise (use right hand rule) because the flux increases. Similarly, if the bar moves on the rail to the right, as in figure 14.5 (II), the external magnetic flux through the area enclosed by the loop decreases with time, so the direction of induced current must be clock-wise. The induced current tends to maintain the original flux through the area enclosed by the current loop.

Figure 14.5 (I) Motion of a conductor in magnetic field
Figure 14.5 (II) Motion of a conductor in magnetic field

It follows from Lenz's law that the energy of induced current in a conductor is gained at the expense of the energy spent in opposing the magnetic field of the induced current. In above experiment (figure 14.4) if you push the magnet towards the loop or pull it away from the loop, a resisting force is experienced and this has to do the work. From the principle of conservation of energy, this work must exactly be equal to the thermal energy transfers in the coil because these are the only two energy transfers that take place in this system. The faster, the magnet is moved the more rapidly work is done, and the greater the rate of production of thermal energy in the coil.

Example 14.2
A conducting rod moves with a constant velocity v in a direction perpendicular to a long straight wire carrying a current i as shown in figure 14.6, Show that magnitude of the emf generated between the ends of the rod is |ε|=μoiiv/2πr

Solution:
Let a long straight conductor carrying current i produces a magnetic field B at a distance r i.e.
B = μoi / 2πr
In this magnetic field a conducting rod moves with a constant velocity v, for which the rate of doing work is

Figure 14.6 Motion of a conducting rod.

Electromagnetic Induction 305
P = Fv = Bilv = μoi²lv / 2πr
Which will be converted into induced emf and the corresponding rate of change of electric energy is
P = |ε|i
from, work energy conversion law
|ε|i = μoi²lv / 2πr
|ε| = μoilv / 2πr ... 14.3
It shows that emf decreases with the increase of r, as we expect.

14.3 Induction and Energy Transformation

As in the experiment of figure 14.7, a straight rod of wire of length l is moving through a uniform magnetic field directed perpendicular into the plane with constant velocity v under some external force. The electrons in the conductor experience a magnetic force
F = q(v x B)
Which is directed to the length l, perpendicular to both v and B. Due to this force the electrons move to the lower end and accumulate there, leaving a net positive charge at the upper end of the conductor. As a result of such charge separation, an electric field inside the conductor is set up. This accumulation of charge at both ends remains, until the magnetic force (qvB) is balanced by the upward electric force (qE). At this point, electrons move only with random thermal motion. Thus at equilibrium,
qE = Bqv
E = vB
The corresponding potential difference produced in the conductor across the ends is
V = El = vBl ... 14.4
This shows that the potential difference is maintained between the ends of the conductor as long as the conductor continues to move through the uniform magnetic field. The polarity of the potential difference depends on the direction of motion.

Let us assume that the bar of length l has no resistance and stationary part of the closed circuit has resistance R, then the force produced on the free charge set up an induced current In this case, the rate of change of magnetic flux through the loop and the corresponding induced emf across the moving bar are proportional to the change in area of the loop. If the area enclosed by the circuit at any instant is lx, then the magnetic flux
ΦB = BA = Blx


[Page 154]
306 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
Using Faraday's law, the induced emf is
ε = -dΦB/dt = -Blv ... 14.5
Now to find the magnitude of induced current, we cannot apply the potential difference, because no battery is in the circuit. However we can use energy method. We have the induced emf ε, is the energy transferred to the moving charges in maintaining the current. The quantity iR is the energy per unit charge transferred from the moving charges to thermal energy within the loop. So from the conservation of energy, we have;
ε = iR
∴ i = ε/R
∴ i = Blv/R
Now the rate at which the thermal energy appeared in the loop when the conductor is pulled or pushed is given by
P = i²R
∴ P = B²l²v²/R ... 14.6
This is equivalent work done that the conductor is pulled through a magnetic field at constant velocity, which manifests itself as a small increase in the temperature of the loop, because the rate of work done on the rod to we have it through a magnetic field is
Papplied = Fv
= BIlv
= B²l²v²/R
Therefore, the work that we do in pulling the conductor through the magnetic field appears as thermal energy.
In short energy transformation taken place is sequence as:
Mechanical energy → Electrical energy → Thermal energy.

A helicopter has blades of length 3 m extending out from a central hub and rotating at 2 rev/s. If the vertical component of the earth's magnetic field is 50 µT, what will be the induced emf between the blade tip and central hub?
Solution:
The induced emf dε, due to the rotation of the helicopter blades in the magnetic field B is
dε = B v dr

= 50x10⁻⁶ x 2π x 2 x 3² / 2
= 2.83 x 10⁻³ V
= 2.83 mV

14.4 Induced Electric Field
When a conductor moves in a magnetic field, change in magnetic field is produced which induces an emf and a current in the conductor or in a current loop. Similar to the current and electric field in electricity, we can relate an induced current in the conducting loop to an electric field created in the conductor as a result of changing magnetic flux. It is also noted that existence of electric field is independent of the presence of any test charge which suggest that even in the absence of a conducting loop a changing magnetic field would still create an electric field in the free space.
This induced electric field is non-conservative unlike to the electrostatic field produced by stationary charge. To study the situation, a conducting loop of radius r in a magnetic field perpendicular to the plane of the loop is considered. If the magnetic field is changing with time, then from the Faraday's law, the induced emf,
ε = -dΦB/dt is produced. The induction of electric current in the conducting loop implies the presence of an induced electric field E. This electric field must be tangent to the loop. If q be the charges in the loop, moving along the direction of electric field E with force qE (see Fig. 14.8). The work done by the electric field in moving a test charge q around the loop is qε and the work done in moving the charge by the field E is
qE (2πr), so we have;
qε = qE x (2πr) ... 14.7
∴ E = ε / 2πr
Hence the line integral of this field is not zero around a closed loop; rather, it represents the work done by the field E per unit charge i.e.
∮E.dl = ε ... 14.8
If we combine Equation 14.8 with Faraday's law in Equation 14.1, we can rewrite Faraday's law as
∮E.dl = -dΦB/dt ... 14.9
This equation implies that a changing magnetic field induces an electric field.


[Page 155]
308 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

As an example, for the circular loop of figure 14.8, the flux is given by
ΦB = BA = πr²B ... 14.10
and ∮E.dI = E 2π r ... 14.11

From Equation 14.9 and 14.10, we have
E = 1/2πr dΦB/dt
E = -r/2 dB/dt ... 14.12

Thus a changing magnetic field acts as a source of electric field, but that differs from an electrostatic field in two ways: It is non-conservative, and its integral over the closed surface is zero. It may also be noted that this situation is analogous to that of displacement current to be discussed in section 14.10, in which a changing electric field acts as a source of magnetic field.

Example 14.4
A long solenoid of radius "a" has n turns / m and carries a sinusoidally varying current I = I0 sin ωt. Determine the magnitude of induced electric field inside and outside the solenoid at a distance r from its long central axis.

(Diagrams omitted)

Solution:-
(i) For inside the solenoid r < a. figure 14.9 (b) shows the cross-sectional view of the solenoid. The magnetic flux within the integration loop is
ΦB = BA = B πr²
Thus the induced emf is
ε = - dΦB/dt = d(Bπr²)/dt
= -πr² dB/dt
For solenoid B = μ0 n I0 sin ωt
ε = -πr² μ0 n I0 ω cos ωt
From Equation 14.9
∮E.dl = -πr² μ0 n I0 ω cos ωt
E × 2πr = -πr² μ0 n I0 ω cos ωt
E = (μ0 n I0 ωr/2) (-r cos ωt)

(ii) For outside the solenoid we have, r > a and within the loop, the magnetic flux,
ΦB = BA = μ0 n I π a²

ε = - π μ0 n I0 a² d(sin ωt)/dt
= -π ω μ0 n I0 a² cos ωt
ε = ∮E.dl = -πa² μ0 n I0 ω cos ωt
E × 2πr = -πa² μ0 n I0 ω cos ωt
E = (μ0 n I0 ωa²) / 2r (- cos ωt) ... 14.13
Hence we conclude that the magnitude of electric field induced inside the solenoid increases with r and falls off as 1/r outside it varying sinusoidally with time.

14.5 Eddy current
The electric current induced inside a bulk of a solid metal placed in variable magnetic field is called eddy current. It was first studied by French physicist J.B. Foucault's, so in honor of his work it is called Foucault current.

The rotor of an electric motor and core of a transformer operate in variable magnetic field; so eddy current should circulate through them. The energy spent on inducing eddy current is transformed into the internal energy of the rotor and the core that is into heat. Since such parts are made of ferromagnetic materials, losses due to eddy current are not the only loss others being due to hysteresis. To reduce the harmful effects of eddy current parts designed to work in variable magnetic fields are made of laminated core. But ferrite has very high specific resistance. So virtually no eddy currents are induced which appreciably reduces losses.

If eddy currents are the result of the motion of a body in a magnetic field, then in compliance with Lenz's law, these currents should arrest the motion of the body, Eddy currents have many practical uses. These are used in balance as a eddy current braking, rotating machines, rapid - transit turns, heat treatment of parts, melting alloys in induction furnaces metal detectors etc. Eddy current devices are also used to locate buried treasure such as bottle caps and lost moneys.

Example 14.5
A rectangular loop of a conductor having mass m, resistance R, length l and breath b falls from rest into magnetic field B. During the time interval before the top edge of the loop reaches the field, the loop approaches a terminal speed vt. Show that vt = mgR / B²b².
Also explain significance of the relation.

Solution:
When the loop of top edge reaches the magnetic field, circulating current called eddy currents are induced in the bulk piece. The current i measured by relation
i = ε/R
Where ε is induced emf flux and R is resistance. In such case the induced emf is (see figure 14.10)
ε = Bbv
During the fall the lower edge of the figure, loop experienced an opposing or retarding force due to the current which makes the velocity of the loop terminal i.e. v = vt. At this equilibrium condition,


[Page 156]
310 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

mg = B i b
Substituting the value of i and ε writing v = vT, we have
∴ vT = m g R / B²h² ... 14.14
The terminal velocity is directly proportional to the resistance and inversely varies with square of magnetic field. (Explain why?)

14.6 Self Induction

An induced emf E appears in a coil if we change the current in the same coil as shown in figure 14.11. This phenomenon is called self induction and emf appeared is called a self induced emf. It obeys Faradays law of electromagnetic induction. This phenomenon was discovered by the American scientist Joseph Henry in 1832. When a current flows through a coil or solenoid, it sets up a magnetic field. The field threads the coil which produces it. If the current i through the coil is changed by means of variable resistor, flux linked with coil changes. An emf is then induced in the coil. As per Lenz's law, the direction of induced emf will be opposite if the current i is increasing but in the same direction if it is decreasing, see figure 14.12 (a) and (b).

Figure 14.11 if the current in a coil is changed by varying contact position on a variable resistor, a self induced emf appears in the coil while the current is changing

Figure 14.12 Direction of self induced emf

Suppose that a circuit has the self inductance of a circuit is therefore the self induced emf per unit rate of change of current.

The coefficient of self induction depends on the geometry of the coil and independent of the magnitude of the current. N turns of wire and that a flux Φ passes through each turn, then according to Henry the total magnetic flux linked to the coil is directly proportional to the current flowing through it, i.e.

NΦB ∝ i
or, NΦB = Li ... 14.15
∴ L = NΦB / i ... 14.16

Here L is constant of the coil called self-inductance. The unit of L is weber / Ampere → Henry (H). The iron - cored coil used for smoothing the rectified supply current to a TV set are usually very large and have an inductance of about 30 H.

If Φ and i change with time, then from Equation 14.15, we have

Electromagnetic Induction 311

N dΦB / dt = L di / dt and since the self induced emf ε is
ε = - N dΦB / dt it follows that
ε = - L di / dt ... 14.17

Moreover, the circuit element which stores energy in the magnetic field surrounding its current carrying wires having the inductance is called inductor. It is represented by the symbol

14.7 Mutual Inductance

The phenomenon of the production of an emf in one circuit when the current changes in another circuit is called mutual inductance. The mutual inductance, M, of two coils, figure 14.13, is defined by the equations

M = - εs / (dip / dt)
and M = Ns Φs / Ip

Figure 14.13 An alternating current IP in the primary coil creates an alternating magnetic field. This changing field induces an emf in the secondary coil.

Where Ns, Φs and Ip are number of turns of coil in secondary, flux linked with secondary and current flowing in primary coil.

Example 14.6
Calculate inductance of a solenoid having length l, number of turns N, area of cross-section A and current i is flowing.
Solution:
Let the magnetic field B inside a solenoid carrying a current i is,
B = μ0 N i / l
The total flux linkages in the length l and area A is
N ΦB = NBA = μ0 N² A i / l
Now using relation (14.16) we get
L = μ0 N² A / l
and the inductance per unit length
L/l = μ0 N² A ... 14.19


[Page 157]
312 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
The inductance like capacitance depends only on geometric factors. The dependence on the square of the number turns per unit length is to be expected.

Example: 14.7
Find an expression of the self inductance of a toroid having N number of turns, radius r and carrying current i.

Solution:
Let a toroid of rectangular cross-section area A carrying current i be considered. The external and internal radius of the toroid are b and a. The elementary part of the cross-section be dr at a resistance r from its centre and h be width of the strip. The magnetic flux through the cross-section of the toroid is
Φ = ∫ B·dA = ∫ B(hdr)
Where B = μ₀ N i / 2πr
Φ = ∫ (μ₀ N i h / 2πr) dr = (μ₀ N i h / 2π) ln (b/a)

using Equation (14.16) we get,
L = (μ₀ N² h / 2π) ln (b/a) ... 14.20
Which again shows that inductance depends only on geometric factors and number of turns.

14.8 LR Circuits
Let a resistance R and inductor L are connected in series with a battery of emf ε and switch (a,b) as shown in figure 14.14. A is ammeter which measures the flow of current.

When the switch is moved towards a, the current in the resistor starts to rise. If the inductor were not present, the current would quickly rise to a steady value ε/R. The inductance of the inductor results in a back emf, an inductor in the circuit opposes changes in the current in the circuit. The inductor attempts to keep the current same as it was before the change occurred. If the battery voltage in the circuit is increased, so that the current rises, the inductor opposes this change and so it does not rise instantaneously. If the battery voltage is decreased, the presence of the inductor results in a slow drop in the current rather than an immediate drop. Thus the inductor causes the circuit to be sluggish as it reacts to changes in the voltage.

When the switch is towards a, current flows anti-clockwise direction, and then we can apply Kirchhoff's loop rules to this circuit along anti-clockwise direction
ε = V_L + V_R
ε = L di/dt + iR
or, L di/dt + R (i - ε/R) = 0

or, di/dt = - R/L (i - ε/R)
where ε/R = i₀ = maximum steady current
and L/R = τ_L = inductive time constant = 1 / (Ω/H) = [V s / A] / [V/A] = second

Hence we have,
di / (i - i₀) = - dt / τ_L

Integrating both sides we get,
∫ di / (i - i₀) = - ∫ dt / τ_L
or, i = i₀ (1 - e^(-t/τ_L)) ... 14.21

This relation shows how the inductor affects the growth of current. The current does not increases instantly to its final equilibrium values when switch is on, but instead increases according to an exponential function. The physical significance of the time constant follows from Equation 14.21. If we put t = τ_L = L/R in this equation, it reduces to
i = i₀ (1 - e⁻¹) = 0.63 i₀

Thus time constant τ_L is the time it takes the current in the circuit to reach about 63% of its final equilibrium value i₀.

The variation of i with time (t) is shown in figure 14.15. If t → ∞, e^(-t/τ_L) → 0. The current initially increases very rapidly and then gradually approaches the equilibrium value as t → ∞, when di/dt → 0.

Thus initially, an inductor acts to oppose changes in the current through it. A long time latter, it acts like ordinary connecting wire.


[Page 158]
314 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
Now if the switch S is thrown from "a" to "b" as the current reaches i0, then decaying of current starts due to absence of battery in the circuit. Applying Kirchhoff's rule, we get,
iR + L di/dt = 0
The solution of this differential equation is
i = i0 e^(-t/τL) ... 14.22
The decay of current is shown in figure 14.16. As t increases the current decreases exponentially from i0 to zero. The decrease in current is slow if the time constant τL is large.
The time constant, L/R, is the time for the current to decrease to 1/e or about 37% of its final value.
i/i0 = e^-1 = 0.37 = 37 % of maximum current.

Example: 14.8
An inductance L is connected to a battery of emf ε through a resistance. Show that the potential difference across the inductance after time t is VL = ε e^(-(R/L)t). At what time is the potential difference across the inductance equal to that across the resistance such that i = i0/2.

Solution:
Figure 14.14 shows the circuit. The growth of current through RL circuit is
i = i0[1 - e^(-(R/L)t)] = i0[1 - e^(-(R/L)t)]
The potential difference across the inductor is
VL = L di/dt = -L i0 x (-R/L) e^(-(R/L)t)
= i0 R e^(-(R/L)t) = ε e^(-(R/L)t)
when the potential difference across the inductor is equal to that of resistance then we have,
VL = VR
or, ε e^(-(R/L)t) = iR
i.e., e^(-(R/L)t) = iR/ε
According to question i0/i = 2
e^((R/L)t) = 2
t = 0.693 L/R sec.

Electromagnetic Induction 315
14.9 Energy stored in a Magnetic Field
In a coil when the flow of current is interrupted by breaking the circuit, a spark passes across the gap and energy is liberated in the forms of heat and light. The energy has been stored in the magnetic field of the coil. If the current in the coil is first switched on, the back emf opposes the rise of current. The current flows against the back emf, so does work against it. When the current becomes steady, there is no back emf and no more work done against it. The total work done in bringing the current to its final value is stored in the magnetic field of the coil. It is liberated when the current collapses; then the induced emf tends to maintain the current and to do external work of some kind.

To calculate the energy stored in the coil, suppose an inductor is connected to a generator whose terminal voltage can be varied continuously from zero to some final value. As the voltage is increased, the current i in the circuit rises continuously from zero to its final value. While current is rising an induced back emf ε = -L di/dt appears across the inductor.
Thus, generator must do work to push the charges through the inductor against this induced emf. Then the rate at which work being done against the back-emf is
P = iε = Li di/dt
The total work done in bringing the current from zero to steady value i0 is thus
WB = ∫(0 to i0) P dt = L ∫(0 to i0) i di
WB = 1/2 Li0^2 ... 14.23
This work is stored as energy in the inductor, so that
UB = 1/2 Li^2
After the current has reached its final value i0, di/dt = 0 and the power input is zero.
When the current decreases from i0 to zero, inductor acts as a source supplying a total amount of energy to the external circuit.

The energy in the inductor is actually stored in the magnetic field in the coil, similar to the energy of a capacitor is stored in the electric field between the plates. So it is also called magnetic energy. If the inductor is solenoid, then this energy stored by the inductor must lie entirely within the volume of the solenoid. Moreover, the stored energy must be uniformly distributed through the volume of the solenoid (because B is uniform everywhere inside). Thus the energy density of the magnetic field due to the solenoid having volume Al is
uB = UB / Al = (1/2 Li^2) / Al


[Page 159]
316 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Substituting the value of inductance per unit length (l/l) of a solenoid from equation 14.19 and using the magnetic field produced by solenoid B = µ0 n I we get,
uB = B^2 / 2µ0 ... 14.24

When the material inside the solenoid is not air but a material with permeability µ, the energy density in the magnetic field would be
uB = B^2 / 2µ ... 14.25

The equation is true for all magnetic field configurations, no matter how they are generated.

Example: 14.9
Derive an expression for the energy stored in a magnetic field using law of conservation of energy in LR circuit when a battery is connected.

Solution:
Using Kirchhoff's law for LR circuit as shown in figure 14.17 we have,
E = VR + VL = iR + L di/dt
or, iE = i^2 R + Li di/dt
The instantaneous rate at which the source delivers energy to the circuit is P = i E. The instantaneous rate at which energy is dissipated in the resistor is i^2 R and the rate at which energy is stored is Li di/dt in the inductor. Hence the stored energy is
dU/dt = Li di/dt
Integrating both sides we get,
Integral dU = Integral Li di (from 0 to T)
U = Integral Li di (from 0 to T) = 1/2 LI^2
Which is the magnetic energy stored in the coil which is similar to the electric energy (1/2 CV^2) stored in a capacitor.

14.10 Induced Magnetic Field
The Faraday's law deals how a changing magnetic field induces an electric field. The electric field is called induced electric field (see equation 14.4) which is
Integral E.dl = - d(phi B)/dt ... 14.26

Electromagnetic Induction 317
Why not reverse phenomenon? So for the symmetrical counterpart we might have
Integral B.dl = - d(phi E)/dt ... 14.27

This is symmetric with (14.26) but there are two things wrong with it:
* Experiment requires positive sign in place of negative sign. This in itself is a kind of symmetry and in any case, nature requires it.
* The equation (14.27) is dimensionally not correct. To make it correct, we must insert a factor µ0 E0 on the right - hand side. Thus the correct symmetrical counter part of (14.27) is
Integral B.dl = µ0 E0 d(phi E)/dt ... 14.28

This equation is also called Maxwell's law of induction and the magnetic field B is called induced magnetic field.

Equation (14.28) concludes that a changing electric field induces a magnetic field. Ampere's law in a current carrying loop deals that a magnetic field can also be set up by a current in the wire, i.e.
Integral B.dl = µ0 i ... 14.29

This is valid only if current i is steady. In other words this is true in any electric fields present are constant in time and not used for time-varying electric fields.

We now recognize that the Ampere's circuital law is incomplete. Thus there are at least two ways of setting up a magnetic field, by means of a changing electric field and by means of a steady current. In general, we must allow for both possibilities. By in corporating the field of equations (14.26) and (14.27) we arrive at the law in its complete form:
Integral B.dl = µ0 i + µ0 E0 d(phi E)/dt ... 14.30

Maxwell had generalized this important relation. So it is also called Maxwell - Ampere's modified law.

As an example of this sort of induction, we consider the charging of parallel plate capacitor with circular plate (figure 14.18 a). We assume that the charge on the capacitor is being increased at a steady rate by a constant current i in the connecting wire. Then electric field magnitude between the plates must also be increasing at a steady rate. Figure 14.18 (b) is a view of right hand plate of figure 14.18 (a) from between the plates. The electric field is


[Page 160]
318 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

directed into the page. Let us consider a circular loop with radius smaller than that of the plate, as shown in figure 14.18 (b). Because the electric field through the loop is changing, the electric flux through the loop must also be changing. According to Equation 14.26 this changing electric flux induces a magnetic field around the loop as shown in the figure. If we now consider a larger loop, outside the plate, we find that magnetic field is induced around that loop as well. Thus we conclude that while the electric field is changing, magnetic fields are induced between the plates, both inside and outside the gap. When the electric field stops changing, theses induced magnetic field disappear.

However, the magnitude of the induced magnetic field is so small that it can scarcely measured with simple apparatus. This is in sharp contrast to the magnitude of induced electric fields (Faraday's law) which can be measured easily.

In earlier discussion in Ampere's law, we considered no changing electric fields were present so that the term dΦ/dt is zero. But in equation 14.28 we assumed that there were no conduction currents in the space containing the electric field. So the term i is zero. We cannot claim to have derived equation 14.28 from deeper principles. Although our symmetry arguments should have made this equation at least reasonable, it must stand or fall on whether or not its predictions agree with experiment.

Example: 14.10
If a parallel plate capacitor with circular plates as shown in figure 14.19 be charged, prove that the induced magnetic field at radius r in the region between the plates
B = 1/2 μ0 ε0 r dE/dt for r ≤ R and
B = 1/2 μ0 ε0 R^2/r dE/dt for r > R

Solution:
(i) For r ≤ R, using Equation (14.26) i.e.
∮ B.dl = μ0 ε0 dΦE/dt
We get, B x 2πr = μ0 ε0 πr^2 dE/dt
i.e. B = 1/2 μ0 ε0 r dE/dt ...14.31
(ii) For r > R Equation (14.26) reduces to
B x 2πr = μ0 ε0 πR^2 dE/dt
B = μ0 ε0 R^2/2r dE/dt ...14.32

14.11 Displacement Current
Let conduction current i is passed through a capacitor with circular plates as shown in figure 14.20. The current enters the positive plate and leaves the negative plate. The conduction current is not continuous across the capacitor gap because no charge is actually transported across this gap.

Electromagnetic Induction 319

However, we can imagine a current exists across the gap called displacement current. It will remained us the concept of the continuity of current through the capacitor. As the capacitor is being charged or discharged, the changing electric field between the plates may be considered equivalent to a current that acts as a continuation of the conduction current in the wire.

Let the total charge stored by a capacitor be
q = CV = ε0 A (V/d) = ε0 A E

Differentiation with time we get,
id = dq/dt = ε0 A dE/dt = ε0 dΦE/dt ...14.33

This is displacement current which is equal to the conduction current in the wires. Hence we can say that magnetic field can be set up either by conduction current i or by a displacement current id in the capacitor gap. Now from Maxwell - Ampere's law (Equation 14.30) we get;
∮ B.dl = μ0 (i + id) ...14.34

Further displacement current density JD is given by
JD = id/A = ε0 dE/dt ...14.35

When capacitor is saturated or fully charged, the conduction current drops to zero. The electric field between the plates becomes constant, so dE/dt = 0 i.e. the displacement current also drops to zero. The displacement density j a vector having certain magnitude and direction.

The word displacement used here is for historical reasons that need not concern the displacement in mechanics. The displacement current enables us to save the Ampere's law in moral situations. Another benefit of displacement current is generalization of Kirchhoff's rule in a capacitor. Example 14.10 confirms directly the role of displacement current as a source of magnetic field. It is now established beyond a reasonable doubt that id is far from being just a theoretical artifice, is a fundamental fact of nature.


[Page 161]
320 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Example: 14.11
Calculate the displacement current between the capacitor plates of area 1.5x10-2 and rate of electric field change is 1.5x10¹² V/ms. Also calculate displacement current density and induced magnetic field for r= R= 69 mm.

Solution:
By definition of displacement current from (14.33)
id = ε0 A dE/dt = (8.85x10⁻¹²) (1.5x10⁻²) (1.5x10¹²)
= 0.199 A = 199 mA
Which is significantly large current. The displacement current density is
jd = ε0 dE/dt = 8.85x10⁻¹² x 1.5x10¹² = 13.28 A/m².
The induced magnetic field is
B = ½ μ0 r (jd)
= ½ x 4 π x 10⁻⁷ x 69x10⁻³ x 0.199 = 8.62 nT.
This is very small value of magnetic field produced by 199 mA of displacement current. Conduction current of this amount can produce easily significantly detectable value by compass needle. The difference arises because the conduction current is continued to a thin wire but displacement current is spread out over a surface area of the capacitor plates.

14.12 Additional Examples:
1. A uniform magnetic field B is perpendicular to the plane of a circular loop of diameter 10 cm formed from wire of diameter 2.5 mm and resistivity 1.69 x 10⁻⁸ Ω. At what rate must the magnitude of B change to induce a 10 A current in the loop?

Solution:
The resistance of the loop is
R = ρ L/A = (1.69 x 10⁻⁸) [0.1 / π(2.5x10⁻³)²/4] = 1.1 x 10⁻³ Ω.
Using, i = |ε| / R = |dΦB/dt| / R = |d(BA)/dt| / R
= (πr²) |dB/dt| / R
Thus, |dB/dt| = iR / πr² = (10 A)(1.1x10⁻³ Ω) / π(0.05 m)² = 1.4 T/s.

2. The conducting rod shown in figure AE 2, has length L and is being pulled along horizontal, frictionless conducting rails at a constant velocity v. The rails are connected at one end with a metal strip. A uniform magnetic field B, directed out of the page, fills the region in which the rod moves Assuming that L= 10 cm, v= 5 m/s, and B= 1.2 T. What are (a) magnitude and (b) direction of the emf induced in the rod? What are the (c) size and (d) direction of the current in the conducting loop? Assume that the resistance of the rod is 0.4 Ω and that the resistance of the rails and metal strip is negligibly small. (e) At what rate is thermal energy being generated in the rod? (f) What external force on the rod is needed to maintain v? (g) At what rate does this force do work on the rod?

Solution:
(a) Using equation 14.5, magnitude of induced emf ε is given by.
e = BLv = (1.2 T)(0.1 m)(5 m/s) = 0.6 V.
(b) By Lenz's law, the induced emf is clockwise. In the rod itself, we would say the emf is directed up the page.
(c) By Ohm's law, the induced current is i = 0.6 V/0.4 Ω = 1.5 A.
(d) The direction is clockwise.
(e) Power in the circuit is given by P = i² R = 0.9 W.
(f) The force on the rod associated with the uniform magnetic field is directed rightward and has magnitude
F = iLB = (1.5 A)(0.1m)(1.27) = 0.18 N.
To keep the rod moving at constant velocity, therefore, a leftward force (due to some external agent) having that same magnitude must be continuously supplied to the rod.
(g) The power associated with the force being exerted by the external agent:
P = Fv = (0.18 N)(5 m/s) = 0.9 W,
which is the same as our result from part (e).

3. A long solenoid has a diameter of 12 cm. When a current i exists in its windings, a uniform magnetic field of magnitude B = 30 mT is produced in its interior. By decreasing i, the field is caused


[Page 162]
322 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
to decrease at the rate of 6.5 mT/s. Calculate the magnitude of the induced electric field (a) 2.2 cm and (b) 8.2 cm from the axis of the solenoid.
Solution:
(a) The point at which we are evaluating the field is inside the solenoid. The magnitude of the induced electric field from equation 14.12 is
E = 1/2 dB/dt r = 1/2 (6.5 x 10^-3 T/s)(0.022 m) = 7.15 x 10^-5 V/m.
(b) Now the point at which we are evaluating the field is outside the solenoid. The magnitude of the induced field is
E = 1/2 dB/dt R^2/r = 1/2 (6.5 x 10^-3 T/s) (0.06m)^2 / 0.082m
= 1.43 x 10^-4 V/m.

5. A circular coil has a 10 cm radius and consists of 30 closely wound turns of wire. An externally produced magnetic field of magnitude 2.6 mT is perpendicular to the coil. (a) If no current is in the coil, what magnetic flux links its turns? (b) When the current in the coil is 3.8 A in a certain direction, the net flux through the coil is found to vanish. What is the inductance of the coil?
Solution:
(a) The magnetic flux for N turns
Φ_turns = NΦ_B = NBA
= NB(πr^2) = (30)(2.6 x 10^-3 T)(0.1 m)^2 = 2.45 x 10^-3 Wb.
(b) Self inductance is given by,
L = NΦ_B / i = 2.45 x 10^-3 Wb / 3.8A = 6.45 x 10^-4 H.

6. A solenoid having an inductance of 6.3 µH is connected in series with a 1.2 kΩ resistor. (a) If a 14 V battery is connected across the pair, how long will it take for the current through the resistor to reach 80% of its final value? (b) What is the current through the resistor at time t = τ_L?
Solution:
(a) If the battery is switched into the circuit at t = 0, then the current at a later time t is given by
i = E/R (1 - e^-t/τ_L).
where τ_L = L/R. Our goal is to find the time at which i = 0.8 E/R. This means
0.8 = 1 - e^-t/τ_L => e^-t/τ_L = 0.2.
Taking the natural logarithm of both sides, we obtain -t/τ_L = ln (0.2) = -1.609. Thus,
t = 1.609 τ_L = 1.609 L/R = 1.609(6.3 x 10^-6 H) / 1.2 x 10^3 Ω = 8.45 x 10^-9 s.
(b) At t = τ_L the current in the circuit is
i = E/R (1 - e^-1) = (14V / 1.2 x 10^3 Ω)(1 - e^-1) = 7.37 x 10^-3 A.

7. For the given circuit figure AE 7, assume that E = 10 V, R = 6.7 Ω, and L = 5.5 H. The ideal battery is connected at time t = 0. (a) How much energy is delivered by the battery during the first 2 s? (b) How much of this energy is stored in the magnetic field of the inductor? (c) How much of this energy is dissipated in the resistor?

Electromagnetic Induction 323
Solution:
(a) The energy delivered by the battery is
∫ P_battery dt = ∫ (E^2/R (1 - e^-Rt/L)) dt
= E^2/R [t + L/R (e^-Rt/L - 1)]
= (10V)^2 / 6.7Ω [2 + (5.5 H / 6.7 Ω)(e^-(6.7)(2)/5.5 - 1)]
= 18.7 J.
Figure AE 7
(b) The energy stored in the magnetic field is:
U_B = 1/2 L i^2 (t) = 1/2 L (E/R (1 - e^-Rt/L))^2
= 1/2 (5.5 H) [(10V / 6.7 Ω)(1 - e^-(6.7)(2)/5.5)]^2
= 5.1 J.
(c) The difference of the two results gives the amount 'lost' in the resistor:
18.7 J - 5.1 J = 13.6 J.

8. The induced magnetic field at radial distance 6 mm from the central axis of a circular parallel-plate capacitor is 2 x 10^-7 T. The plates have radius 3 mm. At what rate dE/dt is the electric field between the plates changing?
Solution:
According to equation 14.32, the induced magnetic field is
B = μ_0 ε_0 R^2 / 2r * dE/dt, (r ≥ R)
Solving dE/dt is given by
dE/dt = 2Br / μ_0 ε_0 R^2 = (2(2 x 10^-7 T)(6 x 10^-3 m)) / (4π x 10^-7 T*m/A)(8.85 x 10^-12 C^2 / N*m^2)(3 x 10^-3 m)^2
= 2.4 x 10^13 V/m*s

9. As a parallel-plate capacitor with circular plates 20 cm in diameter is being charged, the current density of the displacement current in the region between the plates is uniform and has a magnitude of 20 A/m^2. (a) Calculate the magnitude B of the magnetic field at a distance r = 50 mm from the axis of symmetry of this region. (b) Calculate dE/dt in this region.
Solution:
(a) Using Ampere's law in the region between the plates we have.
∮ B·ds = μ_0 i_enclosed
= B 2πr = μ_0 i_enclosed
B = μ_0 i_enclosed / 2πr = μ_0 (Jπr^2) / 2πr = 1/2 μ_0 Jr


[Page 163]
324 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
= ½ (1.26 × 10⁻⁶ H/m) (20 A/m²) (50 × 10⁻³ m)
= 63. × 10⁻⁷ T.
(b) From id = Jd πr² = ε₀ dΦ_E / dt = ε₀ πr² dE / dt, we get
dE / dt = Jd / ε₀ = 20 A/m² / 8.85 × 10⁻¹² F/m = 2.3 × 10¹² V/m·s.

(a) When does the fuse blow? (b) Sketch a graph of the current i through the inductor as a function of time. Mark the time at which the fuse blows. [Figure 14.24 for exercise 24]

Electromagnetic Oscillation

15.1 LC Oscillation
There can be three basic components in electric circuits with a source of emf. They are resistor, capacitor and inductor. Consider a capacitor C and inductor L is connected as shown in figure 15.1. Consider the capacitor is charged initially so that one plate has charge Qo and the other plate has -Qo. When the switch is closed the capacitor begins to discharge through the inductor.
The current (I) in the inductor is established. The potential difference (p.d.) across the capacitor is V = Q/C where Q is the charge on the capacitor at that instant. This p.d. should be equal to the p.d. across the inductor and equal to the emf - L dI/dt. When Q is zero I in the inductor will reach its maximum value. Since I is not changing. The magnetic field B, in the inductor will be maximum at this instant. The current then begins to decrease since the flowing charge starts to accumulate in the next plate of the capacitor. When the current has dropped to zero, the capacitor will have maximum charge and is equal to the negative of its initial value Qo.

Afterwards, the capacitor plates starts to discharge with current flows in opposite direction. This process of the charge flowing back and forth from one plate of the capacitor to the other through the inductor continues. Thus the process is an oscillatory circuit and is called electromagnetic or LC oscillation. Consequently the energy also oscillates. First the energy is stored as the electric field of the capacitor. As soon as Q becomes zero, I is maximum and all the energy is stored as magnetic energy in the inductor (as magnetic field). In this way the energy remains constant According to the circuit in the electric and in the magnetic field. According to the circuit in figure 15.1, the p.d. across the capacitor and inductor should be equal.


[Page 166]
330 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Let's find the expression for the frequency of oscillation of an LC circuit. The total energy at any instant U in an oscillating LC circuit is the sum of electric and magnetic energies i.e.

U = Uelectric + Umagnetic
= (Q^2 / 2C) + (1/2)LI^2 ... 15.1

This implies that at an arbitrary time, the energy is stored partly as the magnetic field in the inductor and partly in the electric field in the capacitor. The circuit in figure 15.1 has no resistance which indicates there is no energy loss and total energy is constant with time. Even though the current I and charge Q vary. Hence,

dU/dt = d/dt (Q^2 / 2C + 1/2 LI^2)
LI (dI/dt) + (Q/C) (dQ/dt) = 0

But I = dQ/dt

and dI/dt = d^2Q/dt^2

Therefore, L(dQ/dt)(d^2Q/dt^2) + (Q/C)(dQ/dt) = 0

or, d^2Q/dt^2 + Q/C = 0 ... 15.2

This is the differential equation of the LC oscillatory circuit. One should note that equation 15.2 is same as differential equation of simple harmonic motion

d^2y/dt^2 + (k/m)y = 0 ... 15.3

The solution of equation 15.3 is

y = a sin (ωt + φ)

Where a is the amplitude of motion and φ is arbitrary phase angle. But in LC oscillation y corresponding to Q we may write,

Q = Q0 sin (ωt + φ) ... 15.4

with ω as the angular frequency of the electromagnetic oscillations.

Now,
dQ/dt = I = ωQ0 cos (ωt + φ)
d^2Q/dt^2 = - ω^2 Q0 sin (ωt + φ)

and

Hence equation 15.2 reduces to

- ω^2 L Q0 sin (ωt + φ) + (Q0/C) sin (ωt + φ) = 0.

or, (-ω^2 + 1/LC) sin (ωt + φ) = 0 ... 15.5

This is true for all times t if ω^2 = 1/LC providing the angular frequency ω s given the constant value 1/√LC.

The energy stored as the electric filed in the capacitor at any time t is,

UE = (Q^2 / 2C) = (Q0^2 / 2C) sin^2 (ωt + φ) ... 15.6

Similarly the energy stored as magnetic field in the inductor at the same instant is,

UB = (1/2)LI^2 = (1/2)L(dQ/dt)^2
= (1/2) L ω^2 Q0^2 cos^2 (ωt + φ)
= (1/2) L (1/LC) Q0^2 cos^2 (ωt + φ)
= (Q0^2 / 2C) cos^2 (ωt + φ) ... 15.7

When we take φ = 0 then at time t = 0, T, 2T etc,

UE = Q0^2 / 2C and UB = 0.

Thus all energy is stored in the electric field of the capacitor.

And at t = T/2, 3T/2 etc,

UE = 0 and UB = Q0^2 / 2C


[Page 167]
332 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
The energy is stored in the magnetic field of the inductor. Therefore at any time the total energy is,
U = UE + UB = Q0^2 / 2C [sin^2 (ωt + φ) + cos^2 (ωt + φ)]
= Q0^2 / 2C ... 15.8
This shows the total energy is a constant quantity in LC circuit.

Example 15.12
A 1200 pF capacitor is charged by a 500 V battery. It is disconnected from the battery and is connected to a 75 mH inductor at t = 0. Determine (i) the initial charge on the capacitor, (ii) the maximum current, (iii) the frequency and period of oscillation (iv) the total energy oscillating in the system.

Solution:
(i) We have Q0 = CV
= (1200x10^-12) x 500 = 6x10^-7 C = 0.6 μC.
(ii) I = ω Q0 cos (ωt + φ) and
Imax = ω Q0 = Q0 / sqrt(LC) = 6x10^-7 / sqrt(0.075 x 1200x10^-12)
= 63 mA.
(iii) f = ω / 2π = 1 / (2π sqrt(LC)) = 1 / (2π sqrt(0.075 x 1200x10^-12))
= 12 k Hz.
T = 1/f = 6x10^-5 sec
(iv) TE = Q0^2 / 2C = (6x10^-7)^2 / (2 x 1200x10^-12)
= 1.5x10^-4 W.

15.2 LC oscillation with resistance
There is always some value of resistance in an electric circuit. The LC circuit discussed so far is an idealized situation. Therefore let's consider a realistic situation as shown in figure 15.2.
In such circuit energy is loosed in R. Consider the capacitor is given a charge Q0. Now close the switch at t = 0. Due to the presence of the resistance, a part of the energy is converted into thermal energy across it. In such case the oscillation will not be free oscillation as in LC circuit. Using Kirchhoff's law we get,
L di/dt + IR + Q/C = 0 ... 15.9

Electromagnetic Oscillation
I = dQ/dt we may write,
L d^2Q/dt^2 + R dQ/dt + Q/C = 0 ... 15.10
This is differential equation for damped LCR oscillation. The equation is similar to the mechanical damped harmonic oscillation equation.
m d^2x/dt^2 + b dx/dt + kx = 0
So we may compare those equations and come to the conclusion that L plays the same role as m, R plays the role of b and 1/C plays the same role as of k in mechanical oscillations.
In such case the angular frequency of damped e m oscillation (LCR circuit) will be
ω' = sqrt(1/LC - (R/2L)^2) ... 15.11
The solution of equation 15.10 will thus be,
Q = Q0 e^(-R/2L)t sin (ω't + φ) ... 15.12
Oscillations are an important element in many electric devices. There is always some resistance present in as electric circuit. Therefore electrical oscillators generally need a periodic input of pc ver to compensate for the energy converted to heat energy across R.
From equation 15.11 there arises three cases.
1) When R^2 > 4L/C, the discharge in non oscillatory.
2) When R^2 = 4L/C, the discharge is a periodic and critically damped.
3) When R^2 < 4L/C, the discharge is oscillatory.

Example 15.13
A 1 μF capacitor, a 0.2 Henry inductor and a 800 ohm resistor are connected in series. Is the circuit oscillatory?
Solution:
Given C = 1 μF = 1x10^-6 F
L = 0.2 Henry
R = 800 Ω
R^2 = 800^2 = 6.4x10^5
4L/C = 4x0.2 / 1x10^-6 = 8x10^5
Since R^2 ≤ 4L/C, the circuit is oscillatory.


[Page 168]
334 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Example 15.14
What is the maximum value of the resistance for the circuit to be oscillatory in which an inductor of 0.2 Henry and a capacitor of 1200 pF are connected?

Solution:
For the circuit to be oscillatory, the maximum value of resistance is obtained from
R² = 4L/C
or, R = 2√L/C = 2√ 0.2 / 1200 x 10^-12 = 2.582 x 10^4 Ω

Therefore for the discharge to be oscillatory, a resistance less than 2.582 x 10^4 Ω should be connected in series with the given L and C.

15.3 Forced em Oscillation
We observed if there is resistance in an LC circuit, the oscillation will be damped. If we apply an external frequency sinusoidal in nature so as to bring it to behave as an oscillatory circuit, the oscillation becomes forced electromagnetic oscillation. In practical application the external frequency can be applied with the help of an AC frequency generator. Connect the series LCR circuit with an AC frequency generator of emf E = E0 cos ω1 t as shown in figure 15.3.

From the Kirchhoff's circuital law or using the conservation of energy we can write,
L dI/dt + IR + Q/C = E0 cos ω1 t

or, d²Q/dt² + R/L dQ/dt + 1/LC Q = E0/L cos ω1 t ... 15.13

This equation is similar to the forced mechanical oscillation which has the second order differential equation as,
m d²x/dt² + b dx/dt + kx = F0 sin ω t

The solution of such differential equation will look like
x = xm sin (ω1 t - Φ)

Where xm = F0 / √(ω0² - ω1²)² + (2δω1)²

and tan Φ = (ω0² - ω1²) / 2δω1

Electromagnetic Oscillation 335
It should be noted here that ω0² = 1/LC and δ is equal to R/2L. Making the equivalent relation we can rewrite for charge amplitude for the solution of 15.13 is
Q = Q0 sin (ω1 t - Φ)

i.e. Q0 = (E0 / L) / √(ω0² - ω1²)² + (Rω1 / L)² ... 15.14

Since I = dQ/dt we may write the following expression for the current amplitude.
I0 = (ω1 E0 / L) / √(ω0² - ω1²)² + (Rω1 / L)² ... 15.15

or, I0 = E0 / √( (1/ω1 C - ω1 L)² + R²) = E0 / Z ... 15.16 ∴ ω0² = 1/LC

Here Z = √(R² + (ω1 L - 1/ω1 C)²) = √R² + (XL - XC)² is the impedance of the LCR circuit. The inductive reactance XL is given by ω1 L = 2 π f L and capacitive reactance XC by 1/ω1 C = 1/2 π f C respectively. For the current amplitude to be maximum that is for the condition of resonance according to equation 15.16, inductive reactance and capacitive reactance should be equal to each other. The frequency at which the amplitude becomes maximum is called resonance frequency. Hence the resonance frequency fR is obtained in the following way.

At resonance, XL = XC
or, ω L = 1/ω C
or, 2 π fR L = 1 / 2 π fR C
or, fR = 1 / 2π√LC ... 15.17


[Page 169]
336 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

The quality factor Q in a forced oscillation
is the ratio of the voltage drop across L (or across
C) to voltage drop across R at resonance. It is
therefore,

Q = ωR / f2 - f1

Where f1 and f2 are frequency at 1/√2

The quantity f2 - f1 is called the band
width of the oscillatory circuit. Therefore as long
as the band width is small, the better will be the quality factor of the circuit.

Example 15.15
Two capacitor of capacity 5 µF and 2 µF are given. The series combination of the capacitors is
connected to a 10 mH inductor. What will be the resonance frequency in such case?

Solution:
When the capacitors are connected in series, equivalent capacitance is obtained as
1/C = 1/C1 + 1/C2
or,
C = C1C2 / C1 + C2 = 5x2 / 5+2 = 10/7 µF.
Hence resonant frequency
f0 = 1 / 2π√LC = 1 / 2π√10x10^-3 x 10/7 x 10^-6
= 1.33 kHz

15.4 Resonance Curve and Q - Factor
A graph of current versus frequency in LCR circuit is known as resonance curve. The
graph of such a curve for various values of resistance (R) is shown in figure 15.4. For large R
the curve is flat and for small R curve is sharp. The ability of a reactive circuit to store energy
is expressed in terms of the quality factor or Q - factor as explained in above. It is also the
figure of merit which enables us to compare different coils. The Q factor is also defined as
the ratio of the energy stored in the coil to the energy dissipated on the circuit across the
resistance. Hence

Q = Energy stored / Energy dissipated ... 15.19

In case of an inductor the energy stored is I^2 XL t or I^2 ω L t and the dissipated energy
in resistor is I^2 R t, so
Q = ωL / R = 1/R √L/C

Electromagnetic Oscillation 337

Similarly in case of capacitor,
Qc = 1 / ωCR = 1/R √L/C

The larger the Q factor, the greater is the storing capacity for a given dissipation. The
Q factor varies from 5 to 100 for inductive circuit and from about 1400 to 10000 for
capacitive circuits. When a series circuit is in resonance the energy stored in the capacitor is
equal to that stored in inductor. In one quarter cycle the inductor stores energy while in next
quarter cycle it is stored in the capacitor. The energy flows back and forth between
inductance and capacitance. The only loss of energy is due to the energy loss in the
resistance.

Hence in case of LCR circuit Q factor may also be defined as equal to the voltage
magnification in the circuit at resonance. Hence the voltage magnification

α = Q = ImaxXL / ImaxR = XL/R = ωL/R = 1/R √L/C ... 15.20

because current is maximum at resonance.


[Page 170]
338 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
15.4 Additional Examples:
1. An oscillating LC circuit consists of a 75 mH inductor and a 3.6 µF capacitor. If the maximum charge on the capacitor is 2.9 µC, what are (a) the total energy in the circuit and (b) the maximum current?
Solution:
(a) All the energy in the circuit resides in the capacitor when it has its maximum charge. The current is then zero. If Q is the maximum charge on the capacitor, then the total energy is
U = Q²/2C = (2.9 x 10⁻⁶ C)² / 2(3.6 x 10⁻⁶ F) = 1.17 x 10⁻⁶ J.
(b) When the capacitor is fully discharged, the current is a maximum and all the energy resides in the inductor. If I is the maximum current, then U = LI²/2 leads to
I = sqrt(2U/L) = sqrt(2(1.168 x 10⁻⁶ J) / 75 x 10⁻³ H) = 5.58 x 10⁻³ A.
2. In an oscillating LC circuit with C = 64 µF, the current is given by i = (1.6) sin (2500t + 0.68), where t is in seconds, i in amperes, and the phase constant in radians. (a) How soon after t = 0 will the current reach its maximum value? What are (b) the inductance L and (c) the total energy?
Solution:
(a) Comparing the given expression with i = I sin (ωt + φ₀), and substituting
(ωt + φ₀) as 2500t + 0.680 = π/2,
we will get t = 3.56 x 10⁻⁴ s.
(b) Here ω = 2500 rad/s = 1/sqrt(LC)
L = 1/(ω²C) = 1/((2500)²(64 x 10⁻⁶)) = 2.5 x 10⁻³ H.
(c) The energy is
U = ½ LI² = ½ (2.5 x 10⁻³)(1.6)² = 3.2 x 10⁻³ J.
3. In an oscillating LC circuit, when 75% of the total energy is stored in the inductor's magnetic field, (a) what multiple of the maximum charge is on the capacitor and (b) what multiple of the maximum current is in the inductor?
Solution:
(a) The percentage of energy stored in the electric field of the capacitor is (100-75)% or 25%. Hence,
Ue / U = (q²/2C) / (Q²/2C) = 25%
or, q/Q = sqrt(0.25) = 0.5
(b) For the inductor,
U_L / U = (LI²/2) / (LI²/2) = 75%
or, i/I = sqrt(0.75) = 0.866.

Electromagnetic Oscillation 339
4. What resistance R should be connected in series with an inductance L = 220 mH and capacitance C = 12 µF for the maximum charge on the capacitor to decay to 99% of its initial value in 50 cycles? (Assume ω' ≈ ω).
Solution:
Here we assume ω' ≈ ω, Time period T = 2π/ω and angular frequency ω = 1/sqrt(LC).
The required for 50 cycles is
t = 50T = 50(2π/ω) = 50(2πsqrt(LC))
= 50(2πsqrt((220 x 10⁻³ H)(12 x 10⁻⁶ F)))
= 0.5104 s
The maximum charge on the capacitor decays as:
qmax = Qe⁻Rt/2L
where Q is the charge at time t = 0.
Now, ln(qmax / Q) = -Rt / 2L
or, R = -(2L / t) ln(qmax / Q) = -(2(220 x 10⁻³ H) / 0.5104s) ln(0.99)
= 8.66 x 10⁻³ Ω.
5. (a) In an LCR circuit, can the amplitude of the voltage across an inductor be greater than the amplitude of the generator emf? (b) Consider an LCR circuit with Em = 10 V, R = 10Ω, L = 1 H, and C = 1µF. Find the amplitude of the voltage across the inductor at resonance.
Solution:
(a) Yes, the voltage amplitude across the inductor can be much larger than the amplitude of the generator emf.
(b) The amplitude of the voltage across the inductor in an LCR series circuit is given by
VL = IXL = IωL.
We have at resonance; the driving angular frequency and natural angular frequency are equal.
So ω = ω₀ = 1/sqrt(LC).
In our case XL = L/sqrt(LC) = 1H / sqrt(1H * 1.0 x 10⁻⁶ F)
= 1000Ω.
At resonance the capacitive reactance has this same value, and the impedance reduces simply as Z = R, which gives,
I = Em / Z_resonance = Em / R
= 10V / 10Ω = 1A
Therefore the voltage amplitude across the inductor is
VL = IXL = (1A)(1000Ω) = 1 x 10³ V
This is much larger than the amplitude of the generator emf.


[Page 171]
340 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
