16
Chapter
Electromagnetic Waves

In the chapter electromagnetic induction we noticed that time varying magnetic field acts as a source of electric field and that time varying electric field acts as a source of magnetic field. These E and B fields can sustain each other, forming an electromagnetic wave that propagates through space. Visible light emitted by the glowing filament of a light bulb is the example of electromagnetic wave. In this chapter we'll use Maxwell's equations as the theoretical basis for understanding electromagnetic waves.

16.1 Maxwell's Equations

When the field do not vary with time, such as an electric field produced by charges at rest or the magnetic field of a steady current, we can analyze the electric and magnetic fields independently without considering interactions between them. But when the fields vary with time, they are no longer in dependent. According to Faradays' law, time-varying magnetic field is a source of electric field. The modified Ampere's law shows that time-varying electric fields act as a source of magnetic field. The Ampere's law was modified by Maxwell's including displacement current for varying fields and Maxwell summarized this interaction between the two fields. At the same time he discovered that the basic principles of electromagnetism can be expressed in terms of a set four equations that we call Maxwell's equations. He also proved in 1865 that an electromagnetic disturbance showed propagate in free space with a speed equal to that of light and hence the light waves were likely to be electromagnetic in nature.

The set of four basic laws of electromagnetism are: Gauss law of electricity and magnetism, Faraday's law of electromagnetic induction and modified Ampere's law. The full important of the interconnection between electric and magnetic fields and their essential sameness becomes clear only within the framework of special relativity. At present, we explain, the basic laws that describe the behaviour of electromagnetic fields.

The basic laws of electricity and magnetism we have discussed so far can be summarized in integral form called integral form of Maxwell's equations:


[Page 173]
344 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

(a) Gauss's law for electrostatics
∮ E.ds = q / ε0 ... 16.1

(b) Gauss law for magnetism
∮ B.ds = 0 ... 16.2

(c) Faraday's law of induction
∮ E.dl = - dφB / dt ... 16.3

(d) Ampere's law as extended by Maxwell
∮ B.dl = μ0 (i + ε0 dφE / dt) ... 16.4

These equations apply to electric and magnetic fields in vacuum. If material is present, the permittivity, ε and permeability, μ of the material are used. The first Maxwell's equation relates the electric field to the charge distribution that creates it. It also confirms the possibility of isolation of single charge. In equation second, the net magnetic flux through a closed surface is zero which implies that isolated magnetic monopoles have not been observed in nature. Faraday's law of induction describes the electrical effect of a changing magnetic field. It says that changing magnetic field produces an electric field. In other words the time varying magnetic field is the source of the electric field. The fourth equation describes that there are at least two ways of setting up a magnetic field: (a) by changing electric field and by a current. Furthermore, a time varying magnetic field is a source of the electric field.

16.2 Conversion of Integral form of Maxwell's Equations into Differential Form

The basic laws of electricity and magnetism are generally expressed in integral forms for simplicity, but differential forms are also useful for higher studies. Conversions of integral form to differential form require minimum understanding of the vector algebra, particularly of the meaning of gradient, divergence and curl of a vector. Some of the vector related laws are given in the Appendix.

1. Derivation of Maxwell first equation: ∇.E = ρ / ε0

From Gauss theorem of electrostatics, we have the electric flux passing through a closed surface is
∮ E.ds = q / ε0
or,
∮ E.ds = 1 / ε0 ∫ ρdv

Electromagnetic Waves 345

Now using Gauss-Divergence theorem in the left hand side of this relation we get,
∫ (∇.E)dv = 1 / ε0 ∫ ρdv
or,
∇.E = ρ / ε0 ... 16.5

This relation relates the electric field and charge density in electrostatic field.

2. Derivation of Maxwell second equation: ∇.B = 0

We know that the lines of forces are either closed or go off to infinity. But in any closed surface the same numbers of lines of force enter and leave. Thus according to Gauss law of magnetism the magnetic field flux in free space across any closed surface is zero, i.e.
∮ B.ds = 0

Using Gauss-Divergence theorem,
∫ (∇.B) dv = 0
or,
∇.B = 0 ... 16.6

It indicates that the magnetic field is solenoidal. It also confirms that isolated magnetic monopoles have not been observed in nature.

3. Derivation of Maxwell third equation: ∇×E = - ∂B / ∂t

According to Faraday's law of electromagnetic induction when magnetic field varies with time an electric field will be produced. Here we have the electromotive force around a closed path is equal to the time derivative of the magnetic flux through any closed surface bounded by the path i.e.
∮ E.dl = - dφB / dt
or,
∮ E.dl = - ∂/∂t [ ∫ B.ds ]

Using Stoke's theorem we get
∫ (∇×E).ds = - ∂/∂t [ ∫ B.ds ]
or,
∇×E = - ∂B / ∂t ... 16.6

The above relation is true if the area and time are independent variables.

4. Derivation of Maxwell third equation: ∇×B = μ0 (J + ε0 ∂E / ∂t)


[Page 174]
346 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

As soon as variable electric field is applied between the plates of a condenser, the electrons of dielectrics undergo a displacement within the atom. This electronic moment constitutes displacement within the atom This electronic moment constitutes displacement current which flows resulting polarization in the dielectrics. Hence the total electric current in variable field passing through the circuit circulating for continuous current is the sum of displacement current and conduction current. Hence the modification of Ampere's law with displacement current is

∮ B.dl = μ0 (I + Id)

Where Id = displacement current = C dV/dt = ε0 A dE/dt

∴ ∮ B.dl = μ0 ∫ (J + ε0 dE/dt) . ds

Using Stoke's theorem of vector integral we get

∮ (∇ × B).ds = μ0 ∫ (J + ε0 dE/dt) . ds

or, ∇ × B = μ0 (J + ε0 dE/dt) ... 16.7

This relation confirms that magnetic field is set up if electric field changes with time.

Example: 16.1
Using Maxwell's equations prove that, ∇ × (B × E) = pB / ε0

Solution:
Using relation of vector triple product in LHS
∇ × (B × E) = B (∇ . E) - E (∇ . B)
Using Maxwell's equations, we get,
∇ × (B × E) = Bp / ε0 - 0 = Bp / ε0
Where B is magnetic flux density and E is electric intensity.

Example: 16.2
Using Maxwell's equations, prove the charge conservation theorem ∂p/∂t + ∇ . J = 0.

Solution:
Considering the Maxwell's Fourth equation
∇ × B = μ0 (J + ε0 ∂E/∂t)
Taking divergence both sides we get
∇ . (∇ × B) = μ0 ∇ . J + ε0 μ0 ∂/∂t (∇ . E)
It is known that divergence of a curl is always zero and using Maxwell's first equation, we get
∇ . J + ∂p/∂t = 0

This is called charge conservation law which states that the total charge of any system remains constant if no external charges are brought into it.

Electromagnetic Waves 347

Example 16.3
Convert Maxwell equation ∇ × E = - ∂B/∂t
On integrating over a surface S bounded by a closed path, we have
∫ (∇ × E) ds = - ∫ ∂B/∂t ds
Converting surface integral into line integral by Stokes' theorem, we get
∮ E.dl = - ∂/∂t ∮ B.ds

This signifies that the electromotive force around a closed path is equal to the time derivative of the magnetic displacement through any closed surface bounded by the path.

16.3 Different form of Maxwell's Equations
The different forms of Maxwell's equations are

(a) Maxwell's equations in free space
In free space, the charge density p and current density J are zero, so that Maxwell equations there become
(i) ∇ . E = 0 ... 16.8a
or, (ii) ∇ . D = 0 ... 16.8b
(iii) ∇ . B = 0 ... 16.8c
(iv) ∇ × E = - ∂B/∂t ... 16.8d
and ∇ × H = ∂D/∂t
or, ∇ × H = ∂D/∂t

(b) Maxwell's equations in isotropic medium,
In the isotropic medium p = 0, J = 0, D = ε E and B = μ H so that Maxwell equations there become
∴ (i) ∇ . E = 0
(ii) ∇ . H = 0
(iii) ∇ × E = - μ ∂H/∂t


[Page 175]
348 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
(iv) ∇xH = ε ∂E/∂t
(c) Maxwell's equations in conducting medium
We now consider a conducting linear and isotropic medium whose permeability is μ, permittivity is ε and the conductivity is σ. Further, we assume that there is no free charge available i.e. ρ = 0. We can summarize the condition as
ρ = 0, D = εE , J = σE
and B = μH . Then Maxwell's equations becomes
(i) ∇.E = 0
(ii) ∇.H = 0
(iii) ∇xE = - μ ∂H/∂t
(iv) ∇xH = σE + ε ∂E/∂t

(d) Maxwell's equations in slowly varying field
In case of slowly varying field in electrodynamics the terms containing time deviates of Maxwell's equations will be neglected. Thus
(i) ∇.E = ρ/ε0
(ii) ∇.B = 0
(iii) ∇xB = 0
(iv) ∇xB = μ j
In each terms of equation there is only electrostatics or magneto- static field.

16.4 Electromagnetic Wave Equations
A. In Free space
We will now derive the wave equation for electromagnetic waves in free space. In free space, the charge density ρ and the current density j are zero, so that the Maxwell equations there become
div E = 0
div B = 0
curl E = - ∂B/∂t
and curl B = μ0 ε0 ∂E/∂t

Let us take the curl of the equation for curl E; we obtain
curl curl E = - curl ∂B/∂t = - ∂/∂t curl B
But, from above, curl B = μ0 ε0 ∂E/∂t , so that
curl curl E = - μ0 ε0 ∂^2E/∂t^2 ... 16.9
we know that curl curl E = - ∇^2 E + grad div E and from above that div E = 0.
Thus,
curl curl E = - ∇^2 E ... 16.10

Combining equations 16.9 and 16.10, we obtain finally
∇^2 E = μ0 ε0 ∂^2 E/∂t^2 ... 16.11a

Taking curl of B and proceeding as above, we should be able to show that B satisfies the same equation, or
∇^2 B = μ0 ε0 ∂^2 B/∂t^2 ... 16.11b

Equations 16.11 are the equations of electromagnetic wave motion. Being vector equations, they are equivalent to six scalar equations, one for each component of E and of B.
There are many solutions of equations 16.11, corresponding to different kinds of electromagnetic waves-plane, spherical and cylindrical waves being three examples. Let us consider a solution in which two components of E and two of B vanish, that is, in which
Ex = Ez = 0 and Bx = By = 0
Equations 16.11 are satisfied for these assumptions. For the nonvanishing components, Ey and Bz, equations 16.11 reduce to
∂^2 Ey/∂x^2 + ∂^2 Ey/∂y^2 + ∂^2 Ey/∂z^2 = μ0 ε0 ∂^2 Ey/∂t^2
and
∂^2 Bz/∂x^2 + ∂^2 Bz/∂y^2 + ∂^2 Bz/∂z^2 = μ0 ε0 ∂^2 Bz/∂t^2


[Page 176]
350 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

If we make the additional assumption that Ey and Bz are functions of x and t only, the simplified wave equation that results, namely
∂²Ey/∂x² = μ0ε0 ∂²Ey/∂t² and ∂²Bz/∂x² = μ0ε0 ∂²Bz/∂t²
is similar for the vibrating string.

A solution to these equations, as the student may verify by substituting, is
Ey = Em sin (kx - ωt) ... 16.12a
and Bz = Bm sin (kx - ωt) ... 16.12b

We interpret equations 16.12 as an electromagnetic wave travelling in the positive x-direction, as in figure 16.1, with a speed c = ω/k.
c = ω/k = 1/√μ0ε0

B. In Conducting Medium

Now, considering the Maxwell's third equation in differential form in conducting medium i.e.
∇×E = -∂B/∂t
taking curl both sides we get,
∇×(∇×E) = -∇×(∂B/∂t) = -∂/∂t (∇×B)

Using fourth Maxwell's equation we get
∇.(∇.E) - ∇²E = -∂/∂t [μJ + με ∂E/∂t]
For constant charge density of the medium,
∇.(∇.E) = 0
So, ∇²E = εμ ∂²E/∂t² + μσ ∂E/∂t ... 16.13
Similarly taking curl of equation
Where we have used J=σE. σ is conductivity of the medium. This is called plane wave equation or electromagnetic wave equation in conducting medium.

Electromagnetic Waves 351

For free space substituting ρ = 0, J = 0 in above equations, we get
∇²E - μ0ε0 ∂²E/∂t² = 0 ... 16.14a
and ∇²B - μ0ε0 ∂²B/∂t² = 0 ... 16.14b

The one dimensional solution of wave equations 16.14 can also written as
E = E0 ei(kx-ωt) ... 16.15a
B = B0 ei(kx-ωt) ... 16.15b
Here k is wave vector and x is position vector along x - axis. It should be noted that equations 16.12 and 16.15 are similar only the way of expression is different.

Example: 16.3
Prove that the ratio E/B = C, where E and B are magnitude of electric and magnetic field respectively and C is velocity of light.

Solution:
Now expressing the third Maxwell's equation in one dimensions,
i.e. ∂E/∂x = -∂B/∂t
Then using equations 16.15, we have
Here we get, E0 k = ω B0
i.e. E0/B0 = ω/k = λ/T = C
C is velocity of light or the electromagnetic wave.

Example: 16.4
In free space prove that the magnetic energy density is equal to electric energy density.

Solution:
We have electric energy density
uE = 1/2 ε0 E² and magnetic energy density
uB = 1/2 B²/μ0
Using the relation E/B = C in electric energy density
uE = 1/2 ε0 E² = 1/2 ε0 B² C²
= 1/2 ε0 B² (1/μ0ε0) Where C² = 1/μ0ε0
= 1/2 B²/μ0 = uB

Example: 16.5
Prove that B and E are perpendicular to each other in an electromagnetic wave.
Solution:
Now from third Maxwell 's equation we get
del x E = - dB / dt
We have B = B0 e^{i(kx - wt)} for one dimension. So
i j k
d/dx d/dy d/dz = B (-i w)
Ex Ey Ez
or, i(dEz/dy - dEy/dz) + j(dEx/dz - dEz/dx) + k(dEy/dx - dEx/dy) = B w.
Again using the solution E = E0 e^{i(kx - wt)} we have
or, i(ky Ez - kz Ey) + j(kz Ex - kx Ez) + k(kx Ey - ky Ex) = w B
k x E = w B
Hence E, B and k are always perpendicular to each other.

Example: 16.6
Using em plane wave equation for conducting materials, show that the real and imaginary parts of complex wave vector (propagation constant) are equal.
Solution:
The electromagnetic plane wave equation for conducting material is
del^2 E - mu sigma dE/dt = 0
Where the second term of equation 16.13 is zero for conducting medium. Using the solution of differential equation is one dimension.
E = E0 e^{i(kx - wt)}
Substituting this relation in above equation we get
k^2 = i mu sigma w = (1 + i)^2 (mu sigma w / 2)
k = (1 + i) sqrt(mu sigma w / 2)
Hence the wave - vector k is complex and has real and imaginary parts which are
K = kr + ki
So comparing we get,
kr = ki = sqrt(mu sigma w / 2)
Hence real and imaginary parts are equal.

16.5 Energy Transport and Poynting Vector
The light wave, radio, TV, mobile waves are electromagnetic wave which carry energy and as they propagate through space, they transfer energy to objects placed in their path. The rate of flow of energy in an electromagnetic wave per unit area is described by a vector S called Poynting vector, which is the cross-product of electric field intensity and magnetic field, expressed as

S = 1/mu0 E x B ... 16.16

The unit of S is watts/m^2. The direction of S at any point gives the direction of energy transport at that point.
As the electromagnetic wave propagates from one point to another, there is a transfer of electric and magnetic energy or electromagnetic energy. Consider an electromagnetic wave travelling in the right as shown in figure 16.2. The total energy at any instant dt stored in a box while travelling with thin element dx and face area A is
dU = dUE + dUB = (uE + uB) dV
dU = (uE + uB) (A dx)
For free space uE = uB then
dU = 2 uE A dx = 2 (1/2 epsilon0 E^2) A dx
dU/A = epsilon0 E^2 dx
= epsilon0 E B C^2 dt
dU / (A dt) = E B / mu0
S = E B / mu0 ... 16.17
This represents the magnitude of Poynting vector because angle between E and B is always right angle. This represents the power per unit area or wave intensity. Considering sinusoidal, then the average value of S is intensity, then
I = Savg = E0 B0 / 2 mu0 = E0^2 / 2 mu0 C = C B0^2 / 2 mu0
when, I = C uavg = P/A = P / 4 pi r^2 ... 16.18
Hence the intensity of an electromagnetic wave equals the average magnetic energy density multiplied by speed of light.


[Page 178]
354 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
Example: 16.7
The Earth’s mean radius is 6.37×10⁶ m and the mean Earth – Sun distance is 1.5×10¹¹ km.
What fraction of the radiation emitted by the Sun is intercepted by the disk of the Earth?

Solution:
The magnitude of the Poynting vector gives the intensity of the source. The amount of radiation intensity emitted by the sun is
I₁ = P/A = P / 4πR² = P / 4×π×(1.5×10¹¹)²
and the intensity recorded by the disk of the earth is
I₂ = P/A' = P / πr² = P / π(6.37×10⁶)²
Hence the fraction is
I₁ / I₂ = P / 4π(1.5×10¹¹)² / P / π(6.37×10⁶)² = 4.51×10⁻¹⁰

Example: 16.8
Sun light just outside the Earth’s atmosphere has an intensity of 1.4 kW/m². Calculate maximum electric and magnetic fields for sun light, assuming it to be a plane wave.

Solution:
We have the magnitude of the poynting vector is
|S| = I = E₀² / 2μ₀ C = C B₀² / 2μ₀
Hence we have the magnitude of electric field,
E₀ = √2μ₀ C I = √2×4×π×10⁻⁷×3×10⁸×1.4×10³ = 1.03 kV/m
Again the magnitude of magnetic field,
B₀ = √2μ₀I / C = 3.43 μT
Which are high values of electric and magnetic fields.

16.6 Radiation Pressure
An electromagnetic wave transports energy as well as linear momentum. As this momentum is absorbed by the surface of medium, pressure is exerted on it. The corresponding force involved must be small in comparison to the force of our daily experiences.

Let a parallel beam of radiation fall on an object for a time Δt and is totally absorbed by the object. Maxwell showed that if the surface absorbs ΔW amount of energy then the magnitude of change of momentum Δp is
Δp = ΔW / C ... 16.19
This represents the total absorption of the radiation, where C is velocity of light. The direction of the momentum change is the direction of the incident beam. If the radiation from the surface is totally reflected back along its original path, then the magnitude of momentum change for reflection is double that of absorption

Δp = 2ΔW / C ... 16.20
From Newton’s law of motion, the change in pressure is
Pₐ = ΔF / A = 1/A (Δp / Δt)
Where Δp is change in momentum and Pₐ is pressure for absorption of radiation,
Pₐ = 1/A (ΔW / CΔt) = 1/AC (ΔW / Δt)
i.e. Pₐ = 1/C (ΔW / AΔt) = I/C ... 16.21
Where I is intensity of radiation. It is also equivalent to the magnitude of Poynting vector (S). Relation 16.21 represents the radiation pressure for field absorption, but for total reflection back along the path is
Pᵣ = 2I / C ... 16.22

For direct sun light, the value of I before it passes through the earth’s atmosphere is approximately 1.4 kw/m². The corresponding average pressure on a completely absorbing surface is
Pₐ = 1.4×10³ / 3×10⁸ = 4.7×10⁻⁶ Pa

The average pressure on a totally reflecting surface is twice the above value. Although the radiation pressure are very small. They have been measured with sensitive instruments such as, torsion balance as shown in figure 16.3. The radiation pressure was first measured by E.F. Nichols and G.F. Hull in 1903. The pressure measured by them is about 10⁻¹⁰ times smaller than atmospheric pressure.

The radiation pressure of sun light is much larger inside the sun than at the earth’s surface. Small grains of dust released by comets experience the radiation pressure from sun light. This pressure pushes the grains of dust away from the sun, when we observe them as the tail of the comet. Inside the stars, radiation pressure is so great that it substantially augments the gas pressure within the star and so helps to prevent the star from collapsing under its own gravity.


[Page 179]
356 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

Example: 16.9
A radio wave transmits 25 W/m² of power per unit area. A flat surface of area A is perpendicular to the direction of propagation of the wave. Calculate the radiation pressure on it, assuming the surface is a perfect absorber.

Solution:
The radiation pressure for total absorption of radiation is
Pr = I / c = 25 / (3 x 10^8)
= 8.34 x 10^-8 Pa
= 83.4 n Pa.

Example: 16.10
If the intensity of direct sun light at a point on the surface of the earth is 0.78 kW/m². Find (a) the average momentum density and (b) average momentum flow rate in the sun light.

Solution:
(a) We have the radiation charge in momentum for absorption (see equation 16.40) is
Δp = ΔW / c
The charge in momentum per unit volume of the radiation i.e. momentum density is;
Δp / V = ΔW / AΔtc = IΔt / AΔtc = I / c²
= 0.78 x 10^3 / 9 x 10^16 = 8.68 x 10^-15 kg m^-2 s^-1.

(b) Again the average momentum flow rate of the radiation is equivalent to radiation pressure. Thus the radiation pressure for absorption is;
Pr = I / c = 0.78 x 10^3 / 3 x 10^8
= 2.6 x 10^-6 Nm^-2.

Electromagnetic Waves 357

16.7 Additional Examples:
1. The maximum electric field 10 m from an isotropic point source of light is 2 V/m. What are (a) the maximum value of the magnetic field and (b) the average intensity of the light there? (c) What is the power of the source?

Solution:
(a) The magnetic field amplitude of the wave is
B0 = E0 / c = (2 V/m) / (2.998 x 10^8 m/s) = 6.7 x 10^-9 T.

(b) The intensity is
I = E0² / 2μ0c = (2 V/m)² / (2 * 4π x 10^-7 T m/A * 2.998 x 10^8 m/s) = 5.3 x 10^-3 W/m².

(c) The power of the source is
P = 4πr² Iavg = 4π (10 m)² (5.3 x 10^-3 W/m²) = 6.7 W.

2. An isotropic point source emits light at wavelength 500 nm, at the rate of 200 W. A light detector is positioned 400 m from the source. What is the maximum rate ∂B/∂t at which the magnetic component of the light changes with time at the detector's location?

Solution:
In electromagnetic wave, variation of magnetic field with position and time is
B = B0 cos (kx - ωt)
∂B/∂t = ωB0 sin (kx - ωt)
Therefore the maximum value of ∂B/∂t is ωB0.
Now relating B0 to the intensity we may write:
B0 = E0 / c = (√2μ0I) / c
From equation 16.18, intensity is related with power P by
I = P / 4πr²
Putting all together we obtain
(∂B/∂t)max = ω (√2μ0I) / c = (2πc / λ) (√2μ0P / (c * √4πr²))
= (√2μ0Pc) / λr = (√2π x 4π x 10^-7 x 200 x 2.998 x 10^8) / (500 x 10^-9 x 400)
= 3.44 x 10^6 T/s.

3. What is the radiation pressure 1.5 m away from a 500 W light bulb? Assume that the surface on which the pressure is exerted faces the bulb and is perfectly absorbing and that the bulb radiates uniformly in all directions.

Solution:
Since the surface is perfectly absorbing, the radiation pressure is given by
Pr = I/c, where I is the intensity.
Since the bulb radiates uniformly in all directions, the intensity a distance r from it is given by
I = P/4πr², where P is the power of bulb.


[Page 180]
358 Fundamentals of Engineering Physics
Thus, Pr = P / (4 * pi * r^2 * c) = 500W / (4 * pi * (1.5m)^2 * (2.998 * 10^8 m/s)) = 5.9 * 10^-8 Pa.

4. A small laser emits light at power 5 mW and wave-length 633 nm. The laser beam is focused until its diameter matches the 1266 nm diameter of a sphere placed in its path. The sphere is perfectly absorbing and has density 5 * 10^3 kg/m^3. What are (a) the beam intensity at the sphere's location, (b) the radiation pressure on the sphere, (c) the magnitude of the corresponding force, and (d) the magnitude of the acceleration that force alone would give the sphere?

Solution:
(a) The cross section area of the beam is πd^2/4, where d is the diameter of the spot (d = 2 λ).
The beam intensity is
I = P / (πd^2 / 4) = 5 * 10^-3 W / (π * (2 * 633 * 10^-9 m)^2 / 4) = 3.97 * 10^9 W/m^2.

(b) The radiation pressure is
Pr = I / c = 3.97 * 10^9 W/m^2 / 2.998 * 10^8 m/s = 13.2 Pa.

(c) In computing the corresponding force, we can use the power and intensity to eliminate the area. We obtain
Fr = (πd^2 / 4) * Pr = (P / I) * Pr = (5 * 10^-3 W * 13.2 Pa) / 3.97 * 10^9 W/m^2 = 1.67 * 10^-11 N.

(d) The acceleration of the sphere is
a = Fr / m = Fr / (ρ * (πd^3 / 6)) * Pr = 6 * (1.67 * 10^-11 N) / (π * (5 * 10^3 kg/m^3) * (2 * 633 * 10^-9 m)^3)
= 3.14 * 10^3 m/s^2.

17 Chapter
Photons and Matter Waves

Quantum mechanics is a generalized form of mechanics applicable to microscopic objects like electrons in the atoms. Macroscopic or large systems can also be described by quantum mechanics but mostly the simpler form of mechanics i.e. classical mechanics is used to describe them. Many things which we have experienced throughout our life are not applicable to quantum mechanics. Various processes and phenomena that take place within a material cannot be explained without having good understanding of quantum mechanics. Although classical mechanics gives us information about various behaviour of different materials, but it fails to explain the microscopic level of the materials. This is the region where quantum mechanics fits the bill.

17.1 Quantization of Energy

In 1902, Planck first introduced the basic idea of quantum theory, but most of the subsequent mathematics development and interpretation were made by a number of distinguished physicists including Einstein, Bohr, Schrodinger, de Broglie, Heisenberg, Dirac etc. Planck had shown that the experimental observation in black body radiation could be explained on the basis that the energy from the body was emitted in separate packets of energy. Each packet was called a quantum of energy and amount of energy E carried was equal to h f i.e.

E = hf ... 17.1

Where h is Planck’s constant (h = 6.63×10⁻³⁴ Js) and f is frequency of the radiation. This is called quantum theory of radiation. Until Planck’s quantum theory, it was considered that the radiation was emitted continuously and not in separate packets of energy.

In 1905 Einstein suggested that the experimental result, in photoelectric effect could be explained by applying the quantum theory of light. He assumed that light of frequency f contains packets or quanta of energy h f. On this basis, light consists of particles called photons. For n number of photons, the total energy is,

En = n h f ... 17.2

Where, n = 0, 1, 2, 3 ... ...


[Page 182]
362 Photons and Matter Waves Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 363

Equation 17.2 tells us that it is possible to have energy of radiation of an amount 1 hf,
2 hf ... but never 1.5 hf or 1.9 hf etc. When the values of a property are restricted to discrete
multiples is quantized.

Light and other electromagnetic radiation sometimes act like wave and some times
like particles. Interference and diffraction demonstrate wave behaviour while emission and
absorption of photons reveals the particle behaviour. Quantum mechanics exerts the concept
of wave-particle duality to include particles that sometime shows wave-like behaviour.

17.2 Electrons and Matter Waves: de Broglie Equation

The phenomena of light i.e. interference, diffraction and polarization can be explained
conceptely by the wave nature of light. However, it failed completely to explain the photo-
electric effect i.e. the emission of electrons from metal surfaces by the action of light. Planck
and Einstein concluded that the energy radiations including both heat and light are emitted
discontinuously as little quanta or photons. This theory opposes directly to the wave theory of
light and it gives particle like properties. Louis de Broglie had arrived at his hypothesis with
the help of Planck's quantum theory of radiation and Einstein theory of relatively and in 1924
he put forward the suggestion that matter like radiation has dual nature. The Millikan oil -
drop and JJ Thomson experiments confirm the particle nature of electrons whereas, GP
Thomson, Davisson and Germer, Young's double slit experiments instead prove that the
electron behaves as wave. Not only electrons, but all the materials particles like photons,
neutrons, atoms or molecules exhibit wave properties. The waves associated with material
particles are called matter waves.

The wavelength of the matter is determined using Planck's theory of radiation i.e.
E = hf = hc / λ ... 17.3

Where h is Planck's constant and f is frequency of radiation. Now applying Einstein
mass energy relationship, the energy associated with photons analogy of matter is
E = mc² ... 17.4

Where, c is velocity of light. Now comparing equations 17.3 and 17.4 we get
λ = h / mc = h / p = h / mv ... 17.5

This is the wavelength of the photon having momentum (m c). This is also called de-
Broglie wavelength or matter waves. Thus it states that the momentum of a particle in motion
is inversely proportional to the wave length. This equation is true for all particles but it is
only with very small particles such as electrons, protons etc.

17.3 Characteristics of the de-Broglie Matter Wave

The main characteristics of de-Broglie matter waves are given as follow:
(i) The de-Broglie waves are different from electromagnetic waves which travel with a
constant velocity v.
(ii) The wave length of the matter waves inversely varies with mass of the particles.
(iii) The wave velocity of de-Broglie wave is greater than the velocity of electromagnetic
waves.
(iv) Two different velocities are associated with a matter particle in motion - one is
mechanical velocity or group velocity (v) and velocity of each component wave is
wave or phase velocity (u).

17.4 Wave and Group Velocity of Matter Waves

According to de-Broglie view, each particles of matter, in motion such as electron,
proton, neutron etc may be regarded as a group of waves or a wave packet as shown in figure 17.1. Each component wave propagates with a
definite velocity called wave velocity or phase velocity. For individual wave, the speed i.e.
phase speed is
u = ω / k = λf ... 17.6

Where ω is angular velocity and k is wave vector.

The phase speed is the rate of advancement of a crest on a single wave, which is a
point of a fixed phase. When a distance consists of a number of component wave, each
travelling with a slightly different velocity, the resultant will be that of a periodicity or
modulation impressed upon the wave packet. The velocity of advance of thus periodicity is
called group velocity (v). The group is formed by superposition of two waves or more than
we obtain the group speed
v = dω / dk ... 17.7

The group velocity of the group packet determines the motion of the particle. It
cannot be greater than velocity of light but phase velocity may be grater than velocity of
light.

We know that the wave vector k = 2π / λ

i.e. dk = - 2π / λ² dλ and dω = 2 π df

Hence the group velocity
v = - λ² df / dλ = - λ² d/dλ (u/λ) from equation 17.6

i.e. v = λ² [u/λ² - 1/λ du/dλ]


[Page 183]
364
Photons and Matter Waves

or,
v = u - λ du / dλ ... 17.8

This gives the relation between wave velocity (u) and group velocity (v). It also shows that in normal condition wave velocity is always greater than group velocity but in free space du/dλ = 0 and v = u. For matter, wave group velocity is equivalent to particle velocity (u).

17.5 The de-Broglie Electron Wave

According to de-Broglie relation, we get that if an electron is accelerated to various velocities, various wave lengths are produced. The higher the electron velocity, the smaller will be the de-Broglie wave length. If the velocity u is given to an electron by accelerating it through a potential difference V than the work done on the electron is e V and the work done is converted into the kinetic energy of the electron is

1/2 mv² = e V

or,
v = √2eV / m

and its corresponding momentum

p = m v = √2meV ... 17.9

Then from de-Broglie equation we have

λ = h / √2emV ... 17.10

Ignoring relativistic consideration, we have

h = 6.625x10⁻³⁴ Js m = 9.11x10⁻³¹ kg e = 1.6x10⁻¹⁹ C

λ = 1.23 / √V n m ... 17.11

It is noted that to produce electron of wave length 1 Å, 150 volts are required. Davisson and Germer in their original experiments of an electron diffraction used electrons of 30 – 600 e V.

Example: 17.1
Show that the de-Broglie wave only travels faster than light.

Solution:
Let the de-Broglie wave velocity
u = λf ... 17.6
Now using Planck's relation of quantization of energy for a photon and Einstein's mass energy relation
hf = mc²

Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 365

f = mc²/h ... 17.12
Using equation 17.12 and de-Broglie wave length in equation 17.6 we get,
u = (mc²/h) (h/mv) = c²/v ... 17.13
The particle velocity v must be less than the velocity of light (c), so de-Broglie phase velocity u is always greater than velocity of light. The relation of phase and group velocities clears the concept.

Example: 17.2
Find the de-Broglie wave length of a 15 kV proton. Mass of proton = 1.67x10⁻²⁷ kg.

Solution:
The momentum of the photon is
p = m v = √2meV = √2x1.67x10⁻²⁷ x 1.6x10⁻¹⁹ x 15x10³
= 2.83x10⁻²¹ kg m s⁻¹.
Hence the de-Broglie wave length is,
λ = h/p = 6.625x10⁻³⁴ / 2.83x10⁻²¹ = 2.34x10⁻¹³ m
= 0.0023 Å.

Example: 17.3
Prove that group speed of a wave packet is identical to the speed of a particle.

Solution:
Let the group speed of a wave packet is
v = dω / dk = d(hω) / d(hk) = dE / dp
Where E = hf, p = h/λ and h = h / 2π
i.e. E = h/2π (2πf) = hω and p = h/2π * 2π/λ = hk
Using E = p²/2m we get,
v = dE / dp = d/dp (p²/2m) = 1/2m * 2p = p/m = v
Hence group velocity is identical to the particle velocity (v).

17.6 The Uncertainty Principle

In the measurement of photon or velocity of a particle at any instant, experimental uncertainties are built into the system. In classical mechanical measurements, there is no fundamental barrier to an ultimate requirement of the apparatus or the procedures i.e. in principle it is possible to make such measurements with arbitrary small uncertainty. Quantum theory predicts, however that "it is fundamentally impossible to make simultaneous measurements of a particle's position and momentum with high accuracy."

In 1927, W. Heisenberg introduced this notion, which is now called Heisenberg uncertainty principle, which states that;

"It is impossible to determine precisely and simultaneously, the values of both the members of a particle of canonically conjugate physical variables which describe the motion of an atomic system."


[Page 184]
366 Photons and Matter Waves

If we measure momentum (p) accurately, then there will be certain error Ax in the simultaneous measurement of position (x). In the same way if position is measured with high accuracy, then there will be Ap error in the measurement of momentum of a wave. The relationship between the distance Ax and the wave number spread Ak depends upon the shape of the wave group and upon how Ax and Ak are defined. According to de-Broglie wave, the group velocity of the wave packet is

v = d ω / d k = d ω / 2πd (1/λ) = λ² d ω / 2π d λ

The de-Broglie wave length of the particle is
λ = h/p or, dλ = -h dp/p²

Hence the group velocity
v = λ²p² / 2πh (d ω / dp)
v = h / 2π (d ω / dp) ≅ h Δω / Δp ... 17.14

It is already proved that the group velocity is equivalent particle velocity moving along X- axis i.e.
v = Δx / Δt = h Δω / Δp

or, Δp Δx = h Δω Δt ... 17.15

If Δt least time period for one complete cycle,
then Δt ≧ 1 / Δω
or, Δt Δω ≧ 1.

Hence from 17.15 we get
Δx Δp ≧ h ... 17.16

This is uncertainty principle of position and momentum. It is clear that the uncertainty principle is only important in considering measurements of small particle comprising on atomic system.

Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 367

Another form of uncertainty principle can be generated by wave packet or group of waves in terms of energy and time. The energy and times are members of particular pairs of physical variables that describes the behaviour of atomic system

ΔE . Δt ≧ h ... 17.17
Where Δt and ΔE are uncertainties in determining the time and energy.

The uncertainty principle is universal and holds for all the pairs of conjugate variables. So it also holds good for angular momentum and angular position or distance as well; i.e.

ΔJ . Δθ ≧ h ... 17.18

The uncertainty principle describes the wave-particle duality of all physical quantities, it does not in any way depend upon the inaccuracies involved in the design and use of measuring instruments. The uncertainty principle is verified by Heisenberg's Gamma Ray Microscope and diffraction of beam of electrons through a single slit.

Applications of Uncertainty Principle:

The uncertainty principle can explain a large number of facts.
(i) Non-existence of electrons and existence of proton and neutrons in nucleus.
(ii) Calculation of binding energy of an electron in an atom.
(iii) Determination of radius of hydrogen atom
(iv) Finite width of the spectral series.
(v) To study the strength of nuclear force and stability of the atom.

Example: 17.4
Using the Kinetic energy relation for a particle having particle velocity; v; prove the uncertainty relations between energy and time.

Solution:
Consider the motion of a particle which is represented by a wave packet. If v be the particle velocity then its corresponding Kinetic energy
E = 1/2 mv²
The uncertainty in the measurement of Kinetic energy is given by
ΔE / Δv = mv
or, ΔE = Δp v = v Δp
If the particle moves through Δx in time Δt, then
ΔE = Δx / Δt Δp
or, ΔE . Δt = Δx . Δp
Hence from equation 17.16 we conclude that
ΔE . Δt ≧ h
This is energy - time uncertainty relation.

Example: 17.5
Using uncertainty principle, calculate the binding energy of a hydrogen atom.


[Page 185]
368 Photons and Matter Waves

Solution:
Let an electron of mass m, charge e and momentum p be considered in an orbit of radius r. The uncertainty in the position Ax of the electron is of the order of 2r, the corresponding uncertainty in momentum component Ap is given by.
Δp ≈ h/2r
The kinetic energy of the electron for momentum p is
E = p^2/2m = 1/2m (h/2r)^2 = h^2/8mr^2
The potential energy of the electron in the field of nucleus is
V = -Ze^2/4πε₀r
Hence the total energy of the electron in the orbit is
W = E + V = h^2/8mr^2 - Ze^2/4πε₀r
W = 10^-20 - 1.5x10^-10 Zr / r^2 ... 17.19
Taking radius r ≈ 10^-10 m we can calculate the binding energy - 13.6 eV for Hydrogen atom same as the value obtained by Bohr's theory of H-atom.

17.7 Wave Motion and Wave Function
Erwin Schrödinger (1926) developed wave mechanics on the bass of de-Broglie's hypothesis of wave particle duality. The wave motion is a form of disturbance, which travels through a medium due to the repeated periodic motion, particles of the medium execute simple harmonic motion about their motion along or perpendicular to the direction of propagation of wave, according as the wave is longitudinal or transverse. For a transverse wave propagating along positive x-axis, the equation of any particle with phase difference φ is
y = A sin (ωt - φ) = A sin (ωt - 2πx/λ) = A sin (ωt - kx)

In exponential form this equation can be expressed as
y = A e^-i(ωt - kx) ... 17.20
Unlike in classical mechanics, the wave function does not enjoy the privilege of having similar analogies in quantum mechanics that will enable us to easily understand the wave function. Despite the difficulties in finding analogies to describe the wave function in quantum mechanics, it is used observable such as energy, momentum, position of particles. The wave displacement function 17.20 is replaced by wave function ψ to describe both wave and particle nature in quantum mechanics.
Hence we have, E=hω, p=hk
y = A e^-i/h(Et-px) ... 17.21
This function in general is a complex quantity and is dependent upon space and time. To peruse the wave function has both the real and imaginary components that will

Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 369
characterise the particle in quantum mechanical domain. It has no physical meaning itself. The only quantity having a physical meaning is the square of its magnitude
P = ψ ψ* = |ψ|^2
This is called probability amplitude or density where ψ* is complex conjugate of ψ.
The probability of finding a particle in a volume dx dy dz is |ψ|^2 dx dy dz or |ψ|^2 dV
The probability of finding a particle in the volume element dx dy dz or dV is ψψ* dV.
The total probability of finding the particle in the entre space is of course unity. i.e.
∫|ψ|^2 dV = ∫ψψ* dV = 1 ... 17.22
A wave function satisfying this relation is called normalized wave function. It is also called normalized conditions. If the product of a function ψ1 (x) and complex conjugate ψ2* (x) of wave function ψ2 (x) vanishes i.e.
b
∫ψ1(x)ψ2*(x)dx = 0 ... 17.23
a
Where a ≤ x ≤ b, then ψ1 (x) and ψ2 (x) are said to be orthogonal in the interval (a,b)
The quantity |ψ|^2 is always real and positive. The time derivative of |ψ|^2 gives the value of probability current density i.e.
J = ∂|ψ|^2 / ∂t ... 17.24
Where J is also called probability flux density.

17.8 A Particle in a Box
The particle confined to an one-dimensional region of space is called particle in a box. If a particle is bouncing elastically back and forth along x-axis between two impenetrable walls separated by a distance l, its motion is easy to describe on the basis of classical view - point because, it does not restrict on the values of a particles momentum and energy, however, quantum mechanical approach to this problem requires appropriate wave function related to the condition of the situation.
The walls being impenetrable, there is no probability of finding particles outside the box. Hence
ψ(x) = 0 for x < 0 and x > l


[Page 186]
370 Photons and Matter Waves
and the potential energy is infinite. If y is zero outside the walls, it must also be zero at the walls. As long as the particle is inside the box, the potential energy of the particle and we can choose its value to be zero. Hence the particle in a box can be defined by the boundary conditions
(i) V = ∞ for x ≤ 0 and x ≥ l
(ii) V = 0 for 0 < x < l which is called potential energy Gaize, and
(iii) ψ (0) = ψ (l) = 0 for x = 0 and x = l ... 17.25
i.e. to leave the region 0 ≤ x ≤ l, a particle would have to perform a infinity large quantity of work, so the probability for the particle to be at x = 0 or, x < l is equal to zero.

The wave function for a particle in the box can be expressed by a real sinusoidal function i.e.
ψ (x) = A sin (2πx / λ) ... 17.26

λ is de-Broglie wavelength of the particles. Now using boundary condition to this wave function, for x = l,
ψ (l) = 0 = A sin (2πl / λ)

i.e. 2πl / λ = nπ

or, λ = 2l / n ... 17.27

Where n = 1, 2, 3, ... . Hence only certain values of wavelength for the particles are allowed . Each value of λ corresponds the quantum state and the number n is called quantum, number. Now the wave function in terms of quantum number is
ψ (x) = A sin (nπx / l) ... 17.28

Now according to de-Broglie wavelength and the relation 17.27 we get the momentum
p = h / λ = nh / 2l

Inside the box of size l the potential energy being zero, the total energy would be
E = p² / 2m = (h² / 8ml²) n²

or, E = [π²h² / 2ml²] n² ... 17.29

Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 371
Hence it is confirmed that the energy of the particle is quantized. The minimum allowed energy state corresponds to the ground state for n = 1 and exited states corresponds to n = 2, 3, 4 ... have energies given by 4E1, 9E1, 16E1 ... etc. According to quantum mechanics, n = 0 is not possible, because the particle can never be at rest, which is one of the main contradiction to the classical point of view. Each quantized energy value is called Eigen values and the corresponding wave function ψn is called Eigen function.

Figure 17.3 First four allowed state for a particle continued to an one dimensional box.
It is observed that the energy of a continued particle is quantized, so can have only discrete sets of values. The discrete values of energy wave function and probability density in a single dimensional case are shown in the figure below.

Example: 17.6
A football of 500 gm is confined between two impenetrable walls of Dasharath Rangashala that can be modelled as a box of length 100 m. Calculate the minimum speed of the ball.

Solution:
The minimum speed corresponds to the state for n = 1. So the Equation 17.29 gives the ground state energy
i.e. E1 = (π² h²) / (2ml²) = h² / (8ml²) = (6.63 x 10^-34)² / (8 x 0.5 x 100²)
= 1.1 x 10^-71 J.
Now we have potential energy V = 0, so
E1 = 1/2 mv²
v = √(2E1 / m) = √( (2 x 1.1 x 10^-71) / 0.5 ) = 6.63 x 10^-36 m/s.
Which is considered to be at rest.

17.9 The Schrödinger Equation
An Australian Theoretical Physicist Erwin Schrödinger derived a fundamental equation of quantum mechanics in the sense of that the Newton's law of motion in classical mechanics. The equation is the wave equation which represents the wave particle duality in the variable ψ (x, t). So the essential features of this equation are the incorporation of de Broglie wave length into classical wave particles. It is based on the deal of the electron as a standing wave around the nucleus. The equation for standing wave comparable with that of stretched string is given by 17.21 i.e.


[Page 187]
372 Photons and Matter Waves

ψ(x, t) = A e^(-i/h(Et-px))

Differentiating this relation twice with respect to x we get,

dψ/dx = Ai/h p e^(-i/h(Et-px)) and

d^2ψ/dx^2 = -p^2/h^2 ψ ... 17.30

If an electron or a particle of mass m and potential energy V be considered moving with a velocity v, then the total energy of the system is

E = K + U = 1/2 m v^2 + V = p^2/2m + V

Multiplying both sides by ψ and rearranging both sides we get,

p^2 ψ = 2m (E - V) ψ

Using equation 17.30 we get,

d^2ψ/dx^2 + 2m/h^2 (E - V) ψ = 0 ... 17.31

This is time independent Schrödinger wave equation in one dimension. For three dimension it can be expressed in terms of Laplacian operator ∇^2 i.e.

∇^2 = ∇.∇ = (∂/∂x i + ∂/∂y j + ∂/∂z k) . (∂/∂x i + ∂/∂y j + ∂/∂z k) = (∂^2/∂x^2 + ∂^2/∂y^2 + ∂^2/∂z^2)

Hence the three dimensional time independent Schrödinger wave equation is

∇^2 ψ + 2m/h^2 (E - V) ψ = 0 ... 17.32

For a free particle in motion V = 0.

Now to derive the time dependent Schrödinger wave equation. again differentiate equation 17.21 with respect to time i.e.

dψ/dt = -i E/h ψ

and substituting the value of E ψ from this relation in 17.32 we get

Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 373

ih dψ/dt = -h^2/2m ∇^2 ψ + V ψ ... 17.33

This time dependent Schrödinger equation of motion in the presence of potential field can be extended to Hamiltonian function i.e.

ih dψ/dt = [-h^2/2m ∇^2 + V] ψ = H^ ψ ... 17.34

Where H^ is known as Hamiltonian operator. Now equation 17.34 is written as

E ψ = H^ ψ ... 17.35

This is another form of Schrödinger wave equation which describes the non-relativistic motion of material particles.

17.10 Application of the Schrödinger Wave Equation

17.10.1 A particle in an one – dimensional infinitely deep potential well

A potential energy versus x-axis diagram is a useful representation for understanding and solving problems with the Schrödinger equation. Because of the shape of the curve in Figure 17.2, the particle in a box is said to be in a square well, where well is an upward facing of the curve and downward facing region of the well is called barrier.

Let a particle of mass m moving inside a box along x-axis be considered. The particle is bouncing back and forth between the walls of the box or infinite potential well. We can imagine at the point x = 0 and x = l then an absolutely impenetrable wall which reflects the particles. A limitation of this type is represented with the aid of the curve of

V(x) = 0 at 0 < x < l and

V(x) = ∞ at x < 0 x > l

These boundary conditions confirm that particle cannot exist outside the well and its wave function is

ψ(x) = 0 for x = 0 and x = l

within the box or the potential well, the Schrödinger equation in one dimension will be

d^2ψ/dx^2 + 2m/h^2 E ψ = 0

or,

d^2ψ/dx^2 + β^2 ψ = 0 ... 17.36

The solution of this equation is


[Page 188]
374 Photons and Matter Waves

ψ(x) = A sin βx + B cos βx ... 17.37

Where β² = 2mE / ℏ² ... 17.38

The boundary conditions can be used to evaluate the constants A and B in equation 17.37.

ψ = 0 at x = 0, then B = 0

and A ≠ 0, since we get from ψ (l) = A sin βl = 0

β = nπ / l ... 17.39

Now from equations 17.37 and 17.39 we get

2mE / ℏ² = n²π² / l²

Thus the energy of the particle is

En = [π²ℏ² / 2ml²] n² ... 17.40

This is identical to the energy of a particle in a box equation 17.29. Each value is called Eigen value and corresponding function is Eigen function. Eigen function from equation 17.37 are

ψ(x) = A sin (nπx / l) ... 17.41

The coefficient A is determined by using normalization condition. The particle is located within the potential well of width l, so

l
∫ ψψ*dx = 1
0

or,

l
A² ∫ sin² (nπx / l) dx = 1
0

e. A = [2/l]^(1/2)

Hence the normalized wave function of the electron is

ψn = [2/l]^(1/2) sin (nπx / l) ... 17.42

The plot of Eigen value and Eigen function are shown in Figure 17.3

17.10.2 Barrier tunnelling

Let a finite potential, V (x) energy function of height V0 in the region of width l be considered. The downward facing region of the potential function is called potential barrier. In such case, when a moving particle encounters, interesting phenomenon occurs. Let a particle of energy E < V0 be incident on the barrier from the left of region I classically region II and III are not allowed and both are forbidden to particle incident from region I. But for quantum mechanics all regions are accessible to the particle regardless of its energy. According to uncertainty principle (ΔE . Δt ≥ ℏ), the particle can be either in the barrier as long as the time interval correspondingly to its energy. But for relatively narrow barrier, the short time interval can allow the particle to cross it. This approaches leads to the barrier tunnelling in which the Schrödinger equation has valid solutions in all the three regions I, II and III as shown in figure 17.4.

The boundary conditions for the problem are defined as

V (x) = 0 for x < 0
= V0 for 0 < x < l
= 0 for x > l ... 17.43

and the wave is continuous for penetration or tunnelling for which

∂ψI / ∂x = ∂ψII / ∂x = ∂ψIII / ∂x for x = 0 and x = l

Where ψI, ψII and ψIII are the respective wave functions in regions I, II and III as indicated in the Figure 17.4. If a particle having energy E < V0 then the particle approaches to the barrier. The particle has some probability of reflection as well as penetration to the region III. The probability of the penetration or tunnelling is greater if l is very small. Now the Schrödinger wave equations for the regions are:

For region I, d²ψI / dx² + 2mE / ℏ² ψI = 0

or, d²ψI / dx² + β² ψI = 0

Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 375


[Page 189]
Where β² = 2mE/ħ² and solution of the differential equation
ψI = A e iβx + B e -iβx ... 17.44

For region II, d²ψII / dx² + 2m(E - V0)/ħ² ψII = 0
or, d²ψII / dx² + α² ψII = 0
Where α² = 2m(E - V0)/ħ² = - 2m(V0 - E)/ħ² and corresponding solution is
ψII = Ce -αx + D e αx ... 17.45

For region III, d²ψIII / dx² + β² ψIII = 0
Then the solution of the differential equation is
ψIII = F e iβx ... 17.46

There is no probability of reflection in region III. Where A, B, C, D ... etc are amplitudes of the corresponding components of each wave, positive sign indicates the incident wave and negative indicates the amplitude of reflected wave.

The probability of tunnelling is described with transmission (T) and reflection (R) coefficients. The transmission coefficient represents the probability that the particle penetrates to the other side of the barrier and reflection coefficient is probability of reflection from the barrier. Now the transmission coefficient is defined by
T = |F|² / |A|²

The coefficients are determined using the boundary conditions 17.43, then for E < V0.
substituting the value of F and A, we get
T = 16E/V0 (1 - E/V0) e -2αl
T = T0 e -2αl ... 17.47

This relation gives the probability of tunnelling through the potential barrier of width l and height V0. The reflection coefficient is
R = |B|² / |A|² = 1 - T

Thus the tunnelling is a quantum mechanical phenomena which manifests the wave nature of matter. This phenomenon is especially important in alpha decay, nuclear fusion. Scanning tunnelling microscope, tunnels diodes, etc.

Example: 17.7
Calculate the permitted energy levels of an electron in one dimensional potential well of width l.
Solution:
Let the mass of the electron (m) = 9.1x10⁻³¹ kg; h = 6.63x10⁻³⁴ Js
Hence the quantized energy of the electron in an atomic system is
En = n²π²ħ² / 2ml² = n²h² / 8ml² = (6.63x10⁻³⁴)² / 8 x 9.1x10⁻³¹ x (2x10⁻¹⁰)² n²
= 1.5x10⁻⁸ n² joules.
Where n = 1, 2, 3, ... etc.

Example: 17.8
A beam of electrons having energy of each 3 eV is incident on a potential barrier of height 4 eV. If the width of the barrier is 20Å, calculate the percentage transmission of the beam through the barrier.

Solution:
We have the transmission coefficient for E < V0 is
T = T0 e -2αl
= 16E/V0 (1 - E/V0) e -2αl
Where α = √2m(V0 - E)/ħ = √2x9.1x10⁻³¹ x (4-3) 1.6x10⁻¹⁹ / 1.054x10⁻³⁴
= 5.12x10⁹
Hence the transmission coefficient is
T = 16x3/4 (1 - 3/4) e -2x5.12x10⁹x20x10⁻¹⁰
= 3x1.28x10⁻⁹ = 3.82x10⁻⁹.
Which confirm that the probability of transmission is very low.


[Page 190]
378 Photons and Matter Waves

17.11 Additional Examples:

1. The meter was once defined as 1650763.73 wavelengths of the orange light emitted by a source containing krypton – 86 atoms. What is the photon energy of that light?

Solution:
The energy of a photon is given by E = hf, where h is the Planck constant and f is frequency. The wavelength λ is related to the frequency by λf = c, so E = hc/λ. Since h = 6.626 × 10⁻³⁴ J·s and c = 2.998 × 10⁸ m/s.
hc = (6.626 × 10⁻³⁴ J·s)(2.998 × 10⁸ m/s) = 19.865 × 10⁻²⁶ J·m.
Thus, E = 19.865 × 10⁻²⁶ / λ.
With λ = (1/1650763.73) m = 6.0578021 × 10⁻⁷ m = 605.78021 nm, we find the energy to be
E = 19.865 × 10⁻²⁶ / 6.0578021 × 10⁻⁷ J
= 2.047 eV.

2. A satellite in Earth orbit maintains a panel of solar cell of area 2.60 m² perpendicular to the direction of the surface light rays. The intensity of the light at the panel is 1.39 kW/m². (a) At what rate does solar energy arrive at the panel? (b) At what rate are solar photons observed by the panel? Assuming that the solar radiation is monochromatic, with a wavelength of 550 nm, and that all the solar radiation striking the panel absorbed. (c) How long would it take for a mole of photon to be absorbed by the panel?

Solution:
(a) The rate at which solar energy strikes the panel is P = IA
P = (1.39 kW/m²)(2.6 m²) = 3.61 kW.

(b) The rate at which solar photons are absorbed by the panel is
R = P / E_ph = P / (hc/λ) = 3.61 × 10³ W / (6.63 × 10⁻³⁴ J·s)(2.998 × 10⁸ m/s) / 550 × 10⁻⁹ m)
= 1 × 10²² photons / s.

(c) The time required is given by
t = N_A / R = 6.02 × 10²³ / 1 × 10²² / s
= 60.2 s.

3. A 100 W sodium lamp (λ = 589 nm) radiates energy uniformly in all directions. (a) At what rate are photons emitted by the lamp? (b) At what distance from the lamp will a totally absorbing screen absorb photons at the rate of 1 photon cm⁻² s⁻¹? (c) What is the photon flux (photon per unit area per unit time) on a small screen 2 m from the lamp?

Solution:
(a) We assume all the power results in photon production at the wavelength λ = 589 nm. Let R be the rate of photon production and E be the energy of a single photon. Then,
P = RE = R hc / λ.
where E = hf and f = c/λ are used. Here h is the Planck constant, f is the frequency of the emitted light, and λ is wavelength. Thus,
R = Pλ / hc = (589 × 10⁻⁹ m)(100W) / (6.63 × 10⁻³⁴ J·s)(3 × 10⁸ m/s) = 2.96 × 10²⁰ photon/s.

Fundamentals of Engineering Physics 379

(b) Let I be the photon flux a distance r from the source. Since photons are emitted uniformly in all directions,
R = 4πr²I
r = √(R / 4πI) = √(2.96 × 10²⁰ photon/s / 4π(1 × 10⁴ photon/m² · s)) = 4.86 × 10⁷ m.
and
(c) The photon flux is
I = R / 4πr² = 2.96 × 10²⁰ photon/s / 4π(2m)² = 5.89 × 10¹⁴ photon/m² · s.

4. A light detector (your eye) has an area of 2 × 10⁻⁶ m² and absorbs 80% of the incident light, which is at wavelength 500 nm. The detector faces an isotropic source, 3 m from the source. If the detector absorbs photons at the rate of exactly 4 s⁻¹, at what power does the emitter emit light?

Solution:
The rate at which photons are absorbed by the detector is related to the rate of photon emission by the light source, so
R_abs = (0.8) R_emit / 4πr² = R_emit.
Given that A_abs = 2 × 10⁻⁶ m² and r = 3 m, with R_abs = 4 photons/s, we find the rate at which photon is emitted to be
R_emit = 4πr² R_abs / (0.8)A_abs = 4π(3m)² / (0.8)(2 × 10⁻⁶ m²) = (4 photons/s) = 2.83 × 10⁸ photons.
Since the energy of each emitted photon is
E_ph = hc / λ = (6.626 × 10⁻³⁴ J·s)(2.998 × 10⁸ m/s) / 500nm
= 19.865 × 10⁻²⁶ J·m / 500nm = 1240 eV·nm / 500nm = 2.48 eV.
the power output of source is then
P_e = R_emit E_ph = (2.83 × 10⁸)(2.48 eV) = 7 × 10⁸ eV/s = 1.1 × 10⁻¹⁰ W.

5. Calculate the de Broglie wavelength of (a) a 1 keV electron, (b) a 1 keV photon, and (c) a 1 keV neutron.

Solution:
(a) Using the value hc = 1240 eV nm, as we obtained above then
λ = h / p = h / √2m_e K = hc / √2m_e c² K = 1240 eV·nm / √(2(511000 eV)(1000 eV)) = 0.0388 nm.
(b) A photon's de Broglie wavelength is equal to its familiar wave-relationship value. Using the value hc = 1240 eV nm,
λ = hc / E = 1240 eV·nm / 1keV = 1.24 nm.
(c) The neutron mass may be found in Appendix A similar to proton. Using the conversion from electron-volts to Joules, we obtain
λ = h / √2m_n K = 6.63 × 10⁻³⁴ J·s / √(2(1.675 × 10⁻²⁷ kg)(1.6 × 10⁻¹⁶ J)) = 9.06 × 10⁻¹³ m.

6. An electron and photon each have a wavelength of 0.2 nm. What is the momentum (in kg·m/s) of the (a) electron and (b) photon? What is the energy (in eV) of the (c) electron and (d) photon?

Solution:
(a) The momentum of the electron is


[Page 191]
380
Photons and Matter Waves

p = h/λ = 6.63x10^-34 J s / 0.2x10^-9m = 3.3 x 10^-24 kg m/s.

(b) The momentum of the photon is the same as that of the electron:
p = 3.3 x 10^-24 kg m/s.

(c) The kinetic energy of the electron is
Ke = p^2 / 2me = (3.3x10^-24 kg m/s)^2 / 2(9.11x10^-31 kg) = 6 x 10^-18 J = 38 eV.

(d) The kinetic energy of the photon is
Kph = pc = (3.3 x 10^-24 kg m/s)(2.998 x 10^8 m/s) = 9.9 x 10^-16 J = 6.2 keV.

7. A non-relativistic particle is moving three times as fast as an electron. The ratio of the de Broglie wavelength of the particle to that of the electron is 1.813 x 10^-4. By calculating its mass, identify the particle.

Solution:
The wavelength associated with the unknown particle is
λp = h / pp = h / m p v p,
where pp is its momentum, mp is its mass, and vp is its speed. The classical relationship pp = mp vp was used.
Similarly, the wavelength associated with the electron is λe = h / me ve, where me is its mass and ve is its speed. The ratio of the wavelengths is
λp / λe = me ve / mp vp
so, mp = (λe / λp) me (ve / vp) = 1 / (1.813 x 10^-4) * 9.109x10^-31 kg * (1/3) = 1.675 x 10^-27 kg.
According to Appendix A, this is the mass of a neutron.

8. (a) Write the wave function ψ(x) in the form ψ(x) = a + ib, where a and b are real quantities, the time-dependent wave function Ψ(x,t) that corresponds to ψ(x) written in this form. (b) Write the time-dependent wave function Ψ(x,t) that corresponds to ψ(x) written in this form.

Solution:
(a) Using Euler's formula e^iθ = cos θ + i sin θ, we write ψ(x) as
ψ(x) = ψ0e^ikx = ψ0 (cos kx + i sin kx) = (ψ0 cos kx) + i(ψ0 sin kx) = a + ib,
where a = ψ0 cos kx and b = ψ0 sin kx are both real quantities.

(b) The time-dependent wave function is
Ψ(x,t) = ψ(x) e^-iωt = ψ0 e^i(kx - ωt)
= [ψ0 cos (kx - ωt)] + i [ψ0 sin (kx - ωt)].

9. The function ψ(x) describes a free particle, for which we assumed that V(x) = 0 in Schrödinger's equation. Assume now that V(x) = V0 a constant of Schrödinger's equation. Show that ψ(x) is still a solution of Schrödinger's equation, with
k = 2π / h * sqrt(2m(E - V0))
now giving the angular wave number k of the particle.

Solution:
For V = V0 Schrödinger's equation becomes
d^2ψ / dx^2 + 8π^2m / h^2 * (E - V0)ψ = 0.
We substitute ψ = ψ0 e^ikx for free particle. The second derivative is then,
d^2ψ / dx^2 = -k^2 ψ0 e^ikx = -k^2 ψ.
The result is
-k^2 ψ + 8π^2m / h^2 * (E - V0)ψ = 0.
Solving for k, we obtain
k = sqrt(8π^2m / h^2 * (E - V0)) = 2π / h * sqrt(2m(E - V0)).

10. A 3 MeV proton is incident on a potential energy barrier of thickness 10 fm and height 10 MeV. What are (a) the transmission coefficient T, (b) the kinetic energy K, the proton will have on the other side of the barrier if it tunnels through the barrier, and (c) the kinetic energy K, it will have if it reflects from the barrier? A 3 MeV deuteron (the same charge but twice the mass as a proton) is incident on the same barrier. What are (d) T, (e) K, and (f) K?

Solution:
(a) The transmission coefficient T for a particle of mass m and energy E that is incident on a barrier of height Vs and width l is given by
T = e^-2αl,
where α = sqrt(8π^2m(Vs - E) / h^2)
For the proton, we have
α = sqrt(8π^2(1.6726x10^-27 kg)(10MeV - 3MeV)(1.6022x10^-13 J/MeV) / (6.6261x10^-34 J s)^2)
= 5.80 2 x 10^14 m^-1.
This gives αl = (5.8082 x 10^14 m^-1)(10 x 10^-15 m) = 5.8082, and
T = e^-2(5.8082) = 9.02 x 10^-6.
The value e^-αl was computed to a greater number of significant digits than usual because an exponential is quite sensitive to the value of the exponent.

(b) Mechanical energy is conserved. Before the proton reaches the barrier, it has kinetic energy of 3 MeV and a potential energy of zero. After passing through the barrier, the proton again has a potential energy of zero. Thus a kinetic energy of 3 MeV.

(c) Energy is also conserved for the reflection process. After reflection, the proton has a potential energy of zero, and thus a kinetic energy of 3 MeV.

(d) The mass of a deuteron is 2.0141 u = 3.3454 x 10^-27 kg. so
α = sqrt(8π^2(3.3454x10^-27 kg)(10MeV - 3MeV)(1.6022x10^-13 J/MeV) / (6.6261x10^-34 J s)^2)
= 8.2143 x 10^14 m^-1.
This gives αl = (8.2143 x 10^14 m^-1)(10 x 10^-15 m) = 8.2143, and
T = e^-2(8.2143) = 7.33 x 10^-8.

(e) As in the case of a proton, mechanical energy is conserved. Before the deuteron reaches the barrier, it has a kinetic energy of 3 MeV and a potential energy of zero.
After passing through the barrier, the deuteron again has a potential energy of zero, thus a kinetic energy of 3 MeV.

(f) Energy is also conserved for the reflection process. After reflection, the deuteron has a potential energy of zero, and thus a kinetic energy of 3 MeV.


[Page 192]
382 Photons and Matter Waves

11. (a) Suppose a beam of 5 eV protons strikes a potential energy barrier of height 6 eV and thickness 0.7 nm, at a rate equivalent to a current of 1000 A. How long would you have to wait - on average - for one proton to be transmitted? (b) How long would you have to wait if the beam consisted of electrons rather than protons?

Solution:
(a) The rate at which incident protons arrive at the barrier is
n = I/e = 1 kA / 1.6 x 10^-19 C = 6.25 x 10^21 /s.
Letting nTt = 1, where T is transmission coefficient (equation 17.47) we find the waiting time t:
t = (nT)^-1 = 1/n exp [2l sqrt(8pi^2 m_p(V_b - E) / h^2)]
= 1/(6.25 x 10^21 /s) exp [ (2pi(0.7nm) / 1240 eV . nm) sqrt(8(938MeV)(6eV - 5eV)) ]
= 3.37 x 10^11 s ≈ 10^4 y,
which is much longer than the age of the universe.

(b) Replacing the mass of the proton with that of the electron, we obtain the corresponding waiting time for an electron:
t = (nT)^-1 = 1/n exp [ 2l sqrt(8pi^2 m_e(V_b - E) / h^2) ]
= (1 / 6.25 x 10^21 /s) exp [ (2pi(0.7nm) / 1240eV . nm) sqrt(8(0.511MeV)(6eV - 5eV)) ]
= 2.1 x 10^-19 s.
The enormous difference between the two waiting times is the result of the difference between the masses of the two kinds of particles.

Appendix
B
Appendix
Vector Identities

1. The Gradient
Let φ (x, y, z) be a scalar function of position in space, that is, of the coordinates x, y, z. If the coordinates x, y, z are increased by dx, dy, dz, respectively, we have
dφ = ∂φ/∂x dx + ∂φ/∂y dy + ∂φ/∂z dz ... i

If we denote by dx the vector representing the displacement specified by dx, dy, dz, then
dr = i dx + j dy + k dz ... ii

In vector analysis a certain vector differential operator ∇ (read "del") defined by
∇ = i ∂/∂x + j ∂/∂y + k ∂/∂z ... iii

plays a very prominent role. The gradient of a scalar function φ(x, y, z) is defined by
grad φ = i ∂φ/∂x + j ∂φ/∂y + k ∂φ/∂z ... iv

Operating with ∇ on the scalar function φ(x, y, z), we get
∇φ = i ∂φ/∂x + j ∂φ/∂y + k ∂φ/∂z = a vector ... v

This is just expression 8.4 defined as the gradient of φ.

Now, from i and ii, we have
dφ = (i ∂φ/∂x + j ∂φ/∂y + k ∂φ/∂z) . (i dx + j dy + k dz) ... vi

The equation
φ (x, y, z) = constant


[Page 199]
Fundamentals of Engineering Physics

2. The Divergence and Gauss's Theorem
The scalar product of the vector operator ∇ and a vector A gives a scalar that is called the divergence of A; that is,
∇.A = ∂Ax/∂x + ∂Ay/∂y + ∂Az/∂z ... vii

3. Gauss's Theorem
This is one of the most important theorems of vector analysis. It states that the volume integral of the divergence of a vector field A taken over any volume V is equal to the surface integral of A taken over the closed surface surrounding the volume V; that is,
∫∫∫ (∇.A)dv = ∫∫ A.ds ... viii

Figure A.1 a
Figure A.1 b

4. The Curl of a Vector Field
If A is a vector field, the curl, or rotation, of A is defined as the vector function of space obtained by taking the vector product of the operator ∇ and A. That is,
curl A = ∇ x A
= i (∂Az/∂y - ∂Ay/∂z) + j (∂Ax/∂z - ∂Az/∂x) + k (∂Ay/∂x - ∂Ax/∂y) ... ix

This may be written conveniently in the following determinantal form:
∇ x A = | i j k |
 | ∂/∂x ∂/∂y ∂/∂z | ... x
 | Ax Ay Az |

If A = ∇Φ,
∇ x A = ∇ x (∇Φ) = i (∂^2Φ/∂y∂z - ∂^2Φ/∂z∂y) ... xi

5. Stokes's Theorem
∮ A.dl = ∫∫ (∇ x A).ds ... xii

This relation is known as Stokes's Theorem. It states that the surface integral of the curl of a vector field A taken over any surface S is equal to the line integral of A around the periphery of the surface.

If the surface to which Stokes's theorem is applied is a closed surface, the length of the periphery is zero and then
∮ (∇ x A).ds = 0 .... xiii

By the use of Stokes's theorem we see that, if ∇ x A = 0 everywhere, A is the gradient of a scalar function, because if ∇ x A = 0, then the line integral of A around any closed curve vanishes. This is just the condition that A should be the gradient of a scalar function.
